If you\u2019re a grammar lover, maybe you\u2019ve found yourself fighting the urge to correct a misspelled menu item at a restaurant. Perhaps you\u2019ve even corrected a \u201cwho\u201d to a \u201cwhom\u201d when your friend used the wrong word in a story.   \r\n\r\nWhen it comes to grammar correction and education, where is the line between agitating and helping? As grammar lovers, our intentions are often good. We care about language and communication, and we want to make sure people are understood clearly. However, grammar lovers too often overstep the boundaries of appropriate correction.\r\n\r\nHere\u2019s a tip: Whenever you feel the urge to correct someone\u2019s grammar, take a step back and ask yourself: does this person want to be corrected? If the answer is no, or even just maybe,  keep your knowledge to yourself. Sometimes it\u2019s hard, especially if an error is repeated over and over again. But remember that an essential part of good communication is building good relationships, and correcting people\u2019s grammar without asking often hurts relationships rather than helping them.\r\n\r\nInstead of correcting others\u2019 grammar, try modeling good grammar in your own spoken and written communication. If people see and hear that you know how to construct a sentence correctly, they might reach out to you with questions about their grammar, spelling, and punctuation. These are your opportunities for education, not the moments when people make mistakes.\r\n\r\nAbove all, remember that communication\u2014correctly formatted or not\u2014is all about understanding. Give a little grace when it comes to grammar mistakes, and you\u2019ll find that your communication improves overall.","post_title":"Correcting People's Grammar: Just Don't Do It","post_excerpt":"","post_status":"publish","comment_status":"open","ping_status":"open","post_password":"","post_name":"correcting-peoples-grammar-just-dont-do-it","to_ping":"","pinged":"","post_modified":"2015-06-07 06:07:28","post_modified_gmt":"2015-06-07 13:07:28","post_content_filtered":"","post_parent":0,"guid":"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/?p=14631","menu_order":0,"post_type":"post","post_mime_type":"","comment_count":"0","filter":"raw"},{"ID":14627,"post_author":"1001005","post_date":"2015-06-03 08:01:25","post_date_gmt":"2015-06-03 15:01:25","post_content":"[media]&lt;img class=\"aligncenter size-full wp-image-14705\" src=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/wp-content\/uploads\/2015\/05\/cry-wolf.jpg\" alt=\"&quot;Cry wolf&quot; does not mean &quot;cry, wolf.&quot; \" width=\"600\" height=\"314\" \/&gt;[\/media]\r\n\r\nThese idioms about animals are the cat\u2019s meow. Here\u2019s a short list of animal-related idioms and what they mean:\r\n1. &lt;b&gt;Curiosity Killed the Cat:&lt;\/b&gt; asking a lot of questions can get you into trouble.\r\n&lt;i&gt;I\u2019d be careful with your investigation. Curiosity killed the cat, after all.&lt;\/i&gt;\r\n\r\n2. &lt;b&gt;Cry Wolf:&lt;\/b&gt; give a cry for help or alarm when there is no danger.\r\n&lt;i&gt;He cried wolf so many times before that when he was attacked, no one came to his rescue.&lt;\/i&gt;\r\n\r\n3. &lt;b&gt;Beat a Dead Horse:&lt;\/b&gt; talk about an idea or issue over and over again.\r\n&lt;i&gt;I don\u2019t mean to beat a dead horse, but I really think we need to go over our sales strategy again.&lt;\/i&gt;\r\n\r\n4.&lt;b&gt; Sick As a Dog: &lt;\/b&gt;incredibly sick.\r\n&lt;i&gt;I tried to get out of bed this morning, but I\u2019m sick as a dog.&lt;\/i&gt;\r\n\r\n5. &lt;b&gt;For the Birds:&lt;\/b&gt; something that is not worth one\u2019s time; unimportant.\r\n&lt;i&gt;Don\u2019t worry about those little details; they\u2019re for the birds.&lt;\/i&gt;\r\n\r\n6. &lt;b&gt;Smell a rat: &lt;\/b&gt;suspect inauthenticity or deliberate trickery.\r\n&lt;i&gt;He says he just wants to help our cause, but I smell a rat.&lt;\/i&gt;\r\n\r\nIf you want to learn more about idioms, &lt;a title=\"You Can Bet Your Bottom Dollar That We Love Idioms!\" href=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/2015\/you-can-bet-your-bottom-dollar-that-we-love-idioms\/?sf&amp;utm_campaign=Blog_Augment_1&amp;utm_source=Blog_org&amp;utm_medium=social&amp;utm_content=blog_link\" target=\"_blank\"&gt;read this post explaining idioms about money.&lt;\/a&gt;","post_title":"It's a Barnyard Full of Animal Idioms","post_excerpt":"","post_status":"publish","comment_status":"open","ping_status":"open","post_password":"","post_name":"its-a-barnyard-full-of-animal-idioms","to_ping":"","pinged":"","post_modified":"2015-06-03 08:01:25","post_modified_gmt":"2015-06-03 15:01:25","post_content_filtered":"","post_parent":0,"guid":"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/?p=14627","menu_order":0,"post_type":"post","post_mime_type":"","comment_count":"0","filter":"raw"},{"ID":12409,"post_author":"4","post_date":"2015-06-02 02:00:35","post_date_gmt":"2015-06-02 09:00:35","post_content":"[media]&lt;img src=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/wp-content\/uploads\/2015\/06\/Cheshire-Cat2.jpg\" alt=\"We are all mad here. Cheshire Cat Quotation Image\" width=\"600\" height=\"314\" class=\"aligncenter size-full wp-image-14689\" \/&gt;[\/media]\r\n\r\nCats make frequent literary appearances as fascinating, thought-provoking, and sometimes sneaky characters. From early nursery rhymes to contemporary tales, cats are omnipresent stars and sidekicks. To celebrate Adopt-a-Cat month in June, here are five famous cats that feature prominently in some &lt;a href=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/2015\/all-romance-no-grammar-lessons-we-can-learn-from-great-works-of-literature\/\"&gt;much-loved books&lt;\/a&gt;.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;The Cat in the Hat&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\n\r\n&lt;a rel=\"no follow\" href=\"https:\/\/www.flickr.com\/photos\/jdhancock\/5190887407\/sizes\/m\/\"&gt;&lt;img class=\"aligncenter size-full wp-image-12411\" src=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/wp-content\/uploads\/2015\/03\/Untitled3.png\" alt=\"cat, cats in literature, Grammarly\" width=\"500\" height=\"333\" \/&gt;&lt;\/a&gt;\r\n\r\nOne of the best-known title characters in children's literature, readers can easily identify the &lt;a rel=\"no follow\" href=\"http:\/\/www.theguardian.com\/childrens-books-site\/2014\/jan\/30\/pip-jones-top-10-cats-fiction\"&gt;Cat in the Hat&lt;\/a&gt; by his elongated body, striped hat, and red bow tie. In Dr. Seuss's story, the Cat is notorious for pushing boundaries and questioning the limits of his young charges while their parents are away. As a children's book, \"The Cat in the Hat\" tests readers' limits as well as their reading skills. Though this story is provocative, it's also a reading primer that's one of the best-selling children's books of all time.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Puss in Boots&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\n\r\n&lt;a rel=\"no follow\" href=\"http:\/\/www.thegreatcat.org\/origins-story-puss-boots\/\"&gt;Puss in Boots&lt;\/a&gt;, a classic fairy tale and now an animated movie, has its roots in a 16th-century fable by Italian author Giovanni Francesco Straparola. A 17th-century version of the story appeared in the first version of Mother Goose tales, making \"Puss in Boots\" one of the first nursery rhymes. Its basic premise focuses on the well-dressed cat prompting trickery and deceit to ensure riches and success for his undeserving master. The original story's emphasis on lying to get ahead has been adapted and downright ignored in later adaptations of the tale.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;The Cheshire Cat&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\n\r\nThe &lt;a rel=\"no follow\" href=\"http:\/\/flavorwire.com\/397030\/10-great-books-starring-cats\/3\"&gt;Cheshire Cat&lt;\/a&gt;, who appears in Lewis Carroll's \"Alice in Wonderland,\" is best known for his distinctive smile. In the story, this character can appear and disappear as he pleases, dissolving into nothing but a mischievous grin once he's had his say. This motif also appears in a carving in a 16th-century English church, believed to be the inspiration for Carroll's character. Though the cat has a knack\u00a0for volunteering sage advice amidst confusion, he offers puzzling chatter and mystical sayings almost as often. Nevertheless, the Cheshire Cat represents a pleasantly sane character in a bizarre world.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Mrs. Norris&lt;\/strong&gt;&lt;strong&gt;\u00a0&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\n\r\nThis feline character in the \"Harry Potter\" series has a supporting role as the pet of Argus Filch, the unpopular caretaker of Hogwarts School of Witchcraft and Wizardry. A true sidekick, &lt;a rel=\"no follow\" href=\"http:\/\/www.scholastic.com\/browse\/article.jsp?id=5802\"&gt;Mrs. Norris&lt;\/a&gt; is known to have a strong connection with Filch, and even looks like him, with a scrawny build, yellow eyes, and gray fur. Mrs. Norris acts as an excellent spy and even a tattletale at times, alerting her master to unusual events on the school grounds. Throughout the series, the title character and his classmates survive narrow escapes from the sneaky cat, whom they believe can see them even when they're wearing an invisibility cloak.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Richard Parker&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\n\r\nA big cat in the contemporary novel \"Life of Pi,\" Richard Parker co-stars as the Bengal tiger who accompanies the title character throughout the story. The tiger's name is somewhat of an inside joke, as it appears to be the result of improperly completed paperwork. Despite the mismatch, the name sticks as the story develops. Though the two characters aren't friends at the beginning of the novel, they learn to coexist with one another after surviving a sunken ship and a series of struggles on a lifeboat. At the end of hundreds of days stranded at sea, Pi and Richard Parker develop a friendship, lending a happy ending to this unique story.\r\n\r\nThroughout literature, cats serve as trusted companions and sneaky adversaries. Consider adopting one of your own, and create your unique adventure together. &lt;b&gt;What famous character would be your cat's namesake?&lt;\/b&gt;","post_title":"5 Famous Cats in Literature","post_excerpt":"To celebrate Adopt-a-Cat month in June, here are five famous cats that feature prominently in some much-loved books. Which is your favorite?\r\n","post_status":"publish","comment_status":"open","ping_status":"open","post_password":"","post_name":"5-famous-cats-in-literature","to_ping":"","pinged":"","post_modified":"2015-06-02 03:11:54","post_modified_gmt":"2015-06-02 10:11:54","post_content_filtered":"","post_parent":0,"guid":"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/?p=12409","menu_order":0,"post_type":"post","post_mime_type":"","comment_count":"0","filter":"raw"},{"ID":12473,"post_author":"4","post_date":"2015-05-29 00:00:58","post_date_gmt":"2015-05-29 07:00:58","post_content":"[media]&lt;img src=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/wp-content\/uploads\/2015\/05\/3r4t5gfd-670x352.jpg\" alt=\"6 Email Etiquette Tips that May Surprise You\" width=\"600\" height=\"314\" class=\"aligncenter size-large wp-image-14607\" \/&gt;[\/media]\r\n\r\nEmail correspondence is simple, easy, and convenient to quickly contact coworkers and family members across the world. However, it isn't all roses with email. If you don't use the proper etiquette, you can end up annoying your recipients. You've probably already heard the &lt;a href=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/2015\/navigating-email-etiquette-at-work\/\"&gt;basic email etiquette tips&lt;\/a&gt;, like use a specific subject line and reply as quickly as you can, but there is more you can do to ensure that your emails resonate with\u00a0the people you send them to.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Don't Be Sorry to Bother Someone&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\n\r\nWhen you start an email with, \"&lt;a href=\"http:\/\/www.businesswritingblog.com\/business_writing\/2012\/05\/surprising-reaction-to-sorry-to-bother-you-.html\" rel=\u201cnofollow\"&gt;sorry to bother you&lt;\/a&gt;,\" chances are that the recipient already feels bothered by that opening line. Those four words take seconds to read, seconds that the person could have used to find out the real point of your email. Furthermore, if you are sending a business email, you should never apologize for asking someone to do their job. You want to be polite, not obsequious.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Be Small-Screen-Friendly&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\n\r\n&lt;a href=\"https:\/\/www.flickr.com\/photos\/ilamont\/4329363938\/sizes\/m\/\"&gt;&lt;img class=\"aligncenter size-full wp-image-12475\" src=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/wp-content\/uploads\/2015\/03\/Untitled8.png\" alt=\"cell phone, mobile, Grammarly\" width=\"500\" height=\"375\" \/&gt;&lt;\/a&gt;\r\n\r\nEmail is not the exclusive realm of desktop computers and laptops. People are always on the go, and they read their emails on mobile phones and tablets. It isn't easy to read long blocks of text on a tiny screen. Keep that in mind when you're composing your messages. &lt;a href=\"http:\/\/www.feminiya.com\/10-tips-for-perfecting-your-email-etiquettes\/\" rel=\u201cnofollow\"&gt;Keep your paragraphs short&lt;\/a&gt; and your message brief. If you must send a longer message, give a succinct summary near the beginning of the email so the recipient knows what's in store and can go back to read the rest later.\r\n\r\nAlso, think about the font you use. Some artsy fonts may look fabulous on a computer screen, but they could strain the eyes on a mobile screen.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Think Before You CC&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\n\r\nA &lt;a href=\"http:\/\/www.lifehack.org\/articles\/communication\/following-email-etiquette.html\" rel=\u201cnofollow\"&gt;blogger for Lifehack&lt;\/a&gt; gives a personal experience with CC'ed emails: \"I'd say about 90% of messages I've received where I'm not in the To: field but the CC: field were completely and totally useless to me.\" Indeed, oftentimes those emails, intended to keep people in the loop, just end up being irritating white noise in the inbox.\r\n\r\nThis doesn't mean you should never CC anyone, but you should carefully consider who exactly needs the information you're about to send.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Use BCC for Bulk Mail&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\n\r\nWant to use email to invite the universe to your upcoming party? Maybe you want to share your vacation photos with a long list of people. Whatever reason you're sending out email in bulk, don't paste a novel-length block of email addresses at the top of a message. BCC everyone in your list. This also respects the privacy of your recipients, which is especially helpful if everyone who receives the email doesn't know each other.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Do Not Overload on Cuteness&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\n\r\nFancy graphics and fonts do not look good on all browsers and devices and can make an email difficult to read. If you cannot resist the urge to embellish, use small adorable touches that add character but don't overwhelm a message.\r\n\r\nAlong the same lines, if you are sending photographs, it's better to send them as an attachment rather than in the body of an email, so the email itself doesn't take a billion years to load.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Stick to Your Grammar Guns&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\n\r\nEmail is not as formal as a handwritten letter, but you should still &lt;a href=\"http:\/\/www.womansday.com\/relationships\/family-friends\/advice\/a3573\/8-rules-of-email-etiquette-68969\/\" rel=\u201cnofollow\"&gt;respect the English language&lt;\/a&gt;. Capitalize when needed, use punctuation, and give all your emails a read-through before you hit the send button. This applies to both business and personal emails.\r\n\r\nEmail isn't complicated, but there are some rules you need to follow to get the most out of it. Did any of these tips surprise you?","post_title":"6 Email Etiquette Tips that May Surprise You","post_excerpt":"","post_status":"publish","comment_status":"open","ping_status":"open","post_password":"","post_name":"6-email-etiquette-tips-that-may-surprise-you","to_ping":"","pinged":"","post_modified":"2015-06-04 08:19:00","post_modified_gmt":"2015-06-04 15:19:00","post_content_filtered":"","post_parent":0,"guid":"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/?p=12473","menu_order":0,"post_type":"post","post_mime_type":"","comment_count":"0","filter":"raw"},{"ID":14547,"post_author":"1001005","post_date":"2015-05-27 11:54:53","post_date_gmt":"2015-05-27 18:54:53","post_content":"[media]&lt;img src=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/wp-content\/uploads\/2015\/05\/ALUMNUS-ALUMNI-670x362.jpg\" alt=\"ALUMNUS-ALUMNI\" width=\"600\" height=\"314\" class=\"aligncenter size-large wp-image-14615\" \/&gt;[\/media]\r\n\r\nGraduation season is upon us. While some graduates will be asking the question, \u201cWhat should I do with my life?\u201d others will be asking something a little more straightforward: Am I an alumni, alumna, alumnae, or alumnus? While we can\u2019t tell you what the next steps on life\u2019s journey should be, we can certainly clear up any confusion around the second question. Here\u2019s a short explainer:\r\n\r\nMan: alumnus\r\nGroup of men: alumni\r\nWoman: alumna\r\nGroup of women: alumnae\r\nGroup of men and women: alumni \r\n\r\nIf you forget, just go with the standard shortening of all forms of the word: alum\/alums. \r\n\r\nTo learn more about different forms of the same root word, &lt;a href=\"https:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/2014\/conjugating-verbs-doesnt-have-to-make-you-tenseab\/\"&gt;read this post about lay\/lie\/laid&lt;\/a&gt;.\r\n","post_title":"Know Your Latin: Alumni","post_excerpt":"","post_status":"publish","comment_status":"open","ping_status":"open","post_password":"","post_name":"know-your-latin-alumni","to_ping":"","pinged":"","post_modified":"2015-05-27 11:54:53","post_modified_gmt":"2015-05-27 18:54:53","post_content_filtered":"","post_parent":0,"guid":"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/?p=14547","menu_order":0,"post_type":"post","post_mime_type":"","comment_count":"0","filter":"raw"},{"ID":14543,"post_author":"1001005","post_date":"2015-05-27 11:47:41","post_date_gmt":"2015-05-27 18:47:41","post_content":"[media]&lt;img src=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/wp-content\/uploads\/2015\/05\/INTERROBANGS.--670x362.jpg\" alt=\"How to use the interrobang punctuation mark\" width=\"600\" height=\"314\" class=\"aligncenter size-large wp-image-14611\" \/&gt;[\/media]\r\n\r\nFew punctuation marks have as exciting a name as the interrobang. But what does the interrobang do?\r\n\r\nThe &lt;strong&gt;interrobang&lt;\/strong&gt; combines the question mark (?) and the exclamation point (!) into a single punctuation mark. It conveys a question asked in an excited way. For example:\r\nAre you really coming over to my house on Friday?!\r\nYou can\u2019t be serious! You\u2019ve never seen an episode of Friends?!\r\nHe said what?!\r\n\r\nAlthough writers have used punctuation marks in combination in their writing for years, the interrobang was officially created by advertising executive Martin Speckter in 1962. \r\n\r\nMany typefaces don\u2019t include the interrobang with the superimposed question mark and exclamation point. However, you can get the same interrobang point across using the two marks next to each other like this: ?!\r\n","post_title":"Say What?! Meet the Interrobang.","post_excerpt":"","post_status":"publish","comment_status":"open","ping_status":"open","post_password":"","post_name":"say-what-meet-the-interrobang","to_ping":"","pinged":"","post_modified":"2015-05-27 11:47:41","post_modified_gmt":"2015-05-27 18:47:41","post_content_filtered":"","post_parent":0,"guid":"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/?p=14543","menu_order":0,"post_type":"post","post_mime_type":"","comment_count":"0","filter":"raw"},{"ID":12467,"post_author":"4","post_date":"2015-05-27 02:00:30","post_date_gmt":"2015-05-27 09:00:30","post_content":"Nobody writes a perfect first draft. Whether you love the red pen or hate it with a passion, your first draft will require some polishing. The trick is to write prose that's brilliant yet brief, colorful yet concise. Here are five tips for writing concisely.\r\n\r\n[media]&lt;img src=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/wp-content\/uploads\/2015\/05\/always-cut-it-out.1.jpg\" alt=\"always cut it out\" width=\"600 height=\"314\" class=\"aligncenter size-full wp-image-14649\" \/&gt;[\/media]\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Cut Weasel Words&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\n\r\nEven the best writers fall prey to weasel words. These pesky critters sneak into your writing, take up space, and\u00a0contribute nothing. &lt;a href=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/2013\/mistake-of-the-month-unnecessary-modifiers\/\"&gt;Common weasel words&lt;\/a&gt;\u00a0include the\u00a0following:\r\n&lt;ul&gt;\r\n\t&lt;li&gt;Really&lt;\/li&gt;\r\n\t&lt;li&gt;Basically&lt;\/li&gt;\r\n\t&lt;li&gt;Actually&lt;\/li&gt;\r\n\t&lt;li&gt;Very&lt;\/li&gt;\r\n\t&lt;li&gt;More&lt;\/li&gt;\r\n&lt;\/ul&gt;\r\nThey often attack in groups. Consider these weasely phrases:\r\n&lt;ul&gt;\r\n\t&lt;li&gt;\"The vast majority . . .\"&lt;\/li&gt;\r\n\t&lt;li&gt;\"Studies suggest that . . .\"&lt;\/li&gt;\r\n\t&lt;li&gt;\"Some would argue that . . .\"&lt;\/li&gt;\r\n&lt;\/ul&gt;\r\nAfter you write a draft, take time to banish all weasel words back to the wilderness\u00a0where they belong.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Axe Redundancy&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\n\r\nWhy do something twice if you only need to do it once? Redundant phrases clutter your writing and\u00a0hinder its flow.\r\n\r\nConsider the sentence: \"I love the blue color of her eyes.\"\r\n\r\n\"Blue color\" is redundant. Your reader already understands that blue is a color. \"I love her blue eyes\" is\u00a0more concise.\r\n\r\nAnother example: \"The reason I'm reading this is because I love writing.\"\r\n\r\nYou don't need the word \"reason\" and the word \"because\" in the same sentence. One implies the\u00a0other. \"I'm reading this because I love writing\" conveys your point with fewer words; it's more\u00a0concise.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Incorporate Transitional Words and Phrases&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\n\r\nTransitional words and phrases unify ideas and make your writing easy to understand. Without them,\u00a0your prose can come off as choppy and directionless.\r\n&lt;ul&gt;\r\n\t&lt;li&gt;Transitional words like &lt;i&gt;however&lt;\/i, &lt;i&gt;nevertheless&lt;\/i&gt;, and &lt;i&gt;conversely &lt;\/i&gt;pave the way toward your next idea.&lt;\/li&gt;\r\n\t&lt;li&gt;Transitional phrases like&lt;i&gt; for example&lt;\/i&gt; and &lt;i&gt;as shown by&lt;\/i&gt; help readers understand that you're clarifying an argument.&lt;\/li&gt;\r\n&lt;\/ul&gt;\r\nNext time you write, try to incorporate\u00a0one or more &lt;a href=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/handbook\/organization-and-development\/organizing-and-developing-your-ideas\/4\/transitional-words-and-phrases\/\"&gt;transitional words or phrases&lt;\/a&gt;\u00a0to improve cohesion.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Go Easy on the Adverbs&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\n\r\n&lt;a href=\"https:\/\/www.flickr.com\/photos\/cellardoorfilms\/7634010748\/sizes\/m\/\"&gt;&lt;img class=\"aligncenter size-full wp-image-12469\" src=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/wp-content\/uploads\/2015\/03\/Untitled7.png\" alt=\"red pen, Grammarly\" width=\"500\" height=\"335\" \/&gt;&lt;\/a&gt;\r\n\r\nSome writers resist adverbs the way a cat resists a leash. The grammar gods won't strike you down if you insert an &lt;a href=\"http:\/\/www.brainpickings.org\/2013\/03\/13\/stephen-king-on-adverbs\/\" rel=\u201cnofollow\"&gt;adverb&lt;\/a&gt; here or there, but before you do, read these two sentences:\r\n&lt;ol&gt;\r\n\t&lt;li&gt;Adverbs aren't horribly bad, but they're usually not needed.&lt;\/li&gt;\r\n\t&lt;li&gt;Your writing will be more concise if you avoid adverbs.&lt;\/li&gt;\r\n&lt;\/ol&gt;\r\nWhich sentence packs more of a punch? The one without adverbs \u2013 number two. Words that end in\r\n\"ly\" are often just &lt;a href=\"http:\/\/www.quickanddirtytips.com\/education\/grammar\/how-to-eliminate-adverbs\" rel=\u201cnofollow\"&gt;careless intensifiers&lt;\/a&gt;\u00a0that add no real meaning. Vivid writing shows the reader a picture; it doesn't\r\ntell the reader what to think.\r\n&lt;ul&gt;\r\n\t&lt;li&gt;\"I broke my diet again!\" Betty wailed sadly.&lt;\/li&gt;\r\n\t&lt;li&gt;\"I broke my diet again!\" Betty wailed, wiping salty chocolate tears from the corners of her mouth.&lt;\/li&gt;\r\n&lt;\/ul&gt;\r\nThe second example includes no adverbs, and it creates a clearer picture in the reader's mind than the first. We see the salty chocolate crumbs on\u00a0Betty's lips, and we feel her bittersweet anguish.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Skip \"There is\" and \"There are\"&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\n\r\nYou could be the most energetic person on earth, but writing \"There is\" or \"There are\" makes you look lazy. Consider rearranging your sentence parts when these &lt;a href=\"https:\/\/owl.english.purdue.edu\/owl\/owlprint\/539\/\" rel=\u201cnofollow\"&gt;passive&lt;\/a&gt; pairings pop up in your writing.\r\n&lt;ul&gt;\r\n\t&lt;li&gt;\"There are lots of people in this world who love bumpy cake\" becomes \"People around the world love bumpy cake.\"&lt;\/li&gt;\r\n\t&lt;li&gt;\"There is a stink bug in every room of my house\" becomes \"Stink bugs have descended upon every room of my house.\"&lt;\/li&gt;\r\n&lt;\/ul&gt;\r\nPlenty of writers, both amateur and professional, start their sentences with \"There is\" and \"There are.\"\u00a0You needn't be one of them.\r\n\r\nStephen King said, \"To write is human. To edit is divine.\" So the next time you find yourself wrestling with a few extra words, just remember: you're in excellent company. No matter which version of the story you prefer, the great thing about book-to-movie adaptations is that you can enjoy the story twice: once on the page and again on the screen. Here are three young adult series we recommend you read before you see their on-screen counterparts:\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;The &lt;i&gt;Divergent&lt;\/i&gt; series by Veronica Roth&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\nIn the film version of &lt;i&gt;Divergent&lt;\/i&gt;, the first book in the trilogy by Veronica Roth, Shailene Woodley does a wonderful job of conveying protagonist Tris Prior\u2019s determination and bravery as the world around her changes into something she barely recognizes. The movie keeps all the essential scenes from the book, but as movies often do, it compresses some of the relationships and plotlines that are more deeply developed in the novel. For this reason, pick up a copy of &lt;i&gt;Divergent&lt;\/i&gt; and give it a chance before you watch the movie. \r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;&lt;i&gt;The Hunger Games&lt;\/i&gt; series by Suzanne Collins&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\nThere\u2019s no denying that Jennifer Lawrence embodies the character of Katniss Everdeen on screen. But there\u2019s one character who gets a lot more development in the book series, particularly the first book, than in the movies: Gale, Katniss\u2019s best friend. In order to feel the true tension of the Katniss-Peeta-Gale love triangle, you must get to know Gale better. And to get to know Gale better, you must read the books.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;The &lt;i&gt;Chaos Walking&lt;\/i&gt; series by Patrick Ness&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\nThe most obvious reason you should read the &lt;i&gt;Chaos Walking&lt;\/i&gt; series before watching the movies is that the movies haven\u2019t been released yet! This young adult sci-fi series is set in a world where humans can hear each other\u2019s thoughts, which makes for a very loud and often chaotic society. It also makes the writing creative and colorful, which is why the book is worth a read before the movie makes it to the big screen. \r\n\r\nWhich book-to-movie adaptation is your favorite? ","post_title":"3 Young Adult Series You Should Read before You See the Movies","post_excerpt":"","post_status":"publish","comment_status":"open","ping_status":"open","post_password":"","post_name":"3-young-adult-series-you-should-read-before-you-see-the-movies","to_ping":"","pinged":"","post_modified":"2015-05-22 11:07:00","post_modified_gmt":"2015-05-22 18:07:00","post_content_filtered":"","post_parent":0,"guid":"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/?p=14537","menu_order":0,"post_type":"post","post_mime_type":"","comment_count":"0","filter":"raw"},{"ID":14531,"post_author":"1001005","post_date":"2015-05-22 10:44:55","post_date_gmt":"2015-05-22 17:44:55","post_content":"[media]&lt;img src=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/wp-content\/uploads\/2015\/05\/Be-your-own-grammar-doctor-670x362.jpg\" alt=\"using colons in your writing\" width=\"600\" height=\"314\" class=\"aligncenter size-large wp-image-14533\" \/&gt;[\/media]\r\n\r\nColons highlight the information that comes after them. In many cases, you can quickly bypass the information that comes before a colon and simply focus on what\u2019s written after it. For example:\r\nI have one thing to say: I love you.\r\n\r\nColons can also be used to present a list. For example:\r\nI wrote my grocery list this morning: olive oil, tomatoes, pasta, and cheese.\r\n\r\nAn independent clause is followed by a colon if the dependent clause is directly related to the independent clause. The dependent clause should clarify or give more information about the independent clause. For example:\r\nTake some leftovers with you: pasta salad or chicken or whatever you like.\r\n\r\nYou don\u2019t need to capitalize the beginning of the dependent clause after the colon unless it begins with a proper noun or an acronym, or it\u2019s a quote that originally began with a capital letter.\r\n\r\nThere are a few other uses for colons, such as:\r\nSeparating hours, minutes, and seconds:\r\n9:15 a.m.\r\n\r\nSeparating chapters and verses or acts and scenes in literature:\r\nJohn 3:16\r\n\r\nSeparating the speaker\u2019s name from a line of dialogue in a play or film:\r\nRayna: I love you, Deacon.\r\n\r\nAfter the salutation in a business letter:\r\nTo whom it may concern:\r\n\r\nTo learn more about punctuation, &lt;a href=\"https:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/2015\/three-things-you-need-to-know-about-semicolons\/\"&gt;read this blog post about semicolons&lt;\/a&gt;. ","post_title":"Colons: Here's What You Need to Know","post_excerpt":"","post_status":"publish","comment_status":"open","ping_status":"open","post_password":"","post_name":"colons-heres-what-you-need-to-know","to_ping":"","pinged":"","post_modified":"2015-05-22 10:44:55","post_modified_gmt":"2015-05-22 17:44:55","post_content_filtered":"","post_parent":0,"guid":"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/?p=14531","menu_order":0,"post_type":"post","post_mime_type":"","comment_count":"0","filter":"raw"},{"ID":14427,"post_author":"1001005","post_date":"2015-05-21 15:22:57","post_date_gmt":"2015-05-21 22:22:57","post_content":"[media]&lt;img src=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/wp-content\/uploads\/2015\/05\/Nothing-brings-two-people-together-like-sharing-a-great-book.-670x351.jpg\" alt=\"Nothing-brings-two-people-together-like-sharing-a-great-book.\" width=\"600\" height=\"314\" class=\"aligncenter size-large wp-image-14523\" \/&gt;[\/media]\r\n\r\nReading is often a solitary experience. But good books can bring people together in surprising and meaningful ways. Here are three books we think would be great to read with a close friend, or someone with whom you might want to become close friends:\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;&lt;i&gt;The Anne of Green Gables&lt;\/i&gt; series by Lucy Maud Montgomery&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\nThe friendship between Diana Barry and Anne Shirley in the Anne of Green Gables series is famous for providing us with the term \u201cbosom friend.\u201d Anne and Diana are friends through thick and thin, stirring up mischief and helping each other through life\u2019s struggles in equal measure. \r\nRead this book with: your \u201cbosom friend\u201d and reflect on what makes your relationship so special. \r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;&lt;i&gt;The Hobbit&lt;\/i&gt; by J.R.R. Tolkien&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\nNothing solidifies friendships quite like an epic adventure. Follow this group of friends and allies as they battle trolls, giant birds, and other surprising foes in their search for hidden treasure. \r\nRead this book with: a friend who is always up for adventure.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;&lt;i&gt;The Perks of Being a Wallflower&lt;\/i&gt; by Stephen Chbosky &lt;\/strong&gt;\r\nWhen you\u2019re a teenager, parents just don\u2019t seem understand what\u2019s important. But your friends do. Follow along as Charlie learns what it means to be a true friend. \r\nRead this book with: your BFF from high school.\r\n\r\nWhich books do you think friends (or book groups) should read together? Share your suggestions in the comments!","post_title":"3 Books to Read with Your BFF","post_excerpt":"","post_status":"publish","comment_status":"open","ping_status":"open","post_password":"","post_name":"3-books-to-read-with-your-bff","to_ping":"","pinged":"","post_modified":"2015-05-21 15:22:57","post_modified_gmt":"2015-05-21 22:22:57","post_content_filtered":"","post_parent":0,"guid":"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/?p=14427","menu_order":0,"post_type":"post","post_mime_type":"","comment_count":"0","filter":"raw"},{"ID":12397,"post_author":"4","post_date":"2015-05-21 02:00:25","post_date_gmt":"2015-05-21 09:00:25","post_content":"[media]&lt;img src=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/wp-content\/uploads\/2015\/05\/rephrase1.jpg\" alt=\"5 Memos that went terribly wrong\" width=\"600\" height=\"314\" class=\"aligncenter size-full wp-image-14433\" \/&gt;[\/media]\r\n\r\nIn the world of digital communications, pretty much everyone can relate to an email experience going terribly wrong at work. Ever hit \"reply all\" and sprayed a private message to a group of co-workers and lived to regret it?\r\n\r\nWriting internal business communications shouldn't be &lt;a href=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/2014\/email-presents-major-challenge\/\"&gt;fraught with peril&lt;\/a&gt;, but for these unfortunate executives, things went terribly wrong. From the ridiculous to the tragic, take a look at these five truly terrible business memos.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Western Union Internal Memo&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\n\r\n&lt;a href=\"https:\/\/www.flickr.com\/photos\/nostri-imago\/3407786186\/sizes\/l\"&gt;&lt;img class=\"aligncenter size-full wp-image-12399\" src=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/wp-content\/uploads\/2015\/03\/Untitled1.png\" alt=\"Western Union, Grammarly\" width=\"600\" height=\"400\" \/&gt;&lt;\/a&gt;\r\n\r\nGo back in time to 1876 for the first example of a really ridiculous internal memo (in hindsight). Western Union had a monopoly on fast communication; the telephone had just been invented in March of that year. In a bad move of epic proportions, the business executives at Western Union circulated a memo insisting that the telephone would not be a threat to its business. In part, the memo read,\u00a0&lt;em&gt;\"&lt;\/em&gt;This 'telephone' has too many shortcomings to be seriously considered as a means of communication.\"\r\n\r\nHow did that work out for Western Union? Ask yourself the last time you or anyone you know received a telegram. Enough said.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Greg McKay's Mumbling Memo&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\n\r\nYou probably don't know who &lt;a href=\"http:\/\/www.azcentral.com\/story\/opinion\/editorial\/2015\/02\/12\/greg-mckay-memo\/23316911\/\" rel=\u201cnofollow\"&gt;Greg McKay&lt;\/a&gt; is, but he's infamous at the Arizona Department of Child Safety. After Governor Ducey appointed Greg McKay to head the department, McKay sent his employees a memo that managed to be meandering, vainglorious, self-righteous, and insulting all at the same time. Check out these awesome quotes:\r\n&lt;ul&gt;\r\n\t&lt;li&gt;\"I will begin by announcing I am not worthy of this position. In fact, no one man or woman could fulfill this endeavor.\"&lt;\/li&gt;\r\n\t&lt;li&gt;\"Any projects or pilots that lead our staff down a path of potential lawbreaking will stop now.\"&lt;\/li&gt;\r\n\t&lt;li&gt;\"Please do not be afraid and know that all things done in good faith will be tolerated.\"&lt;\/li&gt;\r\n&lt;\/ul&gt;\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Law Firm's Diversity Memo&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\n\r\nDiversity in the workplace is an admirable goal and the writers of this &lt;a href=\"http:\/\/madamenoire.com\/424197\/invite-attorney-another-race-coffee-lunch-diversity-memo-gone-wrong\/\" rel=\u201cnofollow\"&gt;internal communication&lt;\/a&gt; should be commended for working to achieve that goal. But things went\u00a0downhill quickly. The memo encourages other employees to engage in activities with \"diverse hires\" to make them feel more welcome. The writer suggests some swell ideas: a 20-minute chat, a lunch invite, or even an invitation to a non-work event.\r\n\r\nSo far, so good\u2014and then the hammer drops. Employees are required to record and verify their activities with the HR department to prove they remain\u00a0compliant\u00a0with the new \"inclusivity policy.\" How's that for creating a warm and inclusive workplace?\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Microsoft's Layoff Memo&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\n\r\nMicrosoft boss, Stephen Elop, decided the best way to notify employees their jobs were on the line was with a &lt;a href=\"http:\/\/nymag.com\/daily\/intelligencer\/2014\/07\/microsoft-lays-off-thousands-with-bad-memo.html\" rel=\u201cnofollow\"&gt;mass memo&lt;\/a&gt;. Nothing revolutionary there, even though many would argue a more personal approach. Elop, however, badly mangled the job. The memo, which went out to 12,500 Microsoft employees, opened with a jocular \"Hello there,\" before &lt;a href=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/2015\/make-your-writing-clearer-6-tips-for-re-wording-sentencesa\/?lf\"&gt;rambling on&lt;\/a&gt;\u00a0for 14 paragraphs about Microsoft's role, goals, and future plans. The hapless employees had to read through 10 paragraphs before they hit the key bit\u2014notice of the layoff finally appears in paragraph 11.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Aviva Termination Memo&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\n\r\nAfter UK-based insurance company Aviva Investors fired an employee, the HR department &lt;a href=\"http:\/\/www.digitaltrends.com\/web\/when-email-goes-wrong-uk-company-mistakenly-dismisses-entire-workforce-in-memo-mix-up\/\" rel=\u201cnofollow\"&gt;prepared a memo&lt;\/a&gt;\u00a0for the unlucky worker with instructions for leaving the building. The memo included guidelines for turning over all company property and security passes and urged quick compliance. Unfortunately, the HR staffer sent that memo to the company's entire 1,300-member global workforce. It took over 30 minutes for the company to realize its error and send another &lt;a href=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/2015\/navigating-email-etiquette-at-work\/\"&gt;mass email&lt;\/a&gt; explaining the mistake.\r\n\r\nSo, what did we learn about writing effective memos from the examples above? Don't use your memo to predict the future; be concise; be kind; get to the point; and know your audience.\u00a0Easy, right?\r\n\r\nMay 21 is National Memo Day! Have you ever been on the receiving end of a ridiculous email or memo gone wrong? Let us know in the comments.","post_title":"5 Memos That Went Terribly Wrong","post_excerpt":"","post_status":"publish","comment_status":"open","ping_status":"open","post_password":"","post_name":"5-memos-that-went-terribly-wrong","to_ping":"","pinged":"","post_modified":"2015-05-18 09:43:27","post_modified_gmt":"2015-05-18 16:43:27","post_content_filtered":"","post_parent":0,"guid":"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/?p=12397","menu_order":0,"post_type":"post","post_mime_type":"","comment_count":"0","filter":"raw"},{"ID":12049,"post_author":"4","post_date":"2015-05-19 02:00:17","post_date_gmt":"2015-05-19 09:00:17","post_content":"[media]&lt;img src=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/wp-content\/uploads\/2015\/05\/3245trfd-670x351.jpg\" alt=\"5 Best Short Stories of All Time\" width=\"600\" height=\"314\" class=\"aligncenter size-large wp-image-14415\" \/&gt;[\/media]\r\n\r\nShort stories often go underappreciated, but they represent an art form few authors truly master. For readers, the short story is the perfect literary snack, a choice morsel that fills a spare hour, refreshes the brain, and gives a moment of escape from daily routines. When you need something to nibble and lack the time for a novel, feast your eyes on these tiny tomes with outsized impact.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;For Esme--with Love and Squalor&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\n\r\n&lt;a href=\"https:\/\/www.flickr.com\/photos\/42931449@N07\/6092122923\/sizes\/l\"&gt;&lt;img class=\"aligncenter size-medium wp-image-12051\" src=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/wp-content\/uploads\/2015\/02\/Untitled111-547x410.png\" alt=\"Image via Flickr by photosteve101\" width=\"547\" height=\"410\" \/&gt;&lt;\/a&gt;\r\n\r\nNo list of short stories would be complete without the work of JD Salinger and \"For Esme\" is one of his finest. First published in 1950 in\"The New Yorker\" and anthologized two years later, the story takes place in England during World War II and involves a soldier who meets an adolescent girl during a church visit in Devon. A year later, the soldier suffers a nervous breakdown in the weeks following V-E Day, but a letter from Esme, the young lady, inspires his recovery. This\u00a0&lt;a href=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/2015\/figurative-language-5-tools-to-spice-up-your-writing\/\"&gt;beautifully written&lt;\/a&gt;\u00a0story sings with a message of redemption.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Harrison Bergeron&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\n\r\nWhen life hands you lemons, why not wallow in a deeply &lt;a href=\"http:\/\/www.quickanddirtytips.com\/education\/grammar\/how-do-you-tell-if-something-is-true\"&gt;satirical&lt;\/a&gt; critique of authoritarian dystopia? This story, published in Kurt Vonnegut's \"Welcome to the Monkey House\" collection, is set in the year 2081 and features a society in which everyone is \"handicapped\" by the government to ensure equality among all. The beautiful must wear masks; the intelligent, a headset that blasts shrill sounds to interrupt their thinking. Harrison, the protagonist, rebels against the Handicapper General and attempts to overthrow the tyrannical government. The dark humor and tight journalistic style make this an easy, if somewhat disturbing, read.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;The Three Questions&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\n\r\nTolstoy is the recognized master of epic &lt;a href=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/2013\/writing-great-american-novel-top-three-mistakes-youll-make\/\"&gt;novels&lt;\/a&gt;, but few appreciate his skill with the short story. This story was first published in 1885 and is written in parable form. A king seeks answers to the three questions he considers most important in life and after receiving inadequate responses from the educated men in his kingdom, he looks to a wise hermit in a neighboring village. In an &lt;a href=\"http:\/\/www.quickanddirtytips.com\/education\/grammar\/irony-0\"&gt;ironic twist&lt;\/a&gt;, the king learns the answers to his questions as he helps the hermit care for a severely wounded man who shows up at the hermit's hut. This story is classic for its timeless answers and masterful way they are revealed.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;A Man From the South&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\n\r\nThis Roald Dahl masterpiece was published in 1948 in \"Collier's Magazine\" and was adapted for film in a 1960 episode of \"Alfred Hitchcock Presents\" starring Steve McQueen. In the story, Carlos, an old man, offers a young American his gorgeous green Cadillac if he can light his lighter 10 times in a row. The catch, however, is that if he cannot, the old man will chop off the Gambler's finger. The story involves a mysterious woman, a zinger of a &lt;a href=\"http:\/\/afterdeadline.blogs.nytimes.com\/?s=plot+twist\"&gt;plot twist&lt;\/a&gt;, and an unexpected ending, just the thing to perk up a tired brain.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;A Good Man Is Hard to Find&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\n\r\nIn this 1953 story, Flannery O'Connor tells the tale of a manipulative grandmother and her son, his wife, and his children who encounter a dangerous escaped criminal after their car overturns on a journey from Georgia to Florida. It is a stunning and disturbing story that deals with universal themes of cowardice, selfishness, redemption, and grace -- and coming to terms with a person's true self. The controversial final scene is the subject of endless scholarly debate and will be indelibly stamped in your brain once you've read it.\r\n\r\nNext time you need a quick refresher for a tired brain, pick up an anthology of short stories and curl up on the couch for an empowering hour of literary diversion. What's your favorite short story and why?","post_title":"Need a Pick-Me-Up? 5 Best Short Stories of All Time","post_excerpt":"","post_status":"publish","comment_status":"open","ping_status":"open","post_password":"","post_name":"need-a-pick-me-up-5-best-short-stories-of-all-time","to_ping":"","pinged":"","post_modified":"2015-05-18 08:39:21","post_modified_gmt":"2015-05-18 15:39:21","post_content_filtered":"","post_parent":0,"guid":"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/?p=12049","menu_order":0,"post_type":"post","post_mime_type":"","comment_count":"0","filter":"raw"},{"ID":14419,"post_author":"1001005","post_date":"2015-05-18 09:25:14","post_date_gmt":"2015-05-18 16:25:14","post_content":"[media]&lt;img src=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/wp-content\/uploads\/2015\/05\/IMMIGRATE-is-not-the-same-as-EMIGRATE.--670x351.jpg\" alt=\"IMMIGRATE is not the same as EMIGRATE.-\" width=\"600\" height=\"314\" class=\"aligncenter size-large wp-image-14421\" \/&gt;[\/media]\r\n\r\nThe difference between these two words is all about coming and going. When you immigrate, you\u2019re coming to a new country. When you emigrate, you\u2019re leaving your home country.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Immigrate:&lt;\/strong&gt; to move into a country from another one to stay permanently. \r\nMy ancestors &lt;strong&gt;immigrated&lt;\/strong&gt; to the United States sometime in the 1800s.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Emigrate:&lt;\/strong&gt; to leave the country in which one lives, especially one's native country, to reside elsewhere.\r\nMy ancestors &lt;strong&gt;emigrated&lt;\/strong&gt; from Denmark in search of a fresh start in another country.\r\n\r\nTo learn more about confusing word pairs, &lt;a href=\"https:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/2015\/confusing-words-versus-vs-verses\/\"&gt;read this post&lt;\/a&gt; about the difference between verses and versus. ","post_title":"Stop Confusing These Words: Immigrate and Emigrate","post_excerpt":"","post_status":"publish","comment_status":"open","ping_status":"open","post_password":"","post_name":"stop-confusing-these-words-immigrate-and-emigrate","to_ping":"","pinged":"","post_modified":"2015-05-18 09:25:14","post_modified_gmt":"2015-05-18 16:25:14","post_content_filtered":"","post_parent":0,"guid":"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/?p=14419","menu_order":0,"post_type":"post","post_mime_type":"","comment_count":"0","filter":"raw"},{"ID":14395,"post_author":"1001005","post_date":"2015-05-15 16:01:16","post_date_gmt":"2015-05-15 23:01:16","post_content":"[media]&lt;img src=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/wp-content\/uploads\/2015\/05\/cubism-670x352.jpg\" alt=\"international museum day \" width=\"600\" height=\"314\" class=\"aligncenter size-large wp-image-14397\" \/&gt;[\/media]\r\n\r\nMay 18 is &lt;a href=\"http:\/\/icom.museum\/events\/international-museum-day\/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\u201cnofollow\"&gt;International Museum Day&lt;\/a&gt;. Some of the world\u2019s greatest treasures and most valuable art are housed in museums, but you can also find smaller museums that cater to nearly every taste and interest. Here are five museums literature lovers might want to visit:\r\n\r\n&lt;a href=\"http:\/\/www.monroecountymuseum.org\/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\u201cnofollow\"&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Monroe County Museum in Alabama&lt;\/strong&gt;&lt;\/a&gt;\r\nLocated just down the street from the childhood home of Harper Lee, the author of the famous novel To Kill a Mockingbird, the Monroe County Museum is the home of the famous courtroom in which part of the novel was set. The museum offers school field trip tours and teacher workshops about the history and context of the novel. It is also open to the public. \r\n\r\n&lt;a href=\"http:\/\/www.poemuseum.org\/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\u201cnofollow\"&gt;&lt;strong&gt;The Museum of Edgar Allan Poe in Virginia &lt;\/strong&gt;&lt;\/a&gt;\r\nTake a spooky trip to the Museum of Edgar Allan Poe, which features some of the writer\u2019s letters and personal items, as well as first editions of some of his works. If you\u2019re a real Poe fan, you can even book your wedding at the museum and, as the website says, \u201clive happily evermore.\u201d \r\n\r\n&lt;a href=\"http:\/\/www.matchstickmarvels.com\/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\u201cnofollow\"&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Matchstick Marvels Tourist Center in Iowa&lt;\/strong&gt;  &lt;\/a&gt;\r\nThis quirky museum features models of famous buildings, modes of transportation, and more made out of (you guessed it) matchsticks! Matchstick Marvels features something special for literary fans: a model of Hogwarts School of Witchcraft and Wizardry from the Harry Potter series by J.K. Rowling made from 600,000 matchsticks. You\u2019ll have to see it to believe it.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;&lt;a href=\"http:\/\/en.wikipedia.org\/wiki\/National_Steinbeck_Center\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\u201cnofollow\"&gt;The National Steinbeck Center in California&lt;\/a&gt; &lt;\/strong&gt;\r\nFans of The Grapes of Wrath and East of Eden will love the National Steinbeck Center in Salinas, California. In addition to housing archives of the author\u2019s work, the museum also provides information about American agricultural workers, around whose experiences many of Steinbeck\u2019s novels are centered. \r\n\r\n&lt;a href=\"http:\/\/www.waltwhitman.org\/home\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\u201cnofollow\"&gt;&lt;strong&gt;The Walt Whitman Birthplace State Historic Site and Interpretive Center&lt;\/strong&gt;  &lt;\/a&gt;\r\nYou know you\u2019ve really made it when your childhood home is declared a historic site. The poet Walt Whitman\u2019s home is now a hot spot on the map and is dedicated to literacy and writing. The Center hosts writing workshops, poetry readings, and informational tours. \r\n\r\nDo lovers of the written word flock to a museum in your state or country? Share the details in the comments! ","post_title":"5 Must-Visit Museums for Literature Lovers","post_excerpt":"","post_status":"publish","comment_status":"open","ping_status":"open","post_password":"","post_name":"5-must-visit-museums-for-literature-lovers","to_ping":"","pinged":"","post_modified":"2015-05-15 16:01:16","post_modified_gmt":"2015-05-15 23:01:16","post_content_filtered":"","post_parent":0,"guid":"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/?p=14395","menu_order":0,"post_type":"post","post_mime_type":"","comment_count":"0","filter":"raw"},{"ID":11973,"post_author":"2","post_date":"2015-05-14 02:00:03","post_date_gmt":"2015-05-14 09:00:03","post_content":"&lt;b&gt;How the Grammar Girl Team Answers All Those Grammar Questions&lt;\/b&gt;\r\n\r\n&lt;i&gt;Guest post by Ashley Dodge&lt;\/i&gt;\r\n\r\nEnglish is a complex, complicated, and often confusing language. It seems as if everyone, at one time or another, needs help with grammar. As Grammar Girl\u2019s assistant, I\u2019m lucky enough to help people find the answers to their grammar questions sent in by e-mail, whether it\u2019s how to remember &lt;a href=\"http:\/\/www.quickanddirtytips.com\/education\/grammar\/affect-versus-effect\"&gt;\u201caffect\u201d or \u201ceffect,\u201d&lt;\/a&gt; or how to use the &lt;a rel=\"no follow\" href=\"http:\/\/www.quickanddirtytips.com\/education\/grammar\/how-to-use-semicolons\"&gt;semicolon&lt;\/a&gt;.\r\n\r\nWe get a lot of the same grammar questions, and we also get tons of tips and podcast ideas straight from the e-mail we receive. Mignon and I work as a team to make sure we provide the right, or best, answers to people\u2019s questions, and whenever I run into a particularly challenging or difficult question, I send it to Mignon.\r\n\r\nSometimes, however, there isn\u2019t an answer. This is frustrating not only for me, but for the person who asked the question. A popular and frustrating question people ask is why we say \u201cthe Eiffel Tower,\u201d but we don\u2019t say, \u201cthe Buckingham Palace,\u201d or why we say \u201cI\u2019m going to church,\u201d but not \u201cI\u2019m going to museum.\u201d\r\n\r\nThis is frustrating because we can tell you the answer to your specific question, and we can give you some guidelines about when to use &lt;a rel= \"no follow\" href=\"http:\/\/www.quickanddirtytips.com\/education\/grammar\/when-to-use-articles-before-nouns\"&gt;determiners&lt;\/a&gt; (\u201ca,\u201d \u201cthe\u201d), but we can\u2019t answer the question people want, which is \u201cWhy? Why do you need a determiner with countable singular nouns but not proper nouns?\u201d Often people want the answer to that \u201cWhy?\u201d question, and there isn\u2019t one. It\u2019s just one of the many examples of English\u2019s complexity, complications, and confusion.\r\n\r\n[media]&lt;img src=\"https:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/wp-content\/uploads\/2015\/05\/Ashley-Dodge-Partner-.jpg\" alt=\"Ashley Dodge Grammar Girl Post Quotation\" width=\"600\" height=\"314\" class=\"aligncenter size-full wp-image-14309\" \/&gt;[\/media]\r\n\r\nTo support or find my answers to grammar e-mail, I start with Grammar Girl, of course! I search the &lt;a rel= \"no follow\" href=\"http:\/\/www.quickanddirtytips.com\/grammar-girl\"&gt;Grammar Girl&lt;\/a&gt; website, newsletters, and books. If I can\u2019t find what I\u2019m looking for in any of those sources, I do a Google search; search the dictionary; look through &lt;i&gt;Garner\u2019s Modern American Usage&lt;\/i&gt;, the &lt;i&gt;Chicago Manual of Style&lt;\/i&gt;, the &lt;i&gt;AP Stylebook&lt;\/i&gt;, grammar blogs, books, or websites such as &lt;a href=\"https:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/\"&gt;Grammarly&lt;\/a&gt;; or search &lt;a rel= \"no follow\" href=\"https:\/\/books.google.com\/ngrams\"&gt;Google Ngram&lt;\/a&gt;, which shows how often words or phrases are used in published books.\r\n\r\nThe latter source is fascinating and many people don\u2019t know it exists. Grammar Girl has &lt;a rel= \"no follow\" href=\"http:\/\/www.quickanddirtytips.com\/education\/grammar\/google-ngram-and-impact-as-a-verb\"&gt;written&lt;\/a&gt; about it, and when I use Google Ngram to support or find my answer, this is usually the first time people have heard of it.\r\n\r\nYou might think responding to grammar questions would be easy, but sometimes it\u2019s difficult to craft a polite response to someone who, let\u2019s say, has had an ongoing debate about a specific grammar issue, and the side the writer has chosen is wrong. People don\u2019t like to hear that they\u2019re wrong or are going to lose a bet. I always try to keep a friendly, conversational tone. Mignon wants to provide a comfortable, supportive, and friendly place where you don\u2019t have to worry about us making a comment if you make a grammar mistake. That\u2019s what I try to create.\r\n\r\nWhat\u2019s the best part about answering grammar e-mail? It\u2019s rewarding to know you\u2019re helping people. I find that I\u2019ve learned more myself about grammar, language, usage, and writing just by answering people\u2019s questions.\r\n\r\n&lt;b&gt;About the Author&lt;\/b&gt;\r\n\r\n&lt;i&gt;Ashley Dodge is &lt;a rel= \"no follow\" href=\"http:\/\/www.quickanddirtytips.com\/grammar-girl\" target=\"_blank\"&gt;Grammar Girl\u2019s&lt;\/a&gt;\u00a0assistant, and a writer, social media manager, and lifestyle blogger. She lives in Reno, Nevada where she graduated from the University of Nevada, Reno in 2010 with a bachelor\u2019s degree in journalism.\u00a0 She enjoys knitting hats and having her pug model them. Lewis children&#039;s story quote\" width=\"600\" height=\"314\" class=\"aligncenter size-large wp-image-14295\" \/&gt;[\/media]\r\n\r\nOur memories of the books we read as children tend to stick fondly in our minds for years after we\u2019ve grown up. ","post_title":"21 Books to Read Before Your 21st Birthday","post_excerpt":"","post_status":"publish","comment_status":"open","ping_status":"open","post_password":"","post_name":"21-books-to-read-before-your-21st-birthday1","to_ping":"","pinged":"","post_modified":"2015-05-15 15:03:03","post_modified_gmt":"2015-05-15 22:03:03","post_content_filtered":"","post_parent":0,"guid":"https:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/?p=14269","menu_order":0,"post_type":"post","post_mime_type":"","comment_count":"0","filter":"raw"},{"ID":14197,"post_author":"1001005","post_date":"2015-05-13 00:00:10","post_date_gmt":"2015-05-13 07:00:10","post_content":"[media]&lt;img src=\"https:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/wp-content\/uploads\/2015\/05\/zombies_passive_voice-1204x650.png\" alt=\"How to ID passive voice with zombies tweet\" width=\"600\" height=\"314\" class=\"aligncenter size-full wp-image-14253\" \/&gt;[\/media]\r\n\r\nIf you\u2019re a grammar nerd, chances are you have an opinion about the passive voice. Whether you think it\u2019s acceptable or should be completely avoided, it\u2019s important to understand what passive voice is and how it\u2019s used. \r\n\r\n&lt;i&gt;Passive voice&lt;\/i&gt; occurs when the object of the action in the sentence becomes the subject of the sentence. The opposite of passive voice is &lt;i&gt;active voice&lt;\/i&gt;, in which the subject simply performs the action. Here are some examples:\r\n\r\nLayla threw the ball. (Active voice)\r\nThe ball was thrown by Layla. (Passive voice)\r\n\r\nWe corrected the errors. (Active voice)\r\nThe errors have been corrected. (Passive voice)\r\n\r\nSomeone took the last piece of cake. (Active voice)\r\nThe last piece of cake was taken. (Passive voice)\r\n\r\nActive voice tends to give writing more clarity, which is why many people prefer it. Writing in passive voice can be confusing, vague, and wordy; however, there are instances in which it\u2019s a better option than active voice. For example:\r\n\r\nIncidents of inappropriate behavior were reported. (Passive voice)\r\n\r\nIn this case, the focus of the sentence should be on the incidents, not on the people who reported them. In fact, there are situations in which the subject of the sentence might be unknown or unimportant. In those cases, sometimes using passive voice is preferable to using \u201csomeone\u201d as the subject. Another example:\r\n\r\nThe test results were delivered late in the afternoon. (Passive voice)\r\n\r\nAs in the sentence above, the focus of the sentence is on the test results rather than the person who delivered them.\r\n\r\nDo you have an opinion about passive voice? Share it in the comments!","post_title":"What's the Problem with Passive Voice?","post_excerpt":"","post_status":"publish","comment_status":"open","ping_status":"open","post_password":"","post_name":"whats-the-problem-with-passive-voice","to_ping":"","pinged":"","post_modified":"2015-05-13 06:31:54","post_modified_gmt":"2015-05-13 13:31:54","post_content_filtered":"","post_parent":0,"guid":"https:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/?p=14197","menu_order":0,"post_type":"post","post_mime_type":"","comment_count":"0","filter":"raw"},{"ID":14183,"post_author":"1001005","post_date":"2015-05-08 12:20:50","post_date_gmt":"2015-05-08 19:20:50","post_content":"[media]&lt;img src=\"https:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/wp-content\/uploads\/2015\/05\/43ew2wds1-670x352.jpg\" alt=\"43ew2wds1\" width=\"600\" height=\"314\" class=\"aligncenter size-large wp-image-14185\" \/&gt;[\/media]\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Faint:&lt;\/strong&gt; Lacking strength; inclined to swoon; lacking in courage, spirit, or energy; lacking distinctness; hardly perceptible. For example:\r\nDue to the summer heat, she began to feel dizzy and &lt;strong&gt;faint&lt;\/strong&gt;.\r\nIn the early morning hours, the sunlight is &lt;strong&gt;faint&lt;\/strong&gt; on the horizon.\r\nThe music in the background was &lt;strong&gt;faint&lt;\/strong&gt; and hardly perceptible. \r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Feint:&lt;\/strong&gt; A movement made to confuse the opponent, a dummy; that which is feigned; an assumed or false appearance; an offensive movement resembling an attack in all but its continuance. For example:\r\nAt the last minute, he &lt;strong&gt;feinted&lt;\/strong&gt; to the left and avoided his opponent\u2019s grasp.\r\nAfter an initial &lt;strong&gt;feint&lt;\/strong&gt; of moving North, she continued with her initial plan of heading East. \r\n\r\nTo learn more about homophones, &lt;a href=\"https:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/2015\/learn-the-difference-between-these-confusing-homophones\/ \"&gt;read this post about three confusing word pairs&lt;\/a&gt;. ","post_title":"Know Your Homophones: Feint and Faint","post_excerpt":"","post_status":"publish","comment_status":"open","ping_status":"open","post_password":"","post_name":"know-your-homophones-feint-and-faint","to_ping":"","pinged":"","post_modified":"2015-05-08 12:20:50","post_modified_gmt":"2015-05-08 19:20:50","post_content_filtered":"","post_parent":0,"guid":"https:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/?p=14183","menu_order":0,"post_type":"post","post_mime_type":"","comment_count":"0","filter":"raw"},{"ID":14147,"post_author":"1001005","post_date":"2015-05-08 07:42:42","post_date_gmt":"2015-05-08 14:42:42","post_content":"[media]&lt;img src=\"https:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/wp-content\/uploads\/2015\/05\/t5r4e2wq.jpg\" alt=\"MOTHER&#039;S DAY\" width=\"600\" height=\"314\" class=\"aligncenter size-full wp-image-14169\" \/&gt;[\/media]\r\n\r\nHappy Mother\u2019s Day! How are you celebrating? Breakfast in bed? A handmade card? For many people, an even bigger question than what to do for Mother\u2019s Day is where to place the apostrophe in Mother\u2019s Day.\r\n\r\nSome people write \u201cMothers\u2019 Day,\u201d based on the logic that it is the day to celebrate all mothers. Others simply write \u201cMothers Day,\u201d leaving out the apostrophe altogether possibly because they\u2019re unsure of where to place it.\r\n\r\n&lt;b&gt;The correct placement of the apostrophe is this: Mother\u2019s Day.&lt;\/b&gt;\r\n\r\nAnna Jarvis, who first celebrated Mother\u2019s Day in 1908, specifically wanted Mother\u2019s to be singular possessive. According to Jarvis\u2019s logic, each family ought to celebrate its own mother, instead of having the whole world celebrate mothers collectively. \r\n\r\nEven if your Mother\u2019s Day card has a misplaced apostrophe, chances are that mom will understand. Take some time today to thank and celebrate the mothers in your life. Happy Mother\u2019s Day, moms! Buying Mom a blouse at the mall might be convenient, but creating a heartfelt gift with your own hands means more.\r\n\r\n&lt;a href=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/2014\/mother-blog-posts\/\"&gt;Mother's Day&lt;\/a&gt; is May 10. Check out these five creative ways to help Mom understand just what she means on her special day.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Make a Homemade Craft &lt;\/strong&gt;\r\n\r\nMaybe\u00a0in grade school you crafted a fruit bowl or papier-m\u00e2ch\u00e9 sculpture for Mother's Day.
