Check out these craft ideas:\r\n&lt;ul&gt;\r\n\t&lt;li&gt;Make her a &lt;a href=\"http:\/\/tikkido.com\/node\/972\" rel=\u201cnofollow\"&gt;chocolate bouquet&lt;\/a&gt; using a vase, cardstock, bamboo skewers, and tape. And, of course, chocolate.&lt;\/li&gt;\r\n\t&lt;li&gt;Create a personalized decoupage flower-pot using favorite family photos or floral prints. You'll need a terra-cotta pot, Mod Podge, a brush, and your choice of photos or prints.&lt;\/li&gt;\r\n\t&lt;li&gt;Build a lovely\u00a0&lt;a href=\"http:\/\/littlebirdiesecrets.blogspot.com\/2009\/12\/washer-necklaces.html\" rel=\u201cnofollow\"&gt;necklace out of a washer&lt;\/a&gt;. Apply scrapbook paper and Diamond Glaze to the washer, then adorn with a leather cord.&lt;\/li&gt;\r\n\t&lt;li&gt;If you're feeling ambitious, visit the craft store and string your own special combination of beads on an elastic band. Try to incorporate at least three different types of beads\u2014seed beads, faceted glass, chevrons, heishi discs\u2014for the best look.&lt;\/li&gt;\r\n&lt;\/ul&gt;\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Make Homemade Beauty Products&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\n\r\nDoes your mother love the spa? If so, consider concocting your own version of department store beauty products at home. Whip up a handmade batch of &lt;a href=\"http:\/\/www.marthastewart.com\/271871\/bath-fizzies\" rel=\u201cnofollow\"&gt;bath fizzes&lt;\/a&gt; by Martha Stewart, heart-shaped soaps by According to Kelly, or Neapolitan Ice Cream Body Scrub by Craftster.org. Keep Pinterest in mind, as well; this DIY site offers a never-ending supply of crafty recipes and ideas.\r\n\r\nIf making crafts isn't your cup of tea, consider using pen and paper to create something special for Mom.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Write a Story Starring the Woman Herself&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\n\r\nDoes your mom enjoy reading? Do you have a flair for writing? If you answered yes to either of these questions, consider writing a story with Mom as the protagonist. True story, love story, crime story, &lt;a href=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/2015\/6-ways-to-celebrate-tell-a-fairy-tale-day\/?lf\"&gt;fairy tale&lt;\/a&gt;, a book of poetry\u2014you decide. Just be sure to write Mom a &lt;a href=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/2014\/stay-away-from-these-5-cliche-endings\/\"&gt;happy ending&lt;\/a&gt;.\r\n\r\nYou might even decide to &lt;a href=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/2013\/why-self-publish-instead-of-using-a-conventional-publisher\/\"&gt;self-publish your script&lt;\/a&gt;. Printing a book costs less than you might expect, and here at Grammarly, we offer proofreading help for free.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Assemble a Photo Collage&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\n\r\n&lt;a href=\"https:\/\/www.flickr.com\/photos\/footloosiety\/5484360883\/sizes\/m\/\"&gt;&lt;img class=\"aligncenter size-full wp-image-11991\" src=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/wp-content\/uploads\/2015\/02\/Untitled5.png\" alt=\"Grammarly, Mother's Day\" width=\"500\" height=\"334\" \/&gt;&lt;\/a&gt;\r\n\r\nPhoto collages stir warm memories and touch Mom's heart. Use the Internet resources to learn how to make &lt;a href=\"http:\/\/www.thecollegeprepster.com\/2012\/01\/tutorial-instagram-photostrips.html#comment-form\" rel=\u201cnofollow\"&gt;Instagram photo strips&lt;\/a&gt;, framed collages, and other &lt;a href=\"http:\/\/www.countryliving.com\/homes\/shopping\/photo-gifts#slide-1\" rel=\u201cnofollow\"&gt;gifts made from special photos&lt;\/a&gt;. Whether you throw a few sentimental pics into a frame, fill an album with a visual timeline of family togetherness, or assemble your own family genealogy project, Mom will surely appreciate your efforts. So will the rest of the family.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Buy a Unique Gift&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\n\r\nIf you're short on time or creativity, consider buying a unique gift for Mom made by someone else's hands. We're not talking about that photo print you spied at Wal-Mart. We're talking about a one-of-a-kind piece of art, home decor, jewelry, or clothing.\r\n\r\nThe Internet boasts a treasure trove of online sellers with unique wares. Of course, there's always\u00a0Etsy, but consider these alternatives:\r\n&lt;ul&gt;\r\n\t&lt;li&gt;ArtFire.com: Find homemade goods, art, and vintage products here.&lt;\/li&gt;\r\n&lt;\/ul&gt;\r\n&lt;ul&gt;\r\n\t&lt;li&gt;MadeItMyself.com:\u00a0Find jewelry, clothing, and unique trinkets here.&lt;\/li&gt;\r\n&lt;\/ul&gt;\r\n&lt;ul&gt;\r\n\t&lt;li&gt;UncommonGoods.com:\u00a0Find original artwork\u2014watercolors, acrylics, photos, posters\u2014here.&lt;\/li&gt;\r\n&lt;\/ul&gt;\r\nMothers toil 365 days a year to support their children, grandchildren, and other loved ones. However, this sweet Sunday in May comes but once a year. Will you write a poem, sing a song, or create something altogether different this Mother's Day?","post_title":"5 Creative Ways to Celebrate Mother's Day","post_excerpt":"","post_status":"publish","comment_status":"open","ping_status":"open","post_password":"","post_name":"5-creative-ways-to-celebrate-mothers-day","to_ping":"","pinged":"","post_modified":"2015-05-04 11:37:58","post_modified_gmt":"2015-05-04 18:37:58","post_content_filtered":"","post_parent":0,"guid":"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/?p=11989","menu_order":0,"post_type":"post","post_mime_type":"","comment_count":"0","filter":"raw"},{"ID":12037,"post_author":"4","post_date":"2015-05-06 00:00:45","post_date_gmt":"2015-05-06 07:00:45","post_content":"[media]&lt;img src=\"https:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/wp-content\/uploads\/2015\/05\/54rtfd-670x352.jpg\" alt=\"54rtfd\" width=\"600\" height=\"314\" class=\"aligncenter size-large wp-image-14101\" \/&gt;[\/media]\r\n\r\nSome people let poetry intimidate them because they get the idea that &lt;a href=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/2014\/finding-rhyme-reason-national-poetry-month\/\"&gt;poetry&lt;\/a&gt; is all about rhyming, iambic pentameter, and obscure language. While it is true that some of the most beautiful poems are also difficult to understand, poetry in general isn't that tough. By following some simple guidelines, you can pen verses that are poignant, pure, and easy on the ears.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Employ Imagery&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\n\r\n&lt;a href=\"https:\/\/www.flickr.com\/photos\/pedrosimoes7\/8935913893\/sizes\/m\/\"&gt;&lt;img class=\"aligncenter size-medium wp-image-12039\" src=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/wp-content\/uploads\/2015\/02\/Untitled10-402x410.png\" alt=\"Image via Flickr by pedrosimoes7\" width=\"402\" height=\"410\" \/&gt;&lt;\/a&gt;\r\n\r\nEvoke, don't emote. You can tell someone \"I'm sad\" a million times, but the person will have a hard time empathizing if the words don't have anything to back them up. Imagery, like &lt;a href=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/2014\/knowledge-is-power-using-idioms-to-give-power-to-your-writing\/\"&gt;idioms&lt;\/a&gt;, takes an abstract idea, such as an emotion, and turns it into something concrete. In the mind and heart of the reader, it goes back to being something abstract. As an exercise, try writing down all the items you can think of that you associate with a certain emotion. You could also take a walk and jot down how the sight of certain things makes you feel. Use those images in your next poem.\r\n\r\n&lt;a href=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/handbook\/organization-and-development\/text-level-measurements-adequate-writing\/6\/cliches\/\"&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Avoid Clich\u00e9s&lt;\/strong&gt;&lt;\/a&gt;\r\n\r\nClich\u00e9s are someone else's words. Use them, and you steal away your poetry's true potential. Clich\u00e9s might be convenient to use, and some are easy to find rhymes for, but the use of clich\u00e9s will make your readers turn away the moment they stumble upon those trite and tired truisms.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Keep the Reins on Your Rhymes&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\n&lt;p style=\"padding-left: 60px;\"&gt;\"No more rhymes now, I mean it!\"&lt;\/p&gt;\r\n&lt;p style=\"padding-left: 60px;\"&gt;\"Anybody want a peanut?\"&lt;\/p&gt;\r\nVizzini from \"The Princess Bride\" may have been inconceivably mean, but he did have a valid point when he told Fezzik to stop rhyming. That isn't to say that all rhyming in poetry is bad; it has its time and place, but you should &lt;a href=\"http:\/\/grammar.yourdictionary.com\/grammar-rules-and-tips\/tips-on-writing-poems.html\" rel=\u201cnofollow\"&gt;never rhyme simply for the sake of it&lt;\/a&gt;. Don't sacrifice the meaning of your poem because you feel obligated to rhyme. The &lt;a href=\"http:\/\/www.writing.com\/main\/view_item\/item_id\/1537612-Basic-Rules-to-Writing-Poetry\" rel=\u201cnofollow\"&gt;same principles apply to metering&lt;\/a&gt;. There is something enthralling about a sonnet in perfect iambic pentameter, but only use forms like that if it truly fits what you're writing about.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Pay Attention to Form&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\n\r\nEven poets who write free verse may choose to stick to a certain form. They may try to make all their lines about the same length, or they may vary the length of each line so the poem has a certain shape. You should consider those things, too. Also, think about how you use empty space in a poem. The way you present your work on paper contributes to the poem's overall message.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Don't Overdo Anything&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\n\r\n&lt;a href=\"http:\/\/education-portal.com\/academy\/lesson\/poetic-devices-definition-types-examples.html\" rel=\u201cnofollow\"&gt;Poetic devices&lt;\/a&gt; like similes, alliteration, repetition, anapest, and onomatopoeia are a great way to add impact to your writing, but overuse can make your poem seem silly. For example, if you want to convey a serious message but start most words in your poem with the letter \"b,\" that will distract readers from what you want to say.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Bare Yourself&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\n\r\nNo, poets don't have to be streakers. However, you should bear in mind that the more honest you are in your poetry, the better. As\u00a0&lt;a href=\"http:\/\/freelance-writing.lovetoknow.com\/Rules_for_Writing_Poetry\" rel=\u201cnofollow\"&gt;lovetoknow.com&lt;\/a&gt; points out, \"This is probably the only rule that you should rely on all the time. If you're not honest with yourself in your poetry, no one else is going to feel anything genuine when they read your work. It can be painfully terrifying to open up and honestly express your feelings on paper, but that's the name of the game.\"\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Learn the Rules and Break Them&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\n\r\nThe above tips are by no means the laws of poetry. Poetry is anarchy. You can do pretty much anything you want with it. However, you should learn as much as you can about it so you can make wise decisions about how to express yourself. For example, you might discover that a sestina was the perfect way to commemorate your anniversary.\r\n\r\nAre you a poet? Share some of your favorite methods for penning gorgeous verse.","post_title":"7 Fundamental Rules of Poetry","post_excerpt":"","post_status":"publish","comment_status":"open","ping_status":"open","post_password":"","post_name":"7-fundamental-rules-of-poetry","to_ping":"","pinged":"","post_modified":"2015-05-13 08:44:42","post_modified_gmt":"2015-05-13 15:44:42","post_content_filtered":"","post_parent":0,"guid":"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/?p=12037","menu_order":0,"post_type":"post","post_mime_type":"","comment_count":"0","filter":"raw"},{"ID":14071,"post_author":"1001005","post_date":"2015-05-05 11:53:58","post_date_gmt":"2015-05-05 18:53:58","post_content":"[media]&lt;img src=\"https:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/wp-content\/uploads\/2015\/04\/Punography-PEAR-670x352.jpg\" alt=\"Punography-PEAR\" width=\"600\" height=\"314\" class=\"aligncenter size-large wp-image-14143\" \/&gt;[\/media]\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Pair:&lt;\/strong&gt; two of something, usually that are similar or go together. For example:\r\nI\u2019m going to buy a new &lt;strong&gt;pair&lt;\/strong&gt; of shoes.\r\nJohnny and Sally make a cute &lt;strong&gt;pair&lt;\/strong&gt;.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Pear:&lt;\/strong&gt; a fruit. For example:\r\nRufus is eating an apple, and I am eating a &lt;strong&gt;pear&lt;\/strong&gt;.\r\nThe &lt;strong&gt;pear&lt;\/strong&gt; was ripe and juicy.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Pare:&lt;\/strong&gt; trim (something) by cutting away its outer edges; cut the skin off of something; reduce (something) in size, extent, quantity, or number, usually in a number of small successive stages. For example:\r\nEach summer he &lt;strong&gt;pares&lt;\/strong&gt; a large number of branches from the trees in the backyard.\r\nWhen making an apple pie, &lt;strong&gt;pare&lt;\/strong&gt; the skin off the apples first.\r\nThey have significantly &lt;strong&gt;pared&lt;\/strong&gt; down the number of people on their team.\r\n\r\nNow that you know the difference between these words see if this sentence makes sense to you: Before I eat them, I will &lt;strong&gt;pare&lt;\/strong&gt; this &lt;strong&gt;pair&lt;\/strong&gt; of &lt;strong&gt;pears&lt;\/strong&gt;.\r\n\r\nTo learn more about confusing words, &lt;a href=\"https:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/2014\/3-same-sounding-word-pairs-that-you-need-to-get-straight\/?sf&amp;utm_campaign=Blog_Augment_1&amp;utm_source=Blog_org&amp;utm_medium=text_link\"&gt;read this post about word pairs&lt;\/a&gt;.\r\n","post_title":"Learn Your Homophones: Pear, Pair, and Pare","post_excerpt":"","post_status":"publish","comment_status":"open","ping_status":"open","post_password":"","post_name":"learn-your-homophones-pear-pair-and-pare","to_ping":"","pinged":"","post_modified":"2015-05-05 11:53:58","post_modified_gmt":"2015-05-05 18:53:58","post_content_filtered":"","post_parent":0,"guid":"https:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/?p=14071","menu_order":0,"post_type":"post","post_mime_type":"","comment_count":"0","filter":"raw"},{"ID":12027,"post_author":"4","post_date":"2015-05-05 00:00:26","post_date_gmt":"2015-05-05 07:00:26","post_content":"[media]&lt;img src=\"https:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/wp-content\/uploads\/2015\/05\/A-wonderful-bird-is-the-pelican.jpg\" alt=\"A wonderful bird is the pelican\" width=\"600\" height=\"314\" class=\"aligncenter size-full wp-image-14173\" \/&gt;[\/media]\r\nEdward Lear's first influential limerick collection, &lt;i&gt;A\u00a0Book of Nonsense&lt;\/i&gt;, hit bookstore shelves nearly 200 years ago. &lt;a href=\"http:\/\/www.poets.org\/poetsorg\/poet\/edward-lear\" rel=\u201cnofollow\"&gt;Lear didn't invent the limerick&lt;\/a&gt;, however; the snappy five-line poems probably sprang to life on the streets and in the taverns of 14th century Britain. Over time, people from all walks of life -- children, scholars, drunks, beggars -- have delighted in the witty limerick. Here's a brief history of five of the world's best-loved limericks.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Hickory Dickory Dock&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\n\r\nHickory Dickory Dock showed up in \"&lt;a href=\"http:\/\/www.bl.uk\/collection-items\/tommy-thumbs-pretty-song-book\" rel=\u201cnofollow\"&gt;Tommy Thumb's Pretty Songbook&lt;\/a&gt;\" in 1744. The famed tale of the timepiece-dwelling mouse appeared beside\u00a0\"Bah Bah, Blacksheep\" and \"Lady Bird, Lady Bird,\" among others. No one knows for certain who penned the book, but it was one of the world's first attempts to entertain children with literature.\r\n&lt;p style=\"padding-left: 60px;\"&gt;\"Hickory dickory dock.&lt;\/p&gt;\r\n&lt;p style=\"padding-left: 60px;\"&gt;The mouse ran up the clock.&lt;\/p&gt;\r\n&lt;p style=\"padding-left: 60px;\"&gt;The clock struck one,&lt;\/p&gt;\r\n&lt;p style=\"padding-left: 60px;\"&gt;And down he run.&lt;\/p&gt;\r\n&lt;p style=\"padding-left: 60px;\"&gt;Hickory dickory dock.\"&lt;\/p&gt;\r\n&lt;strong&gt;The Man from Nantucket&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\n\r\nThe Man from Nantucket serves as inspiration for limericks both dirty and pure. Perhaps you've even heard a \"Rated X\" ending to this story-starter. However, the original Nantucket limerick was quite tame. It appeared in Princeton University's humor magazine, the \"&lt;a href=\"http:\/\/www.tigermag.com\/about-us\/\" rel=\u201cnofollow\"&gt;Princeton Tiger&lt;\/a&gt;,\" in 1902:\r\n&lt;p style=\"padding-left: 60px;\"&gt;\"There once was a man from Nantucket&lt;\/p&gt;\r\n&lt;p style=\"padding-left: 60px;\"&gt;Who kept all his cash in a bucket.&lt;\/p&gt;\r\n&lt;p style=\"padding-left: 60px;\"&gt;But his daughter, named Nan,&lt;\/p&gt;\r\n&lt;p style=\"padding-left: 60px;\"&gt;Ran away with a man,&lt;\/p&gt;\r\n&lt;p style=\"padding-left: 60px;\"&gt;And as for the bucket, Nantucket.\"&lt;\/p&gt;\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Speaking of Geography . . . &lt;\/strong&gt;\r\n\r\nPrinceton wasn't the only publisher of geographically themed limericks. Lear chose Peru as his muse at least once, as shown by the following:\r\n&lt;p style=\"padding-left: 60px;\"&gt;\"There was an Old Man of Peru&lt;\/p&gt;\r\n&lt;p style=\"padding-left: 60px;\"&gt;Who watched his wife making a stew.&lt;\/p&gt;\r\n&lt;p style=\"padding-left: 60px;\"&gt;But once, by mistake,&lt;\/p&gt;\r\n&lt;p style=\"padding-left: 60px;\"&gt;In a stove she did bake&lt;\/p&gt;\r\n&lt;p style=\"padding-left: 60px;\"&gt;That unfortunate Man of Peru.\"&lt;\/p&gt;\r\nLear's story of the unlucky Peru gent blazed a path for hundreds of amateur Peru poems to come, many of which are less than chaste. Middle schoolers excel at crafting this kind of literature, from what we hear. Perhaps a Peru limerick or two lingers in your own adolescent memory.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Shakespearean Limerick&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\n\r\n&lt;a href=\"https:\/\/www.flickr.com\/photos\/tonynetone\/2688212829\/sizes\/m\/\" rel=\u201cnofollow\"&gt;&lt;img class=\"aligncenter size-medium wp-image-12029\" src=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/wp-content\/uploads\/2015\/02\/Untitled9-409x410.png\" alt=\"Shakespeare, limerick, Grammarly\" width=\"409\" height=\"410\" \/&gt;&lt;\/a&gt;\r\n\r\nEven William Shakespeare practiced what some would call the &lt;a href=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/2014\/limericks-lowest-form-poetry\/\"&gt;lowest form of poetry&lt;\/a&gt;. The following limerick about imbibing spirits appeared in \"Othello, Act II, Scene III\":\r\n&lt;p style=\"padding-left: 60px;\"&gt;\"And let me the canakin clink, clink.&lt;\/p&gt;\r\n&lt;p style=\"padding-left: 60px;\"&gt;And let me the canakin clink.&lt;\/p&gt;\r\n&lt;p style=\"padding-left: 60px;\"&gt;A soldier's a man.&lt;\/p&gt;\r\n&lt;p style=\"padding-left: 60px;\"&gt;A life's but a span.&lt;\/p&gt;\r\n&lt;p style=\"padding-left: 60px;\"&gt;Why, then, let a soldier drink.\"&lt;\/p&gt;\r\n&lt;p style=\"padding-left: 60px;\"&gt;The Bard also used limericks in \"King Lear\"\u00a0and \"The Tempest&lt;i&gt;.\"&lt;\/i&gt;&lt;\/p&gt;\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Ogden Nash&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\n\r\nPoet Ogden Nash coined the phrase, \"Candy is dandy, but liquor is quicker.\" Some limerick fans insist he wrote the following limerick about a pelican:\r\n&lt;p style=\"padding-left: 60px;\"&gt;\"A wonderful bird is the pelican,&lt;\/p&gt;\r\n&lt;p style=\"padding-left: 60px;\"&gt;His bill can hold more than his beli-can.&lt;\/p&gt;\r\n&lt;p style=\"padding-left: 60px;\"&gt;He can take in his beak&lt;\/p&gt;\r\n&lt;p style=\"padding-left: 60px;\"&gt;Food enough for a week&lt;\/p&gt;\r\n&lt;p style=\"padding-left: 60px;\"&gt;But I'm damned if I see how the heli-can.\"&lt;\/p&gt;\r\nThis clever verse has also been attributed to Dixon Lanier Merritt, a humorist who lived at the same time. Nash died in 1971 and Merritt in 1972.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Crafting a Limerick&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\n\r\nLimericks are \"closed form\" poems that adhere to a strict template. Want to write your own? Follow these\u00a0guidelines:\r\n&lt;ul&gt;\r\n\t&lt;li&gt;The last word in lines 1, 2, and 5 must rhyme and contain 8-9 syllables each.&lt;\/li&gt;\r\n&lt;\/ul&gt;\r\n&lt;ul&gt;\r\n\t&lt;li&gt;The last word in lines 3 and 4 must rhyme and contain 5-6 syllables each.&lt;\/li&gt;\r\n&lt;\/ul&gt;\r\nOf course, what fun are rules unless they're broken -- or at least bent -- every once in a while? Consider this limerick by &lt;a href=\"http:\/\/www.smbc-comics.com\/?id=3201\" rel=\u201cnofollow\"&gt;Zach Weiner&lt;\/a&gt; of the comic \"Saturday Morning Breakfast Cereal,\" which coherently flows both backward and forward:\r\n&lt;p style=\"padding-left: 60px;\"&gt;\"This limerick goes in reverse&lt;\/p&gt;\r\n&lt;p style=\"padding-left: 60px;\"&gt;Unless I'm remiss&lt;\/p&gt;\r\n&lt;p style=\"padding-left: 60px;\"&gt;The neat thing is this:&lt;\/p&gt;\r\n&lt;p style=\"padding-left: 60px;\"&gt;If you start from the bottom-most verse&lt;\/p&gt;\r\n&lt;p style=\"padding-left: 60px;\"&gt;This limerick's not any worse.\"&lt;\/p&gt;\r\nNow we challenge you to write your own limerick. It's fast, easy, and incredibly satisfying. What will yours be about?","post_title":"The 5 Most Famous Limericks and Their Histories","post_excerpt":"","post_status":"publish","comment_status":"open","ping_status":"open","post_password":"","post_name":"five-most-famous-limericks-and-their-histories","to_ping":"","pinged":"","post_modified":"2015-05-08 13:33:06","post_modified_gmt":"2015-05-08 20:33:06","post_content_filtered":"","post_parent":0,"guid":"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/?p=12027","menu_order":0,"post_type":"post","post_mime_type":"","comment_count":"0","filter":"raw"},{"ID":14067,"post_author":"1001005","post_date":"2015-05-04 12:19:40","post_date_gmt":"2015-05-04 19:19:40","post_content":"[media]&lt;img src=\"https:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/wp-content\/uploads\/2015\/04\/r4edwsq-670x352.jpg\" alt=\"r4edwsq\" width=\"600\" height=\"314\" class=\"aligncenter size-large wp-image-14117\" \/&gt;[\/media]\r\n\r\nOur favorite authors create worlds, characters, and relationships that feel real to us. Here are four groups of siblings from literature we wish we were related to:\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;The March sisters in &lt;i&gt;Little Women&lt;\/i&gt; by Louisa May Alcott&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\nMeg, Jo, Beth, and Amy March show us what true sisterhood is all about. They make up each other's worlds, acting as playmates, enemies, counselors, and friends. Like many sisters, they could be arguing over a pair of shoes one minute and bonding over a family tragedy the next. I\u2019d act out a play in the attic with the March sisters any day.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;The Weasley siblings in the &lt;i&gt;Harry Potter&lt;\/i&gt; series by J.K. Rowling&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\nGinny, Ron, Fred, George, Percy, Charlie, and Bill weren\u2019t always on the best terms (ahem, Percy\u2019s stint in the Ministry of Magic), but in the end, they have each other\u2019s backs. Rowling paints a noisy, cozy picture of the Weasley household in the &lt;i&gt;Harry Potter&lt;\/i&gt; series, with Fred and George teasing their siblings constantly and Mrs. Weasley riding the line between total exasperation and complete control. The Weasleys are wild, but in a fight against Voldemort, you couldn\u2019t ask for anyone better on your team.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Marianne and Elinor Dashwood in &lt;i&gt;Sense and Sensibility&lt;\/i&gt; by Jane Austen&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\nIn matters of the heart, Marianne and Elinor don\u2019t always see eye to eye. In a way, their very different perspectives end up bringing them closer together. Marianne and Elinor\u2019s relationship mirrors what many siblings go through as they grow up together: establishing an understanding of who your siblings are and respecting them, even if they\u2019re very different from the way you want them to be. If I had to move to a cozy cottage in the country, I\u2019d want the Dashwood sisters with me to add both sense and sensibility.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;The Alden siblings in the &lt;i&gt;Boxcar Children&lt;\/i&gt; series by Gertrude Chandler Warner&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\nHenry, Jessie, Violet, and Benny Alden know how to sniff out a mystery...and how to solve it, too. Adopted by their kind, wealthy grandfather, the Alden siblings are free to solve mysteries, have adventures, and generally explore the world to their hearts\u2019 content. If you slid open the door of an abandoned boxcar and found these kids, it would be a pleasant surprise.  \r\n\r\nWho are your favorite siblings from literature? Let us know in the comments!","post_title":"4 Fictional Families We Wish We Were Born Into","post_excerpt":"","post_status":"publish","comment_status":"open","ping_status":"open","post_password":"","post_name":"4-fictional-families-we-wish-we-were-born-into","to_ping":"","pinged":"","post_modified":"2015-05-04 12:19:40","post_modified_gmt":"2015-05-04 19:19:40","post_content_filtered":"","post_parent":0,"guid":"https:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/?p=14067","menu_order":0,"post_type":"post","post_mime_type":"","comment_count":"0","filter":"raw"},{"ID":14061,"post_author":"1001005","post_date":"2015-05-04 12:12:57","post_date_gmt":"2015-05-04 19:12:57","post_content":"[media]&lt;img src=\"https:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/wp-content\/uploads\/2015\/04\/Similes_are_like_metaphors-670x352.jpg\" alt=\"Similes_are_like_metaphors\" width=\"600\" height=\"314\" class=\"aligncenter size-large wp-image-14113\" \/&gt;[\/media]\r\n\r\nSimiles and metaphors are both used to make comparisons or elucidate concepts. \r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Metaphors&lt;\/strong&gt; simply state a comparison. &lt;strong&gt;Similes&lt;\/strong&gt; use the words \u201clike\u201d or \u201cas\u201d to compare things. Because they both make comparisons, all similes are metaphors, but not all metaphors are similes. \r\n\r\nHere are some examples of similes and metaphors:\r\n\r\nLife is like a box of chocolates. (Simile)\r\nMy life is an open book. (Metaphor)\r\nThat baby is as cute as a button! (Simile)\r\nBaby, you\u2019re a firework. (Metaphor)\r\n \r\nBoth similes and metaphors add color and depth to language. Share your favorite similes and metaphors in the comments!","post_title":"What's the Difference between a Simile and a Metaphor?","post_excerpt":"","post_status":"publish","comment_status":"open","ping_status":"open","post_password":"","post_name":"whats-the-difference-between-a-simile-and-a-metaphor","to_ping":"","pinged":"","post_modified":"2015-05-11 08:03:18","post_modified_gmt":"2015-05-11 15:03:18","post_content_filtered":"","post_parent":0,"guid":"https:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/?p=14061","menu_order":0,"post_type":"post","post_mime_type":"","comment_count":"0","filter":"raw"},{"ID":11995,"post_author":"4","post_date":"2015-05-04 08:00:45","post_date_gmt":"2015-05-04 15:00:45","post_content":"[media]&lt;img src=\"https:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/wp-content\/uploads\/2015\/05\/yoda-talk-670x352.jpg\" alt=\"yoda-talk\" width=\"600\" height=\"314\" class=\"aligncenter size-large wp-image-14093\" \/&gt;[\/media]\r\n\r\nWhether you're a diehard Star Wars fan or you're still a newbie, chances are you know Yoda speak when you hear it. The Jedi master's method of speaking includes &lt;a href=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/2015\/rewriting-101-how-to-add-clarity-to-your-sentences\/\"&gt;quirky sentence structures&lt;\/a&gt;, unusual words, and wise phrases. Read on for some theories of Yoda-speak and a guide to talking like Yoda on your own.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Yoda's East African Roots&lt;\/strong&gt;&lt;strong&gt;\u00a0&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\n\r\n&lt;a href=\"https:\/\/www.flickr.com\/photos\/xtyler\/3215653831\/sizes\/m\/\"&gt;&lt;img class=\"aligncenter size-medium wp-image-11997\" src=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/wp-content\/uploads\/2015\/02\/Untitled6-410x410.png\" alt=\"Image via Flickr by Alex Abian\" width=\"410\" height=\"410\" \/&gt;&lt;\/a&gt;\r\n\r\nIf you've ever thought that Yoda's way of speaking sounds almost primeval, you're not far off. A &lt;a href=\"http:\/\/www.huffingtonpost.com\/2011\/10\/14\/yoda-language-study_n_1010720.html\" rel=\u201cnofollow\"&gt;language study&lt;\/a&gt; spearheaded by Stanford University focused on the unique subject-object-verb (SOV) agreement in Yoda's language. Since this structure preceded subject-verb-object (SVO) agreement, it almost certainly has roots in ancient languages. Stanford researchers determined that the structure of Yoda-speak is based on very early East African languages that influenced the development of early modern language that we know today.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Yoda's Old English Roots&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\n\r\nNot only is SOV agreement prevalent in East African languages, but it appears in Old English as well. A side-by-side comparison of Yoda's statements and some of &lt;a href=\"http:\/\/theweek.com\/articles\/442256\/strangely-yoda-speaks\" rel=\u201cnofollow\"&gt;Shakespeare's famous lines&lt;\/a&gt; reveals similarities. Both tend to feature the subject at the beginning of the sentence and the conjugated verb at the end of the sentence. Thus, Yoda's quirky but profound English statements also borrow from its ancestor, Old English.\r\n\r\nIt's also worth noting that even though Yoda's sentence structure may appear odd when compared with modern phrasing, his statements are still recognizable as English. Altering word order to the extent that Yoda does wouldn't be possible in many other languages, let alone understandable.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Yoda's Yiddish Roots&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\n\r\nIn addition to Old English and East African, linguists have also compared Yoda speak to Yiddish-influenced English, prompting interesting results. Yiddish-influenced English often plays with word order, placing verbs at the end of sentences. This frequently results in witty, whimsical phrasings, which moviegoers can also observe in Yoda's speech.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Why Yoda Speaks the Way He Does&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\n\r\nSome Star Wars experts point out that Yoda's sentence structure can be inconsistent. While his speech may lean toward SOV agreement, he often inserts\u00a0&lt;a href=\"http:\/\/scifi.about.com\/od\/starwarsglossaryandfaq\/a\/SWAR_yoda-speak.htm\" rel=\u201cnofollow\"&gt;object-subject-verb (OSV) statements&lt;\/a&gt; into his lexicon as well. Other more rare statements reflect the SVO agreement we're used to in modern English.\r\n\r\nExperts theorize that Yoda deploys his various phrasings purposefully. Since his SOV statements tend to make him sound wise, profound, and clever, it's likely that he uses them strategically. On the rare occasion that he doesn't have an important message to deliver, he may slip back into standard modern English.\r\n\r\nAs &lt;a href=\"http:\/\/www.quickanddirtytips.com\/education\/grammar\/yoda-grammar\" rel=\u201cnofollow\"&gt;Grammar Girl&lt;\/a&gt; explains, though Yoda's speech is memorable, it's not completely unusual in the world of Star Wars. Many of the movie's characters are immigrants from lands far away, and their speech also includes numerous oddities. Like any immigrant, Yoda incorporates his own phrasing into the language of the land.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;How You Can Talk Like Yoda&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\n\r\nYoda speak may be quirky, but it's certainly possible to imitate. To &lt;a href=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/2014\/star-wars\/\"&gt;talk like Yoda&lt;\/a&gt;, start by thinking of something wise to say. This can be as simple as a short, poignant statement. Next, change around the word order of your statement. Place the subject and the most important phrase first. Any negatives in your statement should go to the end.\r\n\r\nBe sure to reaffirm your statements or questions. If you're making an affirmative statement, add \"yes\" at the end. If you're asking a question, add an inquisitive \"hmm?\" at the end. Including these will help emphasize the context of your statement and provide Yoda speak newbies with the clues they need to communicate with the wise one.\r\n\r\nTry incorporating some Yoda speak into your daily life and see how well you're able to communicate with those around you. Does your language sound profound, witty, or both?","post_title":"Speak like Yoda You Can","post_excerpt":"","post_status":"publish","comment_status":"open","ping_status":"open","post_password":"","post_name":"speak-like-yoda-you-can","to_ping":"","pinged":"","post_modified":"2015-05-04 11:27:38","post_modified_gmt":"2015-05-04 18:27:38","post_content_filtered":"","post_parent":0,"guid":"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/?p=11995","menu_order":0,"post_type":"post","post_mime_type":"","comment_count":"0","filter":"raw"},{"ID":12001,"post_author":"4","post_date":"2015-04-30 00:00:20","post_date_gmt":"2015-04-30 07:00:20","post_content":"[media]&lt;img src=\"https:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/wp-content\/uploads\/2015\/05\/Blind-Mouse-670x352.jpg\" alt=\"Blind-Mouse\" width=\"600\" height=\"314\" class=\"aligncenter size-large wp-image-14045\" \/&gt;[\/media]\r\n\r\nMurder, torture, mass death by plague...not exactly the stuff of &lt;a href=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/2014\/happily-ever-after-or-not\/\"&gt;children's literature&lt;\/a&gt;, right? Actually, if you read the rhymes of Mother Goose, it is. Most people don't realize the macabre history of these innocuous-sounding rhymes, but dig beneath the surface, and you'll find Mother Goose poetry is chock-full of gruesome imagery.\r\n\r\nIn fact, many of today's nursery rhymes are sanitized versions of the grim originals. Back in the 1950s, a group of concerned citizens rallied to clean them up lest they terrify little children, a crusade similar to one today's parents wage against violent video games.\r\n\r\nIf you doubt the dark side of good Mother Goose, check out the stories behind these rhymes.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Ring Around the Rosie&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\n\r\n&lt;a href=\"https:\/\/www.flickr.com\/photos\/95072945@N05\/12779343884\/sizes\/l\"&gt;&lt;img class=\"aligncenter size-full wp-image-12003\" src=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/wp-content\/uploads\/2015\/02\/Untitled7.png\" alt=\"Image via Flickr by kelly.sikkema\" width=\"600\" height=\"398\" \/&gt;&lt;\/a&gt;\r\n\r\nChildren love to hold hands and chant this little &lt;a href=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/2014\/finding-rhyme-reason-national-poetry-month\/\"&gt;rhyme&lt;\/a&gt;, falling to the ground in a fit of giggles at the phrase, \"Ashes, ashes, we all fall down!\" What could be better than a ring of roses and a pocket full of posies? Actually, this rhyme reputedly refers\u00a0to the bubonic plague that wiped out 20 percent of the population of London in less than a year. Ring of roses? The ugly rash that heralds the disease. Pocket full of posies? To disguise the smell of sickness. Ashes, ashes? Cremated bodies. Not such a cheery children's rhyme after all.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Three Blind Mice&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\n\r\nThis cute little ditty seems bland enough, other than the somewhat disturbing imagery of a knife-wielding farmer's wife hacking off a few rodent tails. In truth, this rhyme is about the bloody reign of England's Queen Mary I, the fierce Catholic who spent much of her time on the throne executing Protestants. The three mice in this rhyme refer to Nicholas Ridley the Bishop of London, Hugh Latimer, and Archbishop\r\nThomas Cranmer, who were burned at the stake for heresy. The blindness is a &lt;a href=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/2015\/figurative-language-5-tools-to-spice-up-your-writing\/\"&gt;device&lt;\/a&gt; referring to their refusal to see Catholicism as the true faith.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Mary Mary Quite Contrary&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\n\r\nWhat could be more innocuous than a little agricultural advice? This sweet rhyme conjures up a garden full of blooms tended by smiling maidens surrounded by tinkling bells and cockle shells. In truth, however, this is another tribute to Bloody Mary and her murdering methods. Silver bells and cockle shells were medieval torture devices, and the garden is a &lt;a href=\"http:\/\/www.quickanddirtytips.com\/education\/grammar\/metaphors-and-similes\" rel=\u201cnofollow\"&gt;metaphor&lt;\/a&gt; for the fast-growing graveyards that accompanied her reign.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Jack and Jill Went Up the Hill&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\n\r\nReading this rhyme brings to mind a pair of laughing children merrily filling their buckets at the well and suffering a small slip as they skip down the hill. Actually, most theorists attribute the story behind this rhyme as that of France's King Louis XVI and his wife Marie-Antoinette, who were found guilty of treason and publicly beheaded by the guillotine.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;London Bridge is Falling Down&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\n\r\nOn the surface, this utterly harmless little rhyme seems to be just another story set to a tune and turned into a playground game for children. The rhyme refers to the famous bridge of stone built by Henry II in the 1100s; no one is certain of the identity of \"my fair lady.\" However, the second verse is where things get gruesome. The line \"Set a man to watch all night\" refers to the practice of embedding living human beings into bridges and foundational walls to act as a guardian spirit. In the Oxford Dictionary of Nursery Rhymes, three bridges are cited where the bodies of adults or children were &lt;a href=\"http:\/\/www.gutenberg.org\/files\/38274\/38274-h\/38274-h.htm\" rel=\u201cnofollow\"&gt;found embedded in the walls&lt;\/a&gt;. For the record, there's no evidence any human beings were used to build the London Bridge.\r\n\r\nSo you see, old Mother Goose wasn't exactly the benign feathered scribe depicted in &lt;a href=\"http:\/\/www.quickanddirtytips.com\/education\/writing\/common-mistakes-to-avoid-when-writing-a-childrens-book\" rel=\u201cnofollow\"&gt;children's books&lt;\/a&gt;. There's a sinister streak in her collection of kiddie poems.","post_title":"The Dark Side of Mother Goose","post_excerpt":"","post_status":"publish","comment_status":"open","ping_status":"open","post_password":"","post_name":"the-dark-side-of-mother-goose1","to_ping":"","pinged":"","post_modified":"2015-05-13 08:27:05","post_modified_gmt":"2015-05-13 15:27:05","post_content_filtered":"","post_parent":0,"guid":"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/?p=12001","menu_order":0,"post_type":"post","post_mime_type":"","comment_count":"0","filter":"raw"},{"ID":12007,"post_author":"4","post_date":"2015-04-29 00:00:49","post_date_gmt":"2015-04-29 07:00:49","post_content":"[media]&lt;img src=\"https:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/wp-content\/uploads\/2015\/04\/Teaching-optimism-670x352.jpg\" alt=\"Teaching-optimism\" width=\"600\" height=\"314\" class=\"aligncenter size-large wp-image-13997\" \/&gt;[\/media]\r\n\r\nMay 5 is\u00a0&lt;a href=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/2014\/did-your-writing-skip-a-grade\/\"&gt;National Teacher Day&lt;\/a&gt;. In advance of the holiday, it's important to remember that you wouldn't be where you are today without the teachers that influenced you throughout your life. Whether your favorite educators are from elementary school, high school, or college, make sure to tell them what a positive effect they've had on your life. Here are six ways to celebrate National Teacher Day.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Write a Thoughtful Letter&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\n\r\n&lt;a href=\"https:\/\/www.flickr.com\/photos\/bunchesandbits\/6354798575\/sizes\/m\/\"&gt;&lt;img class=\"aligncenter size-medium wp-image-12009\" src=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/wp-content\/uploads\/2015\/02\/Untitled8-410x410.png\" alt=\"Image via Flickr by Bunches and Bits [Karina}\" width=\"410\" height=\"410\" \/&gt;&lt;\/a&gt;\r\n\r\nSometimes heartfelt words are the best gift a teacher can receive. Think about how significantly your favorite teacher has impacted your life\u00a0and take a minute to write down exactly how much she or he has done for you. Whether a great elementary science teacher influenced you to become the biologist you are today or a tough high school literature teacher challenged you to become the writer you are now, let that teacher know. Include the kids in this project and help them write a short but sweet note to their current favorite teacher.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Help With Classroom Chores&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\n\r\nWhen time is short, teachers can't always do everything they want to for their classrooms. Many teachers could use a helping hand with &lt;a href=\"http:\/\/www.ptotoday.com\/pto-today-articles\/article\/1105-teacher-appreciation-9-ways-to-celebrate\" rel=\"nofollow\"&gt;projects, large and small&lt;\/a&gt;. Talk with your favorite former teacher or one that the kids love and find out how you can help. Be prepared to pitch in with photocopying educational materials, hanging art projects, or taking on lunchtime duty. For many teachers, having a little extra time each week or month is the greatest gift.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Provide Lunch&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\n\r\nTeachers work long hours, and they don't always have time to throw together a nutritious lunch in the morning. On National Teacher Day, tell your kids' teachers that &lt;a href=\"http:\/\/www.educationworld.com\/a_admin\/admin\/admin162.shtml\" rel=\"nofollow\"&gt;lunch is on you&lt;\/a&gt;. Make lunch for a favorite teacher or partner with other parents at your kids' school to put together a lavish lunchtime spread. If each parent volunteers for one course, your gourmet meal will come together in a flash. Choose healthy foods to keep your teachers nourished throughout the day, and don't forget something sweet to finish the meal.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Volunteer in the Classroom&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\n\r\nMany schools have programs that allow parents to volunteer their time in the classroom. Whether you have an hour or a whole week to give each month, volunteering your time can significantly impact how much a teacher can accomplish in the classroom. Depending on the teacher's needs, you may be able to help coordinate an educational field trip, prepare activities in the classroom, or even organize seasonal events.\r\n\r\nWhatever you do, know that donating your time makes the day much smoother, more efficient, and much more fun for teachers and their students. Check with a teacher or the school's administrative staff to find out more about the school's volunteer program.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Read About Great Teachers&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\n\r\nThere are plenty of &lt;a href=\"http:\/\/www.readwritethink.org\/classroom-resources\/calendar-activities\/teacher-appreciation-week-honors-20501.html\" rel=\"nofollow\"&gt;excellent books&lt;\/a&gt; about truly great teachers for every grade level. Choose an appropriate one to read with the kids and help them pick out the qualities that make a teacher truly great. Encourage them to think about which of their teachers have these qualities, and be sure to celebrate them on National Teacher Day.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Tell the Newspaper&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\n\r\nTeaching can feel like a thankless job, so there's no better way to celebrate National Teacher Day than to shout from the rooftops just how great your favorite teacher is. If you can't quite do that, &lt;a href=\"http:\/\/www.nea.org\/grants\/55148.htm\" rel=\"nofollow\"&gt;tell your local newspaper&lt;\/a&gt; instead. Contact the newsroom about establishing a thank you section in the paper and encourage others in your community to write a note about an educator who has made a difference in their lives.\r\n\r\nHowever you choose to celebrate, remember to give the educators who taught you the basics of &lt;a href=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/2014\/3-punctuation-mistakes-that-can-make-you-look-like-a-cannibal\/\"&gt;punctuation&lt;\/a&gt;, &lt;a href=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/2015\/learn-the-difference-between-these-confusing-homophones\/\"&gt;homophones&lt;\/a&gt;, and everything in between a day of recognition. Donate your time, your cooking prowess, or your thoughtful words, and celebrate your teachers and their impact on your life. How will you celebrate National Teacher Day?","post_title":"6 Ways to Celebrate National Teacher Day","post_excerpt":"","post_status":"publish","comment_status":"open","ping_status":"open","post_password":"","post_name":"6-ways-to-celebrate-national-teacher-day","to_ping":"","pinged":"","post_modified":"2015-04-28 09:11:42","post_modified_gmt":"2015-04-28 16:11:42","post_content_filtered":"","post_parent":0,"guid":"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/?p=12007","menu_order":0,"post_type":"post","post_mime_type":"","comment_count":"0","filter":"raw"},{"ID":14049,"post_author":"1001005","post_date":"2015-04-28 10:25:56","post_date_gmt":"2015-04-28 17:25:56","post_content":"[media]&lt;img src=\"https:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/wp-content\/uploads\/2015\/04\/wet-670x352.jpg\" alt=\"wet\" width=\"600\" height=\"314\" class=\"aligncenter size-large wp-image-14055\" \/&gt;[\/media]\r\n\r\nIt\u2019s no wonder that many people misspell the phrase &lt;strong&gt;\u201cwhet your appetite.\u201d&lt;\/strong&gt; After all, your mouth waters when your appetite is stirred, so why wouldn\u2019t the phrase be spelled as \u201cwet your appetite\u201d? \r\n\r\nIn its most literal sense, \u201cwhet\u201d means to sharpen like you would a knife or blade. When used in the phrase \u201cwhet your appetite,\u201d it means to arouse interest or eagerness, to metaphorically sharpen your appetite. When your appetite is whetted, you\u2019re hungry and ready to eat! \r\n\r\nIf you want to learn more about idioms and odd phrases, &lt;a href=\"https:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/2015\/funny-phrases-nip-it-in-the-bud\/?sf&amp;utm_campaign=Blog_Augment_1&amp;utm_source=Blog_org&amp;utm_medium=text_link\"&gt;read this post about \u201cnip it in the bud.\u201d &lt;\/a&gt;\r\n\r\n","post_title":"Funny Phrases: Whet Your Appetite","post_excerpt":"","post_status":"publish","comment_status":"open","ping_status":"open","post_password":"","post_name":"funny-phrases-whet-your-appetite","to_ping":"","pinged":"","post_modified":"2015-04-28 10:25:56","post_modified_gmt":"2015-04-28 17:25:56","post_content_filtered":"","post_parent":0,"guid":"https:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/?p=14049","menu_order":0,"post_type":"post","post_mime_type":"","comment_count":"0","filter":"raw"},{"ID":13769,"post_author":"1001005","post_date":"2015-04-27 14:44:35","post_date_gmt":"2015-04-27 21:44:35","post_content":"[media]&lt;img src=\"https:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/wp-content\/uploads\/2015\/04\/Speak-like-a-S.-670x352.jpg\" alt=\"Speak-like-a-S.\" width=\"600\" height=\"314\" class=\"aligncenter size-large wp-image-14007\" \/&gt;[\/media]\r\n\r\nScientific writing is known for its precision and accuracy. Other forms of written and spoken communication, however, are often filled with confusing and ambiguous vocabulary. Here are four words we\u2019ve poached from the scientific world that should be adopted in non-scientific communication.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Abstract&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\nIn scientific writing, an abstract summarizes the key points of a presentation or paper. Written scientific research is often lengthy, so an abstract serves as a way to let the reader know what he or she is getting into up front.\r\nMost people use the word \u201cabstract\u201d to describe art or theoretical concepts. But if you use its scientific meaning, whether you\u2019re writing a long email or a detailed list of directions, you can create an abstract that summarizes the key points.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Volume &lt;\/strong&gt;\r\nIn our everyday lives, we often talk about volume in terms of noise. In science, volume is the amount of space occupied by an object measured in three dimensions, expressed in cubic units. Try using \u201cvolume\u201d as a more specific way of talking about how much space is available for or taken up by an object.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Hypothesis&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\nIn the scientific world, a hypothesis is a supposition made on the basis of limited evidence as a starting point for further investigation.\r\nIn our everyday conversation, the word \"hypothesis\" can often be substituted for the word \"idea.\" For example, if you're planning a bake sale and you base your sales projections on how many cookies and cupcakes you sold last year, you might say you have \"an idea\" of how much money you'll make this year. Since your idea is based on evidence from the year before, it's actually a hypothesis. \r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Conductor&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\nA train conductor, a symphony conductor...we know conductors as people who orchestrate actions. In the scientific world, a conductor is a material or an object that conducts heat, electricity, light, or sound. For example, the cord between your lamp and the wall is a conductor of electricity. (Interestingly, the plastic or rubber casing around the wire acts as an insulator and the internal metal wiring is the strict conductor.) Once you know what a conductor is, you\u2019ll start seeing them everywhere! Use this word from the science world to identify the conductors around you \u2014 even in a literary sense!\r\n\r\nWhat other scientific words should be adopted for everyday use? Share your suggestions in the comments!","post_title":"Speak Like a Scientist: 4 Words to Try Using Differently","post_excerpt":"","post_status":"publish","comment_status":"open","ping_status":"open","post_password":"","post_name":"speak-like-a-scientist-4-words-to-try-using-differently","to_ping":"","pinged":"","post_modified":"2015-04-27 14:44:35","post_modified_gmt":"2015-04-27 21:44:35","post_content_filtered":"","post_parent":0,"guid":"https:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/?p=13769","menu_order":0,"post_type":"post","post_mime_type":"","comment_count":"0","filter":"raw"},{"ID":13755,"post_author":"1001005","post_date":"2015-04-27 08:49:38","post_date_gmt":"2015-04-27 15:49:38","post_content":"[media]&lt;img src=\"https:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/wp-content\/uploads\/2015\/04\/5r3ew.jpg\" alt=\"Verses and versus do not mean the same thing\" width=\"600\" height=\"314\" class=\"aligncenter size-full wp-image-13985\" \/&gt;[\/media]\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Versus: &lt;\/strong&gt;against (especially in sports and legal use); as opposed to, in contrast to. (Often abbreviated as vs.) For example:\r\n\r\nThe rivalry of the Green Monkeys &lt;i&gt;versus&lt;\/i&gt; the Blue Barracudas has raged for years. \r\nI\u2019m weighing the pros and cons of the white-and-gold dress &lt;i&gt;versus&lt;\/i&gt; the blue-and-black dress.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Verses: &lt;\/strong&gt;writing arranged with a metrical rhythm, typically having a rhyme; small sections of the Jewish or Christian Bible; several similar units of a song. For example:\r\n\r\nDue to her writer\u2019s block, the poet could only complete a few &lt;i&gt;verses&lt;\/i&gt; each day.\r\nJohn 3:16 is one of the most well-known &lt;i&gt;verses&lt;\/i&gt; in the Bible.\r\nI like this song, but the &lt;i&gt;verses&lt;\/i&gt; are hard to remember.\r\n\r\nFor explanations of other confusing word pairs read &lt;a href=\"https:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/2014\/learn-the-difference-between-these-commonly-confused-words\/?utm_campaign=Blog_Augment_1&amp;utm_source=Blog_org&amp;utm_medium=text_link\"&gt;this blog post&lt;\/a&gt; about the difference between then and than.\r\n","post_title":"Confusing Words: Versus vs. Verses","post_excerpt":"","post_status":"publish","comment_status":"open","ping_status":"open","post_password":"","post_name":"confusing-words-versus-vs-verses","to_ping":"","pinged":"","post_modified":"2015-04-27 14:21:42","post_modified_gmt":"2015-04-27 21:21:42","post_content_filtered":"","post_parent":0,"guid":"https:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/?p=13755","menu_order":0,"post_type":"post","post_mime_type":"","comment_count":"0","filter":"raw"},{"ID":13725,"post_author":"1001005","post_date":"2015-04-24 08:31:46","post_date_gmt":"2015-04-24 15:31:46","post_content":"[media]&lt;img src=\"https:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/wp-content\/uploads\/2015\/04\/First-Fig\u201d-by-Edna-St.-Vincent-Millay.jpg\" alt=\"First Fig\u201d by Edna St. Vincent Millay\" width=\"600\" height=\"314\" class=\"aligncenter size-full wp-image-13869\" \/&gt;[\/media]\r\n\r\nReading poetry is a wonderful experience, but have you ever memorized and recited a poem? Saying the words aloud gives the poem new life and highlights some of the nuances in the language. Here are three short poems you can memorize and recite for your friends and family.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;&lt;i&gt;\u201cFirst Fig\u201d &lt;\/i&gt; by Edna St. Vincent Millay&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\nMy candle burns at both ends;\r\nIt will not last the night;\r\nBut ah, my foes, and oh, my friends\u2014\r\nIt gives a lovely light.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;&lt;i&gt;\u201cDevotion\u201d&lt;\/i&gt; by Robert Frost&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\nThe heart can think of no devotion\r\nGreater than being shore to the ocean \u2014\r\nHolding the curve of one position,\r\nCounting an endless repetition.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;&lt;i&gt;\"Awaking in New York\"&lt;\/i&gt; by Maya Angelou&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\nCurtains forcing their will   \r\nagainst the wind,\r\nchildren sleep,\r\nexchanging dreams with   \r\nseraphim. Share this poem with your friends!&lt;\/b&gt;\r\n\r\n\r\n&lt;b&gt;\"On Work\" from &lt;i&gt;&lt;a href=\"http:\/\/gutenberg.net.au\/ebooks02\/0200061h.html\"&gt;The Prophet&lt;\/a&gt;&lt;\/i&gt;&lt;\/b&gt;\r\nKahlil Gibran\r\n\r\n\"Always you have been told that work is a curse and labour a misfortune. \r\nBut I say to you that when you work you fulfil a part of earth's furthest dream, assigned to you when that dream was born, \r\nAnd in keeping yourself with labour you are in truth loving life, \r\nAnd to love life through labour is to be intimate with life's inmost secret.\" \r\n\r\n\r\n&nbsp;\r\n\r\n&lt;hr \/&gt;\r\n\r\nEvery weekday in April, we will be sharing a poem, an excerpt of poetry, or a feature on a poet. Our celebration will feature poetry from every era, and we ask our friends to join us throughout the month by sharing their favorite poetry under the tag #PoetryMonth.\r\n\r\nOther daily poetry:\r\n&lt;a href=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/2015\/a-daily-dose-of-poetry-day-1\/?sf&amp;utm_campaign=PoetryMonth15&amp;utm_source=Blog_org&amp;utm_medium=text_link\"&gt;Day One&lt;\/a&gt;\r\n&lt;a href=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/2015\/a-daily-dose-of-poetry-day-2\/?sf&amp;utm_campaign=PoetryMonth15&amp;utm_source=Blog_org&amp;utm_medium=text_link\"&gt;Day Two&lt;\/a&gt;","post_title":"A Daily Dose of Poetry: Day 3","post_excerpt":"","post_status":"publish","comment_status":"open","ping_status":"open","post_password":"","post_name":"a-daily-dose-of-poetry-day-3","to_ping":"","pinged":"","post_modified":"2015-04-24 06:24:12","post_modified_gmt":"2015-04-24 13:24:12","post_content_filtered":"","post_parent":0,"guid":"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/?p=13061","menu_order":0,"post_type":"post","post_mime_type":"","comment_count":"0","filter":"raw"},{"ID":13721,"post_author":"1001005","post_date":"2015-04-24 05:17:48","post_date_gmt":"2015-04-24 12:17:48","post_content":"[media]&lt;img src=\"https:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/wp-content\/uploads\/2015\/04\/64trfeds.jpg\" alt=\"You&#039;re the cat&#039;s meow!\" width=\"600\" height=\"315\" class=\"aligncenter size-full wp-image-13807\" \/&gt;[\/media]\r\n\r\nLanguage changes over time. The popularity of words, especially slang or words related to technology or trends, ebbs and flows. Some long-forgotten words, however, are worth resurrecting. If you\u2019re looking to add a retro update to your vocabulary, here are a few words and phrases from the last hundred years to try out. \r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;1920s: The cat's meow: &lt;\/strong&gt;The best or greatest.\r\nYour iPhone case is the cat\u2019s meow!\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;1960s: Bummer: &lt;\/strong&gt;a bad experience.\r\nI thought Taylor Swift\u2019s concert was a bummer. She only played songs from her new album instead of her older hits.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;1970s: Bad: &lt;\/strong&gt;Ironically, if something is \u201cbad\u201d it is really good, stylish, or cool.\r\nDid you see North West\u2019s new Louis Vuitton sneakers? They\u2019re totally bad! \r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;1970s: Dig It: &lt;\/strong&gt;To understand or agree.\r\nWhat an insightful Ted Talk this is. I dig it.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;1980s: Pie-hole: &lt;\/strong&gt;a slang term for mouth.\r\nI can\u2019t wait to stuff my pie-hole with this vegan, gluten-free pizza!\r\n\r\nWhich slang words do you think people should start using again? Fifteen&lt;\/a&gt;","post_title":"A Daily Dose of Poetry: Day 16","post_excerpt":"","post_status":"publish","comment_status":"open","ping_status":"open","post_password":"","post_name":"a-daily-dose-of-poetry-day-16","to_ping":"","pinged":"","post_modified":"2015-04-23 07:38:08","post_modified_gmt":"2015-04-23 14:38:08","post_content_filtered":"","post_parent":0,"guid":"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/?p=13641","menu_order":0,"post_type":"post","post_mime_type":"","comment_count":"0","filter":"raw"},{"ID":11977,"post_author":"4","post_date":"2015-04-22 00:00:26","post_date_gmt":"2015-04-22 07:00:26","post_content":"[media]&lt;a rel=\"nofollow\" href=\"http:\/\/http:\/\/www.speedbump.com\/index.html\"&gt;&lt;img src=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/wp-content\/uploads\/2015\/04\/block-of-death.jpg\" alt=\"One last case of writers block cartoon \" width=\"600\" height=\"314\" class=\"aligncenter size-full wp-image-13709\" \/&gt;&lt;\/a&gt;[\/media]\r\n\r\nWant to write a bestselling novel? Or maybe you're more the screenplay type that wants to go straight to Hollywood. Whatever your writing goals are, sometimes the biggest obstacles between them and you is a nasty case of &lt;a href=\"http:\/\/www.huffingtonpost.com\/grammarly\/5-ways-to-find-new-story-ideas_b_6053536.html\"&gt;writer's block&lt;\/a&gt;. How can you free up your creative juices and write a story worth telling? Here are some ideas to get you started.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Go &lt;a href=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/2013\/word-nerd\/\"&gt;Wild for Words&lt;\/a&gt;&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\n\r\nStephen King holds thesauruses (&lt;i&gt;thesauri&lt;\/i&gt; for you prescriptive Latin-lovers) in disdain, but don't be afraid to rebel against his viewpoint. Learning new and &lt;a  rel=\"nofollow\" href=\"http:\/\/www.oxforddictionaries.com\/words\/archaic-words\"&gt;archaic words&lt;\/a&gt; can help you think from a different angle; those new words can serve as the seed for your story.\r\n\r\nOne way to approach this is to find a word you like and write it in the center of a piece of paper. Use word association to create an entire web on that paper; you might be pleasantly surprised at where your thoughts take you.\r\n\r\nForeign language words might also give you a nudge in the right direction. For example, German has a word that refers specifically to the \"glad it wasn't me\"\u00a0attitude that some people take up when something bad happens to someone else. Chinese has a word that literally means \"horse horse tiger tiger\" and means \"so-so.\"\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Collaborate&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\n\r\nSometimes writers go too deep into the \"me zone\" and end up isolated. Whether you get in touch with fellow writers online, on campus, or in a local writing group, your peers might be able to give you the boost that you need. Listen to their ideas and contribute your own; you'll come up with something that neither of you could have concocted on your own. &lt;a  rel=\"nofollow\" href=\"http:\/\/www.thebookdesigner.com\/2014\/08\/21-tips-for-creating-a-successful-writing-collaboration\/\"&gt;Collaborative writing&lt;\/a&gt; is a challenge, but the rewards can be beautiful.\r\n\r\nCheck out some information on Grammarly's annual collaborative novel, GrammoWriMo, &lt;a href=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/grammowrimo\"&gt;here&lt;\/a&gt;.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Write Poorly&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\n\r\nGrammarly is all about helping you to improve your writing, but bad writing also has its benefits. Bad writing isn't constrained by your inner editor's madness. When you accept that you're not at your best, the words flow freely. Sure, you might throw away what you work on, but you'll have a fresh mindset.\r\n\r\n&lt;a href=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/2013\/beaten-path-nanowrimo-writing-tips\/\"&gt;NaNoWriMo&lt;\/a&gt;\u00a0is a fantastic opportunity to write poorly. Writing 50,000 words in 30 days is tough, but if you commit yourself to the goal, you'll end up with something you can take pride in\u2014even if it needs a major rewrite to make it presentable.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Play Games&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\n\r\n&lt;a href=\"https:\/\/www.flickr.com\/photos\/25559122@N06\/8054926394\/sizes\/m\/\"&gt;&lt;img class=\"alignleft size-full wp-image-11979\" src=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/wp-content\/uploads\/2015\/02\/Untitled4.png\" alt=\"Scrabble, Grammarly\" width=\"500\" height=\"389\" \/&gt;&lt;\/a&gt;\r\n\r\nThere are endless games out there that can stimulate your thinking and get you into story mode. You can participate in &lt;a  rel=\"nofollow\" href=\"http:\/\/www.languageisavirus.com\/writing-games.html\"&gt;interactive creative writing games&lt;\/a&gt; online, or you can just sit down with some Mad Libs and see what happens. Board games are a good way to get you thinking, too. Games like &lt;a href=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/2015\/7-awesome-and-underused-scrabble-words\/?lf&amp;utm_source=Blog_org&amp;utm_campaign=Blog_Augment_1&amp;utm_medium=text_link\"&gt;Scrabble&lt;\/a&gt;, Quelf, and Cranium will have you thinking about words\u2014and perhaps the world in general\u2014in a different light.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Go to Boot Camp&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\n\r\nWriting boot camps are structured events wherein there is designated time to write and be productive. There may also be lectures and other encouragement. &lt;a rel=\"nofollow\" href=\"https:\/\/www.insidehighered.com\/blogs\/gradhacker\/writing-boot-camps\"&gt;Participating in a boot camp&lt;\/a&gt; can help you become more goal-oriented in your writing. The people you meet at a camp will inspire you to stay focused on improving your storytelling skills.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Veg Out With Media&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\n\r\nSteal some ideas! Watch movies and TV shows, dive into a new book, or look at photography and other artwork. Pick out the elements that touch you and adapt them; make them your own in your next project. If you're really in love with a particular book or show, you might even try your hand at fan fiction just for the fun of it.\r\n\r\nSometimes words might pour out of you like water out of a broken faucet. At other times, you might feel like you need to call a plumber to come unclog the pipes. When the latter happens, use the above tips to &lt;a href=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/2014\/waking-the-muse-how-to-find-inspiration\/\"&gt;find inspiration for your writing&lt;\/a&gt;\u00a0and get the ideas flowing. Do you have any special techniques you use to give your storytelling a kick?","post_title":"6 Cool Ways to Overcome Writer's Block","post_excerpt":"","post_status":"publish","comment_status":"open","ping_status":"open","post_password":"","post_name":"6-coolest-ways-to-write-a-story","to_ping":"","pinged":"","post_modified":"2015-04-23 07:35:23","post_modified_gmt":"2015-04-23 14:35:23","post_content_filtered":"","post_parent":0,"guid":"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/?p=11977","menu_order":0,"post_type":"post","post_mime_type":"","comment_count":"0","filter":"raw"},{"ID":13331,"post_author":"1001005","post_date":"2015-04-21 08:04:49","post_date_gmt":"2015-04-21 15:04:49","post_content":"[media]&lt;img src=\"https:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/wp-content\/uploads\/2015\/04\/\u2014Robert-Louis-Stevenson.jpg\" alt=\"Spring comes with a bound, Spring the deliverer,\" width=\"600\" height=\"314\" class=\"aligncenter size-full wp-image-13733\" \/&gt;[\/media]\r\n\r\nApril is not only National Poetry Month, but also a time of year when flowers bloom and trees sprout new leaves. It\u2019s spring, a season of hope and renewal! Celebrate spring with these three poems about the beauty of the season.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;&lt;em&gt;\"Flower God, God of the Spring\"&lt;\/em&gt; by Robert Louis Stevenson&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\nFlower god, god of the spring, beautiful, bountiful,\r\nCold-dyed shield in the sky, lover of versicles,\r\nHere I wander in April\r\nCold, grey-headed; and still to my\r\nHeart, Spring comes with a bound, Spring the deliverer,\r\nSpring, song-leader in woods, chorally resonant;\r\nSpring, flower-planter in meadows,\r\nChild-conductor in willowy\r\nFields deep dotted with bloom, daisies and crocuses:\r\nHere that child from his heart drinks of eternity:\r\nO child, happy are children!\r\nShe still smiles on their innocence,\r\nShe, dear mother in God, fostering violets,\r\nFills earth full of her scents, voices and violins:\r\nThus one cunning in music\r\nWakes old chords in the memory:\r\nThus fair earth in the Spring leads her performances.\r\nOne more touch of the bow, smell of the virginal\r\nGreen - one more, and my bosom\r\nFeels new life with an ecstasy. \r\n\r\n&lt;em&gt;&lt;strong&gt;\"O Were my Love yon Lilac Fair\" by Robert Burns&lt;\/strong&gt;&lt;\/em&gt;\r\nO were my Love yon lilac fair,     \r\n  Wi' purple blossoms to the spring,     \r\nAnd I a bird to shelter there,     \r\n  When wearied on my little wing;     \r\nHow I wad mourn when it was torn             \r\n  By autumn wild and winter rude!     \r\nBut I wad sing on wanton wing     \r\n  When youthfu' May its bloom renew'd.     \r\n \r\nO gin my Love were yon red rose     \r\n  That grows upon the castle wa',      \r\nAnd I mysel a drap o' dew,     \r\n  Into her bonnie breast to fa';     \r\nO there, beyond expression blest,     \r\n  I'd feast on beauty a' the night;     \r\nSeal'd on her silk-saft faulds to rest,      \r\n  Till fley'd awa' by Phoebus' light.     \r\n \r\n&lt;strong&gt;\"More Than Enough by Marge Piercy\"&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\nThe first lily of June opens its red mouth.\r\nAll over the sand road where we walk\r\nmultiflora rose climbs trees cascading\r\nwhite or pink blossoms, simple, intense\r\nthe scene drifting like colored mist.\r\n\r\nThe arrowhead is spreading its creamy\r\nclumps of flower and the blackberries\r\nare blooming in the thickets. Season of\r\njoy for the bee. The green will never\r\nagain be so green, so purely and lushly\r\n\r\nnew, grass lifting its wheaty seedheads\r\ninto the wind. Rich fresh wine\r\nof June, we stagger into you smeared\r\nwith pollen, overcome as the turtle\r\nlaying her eggs in roadside sand.\r\n\r\nWant more poetry? ","post_title":"Let Your Love of Poetry Bloom: 3 Poems about Spring","post_excerpt":"","post_status":"publish","comment_status":"open","ping_status":"open","post_password":"","post_name":"let-your-love-of-poetry-bloom-3-poems-about-spring","to_ping":"","pinged":"","post_modified":"2015-04-21 08:04:49","post_modified_gmt":"2015-04-21 15:04:49","post_content_filtered":"","post_parent":0,"guid":"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/?p=13331","menu_order":0,"post_type":"post","post_mime_type":"","comment_count":"0","filter":"raw"},{"ID":13591,"post_author":"1001005","post_date":"2015-04-20 03:06:30","post_date_gmt":"2015-04-20 10:06:30","post_content":"[media]&lt;img src=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/wp-content\/uploads\/2015\/04\/4e2sw.jpg\" alt=\"Daniel Quilp, The Old Curiosity Shop\" width=\"600\" height=\"314\" class=\"aligncenter size-full wp-image-13655\" \/&gt;[\/media]\r\n\r\nThe great English writer Charles Dickens is known for his well-crafted characters. While some of the characters we meet in Dickens\u2019 novels are endearing heroes, others are sinister villains. Here are three Dickens characters you would never want to meet.\r\n\r\nBill Sikes in &lt;i&gt;Oliver Twist&lt;\/i&gt;\r\nA career criminal, violent abuser, and murderer, Bill Sikes is at the top of the list of characters to avoid. This is how Dickens describes him:\r\n\u201c...a stoutly-built fellow of about five-and-thirty, in a black velveteen coat, very soiled drab breeches, lace-up half boots, and grey cotton stockings which enclosed a bulky pair of legs, with large swelling calves\u2014the kind of legs, which in such costume, always look in an unfinished and incomplete state without a set of fetters to garnish them. He had a brown hat on his head, and a dirty belcher handkerchief round his neck: with the long frayed ends of which he smeared the beer from his face as he spoke. He disclosed, when he had done so, a broad heavy countenance with a beard of three weeks' growth, and two scowling eyes; one of which displayed various parti-coloured symptoms of having been recently damaged by a blow.\u201d\r\n\r\nDaniel Quilp in &lt;i&gt;The Old Curiosity Shop&lt;\/i&gt;\r\nDaniel Quilp is a sneaky manipulator who uses secrets as currency. A condescending liar, Quilp can\u2019t be trusted. If you met him on the street, it would be wise to keep your head down and walk the other way.\r\n\r\nMiss Havisham in &lt;i&gt;Great Expectations&lt;\/i&gt; \r\nAlthough her cruelty stems from the deep pain of betrayal and rejection, Miss Havisham is cruel nonetheless. She meddles in both Pip and Estella\u2019s lives, encouraging love in one and brutal indifference in the other. Miss Havisham succeeds in hurting everyone close to her and ruins the lives of others as a consequence. She\u2019s destructive, manipulative, and cruel. Watch out!\r\n\r\nWhich Dickens character is your favorite villain? Let us know in the comments! children\" width=\"600\" height=\"314\" class=\"aligncenter size-full wp-image-13625\" \/&gt;[\/media]\r\n\r\nMany of us have special memories of books that changed our worlds as children. I\u2019ll never forget snuggling up next to my dad while he read a section of C.S. Lewis\u2019s &lt;i&gt;The Chronicles of Narnia&lt;\/i&gt; to my brother and me each night before bed. Here are five books and series for kids that we recommend re-reading as an adult:\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;&lt;i&gt;The Little House series&lt;\/i&gt; by Laura Ingalls Wilder&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\nBased on Wilder\u2019s experiences growing up on the American frontier, the &lt;i&gt;Little House&lt;\/i&gt; series paints an intimate portrait of an exciting time in American history. Read along as Laura and her family encounter bears, battle the elements, and survive in the early American West.\r\n \r\n&lt;strong&gt;&lt;i&gt;The Giving Tree&lt;\/i&gt; by Shel Silverstein &lt;\/strong&gt;\r\nWhile this popular children\u2019s book is intended for young readers, adults have found inspiration within its pages, too. &lt;i&gt;The Giving Tree&lt;\/i&gt; tells the story of a boy growing up alongside a tree and the relationship of give and take between them. It\u2019s a short picture book and worth another read from an adult perspective.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;&lt;i&gt;The Little Prince&lt;\/i&gt; by Antoine de Saint-Exup\u00e9ry&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\n&lt;i&gt;The Little Prince&lt;\/i&gt; is one of those rare children's books that captivates young readers on one level and touches the hearts of adult readers on another. On the surface, it is about a pilot who crashes his plane in the desert and encounters a strange young boy who has traveled from his home on a distant asteroid. At a deeper level, &lt;i&gt;The Little Prince&lt;\/i&gt; is a story about being present in your own life \u2014 about paying attention to everyday pleasures and making time for friends.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;&lt;i&gt;Fantastic Mr. Fox&lt;\/i&gt; by Roald Dahl &lt;\/strong&gt;\r\nMany people are familiar with Wes Anderson\u2019s movie adaptation of &lt;i&gt;Fantastic Mr. Fox&lt;\/i&gt;, but if you haven\u2019t read the book, you\u2019re missing out. Roald Dahl is the author of many other favorite children\u2019s books, including &lt;i&gt;James and the Giant Peach&lt;\/i&gt;, &lt;i&gt;Charlie and the Chocolate Factory&lt;\/i&gt;, and &lt;i&gt;Matilda&lt;\/i&gt;. Pick up &lt;i&gt;Fantastic Mr. Fox&lt;\/i&gt; to enjoy a fun-filled journey through the animal world.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;&lt;i&gt;Daddy's Little Girl&lt;\/i&gt; by Peter Wilson &lt;\/strong&gt;\r\n&lt;i&gt;Daddy\u2019s Little Girl&lt;\/i&gt; tells the story of a father\u2019s love for his daughter through the years. The book is short and sweet and guaranteed to bring a tear to most fathers\u2019 and daughters\u2019 eyes. \r\n\r\n&lt;b&gt;Which books for children do you recommend rereading as an adult? Here are 5 facts about libraries that will amaze you.\r\n\r\n1. According to the American Library Association, 58% of adults in the United States have a library card.\r\n\r\n2. According to the American Library Association, students in the United States make 1.5 billion visits to school libraries during the school year. \r\n\r\n4. The highest library in the world is in Shanghai, China, 60 floors (over 757 feet) above street level. \r\n\r\n5. The largest overdue fine for a library book was $345.14. share this poem with your friends!&lt;\/b&gt;\r\n[media]&lt;img src=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/wp-content\/uploads\/2015\/04\/t4rf3eswq.jpg\" alt=\"If you forget me Pablo Neruda excerpt\" width=\"600\" height=\"314\" class=\"aligncenter size-full wp-image-13597\" \/&gt;[\/media]\r\n\r\n&lt;hr \/&gt;  \r\n\r\n&lt;b&gt;\"If You Forget Me\" &lt;\/b&gt;\r\nPablo Neruda\r\n\r\nI want you to know\r\none thing. \r\n\r\nYou know how this is: \r\nif I look \r\nat the crystal moon, at the red branch \r\nof the slow autumn at my window, \r\nif I touch \r\nnear the fire \r\nthe impalpable ash \r\nor the wrinkled body of the log, \r\neverything carries me to you, \r\nas if everything that exists, \r\naromas, light, metals, \r\nwere little boats \r\nthat sail \r\ntoward those isles of yours that wait for me. \r\n\r\nWell, now, \r\nif little by little you stop loving me \r\nI shall stop loving you little by little. \r\n\r\nIf suddenly \r\nyou forget me \r\ndo not look for me, \r\nfor I shall already have forgotten you. \r\n\r\nIf you think it long and mad, \r\nthe wind of banners \r\nthat passes through my life, \r\nand you decide \r\nto leave me at the shore \r\nof the heart where I have roots, \r\nremember \r\nthat on that day, \r\nat that hour, \r\nI shall lift my arms \r\nand my roots will set off \r\nto seek another land. \r\n\r\nBut \r\nif each day, \r\neach hour, \r\nyou feel that you are destined for me \r\nwith implacable sweetness, \r\nif each day a flower \r\nclimbs up to your lips to seek me, \r\nah my love, ah my own, \r\nin me all that fire is repeated, \r\nin me nothing is extinguished or forgotten, \r\nmy love feeds on your love, beloved, \r\nand as long as you live it will be in your arms \r\nwithout leaving mine. \r\n\r\nWant to read more by Neruda? Ten&lt;\/a&gt;","post_title":"A Daily Dose of Poetry: Day 11","post_excerpt":"","post_status":"publish","comment_status":"open","ping_status":"open","post_password":"","post_name":"a-daily-dose-of-poetry-day-11","to_ping":"","pinged":"","post_modified":"2015-04-16 08:31:00","post_date_gmt":"2015-04-15 15:31:00","post_content":"&lt;b&gt;Help us to celebrate National Poetry Month; share this poem with your friends!&lt;\/b&gt;\r\n[media]&lt;img src=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/wp-content\/uploads\/2015\/04\/Maya-Angelou-22Still-I-Rise22a.jpg\" alt=\"Still I Rise excerpt by Maya Angelou\" width=\"600\" height=\"314\" class=\"aligncenter size-full wp-image-13601\" \/&gt;[\/media]\r\n\r\n&lt;hr \/&gt;  \r\n\r\n&lt;b&gt;\"Still I Rise\"&lt;\/b&gt;\r\nMaya Angelou\r\n\r\nYou may write me down in history\r\nWith your bitter, twisted lies,\r\nYou may tread me in the very dirt\r\nBut still, like dust, I'll rise.\r\n\r\nDoes my sassiness upset you? \r\nWhy are you beset with gloom? \r\n'Cause I walk like I've got oil wells\r\nPumping in my living room.\r\n\r\nJust like moons and like suns,\r\nWith the certainty of tides,\r\nJust like hopes springing high,\r\nStill I'll rise.\r\n\r\nDid you want to see me broken? \r\nBowed head and lowered eyes? \r\nShoulders falling down like teardrops.\r\nWeakened by my soulful cries.\r\n\r\nDoes my haughtiness offend you? \r\nDon't you take it awful hard\r\n'Cause I laugh like I've got gold mines\r\nDiggin' in my own back yard.\r\n\r\nYou may shoot me with your words,\r\nYou may cut me with your eyes,\r\nYou may kill me with your hatefulness,\r\nBut still, like air, I'll rise.\r\n\r\nDoes my sexiness upset you? \r\nDoes it come as a surprise\r\nThat I dance like I've got diamonds\r\nAt the meeting of my thighs? \r\n\r\nOut of the huts of history's shame\r\nI rise\r\nUp from a past that's rooted in pain\r\nI rise\r\nI'm a black ocean, leaping and wide,\r\nWelling and swelling I bear in the tide.\r\nLeaving behind nights of terror and fear\r\nI rise\r\nInto a daybreak that's wondrously clear\r\nI rise\r\nBringing the gifts that my ancestors gave,\r\nI am the dream and the hope of the slave.\r\nI rise\r\nI rise\r\nI rise\r\n\r\nDo you love Maya Angelou's poetry?
