Two&lt;\/a&gt;\r\n&lt;a href=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/2015\/a-daily-dose-of-poetry-day-3\/?sf&amp;utm_campaign=PoetryMonth15&amp;utm_source=Blog_org&amp;utm_medium=text_link\"&gt;Day Three&lt;\/a&gt;\r\n&lt;a href=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/2015\/a-daily-dose-of-poetry-day-4\/?sf&amp;utm_campaign=PoetryMonth15&amp;utm_source=Blog_org&amp;utm_medium=text_link\"&gt;Day Four&lt;\/a&gt;\r\n&lt;a href=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/2015\/a-daily-dose-of-poetry-day-5\/?sf&amp;utm_campaign=PoetryMonth15&amp;utm_source=Blog_org&amp;utm_medium=text_link\"&gt;Day Five&lt;\/a&gt;\r\n&lt;a href=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/2015\/a-daily-dose-of-poetry-day-6\/?sf&amp;utm_campaign=PoetryMonth15&amp;utm_source=Blog_org&amp;utm_medium=text_link\"&gt;Day Six&lt;\/a&gt;\r\n&lt;a href=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/2015\/a-daily-dose-of-poetry-day-7\/?sf&amp;utm_campaign=PoetryMonth15&amp;utm_source=Blog_org&amp;utm_medium=text_link\"&gt;Day Seven&lt;\/a&gt;\r\n&lt;a href=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/2015\/a-daily-dose-of-poetry-day-8\/?sf&amp;utm_campaign=PoetryMonth15&amp;utm_source=Blog_org&amp;utm_medium=text_link\"&gt;Day Eight&lt;\/a&gt;\r\n&lt;a href=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/2015\/a-daily-dose-of-poetry-day-9\/?sf&amp;utm_campaign=PoetryMonth15&amp;utm_source=Blog_org&amp;utm_medium=text_link\"&gt;Day Nine&lt;\/a&gt;","post_title":"A Daily Dose of Poetry: Day 10","post_excerpt":"","post_status":"publish","comment_status":"open","ping_status":"open","post_password":"","post_name":"a-daily-dose-of-poetry-day-10","to_ping":"","pinged":"","post_modified":"2015-04-16 05:55:15","post_modified_gmt":"2015-04-16 12:55:15","post_content_filtered":"","post_parent":0,"guid":"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/?p=13469","menu_order":0,"post_type":"post","post_mime_type":"","comment_count":"0","filter":"raw"},{"ID":13325,"post_author":"1001005","post_date":"2015-04-15 05:03:26","post_date_gmt":"2015-04-15 12:03:26","post_content":"[media]&lt;img src=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/wp-content\/uploads\/2015\/04\/To-squirrel-away-money.jpg\" alt=\"To squirrel away money doesn&#039;t mean giving cash to tree-dwelling rodents. \" width=\"600\" height=\"314\" class=\"aligncenter size-full wp-image-13557\" \/&gt;[\/media]\r\n\r\nIn the United States, April 15 is Tax Day, the Internal Revenue Service\u2019s tax filing deadline. While filing taxes can cause stress and frustration, language lovers can find some solace in these creative idioms about money. Here are three of our favorites:\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Bet one\u2019s bottom dollar&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\nYour \u201cbottom dollar\u201d is the last dollar you have. If you\u2019re betting your bottom dollar, you\u2019re probably very sure that what you\u2019re betting on will turn out the way you think it will. Use of this phrase dates back to the mid-1800s.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Cost an arm and a leg&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\nIt might seem like a simple explanation, but this phrase likely became popular because arms and legs are very precious. You would think twice about purchasing something if it cost you your arm or your leg! According to &lt;a href=\"http:\/\/mentalfloss.com\/article\/56017\/10-wacky-whoppers-about-origins-popular-18th-century-phrases\" target=\"_blank\"&gt;Mentalfloss&lt;\/a&gt;, use of the related phrase, \u201cI\u2019d give my right arm\u201d began as early as the 1600s. \r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Pay the piper&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\nThis idiom supposedly originates with the story of the Pied Piper of Hamelin, Germany. According to the tale, the townspeople of Hamelin paid a piper with a magic flute to lure an infestation of rats out of the town with his song. They promised to pay him for his services, but after he rid the town of rats they took back their promise and refused to pay him. In retaliation, the piper used his magic flute to lure the children of the town away from their parents. Nearly all the children in the town were drawn away by the magical song and were never seen again. To \u201cpay the piper\u201d means to make good on your promise or face the consequences.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Squirrel away money&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\nThis idiom means to store away money with the same intensity as a squirrel preparing for winter. You can squirrel away pretty much anything, but most often this idiom is used with money. \r\n\r\n&lt;b&gt;What are your favorite idioms about money? Share them in the comments!&lt;\/b&gt;","post_title":"You Can Bet Your Bottom Dollar That We Love Idioms!","post_excerpt":"","post_status":"publish","comment_status":"open","ping_status":"open","post_password":"","post_name":"you-can-bet-your-bottom-dollar-that-we-love-idioms","to_ping":"","pinged":"","post_modified":"2015-04-15 06:21:27","post_modified_gmt":"2015-04-15 13:21:27","post_content_filtered":"","post_parent":0,"guid":"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/?p=13325","menu_order":0,"post_type":"post","post_mime_type":"","comment_count":"0","filter":"raw"},{"ID":13521,"post_author":"6","post_date":"2015-04-15 04:53:47","post_date_gmt":"2015-04-15 11:53:47","post_content":"[media]&lt;img src=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/wp-content\/uploads\/2015\/04\/librarians-save-lives.png\" alt=\"Librarians save lives Judy Blume quotation\" width=\"600\" height=\"314\" class=\"aligncenter size-full wp-image-13561\" \/&gt;[\/media]\r\n\r\nWe love the Avengers just as much as the next person (unless you are strictly anti-Marvel, to which we must ask: \"Why not DC and Marvel?\"). However, this week is National Library Week and the more we thought about it, the more we realized that librarians are the real superheroes. Here's why:\r\n\r\n&lt;b&gt;1. Librarians know how to locate every witty thing Tony Stark has said as well as every interesting or intelligent thing recorded in history.&lt;\/b&gt;\r\nIron Man is great at cracking a joke (causing massive amounts of collateral damage), but librarians are masters of research. They know that the key to knowledge it not knowing the answer to every question but knowing how to find the answer to any question. This is what lead Einstein to say: \u201cThe only thing that you absolutely have to know is the location of the library.\u201d If you are on a mission to learn about something, talk to a librarian. So, Iron Man you might be able to think of some pretty witty one-liners, but librarians can basically find all the most hilarious one-liners in the history of ever.\r\n\r\n&lt;iframe class=\"aligncenter giphy-embed\" style=\"max-width: 100%;\" src=\"\/\/giphy.com\/embed\/c4hnCnBbA19du\" width=\"480\" height=\"270\" frameborder=\"0\" allowfullscreen=\"allowfullscreen\"&gt;&lt;\/iframe&gt;\r\n\r\n&lt;b&gt;2. Librarians are way more experienced at their jobs.&lt;\/b&gt;\r\nWhile Thor has been saving Earth for at least 1,000 years (more likely 2,200 years), the other Avengers are superhero toddlers. In comparison, librarians have been organizing and caring for books, helping seekers find answers, and leading adventurers to their new favorite books for nearly three millennia. (That's not including the cumulative experience of all those amazing library workers.)\r\n\r\n&lt;iframe class=\"aligncenter giphy-embed\" style=\"max-width: 100%;\" src=\"\/\/giphy.com\/embed\/H1N1gMteRGLbq\" width=\"480\" height=\"360\" frameborder=\"0\" allowfullscreen=\"allowfullscreen\"&gt;&lt;\/iframe&gt;\r\n\r\n&lt;b&gt;3. They have personal relationships with their communities.&lt;\/b&gt;\r\nSure, the Avengers are fighting to save Earth, but in-between epic battles that destroy cities (in multiple worlds), they remain somewhat detached from their communities. In contrast, librarians are on the ground every day helping community members find employment resources, teaching children to love reading, and generally making our neighborhoods better places to live.\r\n\r\n&lt;b&gt;4. When saving the world, they don\u2019t destroy anything except simple minds.&lt;\/b&gt;\r\nIt can\u2019t have gone unnoticed that the Avengers are pretty destructive in their defense of Earth. Of course, the attacks the Avengers have countered were corporal threats, but really? Thankfully when librarians wage their war, they don\u2019t destroy anything but stereotypes, hate, and simple thinking.\r\n\r\n&lt;iframe class=\"aligncenter giphy-embed\" style=\"max-width: 100%;\" src=\"\/\/giphy.com\/embed\/DlYVPqQhwIhC8\" width=\"480\" height=\"278\" frameborder=\"0\" allowfullscreen=\"allowfullscreen\"&gt;&lt;\/iframe&gt;\r\n\r\n&lt;b&gt;5. Librarians are all about sharing. &lt;\/b&gt;\r\nUnlike the Avengers, librarians don\u2019t keep their superpowers to themselves. Librarians love to share information \u2014 how to find something, which book to read if you want to learn more about anything, what author to read if you enjoy doing X, Y, or Z. Librarians are all about sharing their skills to cultivate armies of people capable of taking knowledge and learning into their own hands.\r\n\r\n&lt;b&gt;6. Librarians don\u2019t fight with weapons. They fight with ideas. &lt;\/b&gt;\r\n&lt;a href=\"https:\/\/fremontlibraries.wordpress.com\/2008\/04\/17\/thirteen-famous-librarians-real-and-fictional\/ \" rel=\u201dnofollow\u201d&gt;Some librarians&lt;\/a&gt; have effected massive change with their ideas. But all librarians help their communities by providing better access to better information.\r\n\r\n&lt;b&gt;7. They don\u2019t get a break. &lt;\/b&gt;\r\nAfter saving the world, the Avengers go on vacation or work on other projects that don\u2019t require them to be an Avenger. Librarians don\u2019t get much of a break. They are dedicated professionals working day after day to rid the planet of ignorance. It\u2019s tiring work, but they are indefatigable.\r\n\r\n&lt;b&gt;8. They are accessible. &lt;\/b&gt;\r\nAs stated earlier, librarians are in their communities. This means that when anyone from the neighborhood has a question or wants some reading advice, they are there \u2014 ready. I have a difficult time believing that the Avengers are so easy to find or generous with their time.\r\n\r\n&lt;b&gt;9. Librarians have lifted stacks of books heavier than Thor\u2019s hammer.&lt;\/b&gt;\r\nLet\u2019s face it. These people move a lot of books. Books are heavy.\r\n\r\n&lt;iframe class=\"aligncenter giphy-embed\" style=\"max-width: 100%;\" src=\"\/\/giphy.com\/embed\/kbkhRXj6rGmUU\" width=\"480\" height=\"200\" frameborder=\"0\" allowfullscreen=\"allowfullscreen\"&gt;&lt;\/iframe&gt;\r\n\r\n&lt;b&gt;10. They are inspiring.&lt;\/b&gt;\r\n\r\n&lt;i&gt;\"I'm of a fearsome mind to throw my arms around every living librarian who crosses my path, on behalf of the souls they never knew they saved.\" \u2014 Barbara Kingsolver&lt;\/i&gt;\r\n\r\nWe're not trying to say that the Avengers can\u2019t inspire you to be a better person, but librarians have a few advantages:\r\n1) They are relatable.\r\n2) They are accessible.\r\n3) They are real.\r\nFor inspiration to be effective, we have to know that it is possible to achieve. When we see intelligent, creative, and hard-working people around us, it provides a compelling example for us to emulate. Trying to emulate the Hulk is probably just going to get you a few awkward looks and some vandalism fines.\r\n\r\n&lt;b&gt;11. They often do all of this with budget cuts. &lt;\/b&gt;\r\nThough not strictly so, a librarian\u2019s ability to \u201cmake due\u201d borders on supernatural. The truth is that librarians are waging war on ignorance with pure human talent and a Xerox machine running out of toner. No military elixirs, mishaps with gamma radiation, or \u201cbillionaire philanthropists\u201d help librarians to provide interesting seasonal programming, update the computing systems, or cover subscriptions to several cutting-edge journals. The work is done on shrinking public budgets and local donations.\r\n\r\n&lt;iframe class=\"aligncenter giphy-embed\" style=\"max-width: 100%;\" src=\"\/\/giphy.com\/embed\/128MHrlrHNwwU0\" width=\"480\" height=\"216\" frameborder=\"0\" allowfullscreen=\"allowfullscreen\"&gt;&lt;\/iframe&gt;\r\n\r\n&lt;b&gt;This week as National Library Week and April 14th was National Library Workers Day. Tell us about your amazing library or librarians in the comments! &lt;\/b&gt;","post_title":"11 Reasons Librarians Are Better Superheroes Than The Avengers","post_excerpt":"This week as National Library Week and April 14th was National Library Workers Day. The rim\r\nOf the sky will be the colour of hard crimson,\r\nAnd your heart, as it was then, will be on fire.\r\n\r\nThat day in Moscow, it will all come true,\r\nwhen, for the last time, I take my leave,\r\nAnd hasten to the heights that I have longed for,\r\nLeaving my shadow still to be with you. \r\n\r\nRead more poetry from Akhmatova in her &lt;i&gt;http:\/\/www.amazon.com\/Final-Meeting-Selected-Poetry-Akhmatova-ebook\/dp\/B00E9B0YAC\/ref=sr_1_2?ie=UTF8&amp;qid=1428999238&amp;sr=8-2&amp;keywords=akhmatova\"&gt;Final Meeting: Selected Poems of Anna Akhmatova&lt;\/a&gt;&lt;\/i&gt;. \r\n&nbsp;\r\n\r\nIs Anna Akhmatova your poet-soulmate? 9","post_excerpt":"","post_status":"publish","comment_status":"open","ping_status":"open","post_password":"","post_name":"a-daily-dose-of-poetry-day-9","to_ping":"","pinged":"","post_modified":"2015-04-15 02:00:33","post_date_gmt":"2015-04-13 09:00:33","post_content":"&lt;em&gt;&lt;a href=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/wp-content\/uploads\/2015\/03\/11079571_10206611763552214_2586068504717953290_n.jpg\"&gt;&lt;img class=\"alignleft size-medium wp-image-12833\" src=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/wp-content\/uploads\/2015\/03\/11079571_10206611763552214_2586068504717953290_n-410x410.jpg\" alt=\"Anna Marketti, Grammarly, poetry\" width=\"410\" height=\"410\" \/&gt;&lt;\/a&gt;&lt;i&gt;Guest post by Anna Marketti&lt;\/i&gt;&lt;\/em&gt;\r\n\r\nThere is poetry in the way his eyelids flutter while he sleeps, his curls gently drooping down over his forehead. His complete resignation to the world inspires a similar sense of submission in my writing- deconstructing that wall between the words I know how to spell and the words I truly want to say. He snores, but lightly, the same way not every stanza may be perfect, filled with heavy, misplaced words every now and then. The curve of his lips, revealing that perfectly imperfect smile, as they prepare to kiss mine, no regard for the fact that neither of us have brushed our teeth yet- it inspires a want, a need to let each word be filled with urgent sincerity.\r\n\r\nThere is poetry in the way a cup of coffee tastes, strikingly bitter, but leaving a trail of wholesome warmth throughout your body. That first biting sip, too hot and scalding your tired tongue, inspires that necessary first word, sometimes forced and clumsy. The brown ring left at the bottom of the mug, a gentle reminder that all good things must come to an end- even the best sentence. The dancing flavors in your mouth, hints of this nut and notes of that fruit, inspire a melding of techniques, styles, and formats- not every poem has to rhyme.\r\n\r\nHere is poetry- sectioned out five, seven five. Heard of a haiku? Floats so delicate, you can hardly notice it. So many poem styles. Can\u2019t forget iambic pentameter. It\u2019s what made Shakespeare so very well known. The list can go on, poetry is infinitely diverse. It can be as breathtaking as a fawn, or as horrid as a curse. Boasting endless combinations, see, poetry is anything and everything in between. I\u2019m now on paragraph three, and still bitter that your strict essay structure is a rule- how mean! (A pause to observe this is actually paragraph four. Poets are seldom skilled in math.) Poetry influences my writing in that it dares me. It dares me to veer from tradition. It dares me to write what I feel rather than simply what is required. Hey- I only have ten more words to the minimum! (Rats.)\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;About the Author&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\n\r\nEmerson College class of 2017. Writer for Allston Pudding, Sound of Boston, Emertainment Monthly, and myself. Lover of cats, coffee, and Chuck Palahniuk. It's all for Kevin.","post_title":"Paragraphs of Poetry","post_excerpt":"","post_status":"publish","comment_status":"open","ping_status":"open","post_password":"","post_name":"paragraphs-of-poetry","to_ping":"","pinged":"","post_modified":"2015-03-25 10:12:30","post_modified_gmt":"2015-03-25 17:12:30","post_content_filtered":"","post_parent":0,"guid":"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/?p=12741","menu_order":0,"post_type":"post","post_mime_type":"","comment_count":"0","filter":"raw"},{"ID":13381,"post_author":"2","post_date":"2015-04-12 02:45:38","post_date_gmt":"2015-04-12 09:45:38","post_content":"&lt;i&gt;This Monday, April 13, is Scrabble Day, and Grammarly is celebrating with our fellow word-lovers, &lt;\/i&gt;&lt;a href=\"http:\/\/www.dictionary.com\"&gt;&lt;i&gt;Dictionary.com&lt;\/i&gt;&lt;\/a&gt;&lt;i&gt;!&lt;\/i&gt;\r\n\r\n&lt;i&gt;Guest post by Michele Turner, CEO at Dictionary.com&lt;\/i&gt;\r\n\r\nCan you play a Q without a U in Scrabble? Whether you\u2019re playing Scrabble, Words With Friends, or any other fun word game, here is &lt;a href=\"http:\/\/dictionary.reference.com\/slideshows\/q-without-u\"&gt;a list&lt;\/a&gt; of nine high-scoring solutions for the \u201cQ conundrum,\u201d so that you can make winning words with the letter Q -- without its traditional letter companion, the U.\r\n&lt;ul&gt;\r\n\t&lt;li&gt;&lt;b&gt;Qi&lt;\/b&gt; \u2013 a variant spelling on the word chi, qi is the vital life force believed to circulate around the body and through the universe in Chinese medicine.&lt;\/li&gt;\r\n\t&lt;li&gt;&lt;b&gt;Qat&lt;\/b&gt; \u2013 an evergreen shrub of Arabia and Africa, the leaves of which are used as a narcotic when chewed or made into a beverage.&lt;\/li&gt;\r\n\t&lt;li&gt;&lt;b&gt;Faqir&lt;\/b&gt; \u2013 a Muslim or Hindu religious ascetic or mendicant monk commonly considered a wonder-worker or a dervish. \u00a0Played on a triple word, this term could work wonders for your game. Can also be spelled &lt;i&gt;fakir&lt;\/i&gt; or &lt;i&gt;faquir&lt;\/i&gt;.&lt;\/li&gt;\r\n\t&lt;li&gt;&lt;b&gt;QWERTY&lt;\/b&gt; \u2013 yes, this acronym coined in the 1920s to describe the standard keyboard, can be used in the game.&lt;\/li&gt;\r\n\t&lt;li&gt;&lt;b&gt;Qaid&lt;\/b&gt; \u2013 refers to a tribal chief, judge, or senior official, and it stems from the Arabic word meaning \u201cleader.\u201d It entered the English language in the mid-1800s and is sometimes spelled &lt;i&gt;caid&lt;\/i&gt;.&lt;\/li&gt;\r\n\t&lt;li&gt;&lt;b&gt;Qadi&lt;\/b&gt; \u2013 related to &lt;i&gt;qaid&lt;\/i&gt;, this word refers to a judge in a Muslim community. It\u2019s a useful variant when trying to stack words to play\u00a0&lt;i&gt;do&lt;\/i&gt;\u00a0or\u00a0&lt;i&gt;it&lt;\/i&gt;.&lt;\/li&gt;\r\n\t&lt;li&gt;&lt;b&gt;Sheqel&lt;\/b&gt; \u2013 an ancient unit of weight equivalent to about a quarter or half an ounce. Today it is commonly used to refer to the Israeli currency, though it is also a slang term meaning money. Also spelled &lt;i&gt;shekel&lt;\/i&gt;.&lt;\/li&gt;\r\n\t&lt;li&gt;&lt;b&gt;Qindar&lt;\/b&gt; \u2013 a monetary denomination in Albania that is equivalent to 100th of a lek, the standard monetary unit. Also spelled &lt;i&gt;qintar.&lt;\/i&gt;&lt;\/li&gt;\r\n\t&lt;li&gt;&lt;b&gt;Qoph&lt;\/b&gt; \u2013 the 19th letter of the Hebrew alphabet.&lt;\/li&gt;\r\n&lt;\/ul&gt;\r\nWant more for your word game arsenal? Get Scrabble and Words With Friends word values by searching words at Dictionary.com. Search results will show a word\u2019s corresponding score on the right hand side of the page, truly bringing words \u2013 and your game \u2013 to life! Keep these ten valuable words in mind as well; which will all rack up points in both games.\r\n&lt;ul&gt;\r\n\t&lt;li&gt;&lt;b&gt;Chapeau&lt;\/b&gt; \u2013 a hat.&lt;\/li&gt;\r\n\t&lt;li&gt;&lt;b&gt;Convex&lt;\/b&gt; \u2013 having a surface that is curved or rounded outward.&lt;\/li&gt;\r\n\t&lt;li&gt;&lt;b&gt;Exequy&lt;\/b&gt; \u2013 a funeral procession.&lt;\/li&gt;\r\n\t&lt;li&gt;&lt;b&gt;Frequent&lt;\/b&gt; \u2013 happening or occurring at short intervals.&lt;\/li&gt;\r\n\t&lt;li&gt;&lt;b&gt;Jacquard\u2013&lt;\/b&gt; a fabric with an elaborately woven pattern produced on a Jacquard loom.&lt;\/li&gt;\r\n\t&lt;li&gt;&lt;b&gt;Jaywalk&lt;\/b&gt; \u2013 to cross a street at a place other than a regular crossing or in a heedless manner, as diagonally or against a traffic light.&lt;\/li&gt;\r\n\t&lt;li&gt;&lt;b&gt;Kumquat&lt;\/b&gt; \u2013 small, round or oblong citrus fruit having a sweet rind and acid pulp, used chiefly for preserves.&lt;\/li&gt;\r\n\t&lt;li&gt;&lt;b&gt;Mystique&lt;\/b&gt; \u2013 an aura of mystery of mystical power surrounding a particular occupation or pursuit.&lt;\/li&gt;\r\n\t&lt;li&gt;&lt;b&gt;Quixotic&lt;\/b&gt; \u2013 extravagantly chivalrous or romantic; visionary, impractical, or impracticable.&lt;\/li&gt;\r\n\t&lt;li&gt;&lt;b&gt;Quiz&lt;\/b&gt; \u2013 an informal test or examination of a student or class.&lt;\/li&gt;\r\n&lt;\/ul&gt;\r\nBroadening your command of language by building your vocabulary not only gives you an edge in gameplay, it also translates to better communication skills in all parts of your life.\r\n\r\n&lt;i&gt;What\u2019s the best Scrabble word you\u2019ve played? How can a board game become so popular that it actually has its own holiday? Those who have played Scrabble know that it\u2019s an addictive, brain-busting game that delights word nerds and language lovers. There\u2019s even an annual US National Scrabble Championship and World Scrabble Championship!\r\n\r\nConrad Bassett-Bouchard, last year\u2019s winner of the National Scrabble Championship, won $10,000 after beating a five-time champion. What\u2019s the best way to prepare for the championship? He suggests studying anagrams so your mind can unscramble your Scrabble tiles with ease. \r\n\r\nAn anagram is a word, phrase, or name formed by rearranging the letters of another word or phrase. If you want to practice for the next Scrabble Championship, test your skills by creating anagrams from the word \u201cGrammarly.\u201d Here are a few to get you started:\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Grammarly&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\nalarm\r\ngray \r\nmama\r\nmarry\r\n\r\nShare your favorite anagrams in the comments!","post_title":"Do You Have What It Takes to Be a Scrabble Champion?","post_excerpt":"","post_status":"publish","comment_status":"open","ping_status":"open","post_password":"","post_name":"do-you-have-what-it-takes-to-be-a-scrabble-champion","to_ping":"","pinged":"","post_modified":"2015-04-10 08:03:31","post_modified_gmt":"2015-04-10 15:03:31","post_content_filtered":"","post_parent":0,"guid":"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/?p=13321","menu_order":0,"post_type":"post","post_mime_type":"","comment_count":"0","filter":"raw"},{"ID":13311,"post_author":"1001005","post_date":"2015-04-10 07:48:04","post_date_gmt":"2015-04-10 14:48:04","post_content":"[media]&lt;img src=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/wp-content\/uploads\/2015\/04\/543rfwdas.jpg\" alt=\"When you absolutely positively need to know, ask a librarian. \" width=\"600\" height=\"314\" class=\"aligncenter size-full wp-image-13341\" \/&gt;[\/media]\r\n\r\nApril 14 is National Library Workers\u2019 Day. Given the literary nature of library work, it\u2019s not surprising that some of our favorite authors were also librarians. Here\u2019s a short list of writers who put in time between the stacks.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Lewis Carroll&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\nBefore he began writing &lt;em&gt;Alice\u2019s Adventures in Wonderland&lt;\/em&gt;, Lewis Carroll (whose real name was Charles Dodgson) worked as a librarian in England.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Jorge Luis Borges&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\nThis Argentinian author had quite a successful career as a librarian. After working as a librarian for years, Borges served as the Director of the National Library of the Argentine Republic.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Madeleine L\u2019Engle&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\nAfter the success of her Newbery Award-winning novel &lt;em&gt;A Wrinkle in Time&lt;\/em&gt;, author Madeleine L\u2019Engle had a career as a librarian in New York City.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Marcel Proust&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\nProust is famous for his novel &lt;em&gt;In Search of Lost Time&lt;\/em&gt;, but he also worked at the Biblioth\u00e8que Mazarine. The Biblioth\u00e8que Mazarine is the oldest public library in France. \r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Beverly Cleary&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\nNewbery Award-winning children\u2019s book author Beverly Cleary also worked as a children\u2019s librarian. Perhaps her experience putting books in the hands of eager readers helped her create some of her famous characters like Ramona Quimby and Henry Huggins. ","post_title":"The Secret to Success? Many of Our Favorite Writers Also Worked as Librarians.","post_excerpt":"","post_status":"publish","comment_status":"open","ping_status":"open","post_password":"","post_name":"the-secret-to-success-many-of-our-favorite-writers-also-worked-as-librarians","to_ping":"","pinged":"","post_modified":"2015-04-10 07:48:04","post_modified_gmt":"2015-04-10 14:48:04","post_content_filtered":"","post_parent":0,"guid":"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/?p=13311","menu_order":0,"post_type":"post","post_mime_type":"","comment_count":"0","filter":"raw"},{"ID":13145,"post_author":"1001005","post_date":"2015-04-10 06:31:06","post_date_gmt":"2015-04-10 13:31:06","post_content":"[media]&lt;img src=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/wp-content\/uploads\/2015\/04\/5te3.jpg\" alt=\"I&#039;m looking forward to purchasing tickets for the 2018 winter Olympics.\" width=\"600\" height=\"314\" class=\"aligncenter size-full wp-image-13337\" \/&gt;[\/media]\r\n\r\nIn a recent Grammarly Pop Quiz email, we asked readers how they would correct this sentence:\r\n&lt;b&gt;I'm looking forward to purchasing tickets for the 2018 winter Olympics.&lt;\/b&gt;\r\n\r\nA: Capitalize the \"W\" in winter.\r\nB: Make \"O\" in Olympics lowercase.\r\nC: Accept the sentence as is.\r\n\r\n44 percent of these readers gave the correct answer, A. Did you choose correctly?\r\n\r\nSeasons are only capitalized when they\u2019re used as proper nouns, as in the sentence above:\r\n&lt;i&gt;The movie will debut in the &lt;b&gt;spring&lt;\/b&gt; of 2016.&lt;\/i&gt; (Spring is not used as a proper noun and doesn\u2019t need to be capitalized.)\r\n&lt;i&gt;The Springfield &lt;b&gt;Autumn&lt;\/b&gt; Harvest Festival is my favorite event of the year.&lt;\/i&gt; (Autumn is part of a proper noun in this sentence as needs to be capitalized.)\r\n&lt;i&gt;This &lt;b&gt;winter&lt;\/b&gt;, I want to learn how to ski.&lt;\/i&gt; (Winter is not used as a proper noun and doesn\u2019t need to be capitalized.)\r\n\r\nIn poetry, the names of the seasons are capitalized when they\u2019re personified. For example:\r\n&lt;i&gt;It\u2019s that time of year again, when &lt;b&gt;Winter&lt;\/b&gt; blankets all creatures with somniferous snow and whispers to them, \u201cRest, children, rest.\u201d&lt;\/i&gt;\r\n\r\n&lt;b&gt;If you\u2019d like to receive our pop quiz emails and other fun emails about grammar and language, sign up for our mailing list in the sidebar!&lt;\/b&gt;","post_title":"Do You Know the Correct Answer to Our Pop Quiz Question?","post_excerpt":"","post_status":"publish","comment_status":"open","ping_status":"open","post_password":"","post_name":"do-you-know-the-correct-answer-to-our-pop-quiz-question","to_ping":"","pinged":"","post_modified":"2015-04-10 06:31:06","post_modified_gmt":"2015-04-10 13:31:06","post_content_filtered":"","post_parent":0,"guid":"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/?p=13145","menu_order":0,"post_type":"post","post_mime_type":"","comment_count":"0","filter":"raw"},{"ID":13141,"post_author":"1001005","post_date":"2015-04-09 07:31:27","post_date_gmt":"2015-04-09 14:31:27","post_content":"[media]&lt;img src=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/wp-content\/uploads\/2015\/04\/reading-in-bed1.jpg\" alt=\"I tried everything to get to sleep last night. Well, everything except closing the book \u2014 let&#039;s not get too crazy. \" width=\"600\" height=\"314\" class=\"aligncenter size-full wp-image-13275\" \/&gt;[\/media]\r\n\r\nFor many book lovers, reading in bed is one of life\u2019s greatest pleasures. In order to enjoy the experience to the fullest, it\u2019s important to choose the right book for the right time. Here are three books we recommend reading in bed.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;When you want to stay in bed longer: &lt;i&gt;His Dark Materials&lt;\/i&gt; series by Philip Pullman &lt;\/strong&gt;\r\nAlthough this series is written for children, it\u2019s a pleasure to read as an adult. Let Pullman\u2019s writing transport you to a magical world that parallels our own. Talking animals, daring adventures, epic battles\u2014this series has it all. Stay in bed for a few extra hours and enjoy this magical series.\r\n \r\n&lt;strong&gt;When you want to fall gently asleep: &lt;i&gt;The Grapes of Wrath&lt;\/i&gt; by John Steinbeck &lt;\/strong&gt;\r\nDon\u2019t get me wrong\u2014this novel is anything but boring. John Steinbeck\u2019s &lt;i&gt;The Grapes of Wrath&lt;\/i&gt; is a beautifully layered masterpiece. But the cadence of the language and the dry, dusty expanse of the scenery make for a soothing read that will lull your brain into a sleep-ready state. \r\n \r\n&lt;strong&gt;When you want to induce a dreamlike state: &lt;i&gt;Hard-Boiled Wonderland and the End of the World&lt;\/i&gt; by Haruki Murakami &lt;\/strong&gt;\r\nMurakami\u2019s novel blends sci-fi and existentialism to create a dreamy, modernist experience. If you have trouble sleeping, read a few pages of this book and find yourself transported into a state that feels like dreaming but is still grounded in reality. Lean back against the pillow, pull that blanket up, and enjoy.\r\n\r\nWhat's your favorite book to read in bed? Share your thoughts in the comments!","post_title":"Can't Sleep? Here are 3 Books You Should Read in Bed","post_excerpt":"","post_status":"publish","comment_status":"open","ping_status":"open","post_password":"","post_name":"cant-sleep-here-are-3-books-you-should-read-in-bed","to_ping":"","pinged":"","post_modified":"2015-04-09 07:31:27","post_modified_gmt":"2015-04-09 14:31:27","post_content_filtered":"","post_parent":0,"guid":"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/?p=13141","menu_order":0,"post_type":"post","post_mime_type":"","comment_count":"0","filter":"raw"},{"ID":13047,"post_author":"6","post_date":"2015-04-09 07:29:32","post_date_gmt":"2015-04-09 14:29:32","post_content":"&lt;b&gt;Help us to celebrate National Poetry Month; share this poem with your friends!&lt;\/b&gt;\r\n[media]&lt;img src=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/wp-content\/uploads\/2015\/04\/poetry-fire.jpg\" alt=\"poetry. is the fire leaving my body.\" width=\"600\" height=\"314\" class=\"aligncenter size-full wp-image-13271\" \/&gt;[\/media]\r\n\r\n&lt;hr \/&gt;  \r\n\r\n&lt;b&gt;Excerpt from &lt;i&gt;&lt;a href=\"http:\/\/www.amazon.com\/salt-nayyirah-waheed-ebook\/dp\/B00FPTPLQO\/ref=sr_1_1?s=books&amp;ie=UTF8&amp;qid=1427973225&amp;sr=1-1&amp;keywords=salt.\"&gt;salt.&lt;\/a&gt;&lt;\/i&gt;&lt;\/b&gt;\r\nNayyirah Waheed\r\n\r\npoetry.\r\nis the fire leaving my body. \r\n\r\nRead more poetry by Nayyriah Waheed by visiting her &lt;a href=\"http:\/\/nayyirahwaheed.tumblr.com\/\"&gt;Tumblr&lt;\/a&gt; and following her &lt;a href=\"https:\/\/twitter.com\/nayyirahwaheed\"&gt;on Twitter&lt;\/a&gt;. \r\n&nbsp;\r\n\r\n&lt;hr \/&gt;\r\n\r\nEvery weekday in April, we will be sharing a poem, an excerpt of poetry, or a feature on a poet. share this poem with your friends!&lt;\/b&gt;\r\n[media]&lt;img src=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/wp-content\/uploads\/2015\/04\/43erds.jpg\" alt=\"&quot;Of Man\u2019s First Disobedience, and the Fruit  Of that Forbidden Tree, whose mortal taste  Brought Death into the World . . .&quot;\" width=\"600\" height=\"314\" class=\"aligncenter size-full wp-image-13233\" \/&gt;[\/media]\r\n\r\n&lt;hr \/&gt;\r\n\r\nIn the initial lines of his epic poem, John Milton outlines his subject \u2014 mankind's fall from grace in the garden of Eden. In the introduction, Milton invokes his muse, the Holy Spirit, asserts the virtuousness and originality of his epic (over and above perviously written epics on the same topic), and prays for guidance and protection from heresy as he aims to clarify God's motives and reasoning. \r\n\r\n&lt;i&gt;Paradise Lost&lt;\/i&gt; is in the public domain and can be downloaded for free via &lt;a href=\"https:\/\/www.gutenberg.org\/ebooks\/20\"&gt;Project Gutenburg&lt;\/a&gt;.  \r\n\r\n&lt;b&gt;Excerpt from &lt;i&gt;Paradise Lost&lt;\/i&gt;&lt;\/b&gt;\r\nJohn Milton\r\n\r\nOf Man\u2019s First Disobedience, and the Fruit\r\nOf that Forbidden Tree, whose mortal taste\r\nBrought Death into the World, and all our woe,\r\nWith loss of Eden, till one greater Man\r\nRestore us, and regain the blissful\r\nSeat, Sing Heav\u2019nly Muse, that on the secret top\r\nOf Oreb, or of Sinai, didst inspire\r\nThat Shepherd, who first taught the chosen\r\nSeed, In the Beginning how the Heav\u2019ns and Earth\r\nRose out of Chaos: Or if Sion Hill\r\nDelight thee more, and Siloa\u2019s Brook that flow\u2019d\r\nFast by the Oracle of God; I thence\r\nInvoke thy aid to my advent\u2019rous Song,\r\nThat with no middle flight intends to soar\r\nAbove th\u2019 Aonian Mount, while it pursues\r\nThings unattempted yet in Prose or Rhyme.\r\n\r\n(Lines 1-26)\r\n\r\n&nbsp;\r\n\r\n&lt;hr \/&gt;\r\n\r\nThere are hundreds of ways you can celebrate National Poetry Month, here are &lt;a href=\"http:\/\/www.poets.org\/national-poetry-month\/30-ways-celebrate-national-poetry-month\"&gt;30 suggestions&lt;\/a&gt; from the Academy of American Poets.\r\n\r\nEvery weekday in April, we will be sharing a poem, an excerpt of poetry, or a feature on a poet. Our celebration will feature international poetry from every era, and we ask our friends to join us throughout the month by sharing their favorite poetry under the tag #PoetryMonth.\r\n\r\nOther daily poetry: \r\n&lt;a href=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/2015\/a-daily-dose-of-poetry-day-1\/?sf&amp;utm_campaign=PoetryMonth15&amp;utm_source=Blog_org&amp;utm_medium=text_link\"&gt;Day One&lt;\/a&gt;\r\n&lt;a href=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/2015\/a-daily-dose-of-poetry-day-2\/?sf&amp;utm_campaign=PoetryMonth15&amp;utm_source=Blog_org&amp;utm_medium=text_link\"&gt;Day Two&lt;\/a&gt;\r\n&lt;a href=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/2015\/a-daily-dose-of-poetry-day-3\/?sf&amp;utm_campaign=PoetryMonth15&amp;utm_source=Blog_org&amp;utm_medium=text_link\"&gt;Day Three&lt;\/a&gt;\r\n&lt;a href=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/2015\/a-daily-dose-of-poetry-day-4\/?sf&amp;utm_campaign=PoetryMonth15&amp;utm_source=Blog_org&amp;utm_medium=text_link\"&gt;Day Four&lt;\/a&gt;\r\n\r\n","post_title":"A Daily Dose of Poetry: Day 5","post_excerpt":"","post_status":"publish","comment_status":"open","ping_status":"open","post_password":"","post_name":"a-daily-dose-of-poetry-day-5","to_ping":"","pinged":"","post_modified":"2015-04-08 03:58:42","post_modified_gmt":"2015-04-08 10:58:42","post_content_filtered":"","post_parent":0,"guid":"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/?p=12951","menu_order":0,"post_type":"post","post_mime_type":"","comment_count":"0","filter":"raw"},{"ID":13077,"post_author":"1001005","post_date":"2015-04-07 06:49:16","post_date_gmt":"2015-04-07 13:49:16","post_content":"[media]&lt;img src=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/wp-content\/uploads\/2015\/04\/Love-the-subjunctive-mood.jpg\" alt=\"Love the subjunctive mood. \" width=\"600\" height=\"314\" class=\"aligncenter size-full wp-image-13207\" \/&gt;[\/media]\r\n\r\nThe subjunctive mood sounds complex at first (who knew that verbs had moods?), but it\u2019s actually a fairly simple concept. The subjunctive is usually the third-person form of the verb with the \u2011s dropped. For example, when using the verb &lt;i&gt;to be&lt;\/i&gt; in the subjunctive, the present tense is &lt;i&gt;be&lt;\/i&gt;, and the past tense is &lt;i&gt;were&lt;\/i&gt;. Here are a few other examples:\r\n\r\n&lt;i&gt;It is recommended that she &lt;b&gt;drink&lt;\/b&gt; water throughout the day to stay hydrated.\r\nI demand that your brother &lt;b&gt;attend&lt;\/b&gt; my birthday party.\r\nThe author suggests that students &lt;b&gt;submit&lt;\/b&gt; their college applications early.&lt;\/i&gt;\r\n\r\n&lt;b&gt;The subjunctive is usually used with verbs like &lt;i&gt;desire, ask,&lt;\/i&gt; and &lt;i&gt;require&lt;\/i&gt;, where whatever is desired or asked or required may not actually be done.&lt;\/b&gt; If you\u2019re unsure whether you should use the subjective or not, check to see if your sentence has this kind of structure.\r\n\r\n&lt;b&gt;The subjunctive mood also comes into play with conditionals.&lt;\/b&gt; In these cases, the form of the verb &lt;i&gt;to be&lt;\/i&gt; is &lt;i&gt;were&lt;\/i&gt;. These are sentences in which you\u2019re expressing a wish, hope, or fantasy. For example:\r\n\r\n&lt;i&gt;I wish I &lt;b&gt;were&lt;\/b&gt; tall enough to ride the roller coaster.\r\nIf I &lt;b&gt;were&lt;\/b&gt; a bird, I would fly far away from here.&lt;\/i&gt; \r\n\r\nTo learn more about verb tenses, &lt;a href=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/2014\/conjugating-verbs-doesnt-have-to-make-you-tenseab\/\"&gt;read this post&lt;\/a&gt; about how to conjugate the various forms of \u201clay.\u201d  ","post_title":"The Skinny on the Subjunctive","post_excerpt":"","post_status":"publish","comment_status":"open","ping_status":"open","post_password":"","post_name":"the-skinny-on-the-subjunctive","to_ping":"","pinged":"","post_modified":"2015-04-07 06:49:16","post_modified_gmt":"2015-04-07 13:49:16","post_content_filtered":"","post_parent":0,"guid":"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/?p=13077","menu_order":0,"post_type":"post","post_mime_type":"","comment_count":"0","filter":"raw"},{"ID":13181,"post_author":"6","post_date":"2015-04-07 02:13:55","post_date_gmt":"2015-04-07 09:13:55","post_content":"[media]&lt;img src=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/wp-content\/uploads\/2015\/04\/5y6t4egdfv.jpg\" alt=\"His correct use of &quot;whom&quot; makes me think this cat isn&#039;t messing around.\" width=\"600\" height=\"314\" class=\"aligncenter size-full wp-image-13183\" \/&gt;[\/media]\r\n\r\nWhen it comes to formal writing for school or work, it's important that if you use who and whom, you know how to use them correctly. Here is one simple trick for checking your use of whom. \r\n\r\n&lt;b&gt;If you can replace whom with him, you have most likely used it correctly. \r\nIf you replace who with he and the sentence no longer makes sense, you should use whom. &lt;\/b&gt;\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n","post_title":"The Best Tip for Using \"Whom\" In Formal Writing","post_excerpt":"","post_status":"publish","comment_status":"open","ping_status":"open","post_password":"","post_name":"the-best-tip-for-using-whom-in-formal-writing","to_ping":"","pinged":"","post_modified":"2015-04-07 02:13:55","post_modified_gmt":"2015-04-07 09:13:55","post_content_filtered":"","post_parent":0,"guid":"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/?p=13181","menu_order":0,"post_type":"post","post_mime_type":"","comment_count":"1","filter":"raw"},{"ID":12929,"post_author":"6","post_date":"2015-04-07 01:36:49","post_date_gmt":"2015-04-07 08:36:49","post_content":"&lt;b&gt;It's National Poetry Month. Share this poem with your friends!&lt;\/b&gt;\r\n[media]&lt;img src=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/wp-content\/uploads\/2015\/04\/emily-D-soul3.jpg\" alt=\"The Soul should always stand ajar.That if the heaven inquire, He will not be obliged to wait . . .\" width=\"600\" height=\"314\" class=\"aligncenter size-full wp-image-13217\" \/&gt;[\/media]\r\n\r\n&lt;hr \/&gt;\r\n\r\n&lt;b&gt;Part Four: Time and Eternity, CXXI&lt;\/b&gt;\r\nEmily Dickinson\r\n\r\nTHE SOUL should always stand ajar.\t\r\n  That if the heaven inquire,\t\r\nHe will not be obliged to wait,\t\r\n  Or shy of troubling her.\t\r\n  \r\nDepart, before the host has slid\t       \r\n  The bolt upon the door,\t\r\nTo seek for the accomplished guest\u2014\t\r\n  Her visitor no more.\r\n\r\n&nbsp;\r\n\r\n&lt;hr \/&gt;\r\n\r\nEvery weekday in April, we will be sharing a poem, an excerpt of poetry, or a feature on a poet. \/&gt;[\/media] \r\n\r\n\r\nWhat do you think?\r\n\r\nShare your opinion in the comments!","post_title":"Discussion: Do we still need to learn spelling?","post_excerpt":"","post_status":"publish","comment_status":"open","ping_status":"open","post_password":"","post_name":"discussion-do-we-still-need-to-learn-spelling","to_ping":"","pinged":"","post_modified":"2015-04-06 03:49:40","post_modified_gmt":"2015-04-06 10:49:40","post_content_filtered":"","post_parent":0,"guid":"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/?p=13099","menu_order":0,"post_type":"post","post_mime_type":"","comment_count":"0","filter":"raw"},{"ID":13071,"post_author":"1001005","post_date":"2015-04-06 03:42:39","post_date_gmt":"2015-04-06 10:42:39","post_content":"[media]&lt;img src=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/wp-content\/uploads\/2015\/04\/5e3w.jpg\" alt=\"Language evolves.\" width=\"600\" height=\"315\" class=\"aligncenter size-full wp-image-13093\" \/&gt;[\/media]\r\n\r\nThe &lt;i&gt;Associated Press Stylebook&lt;\/i&gt; is a guide for style and usage in written news reporting. Why do writers need a style guide? A style guide helps writers know what the rules are: whether the Oxford comma should be used or not, when to spell out numerals, how to capitalize the names of organizations, etc. Writers may use other style guides in different situations, including the &lt;i&gt;Chicago Manual of Style&lt;\/i&gt; and the &lt;i&gt;American Medical Association Manual of Style&lt;\/i&gt;.\r\nEach year, the &lt;i&gt;AP Stylebook&lt;\/i&gt; is updated to include new terms and popular word usages. Here are two interesting updates being made to this year\u2019s Stylebook:\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Social media terminology&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\nSocial media users, rejoice! The terms \u201cfavorite\u201d (used as a verb) and \u201cmeme\u201d have been added to the AP Stylebook this year. \r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Global warming&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\nIn the past, it\u2019s been unclear whether journalists should use the term \u201cglobal warming\u201d or \u201cclimate change.\u201d Now writers don\u2019t have to choose because the AP has declared that the terms can be used interchangeably. \r\n\r\nThe AP bases its Stylebook updates on common usage of words so that the language writers use is accurate and makes sense to readers. \r\nWhat terminology do you think should be added to or clarified in the &lt;i&gt;AP Stylebook&lt;\/i&gt;? Share your thoughts in the comments!","post_title":"Hot Off the Presses: New changes to the AP Stylebook","post_excerpt":"","post_status":"publish","comment_status":"open","ping_status":"open","post_password":"","post_name":"hot-off-the-presses-new-changes-to-the-ap-stylebook","to_ping":"","pinged":"","post_modified":"2015-04-14 08:41:58","post_modified_gmt":"2015-04-14 15:41:58","post_content_filtered":"","post_parent":0,"guid":"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/?p=13071","menu_order":0,"post_type":"post","post_mime_type":"","comment_count":"0","filter":"raw"},{"ID":12733,"post_author":"2","post_date":"2015-04-06 02:00:48","post_date_gmt":"2015-04-06 09:00:48","post_content":"&lt;i&gt;&lt;em&gt;Guest post by\u00a0Kaytee Green&lt;\/em&gt;&lt;\/i&gt;\r\n&lt;p style=\"text-align: center;\"&gt;&lt;i&gt;&lt;em&gt;\u201cPoetry is when an emotion has found its thought and the thought has found words.\u201d &lt;\/em&gt;&lt;\/i&gt;(Robert Frost)&lt;\/p&gt;\r\nWe call poetry words, but this is wrong. Words are words. Poetry is something more. Poetry is something that inhabits the depths of a man\u2019s soul and moves his pen to give life to words and so ascribe what some call poetry.\r\n\r\nPoetry is what lies behind the words, prompting them into being. Take a strand of expression, a strand of passion, and a strand of order. Let the lithe fingers of language plait them together with experience born of wisdom till they spill onto a page in verses. Then read them aloud with the voice of feeling. Shout them from the tree tops with the peril of passion. Quote them in the assembly with the tone of Wisdom. Whisper them to your children with an air of mystery. This is poetry.\r\n\r\nPoetry is not tucked away in a clever rhyme, nor strung about in garrulous language. Rhymes are rhymes and words are words whether garrulous or otherwise. Poetry is something more. True Poetry secrets itself away in the heart of us each. Those words that touch us deep within, that quaff the extremities of our heart; those are but the expression of Poetry.\r\n\r\nPoetry, in essence, is a wordless phrase. It is a song, a song of magnificence that touches and stirs our inner beings. Poetry is the aged wind rustling the great pines in a whisper of remorse for bygone days. It is the still yet powerful voice of one whom we love. Poetry is the aroma of Mother\u2019s molasses cookies in the tangy chill of a fall afternoon. Poetry is the great sea crashing and churning in all her splendor, as if she would break from her seething bed and swallow the earth whole. Poetry is a child after the rain, tumbling in the mud with a puppy and laughing at the water-drenched world. Poetry is a song \u2013 the song of life \u2013 that stirs our inner beings. Poetry is that part of us tied to something beyond.\r\n\r\nPoetry influences literature like the presence of air influences existence. I could extricate it. I could ignore its presence. I could type out long blocks of dry words with no regard for sentiment, sound, or presentation and pretend I have no need of such flippant language. But my writing would die. My words would stand lifeless, as I too, would lay lifeless should I attempt to exist while denying myself that which gives life. Words prescribe reality, and poetry gives life to the cold harshness of that reality.\r\n\r\n&lt;img class=\"alignleft size-medium wp-image-12747\" src=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/wp-content\/uploads\/2015\/03\/DSCN0274-297x410.jpg\" alt=\"Kaytee Green, poetry, Grammarly\" width=\"297\" height=\"410\" \/&gt;Poetry is that part of us tied to something beyond, and that something beyond gives life from beyond. Man is a creation molded after the image of a God. As such, man longs for something beyond and that something sometimes spills forth into a beautiful thing, phrase, or expression. And when we hear the phrase prompted by the beyond, it touches that part of us longing for more. This is Poetry.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;About the Author&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\n\r\nKaytee Green, daughter of missionaries, grew up overseas in Eastern Europe before moving to the United States for college. She intends to attend Moody Bible Institute in fall of 2015 and study communications. Her favourite pastimes include traveling, reading, Tae Kwon Do, and piano.","post_title":"Poetry Is","post_excerpt":"","post_status":"publish","comment_status":"open","ping_status":"open","post_password":"","post_name":"poetry-is","to_ping":"","pinged":"","post_modified":"2015-03-24 10:23:09","post_modified_gmt":"2015-03-24 17:23:09","post_content_filtered":"","post_parent":0,"guid":"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/?p=12733","menu_order":0,"post_type":"post","post_mime_type":"","comment_count":"1","filter":"raw"},{"ID":12889,"post_author":"1001017","post_date":"2015-04-03 10:57:38","post_date_gmt":"2015-04-03 17:57:38","post_content":"[media]&lt;img src=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/wp-content\/uploads\/2015\/03\/53rfewdsx-670x352.jpg\" alt=\"bugaboo and bugbear\" width=\"670\" height=\"352\" class=\"aligncenter size-large wp-image-13087\" \/&gt;[\/media]\r\n\r\nIf you\u2019ve ever played Dungeons &amp; Dragons or listened to Destiny\u2019s Child, chances are likely that you have heard the words bugbear and bugaboo. For the D&amp;D players of the world, a bugbear is a hairy, giant-like goblin. For Destiny\u2019s Child fans, a bugaboo is a particularly annoying boy who just won\u2019t stop calling you (or paging you, or showing up to your house unannounced). These definitions aren\u2019t exactly what the words were used for back when they first came into existence during\u00a0the Middle Ages. In fact, both bugbear and bugaboo have interesting backstories that involve our childhood nemesis, the bogeyman.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Bugbear&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\n&lt;ol&gt;\r\n\t&lt;li&gt;An ongoing problem; a recurring obstacle or adversity.&lt;\/li&gt;\r\n\t&lt;li&gt;A source of dread; resentment; or irritation&lt;\/li&gt;\r\n\t&lt;li&gt;An imaginary creature meant to inspire fear in children.&lt;\/li&gt;\r\n&lt;\/ol&gt;\r\nThough linguists can\u2019t be certain on exactly where the word bogey originated, they all agree that it originally existed as a proper name for the Devil. Scary, right? It\u2019s no wonder, then, that parents began telling their children stories of the bogeyman coming to get them when they didn\u2019t do their chores or go to bed on time or whatever it is that children got reprimanded for in the Middle Ages. At some point, the rhetoric around the bogeyman changed, and instead of becoming a creature that would take children who didn\u2019t go to bed on\u00a0time, it became the bugbear who would get you if you tried to sneak out at night. As the word might suggest, a bugbear was a bear-like imaginary creature also meant to incite fear in children (and teens). Today, a bugbear isn\u2019t so much a creepy creature or giant-like goblin but a word that represents an ongoing problem; a recurring obstacle or adversity. Some might consider a bugbear to be getting politicians to agree on legislation for public school funding, for example. Alternatively, a bugbear can represent a source of dread, resentment, or irritation. Many Americans, for example, consider doing their taxes a real bugbear.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Bugaboo&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\n&lt;ol&gt;\r\n\t&lt;li&gt;An imaginary object of fear&lt;\/li&gt;\r\n\t&lt;li&gt;Something that causes fear or distress out of proportion to its importance&lt;\/li&gt;\r\n&lt;\/ol&gt;\r\nWhile bugbear is a popular term in Europe, in North America the more popular word is\u00a0bugaboo and usually refers\u00a0to\u00a0a dreadful imaginary object than a creature. In this instance, it turns out that Destiny\u2019s Child &lt;a title=\"Destiny's Child &quot;BugABoo&quot;\" href=\"https:\/\/youtu.be\/9i-y0G8nNA8\" target=\"_blank\"&gt;got it wrong&lt;\/a&gt;. When you think about it, that guy who just won\u2019t stop calling you is more of a bugbear than a bugaboo\u2014the guy the girls are singing about is definitely real. If we took Destiny\u2019s Child\u2019s use of bugaboo in the literal sense, it seems like Beyonc\u00e9 et al. are totally overreacting to the bogeyman instead of protecting their very important personal spaces. Also, once you learn the definition of bugaboo, it makes it hard to look at that &lt;a title=\"Bugaboo Baby Accessories\" href=\"https:\/\/www.bugaboo.com\" target=\"_blank\"&gt;cute line of baby accessories&lt;\/a&gt; that also bears the name quite the same way. Is strolling your baby suddenly an activity that causes fear or distress out of proportion to its importance (the importance being getting your baby from point A to point B)? Is your baby just an imaginary object of fear? I\u2019d like to think not.\r\n\r\nSo, while the two words are similarly defined (and often appear as synonyms of each other), be careful to make sure you\u2019re using the word with the right definition. Even Beyonc\u00e9 can lead us astray sometimes.","post_title":"Beyonc\u00e9 Was Wrong About This Word","post_excerpt":"","post_status":"publish","comment_status":"open","ping_status":"open","post_password":"","post_name":"beyonce-was-wrong-about-this-word","to_ping":"","pinged":"","post_modified":"2015-04-03 10:57:38","post_modified_gmt":"2015-04-03 17:57:38","post_content_filtered":"","post_parent":0,"guid":"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/?p=12889","menu_order":0,"post_type":"post","post_mime_type":"","comment_count":"0","filter":"raw"},{"ID":12799,"post_author":"6","post_date":"2015-04-02 05:00:12","post_date_gmt":"2015-04-02 12:00:12","post_content":"[media]&lt;img class=\"aligncenter size-full wp-image-12801\" src=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/wp-content\/uploads\/2015\/03\/Nevermind.jpg\" alt=\"The Passive-Aggressive Raven\" width=\"700\" height=\"315\" \/&gt;[\/media]\r\n\r\nEdgar Allan Poe is an writer associated with American Romanticism. He is known for his exploration of dark themes in stories as well as poetry. While many people in the U.S. are vaguely aware of Poe (his writing is often taught in English curriculum), many people might not remember or be familiar with his writing as adults. In honor of #PoetryMonth, we have put together a short list of the most famous or culturally significant poems by Poe.\r\n\r\n&lt;b&gt;1. &lt;a href=\"http:\/\/poestories.com\/read\/raven\"&gt;\"The Raven\"&lt;\/a&gt; (1845)&lt;\/b&gt;\r\n\r\n&lt;i&gt;\"Once upon a midnight dreary, while I pondered, weak and weary . . .\"&lt;\/i&gt;\r\n\r\n\"The Raven\" may be Poe's best known poem. This one tops our list because the enormous number of popular cultural references to the poem (see the image above) make it highly relevant to various groups of people.\r\n\r\n&lt;b&gt;2. &lt;a href=\"http:\/\/poestories.com\/read\/annabellee\"&gt;\"Annabel Lee\"&lt;\/a&gt; (1849)&lt;\/b&gt;\r\n\r\n&lt;i&gt;\"Can ever dissever my soul from the soul\/Of the beautiful Annabel Lee . . .\"&lt;\/i&gt;\r\n\r\nA true rival in popularity and cultural ubiquity to \"The Raven,\" \"Annabel Lee\" gloomily explores the depressive, melancholy of lost love. The sorrow of the poem is only enhanced by the clear devotion of the author to his beloved even after death.\r\n\r\n&lt;b&gt;3. &lt;a href=\"http:\/\/poestories.com\/read\/alone\"&gt;\"Alone\"&lt;\/a&gt; (1875)&lt;\/b&gt;\r\n\r\n&lt;i&gt;\"From childhood's hour I have not been\/As others were . . .\"&lt;\/i&gt;\r\n\r\nThis poem is a manifesto for every person who has felt like a black sheep. Poe beautifully describes the feeling of otherness that comes with not fitting the cultural mold. Reading these words, anyone who has ever felt different can take small comfort that their experience has been and remains shared.\r\n\r\n&nbsp;\r\n\r\n&lt;hr \/&gt;\r\n\r\n&lt;b&gt;What's your favorite piece of \"Poe-try\"?&lt;\/b&gt;\r\n\r\nEvery weekday in April, we will be sharing a poem, an excerpt of poetry, or a feature on a poet. Our celebration will feature poetry from every era, and we ask our friends to join us throughout the month by sharing their favorite poetry under the tag #PoetryMonth.","post_title":"3 Must-Read Pieces of \"Poe-try\"","post_excerpt":"","post_status":"publish","comment_status":"open","ping_status":"open","post_password":"","post_name":"3-must-read-pieces-of-poe-try","to_ping":"","pinged":"","post_modified":"2015-03-25 07:26:56","post_modified_gmt":"2015-03-25 14:26:56","post_content_filtered":"","post_parent":0,"guid":"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/?p=12799","menu_order":0,"post_type":"post","post_mime_type":"","comment_count":"0","filter":"raw"},{"ID":12785,"post_author":"6","post_date":"2015-04-02 03:57:21","post_date_gmt":"2015-04-02 10:57:21","post_content":"&lt;b&gt;It's National Poetry Month. Share this poem with your friends!&lt;\/b&gt;\r\n[media]&lt;img src=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/wp-content\/uploads\/2015\/04\/WS-Sword.jpg\" alt=\"William Shakespeare, excerpt from Sonnet LV\" width=\"600\" height=\"315\" class=\"aligncenter size-full wp-image-12977\" \/&gt;[\/media]\r\n\r\n&lt;hr \/&gt;\r\n\r\n&lt;b&gt;\"Sonnet LV\"&lt;\/b&gt;\r\nWilliam Shakespeare\r\n\r\nNot marble, nor the gilded monuments\r\nOf princes, shall outlive this powerful rime;\r\nBut you shall shine more bright in these contents\r\nThan unswept stone, besmear'd with sluttish time.\r\nWhen wasteful war shall statues overturn,\r\nAnd broils root out the work of masonry,\r\nNor Mars his sword nor war's quick fire shall burn\r\nThe living record of your memory.\r\n'Gainst death and all-oblivious enmity\r\nShall you pace forth; your praise shall still find room\r\nEven in the eyes of all posterity\r\nThat wear this world out to the ending doom.\r\nSo, till the judgment that yourself arise,\r\nYou live in this, and dwell in lovers' eyes.\r\n\r\n&nbsp;\r\n\r\n&lt;hr \/&gt;\r\n\r\nDid you know that National Poetry Month was established in 1996 by the Academy of American Poets?\r\n\r\nEvery weekday in April, we will be sharing a poem, an excerpt of poetry, or a feature on a poet. Our celebration will feature poetry from every era, and we ask our friends to join us throughout the month by sharing their favorite poetry under the tag #PoetryMonth.\r\n\r\nOther daily poetry: \r\n&lt;a href=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/2015\/a-daily-dose-of-poetry-day-1\/?sf&amp;utm_campaign=PoetryMonth15&amp;utm_source=Blog_org&amp;utm_medium=text_link\"&gt;Day One&lt;\/a&gt;","post_title":"A Daily Dose of Poetry: Day 2","post_excerpt":"","post_status":"publish","comment_status":"open","ping_status":"open","post_password":"","post_name":"a-daily-dose-of-poetry-day-2","to_ping":"","pinged":"","post_modified":"2015-04-02 05:30:19","post_modified_gmt":"2015-04-02 12:30:19","post_content_filtered":"","post_parent":0,"guid":"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/?p=12785","menu_order":0,"post_type":"post","post_mime_type":"","comment_count":"0","filter":"raw"},{"ID":12981,"post_author":"6","post_date":"2015-04-01 07:36:33","post_date_gmt":"2015-04-01 14:36:33","post_content":"[media]&lt;img src=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/wp-content\/uploads\/2015\/04\/double-negatives.jpg\" alt=\"Apparently double negatives are okay in math but not in English. Cartoon. \" width=\"600\" height=\"315\" class=\"aligncenter size-full wp-image-12983\" \/&gt;[\/media]\r\n\r\nYou probably have been told more than once that &lt;b&gt;double negatives&lt;\/b&gt; are wrong and that you shouldn't use them. However, usually, it's left at that \u2014 without any explanation of what exactly a double negative is or why it's considered incorrect (in standard English). We want to fix that. Here is the essential list of things you must understand about double negatives. \r\n\r\n&lt;b&gt;1. In standard English, each subject-predicate construction should only have one negative form.&lt;\/b&gt;\r\nNegative forms in English are created by adding a negation to the verb.\r\n\r\n&lt;i&gt;I will bake a cake. --&gt; I will not bake a cake.&lt;\/i&gt; \r\n&lt;i&gt;I can go anywhere tonight. --&gt; I cannot go anywhere tonight.&lt;\/i&gt;\r\n&lt;i&gt;We are planning a trip. --&gt; We are not planning a trip.&lt;\/i&gt;\r\n\r\nSometimes there are negative forms of nouns \u2014 such as \"nowhere,\" \"nothing,\" and \"no one\" \u2014 that are used. If these are in a sentence, it is important that the verb in the sentence is not negated. \r\n\r\n&lt;i&gt;He's going nowhere.&lt;\/i&gt; &lt;b&gt;not&lt;\/b&gt; &lt;i&gt;He's not going nowhere.&lt;\/i&gt;\r\n\r\n&lt;b&gt;2. A double negative is a &lt;i&gt;non-standard&lt;\/i&gt; sentence construction that uses two negative forms. &lt;\/b&gt;\r\nDouble negatives are created by adding a negation to the verb &lt;b&gt;and&lt;\/b&gt; to the modifier of the noun (adjectives, adverbs, etc.) &lt;b&gt;or&lt;\/b&gt; to the object of the verb. \r\n\r\n&lt;i&gt;I will bake a cake. --&gt; I won't (will not) bake no cake.&lt;\/i&gt; (verb negation + object negation)\r\n&lt;i&gt;I can go anywhere tonight. --&gt; I can't (cannot) go nowhere tonight. &lt;\/i&gt; (verb negation + modifier negation)\r\n\r\n&lt;b&gt;3. Learning standard English negation is difficult because many languages and some English dialects use double negatives conventionally.&lt;\/b&gt;\r\nThough it's easy to assume that double negatives are simply unnatural aberrations, this assumption is wrong. In many languages worldwide, it is grammatically incorrect to use anything but the double negative! (This is called negative concord.) \r\n\r\n&lt;i&gt;No hay ningun problema.&lt;\/i&gt;(Spanish)\r\n\"There isn't no problem.\" meaning \"There isn't a problem.\"\r\n\r\n&lt;i&gt;\u042f \u043d\u0435 \u0445\u043e\u0447\u0443 \u043d\u0456\u0447\u043e\u0433\u043e \u0457\u0441\u0442\u0438. (Ya ne hochu nichogo yisty.)&lt;\/i&gt; (Ukrainian)\r\n\"I don't want nothing to eat.\" meaning \"I don't want to eat anything.\"\r\n\r\nTo make it more complicated, it's not just foreign languages that conventionally employ double negatives but some dialects of English as well! African American Vernacular English (AAVE), Southern American English, and even some British regional forms use negative concord constructions. Negative concord is even used several times in Chaucer's &lt;i&gt;Canterbury Tales&lt;\/i&gt; (e.g. regarding the Friar: \"Ther nas no man no wher so vertuous.\") \r\n\r\nSo, while double negatives are not correct in standard English, that doesn't make them any less useful in other dialects. We encourage writers to learn how to negate sentences using the standard grammar \u2014 especially for professional settings \u2014 but we love the diversity of English (and language in general) and think that use of dialectal grammar is fine in open, less formal environments. \r\n\r\n\r\n&lt;b&gt;How do you remember not to use double negatives? Do you think double negatives should be considered incorrect?&lt;\/b&gt;\r\n","post_title":"3 Things You Must Know About Double Negatives","post_excerpt":"","post_status":"publish","comment_status":"open","ping_status":"open","post_password":"","post_name":"3-things-you-must-know-about-double-negatives","to_ping":"","pinged":"","post_modified":"2015-04-01 07:36:33","post_modified_gmt":"2015-04-01 14:36:33","post_content_filtered":"","post_parent":0,"guid":"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/?p=12981","menu_order":0,"post_type":"post","post_mime_type":"","comment_count":"0","filter":"raw"},{"ID":12771,"post_author":"6","post_date":"2015-04-01 03:00:09","post_date_gmt":"2015-04-01 10:00:09","post_content":"We're ecstatic. Our celebration will feature poetry from every era, and we ask our friends to join us throughout the month by sharing their favorite poetry under the tag #PoetryMonth.\r\n\r\n&lt;b&gt;Share this poem with your friends!&lt;\/b&gt;\r\n\r\n[media]&lt;img class=\"aligncenter size-full wp-image-12971\" src=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/wp-content\/uploads\/2015\/04\/White-blossom.jpg\" alt=\"White blossom poem by D.H. Day","post_excerpt":"","post_status":"publish","comment_status":"open","ping_status":"open","post_password":"","post_name":"5-puns-that-will-make-you-laugh-out-loud-on-april-fools-day","to_ping":"","pinged":"","post_modified":"2015-03-31 02:42:26","post_date_gmt":"2015-03-30 09:42:26","post_content":"[media]&lt;img class=\"aligncenter size-full wp-image-12897\" src=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/wp-content\/uploads\/2015\/03\/34RTRFDSX.jpg\" alt=\"If you could stop using business-speak all the time, that would be great.\" width=\"600\" height=\"315\" \/&gt;[\/media]\r\n\r\nEvery office has its own sort of language\u2014vocabulary that people frequently use\u00a0when they\u2019re at work but probably wouldn\u2019t use that often otherwise. Most of us are used to office jargon, but we\u2019d like to take a minute to review some of the business-y words that do the job while kind of driving us crazy at the same time. Jargon varies from office to office, but here are five\u00a0of the words and phrases that make us cringe.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Goal-oriented:&lt;\/strong&gt; At work, it\u2019s important to be goal-oriented. After all, if you aren\u2019t working toward a goal, how the heck are you spending your time? But there is one issue with this term: it has become meaningless in its overuse. After seeing this term on multiple resum\u00e9s and in countless slideshows, we think it\u2019s time to find a new word.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Optimize:&lt;\/strong&gt; To optimize means to make the best or most effective use of\u2014to improve efficiency. The way some people speak at work, it seems like nearly everything can be optimized, from workflows to team structures to even refrigerator organization. If you can think of a relationship or process in your office, you can probably optimize it. Optimization is great\u2014who doesn\u2019t love efficiency?!\u2014but overuse of the term can become annoying. Optimize your vocabulary by cutting back on your use of this term.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Synergy:&lt;\/strong&gt; The definition of synergy is \u201cthe interaction or cooperation of two or more organizations, substances, or other agents to produce a combined effect greater than the sum of their separate effects.\u201d But when people use the term synergy in the office, it seems to imply something mystical, almost magical, about working together. We would prefer to keep things simple and more concrete by\u00a0using terms like \u201ccooperation\u201d or \u201ccollaboration\u201d to describe the process of working together.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Impact (used as a verb):&lt;\/strong&gt;\u00a0While it\u2019s now acceptable to use impact as a verb (meaning to affect or influence something), this word is so overused and misused that we vote for stopping its usage completely. People want to \u201cimpact sales,\u201d \u201cimpact the community,\u201d and even \u201cimpact the future.\u201d Make an impact in your office by using &lt;a href=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/words\/impact\"&gt;other synonyms&lt;\/a&gt; instead of impact.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Disrupt: &lt;\/strong&gt;This word has gained popularity in the startup and technology world, but its use has started to become a bit\u2014eh hem\u2014disruptive. Most people use the term to mean something akin to \u201cupsetting the old balance of power\u201d or \u201cbucking expectations and completely changing the way things are done.\u201d There\u2019s even a conference, Techcrunch Disrupt, which\u00a0underscores the use and importance of the word. Do you know what would be truly disruptive? Finding a new term to describe this action.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Which work-related words or jargon drives you crazy? What are your preferred replacements? Share your pet peeves in the comments!&lt;\/strong&gt;","post_title":"Work Jargon We Wish Would Disappear","post_excerpt":"","post_status":"publish","comment_status":"open","ping_status":"open","post_password":"","post_name":"work-jargon-we-wish-would-disappear","to_ping":"","pinged":"","post_modified":"2015-03-30 02:58:00","post_modified_gmt":"2015-03-30 09:58:00","post_content_filtered":"","post_parent":0,"guid":"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/?p=12863","menu_order":0,"post_type":"post","post_mime_type":"","comment_count":"0","filter":"raw"},{"ID":12431,"post_author":"4","post_date":"2015-03-27 02:00:13","post_date_gmt":"2015-03-27 09:00:13","post_content":"When you're speaking out loud, homophones sound alike, but when you're writing them out, it's a different story. Though they have the same pronunciation, homophones have &lt;a href=\"http:\/\/www.quickanddirtytips.com\/education\/grammar\/funny-homophones\"&gt;slightly different spellings&lt;\/a&gt; and totally different definitions. Since using the wrong one can completely change the meaning of your statement, it's important to make sure you have the right word in mind. Here are seven homophone mistakes to avoid.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Than vs. Then&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\n\r\n&lt;a href=\"https:\/\/www.flickr.com\/photos\/cherieb\/201816498\/sizes\/m\/\"&gt;&lt;img class=\"aligncenter size-full wp-image-12433\" src=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/wp-content\/uploads\/2015\/03\/Untitled6.png\" alt=\"store, homophone, Grammarly\" width=\"500\" height=\"371\" \/&gt;&lt;\/a&gt;\r\n\r\nDespite their single letter of difference, misusing \"than\" and \"then\" can change a statement completely. \"Than\" is the word to use in a comparative statement, like \"You're a better writer today than you were yesterday.\" Use \"then\" when describing a sequence of events, like \"Write a good sentence, and then compose a great paragraph.\"\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;To vs. Too&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\n\r\nWhile \"to\" and \"too\" sound the same, remember that the former is a preposition, while the latter is an adverb. \"To\" typically means \"toward,\" while \"too\" can either mean \"excessively\" or \"also.\" For example, you could say, \"When you go to the beach, take me too.\"\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Your vs. You're&lt;\/strong&gt;&lt;strong&gt;\u00a0&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\n\r\nThough it's a common mistake, using the wrong version of this word can &lt;a href=\"http:\/\/www.forbes.com\/sites\/robasghar\/2013\/08\/06\/which-grammar-mistakes-will-keep-you-from-getting-a-good-job\/\"&gt;make your writing look sloppy&lt;\/a&gt;. \"Your\" is always a possessive pronoun, so when you use this word, be sure you're declaring ownership. For instance, you could say, \"your book\" or \"your professional writing sample.\" \"You're,\" on the other hand, is a contraction of the words \"you\" and \"are.\" When using this word, check yourself by reading it with the words \"you are\" in place of the contraction to make sure it makes sense.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Threw vs. Through&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\n\r\n\"Threw\" and \"through\" may be different parts of speech, but they can be &lt;a href=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/2014\/5-homophones-people-just-cant-seem-to-keep-straight\/\"&gt;tough to keep straight&lt;\/a&gt;. The first work in this homophone pair is the past tense of the verb \"throw,\" or the act of tossing something in the air. \"Through,\" however, is an adverb that implies movement through space or time. You could say, for instance, \"The pitcher threw the baseball through the air like a pro.\"\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Stationary vs. Stationery&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\n\r\nMany writers confuse these homophones, since the only spelling difference is a single vowel towards the end of the word. \"Stationary\" and \"stationery,\" however, have very different meanings. \"Stationary\" refers to standing still, while \"stationery\" refers to cards, paper, or other writing materials. Your stationery may be stationary, but never the other way around.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Seas vs. Sees vs. Seize&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\n\r\nAs if two homophones weren't enough, you will find &lt;a href=\"http:\/\/howtowritebetter.net\/are-you-haunted-by-homophones\/\"&gt;three spellings of this sound-alike&lt;\/a&gt;. \"Seas\" is a noun that describes oceans and other large bodies of water, while the verb \"sees\" refers to looking or observing. Finally, \"seize\" means to take control. \"She sees the opposing forces seize the seas\" demonstrates the unique meaning of all three.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;There vs. Their vs. They're&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\n\r\nYou'll also find three of these &lt;a href=\"http:\/\/www.copyblogger.com\/5-common-mistakes-that-make-you-look-dumb\/\"&gt;tricky homophones&lt;\/a&gt;. \"There\" refers to a general location or distance. When describing where you parked your car, for instance, you might say, \"My car is in the parking lot over there.\" \"Their\" serves as a possessive form for more than one person. When referring to the vehicles belonging to a group of people, for example, you would say, \"their cars.\"\r\n\r\nFinally, \"they're\" is a contraction of the words \"they\" and \"are.\" Whenever you use this term, make sure that what you really mean is \"they are.\" For instance, you could say, \"They're parking.\" If you're really ready to show off your knowledge of homophones, try out the statement, \"They're parking their car in that lot over there.\"\r\n\r\nHomophones may sound the same, but their meanings are very different when you use them in writing. Study up on which words you need to use, and when, as many spelling and grammar checkers cannot correct for words that are spelled correctly but used wrong. (Lucky for you &lt;a href=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\"&gt;Grammarly&lt;\/a&gt; can!)\r\n\r\nWhat's your biggest homophone mistake?","post_title":"7 Homophone Mistakes to Avoid","post_excerpt":"","post_status":"publish","comment_status":"open","ping_status":"open","post_password":"","post_name":"7-homophone-mistakes-to-avoid","to_ping":"","pinged":"","post_modified":"2015-03-12 12:58:41","post_modified_gmt":"2015-03-12 19:58:41","post_content_filtered":"","post_parent":0,"guid":"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/?p=12431","menu_order":0,"post_type":"post","post_mime_type":"","comment_count":"1","filter":"raw"},{"ID":12729,"post_author":"4","post_date":"2015-03-27 02:00:02","post_date_gmt":"2015-03-27 09:00:02","post_content":"On January 12, Grammarly launched its &lt;a href=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/2015\/3rd-annual-student-scholarship\/\"&gt;third\u00a0annual scholarship essay competition&lt;\/a&gt;, encouraging students to share their thoughts on one of two writing prompts:\r\n&lt;ul&gt;\r\n\t&lt;li&gt;What is poetry, and how does it influence your writing?&lt;\/li&gt;\r\n\t&lt;li&gt;What is the funniest book you have ever read? The Grammarly team enjoyed reading each essay, and we encourage each of you to keep on writing.","post_title":"Grammarly Announces Winner of 3rd Annual $1,000 Scholarship","post_excerpt":"","post_status":"publish","comment_status":"open","ping_status":"open","post_password":"","post_name":"grammarly-announces-winner-of-3rd-annual-scholarship","to_ping":"","pinged":"","post_modified":"2015-03-25 11:27:49","post_modified_gmt":"2015-03-25 18:27:49","post_content_filtered":"","post_parent":0,"guid":"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/?p=12729","menu_order":0,"post_type":"post","post_mime_type":"","comment_count":"0","filter":"raw"},{"ID":11069,"post_author":"2","post_date":"2015-03-25 08:00:32","post_date_gmt":"2015-03-25 15:00:32","post_content":"&lt;i&gt;Guest post by Meryl K. Evans&lt;\/i&gt;\r\n\r\nThe valet pulled up in my car. I thanked him, tipped him and entered my car. I noticed both turn signals were blinking. What\u2019s up? It took me a minute to realize the valet had turned on the hazard lights. I didn\u2019t even remember if I had ever used them in this car.\r\n\r\nI touched every switch, button and stick searching for the toggle. Sure, I could dig for the instruction manual in the glove compartment, but I didn\u2019t want to hold up the folks behind me. So I asked a nearby valet for help. Click. She pushed the button with the hazard icon above the touchscreen display. Color me embarrassed.\r\n\r\nDespite this, I told my husband what happened. He said many people don\u2019t know where to find the hazard light switch because there was no standard location for it. Well, I won\u2019t forget next time.\r\n\r\nIf I read anything I wrote from five years ago, I flinch. I\u2019m a better writer than I was five years ago. And I hope I\u2019m better five years from now. Doing lots of reading and writing helped me grow as a writer.\r\n\r\nUnlike with the hazard lights, I\u2019ll pick up an instruction manual for writing to learn a few tricks. This would be any book on writing. The first memorable one I read was William Zinsser\u2019s \u201cOn Writing Well.\u201d Within 15 pages, I learned these four tips, which boosted my writing.\r\n\r\nWhy limit to four tips? Many articles provide a long list of tips. It\u2019s so overwhelming that you don\u2019t bother trying any. Keep it simple. Four is doable. And you can use them right now. Learn a handful of writing tricks at a time. Know them and nurture them. They\u2019ll become a habit.\r\n\r\n&lt;b&gt; Trade Five Dollar Words for Cheap Ones&lt;\/b&gt;\r\n\r\nThe first advice is to simplify. Zinsser covers it in one sentence: \u201cEvery word that serves no function, every long word that could be a short word, every adverb that carries the same meaning that\u2019s already in the verb, every passive construction that leaves the reader unsure of who is doing what \u2014 these are the thousand and one adulterants that weaken the strength of a sentence.\u201d\r\n\r\n[media]&lt;img src=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/wp-content\/uploads\/2015\/03\/every-word-that-WZ.jpg\" alt=\"William Zinsser quotation\" width=\"600\" height=\"315\" class=\"aligncenter size-full wp-image-12633\" \/&gt;[\/media]\r\n\r\nOne sentence taught me to swap fancy words for simple ones, cut words ending in -ly and don\u2019t be passive aggressive. Why opt for the snooty \u201cutilize\u201d when you can use the effortless \u201cuse?\u201d And who needs the mind-numbing \u201cnumerous\u201d when the four-lettered \u201cmany\u201d works? Here, the Thesaurus is your friend.\r\n\r\n&lt;b&gt; Cut Very Unnecessary Words&lt;\/b&gt;\r\n\r\nWhy not get to the point by cutting the underlined words? I blame the search engine optimization. People say online content needs to be more than 1,000 words for SEO\u2019s sake. They believe long content earns more love from search engines.\r\n\r\nAnxious to reach the word count goal, writers add a bunch of words especially qualifiers and adverbs, throw in statistics and amend sentences to plug in key phrases until it fits.\r\n\r\nDo we need \u201cvery\u201d to underscore how much we need to drop needless words? We\u2019ve used \u201cvery,\u201d \u201cso\u201d and \u201creally\u201d so often that these words have lost power.\r\n\r\n&lt;b&gt; Split Long Sentences to Create Two Shorter Ones for a Crisper Read&lt;\/b&gt;\r\n\r\nGranted, the quote from Zinsser runs long. But it packs a punch. If he had replaced commas with periods, how would it affect the sentence?\r\n\r\nI\u2019ve edited articles where an entire paragraph contained one sentence. For these, I convert the long sentence into two or three sentences. This breaks multiple thoughts into a single thought for each. It improves readability and clarity.\r\n\r\n&lt;b&gt; Omit Redundant Words&lt;\/b&gt;\r\n\r\nTake a look at this list and see how you can make them better.\r\n&lt;ul&gt;\r\n\t&lt;li&gt;Add a new.&lt;\/li&gt;\r\n\t&lt;li&gt;In order to.&lt;\/li&gt;\r\n\t&lt;li&gt;Overused clich\u00e9.&lt;\/li&gt;\r\n\t&lt;li&gt;Past history.&lt;\/li&gt;\r\n\t&lt;li&gt;Period of time.&lt;\/li&gt;\r\n\t&lt;li&gt;Plan ahead.&lt;\/li&gt;\r\n\t&lt;li&gt;Straight to the point.&lt;\/li&gt;\r\n\t&lt;li&gt;Tall skyscraper.&lt;\/li&gt;\r\n\t&lt;li&gt;Thanks in advance.&lt;\/li&gt;\r\n&lt;\/ul&gt;\r\nI applied these tips to my writing as quickly as I learned where to find the hazard lights button. Next time I crack the car\u2019s user manual, I\u2019ll glean two or three tips. Those will join these three plus the many others I\u2019ve discovered since I started driving my car. In a year or two, the new knowledge will help me improve this article.\r\n\r\nKeep reading and keep writing. The bettering will follow. Oh, and be sure to find your hazard lights now before you need to know how.\r\n\r\n&lt;i&gt;Share your writing tip in the comments.&lt;\/i&gt;\r\n\r\n&lt;b&gt;About the Author&lt;\/b&gt;\r\n\r\n&lt;a href=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/wp-content\/uploads\/2015\/03\/2014-meryl-headshot-square.jpg\"&gt;&lt;img class=\"alignleft wp-image-11077\" src=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/wp-content\/uploads\/2015\/03\/2014-meryl-headshot-square-410x410.jpg\" alt=\"Meryl Evans, writer, content, Grammarly\" width=\"329\" height=\"329\" \/&gt;&lt;\/a&gt;\r\n\r\nMeryl K.
