According to the &lt;a href=\"http:\/\/www.etymonline.com\/index.php?term=too\"&gt;Online Etymology Dictionary&lt;\/a&gt;, &lt;i&gt;too&lt;\/i&gt; comes from the Old English stressed form of the well-known preposition &lt;i&gt;to&lt;\/i&gt; (in the direction of; furthermore). This stressed form eventually came to strictly emphasize addition or agreement, and a distinctive spelling is dated to the 16th century. Remarkably, however, the use of &lt;i&gt;too&lt;\/i&gt; after the verb \u2014 as in \"I went, too!\" \u2014 is recorded only in the early 20th century. \r\n\r\nGiven the etymology of the word, it's not surprising that people struggle with correct use of &lt;i&gt;too&lt;\/i&gt;. Our bet is that people have been struggling with it for at least the last 400 years. In the minutes before the day ends, one experiences such an intense anticipation that it's a momentary sort of cabin fever. What's even more adorable is when children write something they didn't intend to write at all. Whether the culprit is bad handwriting or simply\u00a0not sounding out a word correctly, ensure that you are encouraging your child to write by being supportive of all attempts.\r\n\r\n[media]&lt;img class=\"aligncenter size-full wp-image-12601\" src=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/wp-content\/uploads\/2015\/03\/ewrtgfdcxz.jpg\" alt=\"Kid hores essay. \" width=\"600\" height=\"315\" \/&gt;[\/media]\r\n\r\nIn other words, choke back your laughter if\u00a0you see mistakes like the following five.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Transposing Letters&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\n\r\nConsidering the number\u00a0of letters and pictures that children make, especially for Santa around Christmas time, it shouldn't come as a surprise that Santa is going to be called Satan a few thousand times by children demanding presents. While it's definitely funny, it can also be\u00a0a disturbing sight if you aren't prepared for your child accidentally professing their love for the Devil.\r\n\r\nA couple of slip-ups can be funny, but if transposing words becomes common, make sure you check to see if your child has\u00a0&lt;a href=\"http:\/\/php1.grammarly.com\/blog\/2014\/5-reasons-the-writing-world-%E2%80%A8should-celebrate-dyslexia\/\"&gt;dyslexia&lt;\/a&gt;.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Sounding It\u00a0Out, Literally\u00a0&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\n\r\n&lt;a href=\"https:\/\/www.flickr.com\/photos\/moonlightbulb\/5294380424\/sizes\/m\/\"&gt;&lt;img class=\"aligncenter size-full wp-image-11587\" src=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/wp-content\/uploads\/2015\/02\/Untitled31.png\" alt=\"Image via Flickr by moonlightbulb\" width=\"500\" height=\"375\" \/&gt;&lt;\/a&gt;\r\n\r\nSome children\u00a0spell words in exactly the way that they sound. This can lead to some embarrassing, and\u00a0possibly hilarious, moments for you and your family since\u00a0telling a child to \"sound it out\" doesn't always work as effectively when it comes to spelling. Expecting a five-year-old to account for long and short vowels and proper phonetics in a letter or doodle is overly ambitious. Don't be surprised when you see the word \"peace\" spelled as \"pies.\" Though your child is more likely to be wishing for \"World Pies\" than \"World Peace\" anyway.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Malapropisms Run Rampant with Children\u00a0&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\n\r\nChildren have a pretty limited vocabulary, so they simply aren't aware of the existence of many of the words that adults use. When children attempt to repeat the words they hear, this can often &lt;a href=\"http:\/\/literarydevices.net\/malapropism\/\"&gt;result in a malapropism&lt;\/a&gt;\u00a0because\u00a0the child is substituting an unknown word or sound for one that they &lt;a href=\"http:\/\/php1.grammarly.com\/blog\/2015\/8-embarrassing-yet-common-malapropisms\/\"&gt;already know&lt;\/a&gt;. For example, it's a strong possibility that your child believes that Abraham Lincoln's name is actually April Ham Lincoln because that makes a lot more sense to him.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Oversimplifying Words Can Often Lead to Trouble\u00a0&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\n\r\nTo be fair, the English language has many confusing and just plain silly rules in it\u00a0--\u00a0especially when it comes to spelling.\u00a0&lt;a href=\"http:\/\/www.boredpanda.com\/funny-childrens-spelling-mistakes\/\"&gt;Children will spell things&lt;\/a&gt;\u00a0in the most simple way by sounding it out, but this tactic can often lead to some inappropriate, albeit hilarious, misspellings. Try not to get offended when your little boy hands you a drawing of his favorite place with the caption \"I love the biach.\" Though he just loves the beach, it might seem like he's down with the latest slang words.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong style=\"line-height: 1.5;\"&gt;Sometimes, It's All About Handwriting&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\n\r\nUnfortunately, it's not just spelling mistakes on a child's drawing or letter that lead to hilarious misunderstandings. Which is why you should never underestimate the importance of good\u00a0&lt;a href=\"http:\/\/www.nytimes.com\/2014\/06\/03\/science\/whats-lost-as-handwriting-fades.html?_r=0\"&gt;handwriting&lt;\/a&gt;.\u00a0Kids aren't exactly known for the most decipherable writing, which, for example, can result in an \"l\" being mistaken for an \"r\" in the word \"whole,\" within the sentence \"I love my whole family.\" Yikes.\r\n\r\nAs you can see, children may not be the world's best spellers, at least their methods are much more entertaining than correct grammar and spelling. Have you ever seen a grammar or spelling mistake from a child that made you roar with laughter?","post_title":"5 Funniest Grammar\/Spelling Mistakes in Kids' Letters","post_excerpt":"","post_status":"publish","comment_status":"open","ping_status":"open","post_password":"","post_name":"5-funniest-grammarspelling-mistakes-in-kids-letters","to_ping":"","pinged":"","post_modified":"2015-03-31 04:01:39","post_modified_gmt":"2015-03-31 11:01:39","post_content_filtered":"","post_parent":0,"guid":"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/?p=11585","menu_order":0,"post_type":"post","post_mime_type":"","comment_count":"0","filter":"raw"},{"ID":12607,"post_author":"6","post_date":"2015-03-20 07:20:23","post_date_gmt":"2015-03-20 14:20:23","post_content":"[media]&lt;img class=\"aligncenter size-full wp-image-12647\" src=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/wp-content\/uploads\/2015\/03\/Whomever_Whoever_620_380.jpg\" alt=\"&quot;If you want to avoid the whoever\/whomever problem, rewrite your sentence.&quot; \u2014 Mignon Fogarty\" width=\"600\" height=\"315\" \/&gt;[\/media]\r\n\r\nPerhaps the only thing more confusing for native speakers of English than who\/whom is whoever\/whomever. In addition to rarely being taught in public schools, these pronouns are even more rarely used correctly, making it difficult to internalize the patterns. Despite these issues, it's still considered the mark of an educated person to be able to use \u2014 or at least navigate around \u2014 these words. So, what do you do if you don't have a clue? Here are two effective ways for you to deal with the whoever\/whomever problem.\r\n\r\n&lt;b&gt;1. Learn the difference.&lt;\/b&gt;\r\nThis one is shocking, we know. But before you let panic set in, go pour yourself a tea, have a long-look in the mirror, and remind yourself that if you were somehow able to program the universal remote, you can learn how to use these two words correctly.\r\n\r\n&lt;iframe class=\"aligncenter\" src=\"\/\/giphy.com\/embed\/Cx0JktG3wBWvu?html5=true\" width=\"480\" height=\"270\" frameborder=\"0\" allowfullscreen=\"allowfullscreen\"&gt;&lt;\/iframe&gt;\r\n\r\nFirst, &lt;b&gt;who and whoever are subject pronouns.&lt;\/b&gt; They function the same way &lt;i&gt;I, he, she, we,&lt;\/i&gt; and &lt;i&gt;they&lt;\/i&gt; function in sentences. Subject pronouns are\u00a0the actors in sentences. &lt;b&gt;Whom and whomever are object pronouns.&lt;\/b&gt; They function the same way &lt;i&gt;me, him, her, us,&lt;\/i&gt; and &lt;i&gt;them&lt;\/i&gt; function in sentences.* Object pronouns receive actions (they are the objects of verbs).\r\n\r\nSecond, don't make this harder than it has to be. You can use more familiar subject and object pronouns to sort out which is correct to use. For example:\r\n\r\n&lt;i&gt;Give the correspondence to whoever\/whomever.&lt;\/i&gt; --&gt; &lt;i&gt;Give the correspondence to he\/him.&lt;\/i&gt;\r\n\r\nYou're right. It should be \"whomever\" in exactly the same way that we would always say \"him.\" They are both object pronouns. Of course, not all sentences are as easy as this, but you should be able to whittle a sentence down to its essence to make the subject\/object distinction. The following sentence doesn't work as nicely with the replacement pronouns.\r\n\r\n&lt;i&gt;Report the problem to whoever\/whomever you can find at the bureau.&lt;\/i&gt; (&lt;i&gt;Report the problem to he\/him you can find at the bureau.&lt;\/i&gt; Eek.)\r\n\r\nIt can be simplified by removing the additional information \u2014 the modifier \"(who) you can find at the bureau\" \u2014 then the sentence is as simple as the first. If you are still not sure after the simplification, try replacing \"he\/him\" with \"he\/him who.\" It might sound a little formal, but if it still makes sense, then you know to stick with the subject case. The same trick will work for sentences similar to the following.\r\n\r\n&lt;i&gt;Whoever\/whomever double parked, you're being towed.&lt;\/i&gt; --&gt; &lt;i&gt;He who\/Him who double parked, you're being towed.&lt;\/i&gt;\r\n\r\nClearly, we should stick to the subject case here \u2014\u00a0\"whoever double parked.\"\r\n\r\n&lt;b&gt;2. Rewrite to avoid the issue all together.&lt;\/b&gt;\r\n\r\nGrammar Girl, Mignon Fogarty, has it right \u2014 just re-write. If you still don't feel confident about your understanding of whoever\/whomever, you can always do as the professionals do and rephrase it. When rewriting, try to aim to be specific but brief. The word \"someone\" is a good replacement in many cases.\r\n\r\n&lt;i&gt;Give the correspondence to whoever\/whomever.&lt;\/i&gt; --&gt; &lt;i&gt;Give the correspondence to someone.&lt;\/i&gt;\r\n\r\n&lt;i&gt;Report the problem to whoever\/whomever you can find at the bureau.&lt;\/i&gt; --&gt; &lt;i&gt;Report the problem to someone at the bureau.&lt;\/i&gt; OR &lt;i&gt;Report the problem at the bureau.&lt;\/i&gt;\r\n\r\n&lt;i&gt;Whoever\/whomever double parked, you're being towed.&lt;\/i&gt; --&gt; &lt;i&gt;The person who double parked is being towed.&lt;\/i&gt;\r\n\r\nNow, you can conquer whoever\/whomever with the best of them. Go get 'em, tiger!\r\n\r\n&lt;iframe class=\"aligncenter\" src=\"\/\/giphy.com\/embed\/NJhOlTTz6cbNm?html5=true\" width=\"480\" height=\"200\" frameborder=\"0\" allowfullscreen=\"allowfullscreen\"&gt;&lt;\/iframe&gt;\r\n\r\n&lt;b&gt;What tips do you have for using whoever and whomever?&lt;\/b&gt;\r\n\r\n&nbsp;\r\n\r\n&lt;hr \/&gt;\r\n\r\n*We left &lt;i&gt;you&lt;\/i&gt; out of this list because it's formed the same way for both subject and object cases.","post_title":"2 Effective Ways to Deal with Whoever\/Whomever Confusion","post_excerpt":"","post_status":"publish","comment_status":"open","ping_status":"open","post_password":"","post_name":"2-effective-ways-to-deal-with-whoeverwhomever-confusion","to_ping":"","pinged":"","post_modified":"2015-03-25 09:31:03","post_modified_gmt":"2015-03-25 16:31:03","post_content_filtered":"","post_parent":0,"guid":"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/?p=12607","menu_order":0,"post_type":"post","post_mime_type":"","comment_count":"0","filter":"raw"},{"ID":12641,"post_author":"6","post_date":"2015-03-20 07:07:00","post_date_gmt":"2015-03-20 14:07:00","post_content":"[media]&lt;img src=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/wp-content\/uploads\/2015\/03\/sadly_i_do_my_best_proofreading_after_send.jpg\" alt=\"Sadly, I do my best proofreading after I hit send. \" width=\"600\" height=\"315\" class=\"aligncenter size-full wp-image-12643\" \/&gt;[\/media]\r\n\r\nWe've all been there \u2014 mortified by the consequences of our own lack of care. Catching typos after you've hit send can limit your personal and professional opportunities at worst or be just plain annoying at best. You know that you should proofread but don't because it requires an English degree (right?) and is time-consuming (right?). Actually almost anyone can quickly and easily reduce (dare we say eliminate?) post-send mortifications by following these three proofreading tricks.\r\n\r\n&lt;b&gt;1. Copy your text into a different editor or change the font.&lt;\/b&gt;\r\nOne of the biggest reasons that writing errors make it from draft to sent copy is because your brain is so familiar with the text that it literally auto-corrects the mistakes it sees \u2014 making them almost invisible to you. Unfortunately, the longer you work on a text, the more likely this is to happen. This trick is to help your brain perceive your writing as something fresh so that it can more easily spot oddities.  \r\n\r\n&lt;b&gt;2. Read your text backward.&lt;\/b&gt; \r\nIf you don't have time to step away from your writing for anywhere from few hours to a day or more, try this hack. Read the sentences in backward order. Instead of reading from top to bottom, read bottom to top. This will add novelty to the sentences, making it easier to find errors. \r\n\r\n&lt;b&gt;3. Read your text out loud.&lt;\/b&gt; \r\nEven if you're just whispering away at your desk, reading aloud is a great way to find and spot mistakes, especially missing punctuation or repeated words. As an added bonus, you will likely hear when something doesn't flow properly or sounds awkward. While not necessarily incorrect, these phrases can be re-worded for better clarity in communication. \r\n\r\nThis is a much pared-down version of what we would recommend for more substantial proofreading tasks. However, if you are writing for social media or on a tight timeline, these tips should make a big impact. &lt;b&gt;What is your proofreading process?&lt;\/b&gt;\r\n \r\n\r\n","post_title":"3 Things You Should Do When Speed Proofreading","post_excerpt":"","post_status":"publish","comment_status":"open","ping_status":"open","post_password":"","post_name":"3-things-you-should-do-when-speed-proofreading","to_ping":"","pinged":"","post_modified":"2015-03-20 07:07:00","post_modified_gmt":"2015-03-20 14:07:00","post_content_filtered":"","post_parent":0,"guid":"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/?p=12641","menu_order":0,"post_type":"post","post_mime_type":"","comment_count":"1","filter":"raw"},{"ID":12573,"post_author":"6","post_date":"2015-03-19 06:55:32","post_date_gmt":"2015-03-19 13:55:32","post_content":"[media]&lt;img src=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/wp-content\/uploads\/2015\/03\/never-judge-a-book-by-its-movie.jpg\" alt=\"Never judge a book by its movie. \" width=\"600\" height=\"315\" class=\"aligncenter size-full wp-image-12577\" \/&gt;[\/media]\r\n\r\n&lt;b&gt;1. &lt;i&gt;Great Expectations&lt;\/i&gt; (1947)&lt;\/b&gt;\r\nBook: &lt;i&gt;&lt;a href=\"http:\/\/www.gutenberg.org\/files\/1400\/1400-h\/1400-h.htm\"&gt;Great Expectations&lt;\/a&gt;&lt;\/i&gt; by Charles Dickens\r\n&lt;a href=\"http:\/\/www.rottentomatoes.com\/m\/great_expectations_1947\/\"&gt;Rotten Tomatoes&lt;\/a&gt;: 100% Fresh\r\nThis film adaptation of the literary classic immediately became the standard for Dickens adaptations. However, none have really been able to live up to the power of this version. The use of black and white film (despite the availability of color technology) lends a degree of austerity that reflects themes from the novel quite well on the screen. \r\n\r\n&lt;b&gt;2. &lt;i&gt; The Godfather&lt;\/i&gt; (1972)&lt;\/b&gt;\r\nBook: &lt;i&gt;&lt;a href=\"http:\/\/www.amazon.com\/Godfather-Mario-Puzo-ebook\/dp\/B0022Q8CSC\/ref=sr_1_1?s=books&amp;ie=UTF8&amp;qid=1426771439&amp;sr=1-1&amp;keywords=The+godfather\"&gt;The Godfather&lt;\/a&gt;&lt;\/i&gt; by Mario Puzo\r\n&lt;a href=\"http:\/\/www.rottentomatoes.com\/m\/godfather\/\"&gt;Rotten Tomatoes&lt;\/a&gt;: 99% Fresh\r\nGiven that Puzo assisted with the screenplay, it may not be such a surprise that the film adaptation maintains so much of the book's integrity. This relationship paid off, however \u2014 the film (and its franchise) is known as a cinematic behemoth. It remains one of the most frequently quoted and referenced film franchises in popular culture. \r\n\r\n&lt;b&gt;3. &lt;i&gt;The Big Sleep&lt;\/i&gt; (1947)&lt;\/b&gt;\r\nBook: &lt;i&gt;&lt;a href=\"http:\/\/www.amazon.com\/Big-Sleep-Novel-Philip-Marlowe-ebook\/dp\/B000FA64VO\/ref=sr_1_1?s=books&amp;ie=UTF8&amp;qid=1426772708&amp;sr=1-1&amp;keywords=The+big+sleep\"&gt;The Big Sleep&lt;\/a&gt;&lt;\/i&gt; by Raymond Chandler\r\n&lt;a href=\"http:\/\/www.rottentomatoes.com\/m\/1002352-big_sleep\/\"&gt;Rotten Tomatoes&lt;\/a&gt;: 96% Fresh\r\nThis film is a great example of what skilled casting and shared vision do for a piece of art. Director Howard Hawks captures and keeps the essence of the novel while Bacall and Bogart embody it perfectly. \r\n\r\n&lt;b&gt;4. &lt;i&gt;To Kill A Mockingbird&lt;\/i&gt; (1962)&lt;\/b&gt;\r\nBook: &lt;i&gt;&lt;a href=\"http:\/\/www.amazon.com\/To-Kill-Mockingbird-Harper-Lee\/dp\/0446310786\"&gt;To Kill A Mockingbird&lt;\/a&gt;&lt;\/i&gt; by Harper Lee\r\n&lt;a href=\"http:\/\/www.rottentomatoes.com\/m\/to_kill_a_mockingbird\/\"&gt;Rotten Tomatoes&lt;\/a&gt;: 94% Fresh\r\nThere is a reason that Gregory Peck won Best Actor for his portrayal of Atticus Finch. He nails it and makes the film work. Strong screenplay and outstanding casting make this adaptation utterly wonderful.\r\n\r\n&lt;b&gt;5. &lt;i&gt;The Silence of the Lambs&lt;\/i&gt; (1991)&lt;\/b&gt;\r\nBook: &lt;i&gt;&lt;a href=\"http:\/\/www.amazon.com\/The-Silence-Lambs-Hannibal-Lecter\/dp\/0312924585\/ref=pd_sim_b_3?ie=UTF8&amp;refRID=1FJ7Y4GV75XXD2QXZK3K\"&gt;The Silence of the Lambs&lt;\/a&gt;&lt;\/i&gt; by Thomas Harris\r\n&lt;a href=\"http:\/\/www.rottentomatoes.com\/m\/silence_of_the_lambs\/?search=THE%20SILENCE%20OF%20THE%20LAMBS\"&gt;Rotten Tomatoes&lt;\/a&gt;: 94% Fresh\r\nThis film has become such a landmark in cinema that some may not realize that it was first a best-selling book. It is the careful and intimate adaptation of the dark and difficult themes of the book that make this film adaptation a masterpiece. \r\n\r\n&lt;b&gt;What is your favorite book-to-film adaptation?&lt;\/b&gt;\r\n ","post_title":"5 Book-to-Movie Adaptations Worth Your Time","post_excerpt":"","post_status":"publish","comment_status":"open","ping_status":"open","post_password":"","post_name":"5-book-to-movie-adaptations-worth-your-time","to_ping":"","pinged":"","post_modified":"2015-03-19 07:12:35","post_modified_gmt":"2015-03-19 14:12:35","post_content_filtered":"","post_parent":0,"guid":"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/?p=12573","menu_order":0,"post_type":"post","post_mime_type":"","comment_count":"0","filter":"raw"},{"ID":12563,"post_author":"6","post_date":"2015-03-19 05:37:30","post_date_gmt":"2015-03-19 12:37:30","post_content":"Are you suffering from a bit of writer's block or a general lack of inspiration to take your writing to the next level? Don't worry. We've got you covered. The following 10 quotations by some brilliant writers will reinvigorate your writing muscle and give you some tips for writing better.\r\n\r\n&lt;b&gt;1. \"If it sounds like writing, I rewrite it.\" \u2014 Elmore Leonard&lt;\/b&gt;\r\n[media]&lt;img class=\"aligncenter size-full wp-image-12565\" src=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/wp-content\/uploads\/2015\/03\/32wes.jpg\" alt=\"&quot;If it sounds like writing, I rewrite it.&quot; \u2014 Leonard\" width=\"600\" height=\"315\" \/&gt;[\/media]\r\n\r\n&lt;hr \/&gt;\r\n\r\n&lt;b&gt;2. \u201cIn order to write the book you want to write, in the end you have to become the person you need to become to write that book.\u201d \u2015 Junot D\u00edaz&lt;\/b&gt;\r\n\r\n&nbsp;\r\n\r\n&lt;hr \/&gt;\r\n\r\n&lt;b&gt;3. \u201cAnyone who says writing is easy isn't doing it right.\u201d \u2015 Amy Joy&lt;\/b&gt;\r\n\r\n&nbsp;\r\n\r\n&lt;hr \/&gt;\r\n\r\n&lt;b&gt;4. \u201cDear Aspiring Writer, you are not ready. Stop. Put that finished story away and start another one. In a month, go back and look at the first story. RE-EDIT it. Then send it to a person you respect in the field who will be hard on you. Pray for many many many red marks. Fix them. Then put it away for two weeks. Work on something else. Finally, edit one last time. Now you are ready to sub your first work.\r\n\r\nCriticism is hard to take at first. Trust me, I've been there. But learn to think of crit marks as a knife. Each one is designed to cut away the bad and leave a scar. Scars prove you've lived, learned and walked away a winner. Any writer who tells you they don't need edits is lying. I don't care if they have 100 books out. Edits make you grow and if you aren't growing as a writer, you are dead.\u201d \u2015 Inez Kelley&lt;\/b&gt;\r\n\r\n&nbsp;\r\n\r\n&lt;hr \/&gt;\r\n\r\n&lt;b&gt;5. \u201c...you are a writer the moment you start writing, not when you've sold your first book.\u201d \u2015 Rob Bignell&lt;\/b&gt;\r\n\r\n&nbsp;\r\n\r\n&lt;hr \/&gt;\r\n\r\n&lt;b&gt;6. \u201cIf you want to be a writer, you must do two things above all others: read a lot and write a lot.\u201d \u2014 Stephen King&lt;\/b&gt;\r\n\r\n&nbsp;\r\n\r\n&lt;hr \/&gt;\r\n\r\n&lt;b&gt;7. \u201cSubstitute 'damn' every time you're inclined to write 'very;' your editor will delete it and the writing will be just as it should be.\u201d \u2014 Mark Twain&lt;\/b&gt;\r\n\r\n&nbsp;\r\n\r\n&lt;hr \/&gt;\r\n\r\n&lt;b&gt;8. \u201cHere is a lesson in creative writing. First rule: Do not use semicolons. They are transvestite hermaphrodites representing absolutely nothing.\u00a0&lt;\/b&gt;&lt;b&gt;All they do is show you've been to college.\u201d \u2014 Kurt Vonnegut&lt;\/b&gt;\r\n\r\n&nbsp;\r\n\r\n&lt;hr \/&gt;\r\n\r\n&lt;b&gt;9. \u201cCut out all these exclamation points. An exclamation point is like laughing at your own joke.\u201d \u2014 F. Scott Fitzgerald&lt;\/b&gt;\r\n\r\n&nbsp;\r\n\r\n&lt;hr \/&gt;\r\n\r\n&lt;b&gt;10. \u201cYou can't wait for inspiration. You have to go after it with a club.\u201d \u2014 Jack London&lt;\/b&gt;\r\n\r\n&nbsp;\r\n\r\nWhat's your favorite piece of writing advice?","post_title":"10 Quotes that Will Make You A Better Writer Instantly","post_excerpt":"","post_status":"publish","comment_status":"open","ping_status":"open","post_password":"","post_name":"10-quotes-that-will-make-you-a-better-writer-instantly","to_ping":"","pinged":"","post_modified":"2015-03-19 05:54:42","post_modified_gmt":"2015-03-19 12:54:42","post_content_filtered":"","post_parent":0,"guid":"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/?p=12563","menu_order":0,"post_type":"post","post_mime_type":"","comment_count":"0","filter":"raw"},{"ID":12463,"post_author":"1001005","post_date":"2015-03-19 04:29:31","post_date_gmt":"2015-03-19 11:29:31","post_content":"Post written by Hadiyah Dache\r\n\r\nAh, the old &lt;i&gt;that&lt;\/i&gt; vs. &lt;i&gt;which&lt;\/i&gt; debate. It\u2019s such a common and frustrating grammar rule. But don\u2019t fret! Read on to find our quick and easy tips on how to properly use &lt;i&gt;that&lt;\/i&gt; and &lt;i&gt;which&lt;\/i&gt;.\r\n\r\n[media]&lt;img src=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/wp-content\/uploads\/2015\/03\/78tygh.jpg\" alt=\"That is not the same as which.\" width=\"600\" height=\"315\" class=\"aligncenter size-full wp-image-12559\" \/&gt;[\/media]\r\n\r\n&lt;b&gt;Restrictive Clause vs. Non-restrictive Clause&lt;\/b&gt;\r\nThat\u2019s right, it all comes down to these two kinds of clauses.\r\n\r\nA &lt;b&gt;restrictive clause&lt;\/b&gt; acts as an adjective to modify the subject of the sentence. The restrictive clause is essential to the sentence because it provides information that, if taken out, changes the subject of the sentence completely. A restrictive clause is always preceded by &lt;i&gt;that&lt;\/i&gt;. Here are a few examples:\r\n\r\n&lt;i&gt;The ring &lt;\/i&gt;&lt;b&gt;&lt;i&gt;that&lt;\/i&gt;&lt;\/b&gt;&lt;i&gt; she had custom-made was stolen from the vault.&lt;\/i&gt;\r\n&lt;i&gt;Circus animals &lt;\/i&gt;&lt;b&gt;&lt;i&gt;that &lt;\/i&gt;&lt;\/b&gt;&lt;i&gt;aren\u2019t in cages scare me.&lt;\/i&gt;\r\n&lt;i&gt;Personal computers &lt;\/i&gt;&lt;b&gt;&lt;i&gt;that&lt;\/i&gt;&lt;\/b&gt;&lt;i&gt; utilize retina displays are great for graphic designers.&lt;\/i&gt;\r\n\r\nA restrictive clause is never placed between punctuation.\r\n\r\nA &lt;b&gt;nonrestrictive clause&lt;\/b&gt; typically contains optional information on the subject that isn\u2019t entirely necessary to the sentence. In other words, if you remove a nonrestrictive clause from a sentence, the subject of the sentence remains unaffected. A nonrestrictive clause is usually preceded by &lt;i&gt;which&lt;\/i&gt; (if a pronoun is used at all). Here are a few examples:\r\n\r\n&lt;i&gt;The ring, &lt;\/i&gt;&lt;b&gt;&lt;i&gt;which&lt;\/i&gt;&lt;\/b&gt;&lt;i&gt; was part of a custom jewelry set, was stolen from the vault.&lt;\/i&gt;\r\n&lt;i&gt;Circus animals, &lt;\/i&gt;&lt;b&gt;&lt;i&gt;which&lt;\/i&gt;&lt;\/b&gt;&lt;i&gt; sometimes make cute pets, are rather expensive.&lt;\/i&gt;\r\n&lt;i&gt;Personal computers, &lt;\/i&gt;&lt;b&gt;&lt;i&gt;which&lt;\/i&gt;&lt;\/b&gt;&lt;i&gt; were actually invented in 1964, have come quite a long way.&lt;\/i&gt;\r\n\r\nA nonrestrictive clause is usually placed between commas, dashes, or parentheses.\r\n\r\n&lt;b&gt;\r\nRemember: if your sentence &lt;i&gt;needs&lt;\/i&gt; it, then you\u2019ve written a restrictive clause, and you should use &lt;i&gt;that&lt;\/i&gt;. If the clause gives a little extra, &lt;i&gt;unnecessary&lt;\/i&gt; information, then you\u2019ve written a nonrestrictive clause, and you should use &lt;i&gt;which&lt;\/i&gt;. &lt;\/b&gt;","post_title":"Using That and Which is All About Restrictive and Non-restrictive Clauses","post_excerpt":"","post_status":"publish","comment_status":"open","ping_status":"open","post_password":"","post_name":"using-that-and-which-is-all-about-restrictive-and-non-restrictive-clauses","to_ping":"","pinged":"","post_modified":"2015-03-20 09:58:28","post_modified_gmt":"2015-03-20 16:58:28","post_content_filtered":"","post_parent":0,"guid":"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/?p=12463","menu_order":0,"post_type":"post","post_mime_type":"","comment_count":"2","filter":"raw"},{"ID":11575,"post_author":"4","post_date":"2015-03-19 01:00:26","post_date_gmt":"2015-03-19 08:00:26","post_content":"Merriam-Webster defines grammar quite simply as the set of rules explaining how we use words in the English language. And as language itself has evolved over time, so have the rules of grammar. Given the speed with which written communication has adapted to life in the Internet era, even the strictest style mavens understand that some grammar conventions may no longer apply. Here are some grammar rules today's writers can usually safely ignore.\r\n\r\n[media]&lt;img src=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/wp-content\/uploads\/2015\/03\/6-Outdated-Grammar-Rules-.jpg\" alt=\"Outdated Grammar Rules You Can Forget\" width=\"600\" height=\"315\" class=\"aligncenter size-full wp-image-12547\" \/&gt;[\/media]\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Ending a Sentence With a Preposition&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\n\r\nThe English poet John Dryden proclaimed it \"inelegant\" to &lt;a href=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/2014\/youve-been-lied-to-heres-why-you-absolutely-can-end-a-sentence-with-a-preposition\/\"&gt;end a sentence with a preposition&lt;\/a&gt; simply because it wasn't possible to do so in Latin, the gold standard of the time. In fact, many early grammar rules were heavily influenced by the limitations of Latin. Today, most writers agree with Winston Churchill, who, when asked about the preposition rule, drolly replied, \"This is the sort of bloody nonsense up with which I will not put.\"\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Splitting Infinitives&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\n\r\nThe rule against &lt;a href=\"http:\/\/www.quickanddirtytips.com\/education\/grammar\/split-infinitives\"&gt;split infinitives&lt;\/a&gt; is another relic left over from Latin constructions. In the past, strict grammarians would object to separating the infinitive with a descriptive word, insisting that &lt;i&gt;to quickly run &lt;\/i&gt;must be replaced with &lt;i&gt;to run quickly. &lt;\/i&gt;Now it's universally understood that the writers are free to choose the style that best suits their needs.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Using Possessive Pronouns With Gerunds&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\n\r\nPurists insist that writers treat gerunds as they would any other noun and precede it with a possessive pronoun. \"Mother didn't appreciate my singing during dinner,\" is technically correct, but it is also acceptable to say, \"Mother didn't appreciate me singing during dinner.\" In many cases, pronoun choice is more a stylistic choice than a strict rule of grammar.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Not beginning a Sentence With a Conjunction&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\n\r\n&lt;a href=\"https:\/\/www.flickr.com\/photos\/rowdykittens\/8334859582\/sizes\/o\/\"&gt;&lt;img class=\"aligncenter size-medium wp-image-11577\" src=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/wp-content\/uploads\/2015\/02\/Untitled21-410x410.png\" alt=\"Image via Flickr by Rowdy Kittens\" width=\"300\" height=\"300\" \/&gt;&lt;\/a&gt;\r\n\r\nThe prohibition against opening a sentence with a conjunction is one of the most persistent grammar myths of all time. In fact, the \"Chicago Manual of Style\"\u00a0estimates that as many as 10 percent of the finest sentences ever written began with a conjunction. Since this practice is accepted by all the major style guides, it's safe to say it's okay to &lt;a href=\"http:\/\/grammarist.com\/grammar\/conjunctions-to-start-sentences\/\"&gt;start a sentence with a conjunction&lt;\/a&gt;.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Avoiding Sentence Fragments&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\n\r\nFiction writers recognize the stylistic value of employing a sentence fragment to emphasize a point. In general, it's still the rule to avoid fragments in formal and academic writing, but employed sparingly and judiciously, the &lt;a href=\"http:\/\/www.quickanddirtytips.com\/education\/grammar\/sentence-fragments-0\"&gt;sentence fragment&lt;\/a&gt; is a powerful tool in prose. Of course, as with any subjective matter in writing, the danger lies in using stylistic tricks to disguise sloppy composition.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Using Gendered Pronouns&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\n\r\nThroughout the history of written English, &lt;i&gt;he &lt;\/i&gt;was the preferred pronoun for generic writing. Today, this is no longer the case, a condition causing fits for the current generation of writers. English lacks &lt;a href=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/2015\/are-gender-neutral-pronouns-the-wave-of-the-future\/\"&gt;gender-neutral pronouns&lt;\/a&gt;, and it doesn't seem as though a widely acceptable alternative to &lt;i&gt;he &lt;\/i&gt;and &lt;i&gt;she &lt;\/i&gt;will be available any time soon.\r\n\r\nSince the major style guides caution against using a generic &lt;i&gt;he, &lt;\/i&gt;some writers have resorted to using &lt;i&gt;they &lt;\/i&gt;as a singular pronoun: \"The student needs to understand that they are responsible for bringing lunch.\" Since most editors are not as comfortable with that construction, it's best to rewrite the sentence with a plural noun: \"The &lt;i&gt;students&lt;\/i&gt; need to understand that they are responsible for bringing lunch.\"\r\n\r\nOf course, for the most formal writing, especially in academic situations, it's best to adhere to strict grammatical constructions. But for other writing, especially where style, point of view, and dialect are important to give nuance to a piece, go ahead and ignore the archaic constraints.\r\n\r\n&lt;b&gt;Do you play loose with grammar rules in your daily writing? Which ones annoy or confuse you the most?&lt;\/b&gt;\r\n","post_title":"Remember When? 6 Grammar Rules From the Past","post_excerpt":"","post_status":"publish","comment_status":"open","ping_status":"open","post_password":"","post_name":"remember-when-6-grammar-rules-from-the-past","to_ping":"","pinged":"","post_modified":"2015-03-23 05:02:17","post_modified_gmt":"2015-03-23 12:02:17","post_content_filtered":"","post_parent":0,"guid":"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/?p=11575","menu_order":0,"post_type":"post","post_mime_type":"","comment_count":"0","filter":"raw"},{"ID":12515,"post_author":"6","post_date":"2015-03-18 07:14:58","post_date_gmt":"2015-03-18 14:14:58","post_content":"[media]&lt;img src=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/wp-content\/uploads\/2015\/03\/789iopknjpg.jpg\" alt=\"How many Fs can you count?\" width=\"600\" height=\"315\" class=\"aligncenter size-full wp-image-12543\" \/&gt;[\/media]\r\n\r\n\"Finished files are the result of years of scientific study combined with the experience of years.\"\r\n\r\nThe answer is 6. \r\n\r\nOdds are high that the first or second time you tried to count the Fs in this sentence you overlooked one or more of the Fs in \"of.\" The reason for this is that we often say the words in our heads as we read. Since we read \"of\" as \"ov,\" we tend to miss the F.\r\n\r\n&lt;b&gt;What other tricky word games do you know? Share your favorites in the comments!&lt;\/b&gt;","post_title":"How many Fs did you count?","post_excerpt":"","post_status":"publish","comment_status":"open","ping_status":"open","post_password":"","post_name":"how-many-fs-did-you-count","to_ping":"","pinged":"","post_modified":"2015-03-18 07:14:58","post_modified_gmt":"2015-03-18 14:14:58","post_content_filtered":"","post_parent":0,"guid":"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/?p=12515","menu_order":0,"post_type":"post","post_mime_type":"","comment_count":"0","filter":"raw"},{"ID":12459,"post_author":"1001005","post_date":"2015-03-18 07:01:53","post_date_gmt":"2015-03-18 14:01:53","post_content":"[media]&lt;img src=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/wp-content\/uploads\/2015\/03\/flaunt1.jpg\" alt=\"Flaunt is not equal to flout.\" width=\"600\" height=\"315\" class=\"aligncenter size-full wp-image-12553\" \/&gt;[\/media]\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Flaunt&lt;\/strong&gt; means to parade, display with ostentation; to show off, as with flashy clothing:\r\n\r\n&lt;i&gt;He &lt;strong&gt;flaunted&lt;\/strong&gt; his wealth by wearing a giant, expensive fur coat.\r\nI\u2019m happy you got a promotion at work, but you don\u2019t need to &lt;strong&gt;flaunt&lt;\/strong&gt; your success by handing out your new business cards to everyone.&lt;\/i&gt;\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Flout&lt;\/strong&gt; means to express contempt for the rules by word or action; to scorn:\r\n\r\n&lt;i&gt;The dictator &lt;strong&gt;flouted&lt;\/strong&gt; international law by re-entering the combat zone.\r\nThe members of the senior class &lt;strong&gt;flouted&lt;\/strong&gt; the dress code by wearing baggy pants and crop tops.&lt;\/i&gt;\r\n\r\nFlaunt and flout sound similar but have very different meanings. To learn more about other confusing word pairs, &lt;a href=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/2015\/dont-let-these-two-word-pairs-confuse-you\/\"&gt;read this blog post&lt;\/a&gt;. ","post_title":"Vocabulary Lesson: Flaunt and Flout","post_excerpt":"","post_status":"publish","comment_status":"open","ping_status":"open","post_password":"","post_name":"vocabulary-lesson-flaunt-and-flout","to_ping":"","pinged":"","post_modified":"2015-03-19 03:14:56","post_modified_gmt":"2015-03-19 10:14:56","post_content_filtered":"","post_parent":0,"guid":"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/?p=12459","menu_order":0,"post_type":"post","post_mime_type":"","comment_count":"0","filter":"raw"},{"ID":12531,"post_author":"6","post_date":"2015-03-18 06:56:39","post_date_gmt":"2015-03-18 13:56:39","post_content":"If these Ben Franklin quotes don't make you smarter, they will definitely inspire you to learn something. Get going!\r\n\r\n1. \"An investment in knowledge pays the best interest.\" \u2014 Ben Franklin\r\n[media]&lt;img src=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/wp-content\/uploads\/2015\/03\/invest-in-knowlege.jpg\" alt=\"&quot;An investment in knowledge pays the best interest.&quot; \u2014 Benjamin Franklin\" width=\"600\" height=\"315\" class=\"aligncenter size-full wp-image-12533\" \/&gt; [\/media]\r\n\r\n2. \u201cBeing ignorant is not so much a shame, as being unwilling to learn.\u201d \u2015 Benjamin Franklin\r\n\r\n3. \u201cGenius without education is like silver in the mine.\u201d \u2015 Benjamin Franklin\r\n\r\n4. \u201cThe only thing that is more expensive than education is ignorance.\u201d \u2015 Benjamin Franklin\r\n\r\n&lt;b&gt;What is your favorite education quote?&lt;\/b&gt;\r\n","post_title":"4 Benjamin Franklin Quotes That Will Make You Less Ignorant","post_excerpt":"","post_status":"publish","comment_status":"open","ping_status":"open","post_password":"","post_name":"4-benjamin-franklin-quotes-that-will-make-you-less-ignorant","to_ping":"","pinged":"","post_modified":"2015-03-19 10:31:10","post_modified_gmt":"2015-03-19 17:31:10","post_content_filtered":"","post_parent":0,"guid":"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/?p=12531","menu_order":0,"post_type":"post","post_mime_type":"","comment_count":"0","filter":"raw"},{"ID":12517,"post_author":"6","post_date":"2015-03-18 06:23:14","post_date_gmt":"2015-03-18 13:23:14","post_content":"[media]&lt;img src=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/wp-content\/uploads\/2015\/03\/4356ytrgfd.jpg\" alt=\"Complete the sentence with the correct word. &quot;The necklace was a perfect . . . .  to her eyes.&quot; \" width=\"600\" height=\"315\" class=\"aligncenter size-full wp-image-12521\" \/&gt;[\/media]\r\n\r\nCompliment and complement are two of the most commonly confused words. \r\n\r\n&lt;b&gt;Compliment&lt;\/b&gt; can be a verb or a noun. Compliment as a verb means to flatter; to express a favorable opinion. As a noun, compliment is an expression of praise, congratulation or encouragement.\r\n\r\n&lt;b&gt;Complement&lt;\/b&gt; is a noun, and it has several similar meanings based on context. The two most common meanings are:\r\n1. The totality, the full amount or number which completes something.\r\n2. Something which completes one's equipment, dress etc.; an accessory.\r\n\r\n&lt;b&gt;So, the correct answer to this fill-in-the-blank is complement.&lt;\/b&gt;\r\n\r\nA great way to remember the difference is to think of the following: &lt;i&gt;\"I\"&lt;\/i&gt; give &lt;i&gt;compliments&lt;\/i&gt; to others.","post_title":"Compliment or complement?","post_excerpt":"Can you complete this sentence with the correct word? \"The necklace is a perfect ________________ to her eyes.\" ","post_status":"publish","comment_status":"open","ping_status":"open","post_password":"","post_name":"compliment-or-complement","to_ping":"","pinged":"","post_modified":"2015-03-18 06:24:37","post_modified_gmt":"2015-03-18 13:24:37","post_content_filtered":"","post_parent":0,"guid":"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/?p=12517","menu_order":0,"post_type":"post","post_mime_type":"","comment_count":"0","filter":"raw"},{"ID":12453,"post_author":"2","post_date":"2015-03-17 03:32:48","post_date_gmt":"2015-03-17 10:32:48","post_content":"Post written by Hadiyah Dache\r\n\r\n[media]&lt;img src=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/wp-content\/uploads\/2015\/03\/Vintage-card-3wed.png\" alt=\"It takes me so long to reply to your text messages because I insist on using proper grammar, spelling, and punctuation. \" width=\"600\" height=\"315\" class=\"aligncenter size-full wp-image-12505\" \/&gt;[\/media]\r\n\r\nKeeping up with grammar rules when you're texting and tweeting can be difficult. We get it\u2014your characters are limited and you've got to keep things brief\u2014but the challenge with typing in shorthand is the risk of getting things lost in translation (and autocorrect misinterpreting what you're trying to say entirely). Communicating a clear message through texts and tweets can be even trickier now that emoji use is replacing words altogether. We've all had our moments when what we thought was a clear, simple text completely confused the person on the other end. Here are a few texting tips to help you get your message across:\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;1. Use Acronyms Sparingly&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\nIMHO IDC. IANAL but LMK JIC.\r\n\r\n&lt;i&gt;In my honest opinion (&lt;\/i&gt;IMHO)&lt;i&gt;, I don't care (IDC). I am not a lawyer (IANAL) but let me know (LMK) just in case (JIC).&lt;\/i&gt;\r\n\r\nIf you aren't a world champion of shorthand, it\u2019s easy to get a little apprehensive at the sight of a string of acronyms. For clarity's sake, it's best to stick to 1 or 2 acronyms per text. And usually, if it's longer than a 4-letter acronym, it may be better to take the extra second to type the phrase out.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;2. Know Your Audience&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\nYou: Can You MM@ Market Street? TIA. (&lt;i&gt;Can you meet me @ Market Street? Thanks in advance.&lt;\/i&gt;)\r\nThem: Huh?\r\n\r\nThough we may not realize it, sometimes we can be slightly more conversant in textspeak than the person we're communicating with. While you and your best friend can seemingly have an entire conversation through a series of shorthand texts, someone you text only periodically may not understand your lingo. If you've coined a new acronym, it's probably best to type the phrase out first and then place the acronym in parentheses.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;3. An Emoji Says 1,000 Words&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\nThe good news: there's an emoji for just about everything. The bad news: many of us still aren't sure which emoji means what. For example, the high five emoji is often mistaken for (and used as) praying hands. The difference between the crying, sick, and sweating emoji are so minimal that you probably never noticed there was a distinct emoji for each. And what about the woman with her hand in the air? You may be surprised to learn that she isn't in the middle of a casual hair flip or raising the roof, but instead she's assisting you. (Her proper name is \"Information Desk Lady\".) Review your emoji with \u00a0the &lt;a href=\"http:\/\/www.emoji-cheat-sheet.com\/\"&gt;Emoji Cheat Sheet&lt;\/a&gt; to ensure you use the correct one every time. Who knew the emoji with steam coming from its nose actually means \"triumph\"?\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;4. Capitalization and Punctuation Still Matter&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\nCapitalizing a letter doesn't limit your character count, so there's no need to skip this step. With numbers, for clarity and speed, it's best to use numerals at all times (e.g. I'll meet you @ 4 vs. I'll meet you @ four). Also, using proper punctuation will help your reader better understand what you're trying to convey. Sure, adding that comma may use up an extra character, but it will certainly help avoid confusion and possibly embarrassment.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;5. Sentence Fragments Are Okay&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\n&lt;i&gt;Going to the movies. Back around 9.&lt;\/i&gt;\r\n\r\nIf you leave a word out in a text message response to save some space, it's okay. Chances are your reader will still understand you. Because text messages are such a direct form of communication, you don't have to start each response with I'm\/I am.\r\n\r\nWe hope these tips help you out the next time you\u2019re in a pinch and need to send a quick text or tweet. Do you have some text etiquette of your own to share? Leave a comment or tweet us &lt;a href=\"https:\/\/twitter.com\/grammarly\"&gt;@Grammarly&lt;\/a&gt;!\r\n\r\n&lt;em&gt;Guest blog post by Hadiyah Dache&lt;\/em&gt;","post_title":"BRB, I'm learning these grammar rules for texting!","post_excerpt":"","post_status":"publish","comment_status":"open","ping_status":"open","post_password":"","post_name":"brb-im-learning-these-grammar-rules-for-texting","to_ping":"","pinged":"","post_modified":"2015-03-19 02:45:12","post_modified_gmt":"2015-03-19 09:45:12","post_content_filtered":"","post_parent":0,"guid":"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/?p=12453","menu_order":0,"post_type":"post","post_mime_type":"","comment_count":"0","filter":"raw"},{"ID":11569,"post_author":"4","post_date":"2015-03-17 01:00:28","post_date_gmt":"2015-03-17 08:00:28","post_content":"[media]&lt;img src=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/wp-content\/uploads\/2015\/03\/IRISH.jpg\" alt=\"Proverbs to Test Your Irishness\" width=\"600\" height=\"315\" class=\"aligncenter size-full wp-image-12499\" \/&gt;[\/media]\r\n\r\nWhen Saint Patrick's Day rolls around, everyone embraces a little Irish spirit. Sporting shamrocks and shillelaghs and wearing a bit o' green, friends come together to celebrate this most Celtic tradition -- and no one celebrates like the Irish!\r\n\r\nGiven the enthusiasm with which America endorses this holiday, perhaps it's no surprise that Irish culture has blended so happily with American pop culture. &lt;a href=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/2014\/looking-get-lucky-saint-patricks-day-idioms-may-help\/\"&gt;In honor of Saint Patrick's Day&lt;\/a&gt;, here are a few examples of Irish wisdom and sayings that have come to be part of modern culture.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;May the Road Rise Up to Meet You&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\n\r\n&lt;a href=\"https:\/\/www.flickr.com\/photos\/jpmpinmontreal\/3376735957\/sizes\/l\"&gt;&lt;img class=\"aligncenter size-full wp-image-11571\" src=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/wp-content\/uploads\/2015\/02\/Untitled11.png\" alt=\"Image via Flickr by jpmpinmontreal\" width=\"450\" height=\"300\" \/&gt;&lt;\/a&gt;\r\n\r\nChances are you've seen this famous blessing stitched on a pillow at your Irish granny's house or emblazoned on a plaque hung near her door. This blessing is a nod to the Celtic fondness for using symbols from nature to illustrate God's relationship with man.\u00a0Historians believe that invoking imagery, such as a welcome breeze or the warm sun, made the concept of an all-powerful Christian god more easily understood by common folk.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;It Is Often That a Person's Mouth Broke His Nose&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\n\r\nSure, this one plays into the stereotype of an Irishman's passion for brawling, but this good advice applies to everyone equally. If you don't want a punch in the nose, don't shoot off your mouth. Words to live by, &lt;a href=\"http:\/\/www.irishcentral.com\/roots\/top-ten-old-irish-phrases-and-their-meanings-eirinn-go-brach-137123193-237785321.html\"&gt;Erin go bragh&lt;\/a&gt;.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;A Son Is a Son 'Til He Takes Him a Wife. A Daughter's a Daughter All of Her Life&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\n\r\nThis nugget of wisdom -- that any mother of sons will understand -- comes from the New Testament in the Gospel of Mark: &lt;em&gt;For this cause, a man shall leave his father and mother and cleave to his wife...and they shall be as one flesh. &lt;\/em&gt;It took the Irish, however, to turn it into a snappy little rhyme.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;The Older the Fiddle, the Sweeter the Tune&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\n\r\nThis charming sentiment has several subtle variations, but most recognize it to mean that most things improve over time, or that the best things in life are worth waiting for. This &lt;a href=\"http:\/\/php1.grammarly.com\/blog\/2014\/knowledge-is-power-using-idioms-to-give-power-to-your-writing\/\"&gt;powerful idiom&lt;\/a&gt; was immortalized in popular culture by Gus McCrae, the feisty hero of Larry McMurtry's novel \"Lonesome Dove.\"\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Slainte!&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\n\r\nPronounced &lt;em&gt;slahn-sha, &lt;\/em&gt;this classic Irish toast means \"your health.\" From the Gaelic word for \"safe,\" this pithy exclamation stands in for the wordier, \"I drink to your good health.\" To endear yourself to your Irish friends this St. Patty's Day, grab a pint of Guinness at your local pub, clink bottles with your mates, and shout out a hearty, \"&lt;a href=\"http:\/\/www.irishcentral.com\/opinion\/niallodowd\/slainte-and-what-the-irish-words-means-on-stpatricks-day-118163119-238075881.html\"&gt;Slainte&lt;\/a&gt;!\"\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;May the Cat Eat You and the Devil Eat the Cat&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\n\r\nThe Irish are masters of the elaborate curse, and you'll really feel the burn if your Celtic acquaintance says this to you. Most save this double-barreled insult for the worst of characters. This one is considered worse than \"May you have an itch and no nails to scratch it,\" but not quite as bad as \"May you be a load for four before the year is out,\" with four referring to pall bearers at a funeral.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;You'll Never Plow a Field By Turning It Over in Your Mind&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\n\r\nMany cultures have proverbs admonishing one that nothing gets done by just thinking about it. In fact, the Irish like this sentiment so much, they have several variations on the theme. Another popular Irish proverb along the same line is \"There's no use boiling your cabbage twice.\"\r\n\r\nThere's no denying the Irish have some of the most colorful &lt;a href=\"http:\/\/www.quickanddirtytips.com\/education\/grammar\/crazy-english-idioms\"&gt;idioms&lt;\/a&gt; for imparting everyday folk wisdom and lore. This St. Patrick's Day, celebrate Irish heritage -- even if you can't claim Irish ancestry -- with a liberal sprinkling of common Celtic proverbs.\r\n\r\nDo you have a favorite Irish proverb, prayer, or curse?","post_title":"7 Irish Proverbs Adopted Into Pop Culture","post_excerpt":"","post_status":"publish","comment_status":"open","ping_status":"open","post_password":"","post_name":"7-irish-proverbs-adopted-into-pop-culture","to_ping":"","pinged":"","post_modified":"2015-03-17 03:24:40","post_modified_gmt":"2015-03-17 10:24:40","post_content_filtered":"","post_parent":0,"guid":"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/?p=11569","menu_order":0,"post_type":"post","post_mime_type":"","comment_count":"1","filter":"raw"},{"ID":12445,"post_author":"1001005","post_date":"2015-03-16 02:00:55","post_date_gmt":"2015-03-16 09:00:55","post_content":"[media]&lt;img src=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/wp-content\/uploads\/2015\/03\/paddys-day1.jpg\" alt=\"It&#039;s St. Paddy&#039;s (not Patty&#039;s.) It&#039;s Paddy from &#039;Padraig&#039; not Patty from &#039;Patricia.&#039;\" width=\"600\" height=\"315\" class=\"aligncenter size-full wp-image-12481\" \/&gt;[\/media]\r\n\r\nHappy St. Patrick\u2019s Day! While you\u2019re drinking green beer and counting shamrock leaves, you might end up debating a popular St. Patrick\u2019s Day question: Is it St. Patty\u2019s Day or St. Paddy\u2019s Day?\r\n\r\nIt\u2019s easy to think that Patrick ought to be shortened as \u201cPatty\u201d. The name contains a T rather than a D, after all. However, \u201cPaddy\u201d comes from the Irish name Padraig, which is the reason St. Paddy\u2019s Day is spelled with a D instead of a T. \r\n\r\nThe \u201cPatty\u201d vs. \u201cPaddy\u201d debate is so hot that there\u2019s even a &lt;a href=\"http:\/\/paddynotpatty.com\/\" target=\"_blank\"&gt;website&lt;\/a&gt; and &lt;a href=\"https:\/\/twitter.com\/paddynotpatty\" target=\"_blank\"&gt;Twitter account&lt;\/a&gt; dedicated to promoting the correct spelling.\r\n\r\nCheers to knowing the correct spelling of St. Paddy\u2019s Day! We\u2019ll tackle the meaning of Erin go bragh another time. ","post_title":"Stop saying St. Patty's Day!","post_excerpt":"","post_status":"publish","comment_status":"open","ping_status":"open","post_password":"","post_name":"its-st-paddys-day-not-st-pattys-day","to_ping":"","pinged":"","post_modified":"2015-03-16 03:30:12","post_modified_gmt":"2015-03-16 10:30:12","post_content_filtered":"","post_parent":0,"guid":"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/?p=12445","menu_order":0,"post_type":"post","post_mime_type":"","comment_count":"0","filter":"raw"},{"ID":12309,"post_author":"1001005","post_date":"2015-03-12 06:07:27","post_date_gmt":"2015-03-12 13:07:27","post_content":"[media]&lt;img src=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/wp-content\/uploads\/2015\/03\/yolocaesar.jpg\" alt=\"If Julius Caesar were written for today&#039;s generation. Cartoon by kudelka.com.au\" width=\"600\" height=\"315\" class=\"aligncenter size-full wp-image-12385\" \/&gt;[\/media]\r\n\r\nHave you ever heard someone say, \u201cBeware the Ides of March\u201d? What are the Ides and why should we beware? Here\u2019s an explanation:\r\n\r\nThe Ides of March is a date on the Roman calendar that corresponds to what we know as March 15. For the Romans, the middle of each month (usually the 13th or 15th) was called the Ides. Other times of the month were called the Nones and the Kalends. These periods were determined by the cycle of the moon.\r\n\r\nWhy should we beware the Ides? Julius Caesar was assassinated at a Senate meeting on the Ides of March in 44 BCE. He had been warned by a seer that he would be harmed by that time, but Caesar ignored the prediction.\r\n\r\nThis warning was dramatized in William Shakespeare\u2019s play, &lt;i&gt;Julius Caesar&lt;\/i&gt;:\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Soothsayer:&lt;\/strong&gt; Beware the ides of March.\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Caesar:&lt;\/strong&gt; What man is that?\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Brutus:&lt;\/strong&gt; A soothsayer bids you beware the ides of March.\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Caesar:&lt;\/strong&gt; Set him before me; let me see his face.\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Cassius:&lt;\/strong&gt; Fellow, come from the throng; look upon Caesar.\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Caesar:&lt;\/strong&gt; What say'st thou to me now? Speak once again.\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Soothsayer:&lt;\/strong&gt; Beware the ides of March.\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Caesar:&lt;\/strong&gt; He is a dreamer; let us leave him: pass.","post_title":"Funny Phrases: The Ides of March","post_excerpt":"","post_status":"publish","comment_status":"open","ping_status":"open","post_password":"","post_name":"funny-phrases-the-ides-of-march","to_ping":"","pinged":"","post_modified":"2015-03-12 06:08:18","post_modified_gmt":"2015-03-12 13:08:18","post_content_filtered":"","post_parent":0,"guid":"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/?p=12309","menu_order":0,"post_type":"post","post_mime_type":"","comment_count":"0","filter":"raw"},{"ID":12301,"post_author":"1001005","post_date":"2015-03-12 04:58:41","post_date_gmt":"2015-03-12 11:58:41","post_content":"[media]&lt;img src=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/wp-content\/uploads\/2015\/03\/3edsafd2.jpg\" alt=\"It&#039;s &quot;nip it in the bud&quot; not &quot;nip it in the butt.&quot;\" width=\"600\" height=\"315\" class=\"aligncenter size-full wp-image-12379\" \/&gt;[\/media]\r\n\r\nThe phrase &lt;strong&gt;nip it in the bud&lt;\/strong&gt; means to stop something before it gets going. You\u2019ve probably heard it used in this way:\r\n&lt;i&gt;Nip that bad habit in the bud before it gets out of control!&lt;\/i&gt;\r\n\r\nWhat does this phrase actually mean? &lt;strong&gt;Nip&lt;\/strong&gt; means to remove by pinching, biting, or cutting with two meeting edges of anything; to clip. A &lt;strong&gt;bud&lt;\/strong&gt; is a newly formed leaf or flower that has not yet bloomed. To nip something in the bud means to pinch off a newly formed leaf or flower before it has a chance to grow. When this literal meaning is translated to a figurative sense, it means stopping something before it has a chance to progress further.\r\n\r\nPeople have been saying &lt;strong&gt;nip it in the bud&lt;\/strong&gt; as far back as the 1600s, and the phrase is still a popular idiom today. If you want to learn more about idioms and odd phrases, &lt;a href=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/2015\/funny-phrases-the-hair-of-the-dog\/\"&gt;read this post about the hair of the dog&lt;\/a&gt;. ","post_title":"Funny Phrases: Nip It in the Bud","post_excerpt":"","post_status":"publish","comment_status":"open","ping_status":"open","post_password":"","post_name":"funny-phrases-nip-it-in-the-bud","to_ping":"","pinged":"","post_modified":"2015-03-12 04:59:33","post_modified_gmt":"2015-03-12 11:59:33","post_content_filtered":"","post_parent":0,"guid":"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/?p=12301","menu_order":0,"post_type":"post","post_mime_type":"","comment_count":"0","filter":"raw"},{"ID":12305,"post_author":"1001005","post_date":"2015-03-12 03:44:15","post_date_gmt":"2015-03-12 10:44:15","post_content":"[media]&lt;img src=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/wp-content\/uploads\/2015\/03\/2345H.jpg\" alt=\"Do you have trouble spelling amateur, conscientious, harass, or license? We have a few tips for you. \" width=\"600\" height=\"315\" class=\"aligncenter size-full wp-image-12367\" \/&gt;[\/media]\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Amateur&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\nSomething or someone that is amateur is non-professional. If you remember that amateur ends in a fancy French suffix (-eur), you\u2019ll be able to spell this word correctly in both professional and non-professional situations.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Conscientious&lt;\/strong&gt; \r\nConscientious means thorough, careful, or vigilant. If you have a strong conscience, you will be conscientious. You can remember the conscience by breaking it into \"con\" and \"science.\" Then the similarities between conscience and conscientious will help you remember the spelling for the latter.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Harass&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\nTo harass means to fatigue or to tire with repeated and exhausting efforts. Don\u2019t \"harass\" your fingers by making them type extra letters on the keyboard. Harass contains only one R.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;License&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\nA license is an official authorization. You can remember the C in license by thinking that you don\u2019t always need sense (spelled with an S) to get a license.\r\n\r\n&lt;b&gt;Do you know other spelling tips or tricks? Share them in the comments!&lt;\/b&gt;","post_title":"Train your brain with these 4 spelling tips and tricks","post_excerpt":"","post_status":"publish","comment_status":"open","ping_status":"open","post_password":"","post_name":"train-your-brain-with-these-4-spelling-tips-and-tricks","to_ping":"","pinged":"","post_modified":"2015-03-12 04:50:33","post_modified_gmt":"2015-03-12 11:50:33","post_content_filtered":"","post_parent":0,"guid":"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/?p=12305","menu_order":0,"post_type":"post","post_mime_type":"","comment_count":"0","filter":"raw"},{"ID":12341,"post_author":"4","post_date":"2015-03-09 09:03:02","post_date_gmt":"2015-03-09 16:03:02","post_content":"What do NASA, the\u00a0Department of Health and Human Services, and the\u00a0Department of the Treasury\u00a0have in common? These three Federal agencies use English most effectively (per the &lt;a href=\"http:\/\/www.plainlanguage.gov\/plLaw\/\"&gt;Plain Writing\u00a0Act of 2010&lt;\/a&gt;).\r\n&lt;div&gt;\r\n\r\n[caption id=\"\" align=\"aligncenter\" width=\"827\"]&lt;img class=\"\" src=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/about\/content\/04-press-room\/fedagencies-02.jpg\" alt=\"Plain English, writing, Federal agencies\" width=\"630\" height=\"2983\" \/&gt; Use Plain English, Please[\/caption]\r\n\r\n&lt;\/div&gt;\r\nThe &lt;a href=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\"&gt;Grammarly&lt;\/a&gt; team reviewed the websites of top Federal agencies in the United States for clarity of writing, and found some of the following errors.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Long sentences&lt;\/strong&gt;: Even if they are grammatically correct, long sentences can make your text difficult to understand because they do not flow well. Break up long sentences into several shorter chunks of text for clarity.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Passive voice: &lt;\/strong&gt;Using the passive voice\u2014where the subject does not &lt;em&gt;act,&lt;\/em&gt; but rather is &lt;em&gt;acted upon&lt;\/em&gt; by an outside force\u2014causes confusion in writing. For clarity, make sure that the subject of your sentence is doing the &lt;em&gt;acting&lt;\/em&gt; and that it precedes the action.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Sentence fragments:&lt;\/strong&gt; In order to convey a meaningful idea, sentences require a subject and a verb. The best sentences indicate who or what is doing something, as well as what is being done. For example: &lt;em&gt;I am writing&lt;\/em&gt;.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Squinting modifier: &lt;\/strong&gt;When the meaning of an adjective or an adverb in your sentence is ambiguous, you need to make the sentence clearer. Make sure your modifier is describing the word or phrase before it, or the word or phrase after it.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Unclear antecedent: &lt;\/strong&gt;A pronoun may refer to more than one antecedent, leaving readers unsure of your intended meaning. The best way to fix the problem is to rephrase the sentence in a new way so it is clear which pronoun refers to what.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Redundant Prepositions: &lt;\/strong&gt;Modern English has changed to the point where once-necessary prepositions are now considered unnecessary. (E.g. &lt;em&gt;Where did that report go to?&lt;\/em&gt;) Use of unnecessary prepositions gives a somewhat childish quality to your writing, so you should tighten up your writing as much as possible.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Conjunctions: &lt;\/strong&gt;Conjunctions (e.g. &lt;em&gt;and&lt;\/em&gt;, &lt;em&gt;but&lt;\/em&gt;, &lt;em&gt;if&lt;\/em&gt;) are used to connect words or phrases together in a sentence. Too many conjunctions can cause run-on sentences, so make sure you close separate your sentences into complete thoughts.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Dangling Modifiers: &lt;\/strong&gt;A modifier is considered dangling when it has been \u201cleft hanging\u201d by an identifiable subject or noun. Dangling modifiers can be corrected by providing the modifier with an obvious and appropriate subject, noun, or pronoun.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Split Infinitives: &lt;\/strong&gt;An infinitive combines the root form of a verb with the word \u201cto.\u201d (Examples: &lt;em&gt;To love&lt;\/em&gt;, &lt;em&gt;to read&lt;\/em&gt;, &lt;em&gt;to walk&lt;\/em&gt;, &lt;em&gt;to cry&lt;\/em&gt;, etc.) An infinitive is considered \u201csplit\u201d if a word or phrase comes between the \u201cto\u201d and the verb.&lt;strong&gt;\u00a0&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Redundant Phrases: &lt;\/strong&gt;Repeating the same thing over and over again will not help others to understand your writing. Make sure your message is clear the first time, or explain using different words and phrases.\r\n\r\n&lt;code&gt;To embed this infographic into a blog post, paste the following HTML snippet into your web editor: &lt;textarea style=\"width: 630px; height: 60px;\"&gt;&lt;a href=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/grammar-check\"&gt;&lt;img alt=\"Grammarly: Use Plain English, Please\" src=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/about\/content\/04-press-room\/fedagencies-02.jpg\" width=\"630\" height=\"2983\" \/&gt;&lt;\/a&gt;&lt;\/textarea&gt;&lt;\/code&gt;\r\n\r\nPlease attribute this content to &lt;a href=\"http:\/\/grammarly.com\/grammar-check\"&gt;grammarly.com\/grammar-check&lt;\/a&gt;.","post_title":"Use Plain English, Please","post_excerpt":"","post_status":"publish","comment_status":"open","ping_status":"open","post_password":"","post_name":"use-plain-english-please","to_ping":"","pinged":"","post_modified":"2015-03-10 05:03:03","post_modified_gmt":"2015-03-10 12:03:03","post_content_filtered":"","post_parent":0,"guid":"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/?p=12341","menu_order":0,"post_type":"post","post_mime_type":"","comment_count":"0","filter":"raw"},{"ID":12295,"post_author":"1001005","post_date":"2015-03-09 03:58:17","post_date_gmt":"2015-03-09 10:58:17","post_content":"[media]&lt;img src=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/wp-content\/uploads\/2015\/03\/Blog-post-Happy-Dagger.jpg\" alt=\"O happy dagger! Can you find the apostrophe?\" width=\"600\" height=\"315\" class=\"aligncenter size-full wp-image-12321\" \/&gt;[\/media]\r\n\r\nMost of us know what an apostrophe is. It\u2019s that little punctuation mark you use to &lt;strong&gt;indicate contractions&lt;\/strong&gt;:\r\nCan\u2019t \r\nCould\u2019ve\r\nHasn\u2019t\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Show possession&lt;\/strong&gt;:\r\nSandra\u2019s toothpaste\r\nTyson\u2019s puppy\r\nAbigail\u2019s gift card\r\n\r\nAnd can even sometimes &lt;strong&gt;indicate when something is plural&lt;\/strong&gt;: \r\nCross your T\u2019s and dot your I\u2019s\r\n\r\nHowever, another form of apostrophe is much less common than the uses described above. An apostrophe is also a name for a figure of speech that is often heard in plays and poetry. When the speaker addresses another person, often one who isn\u2019t in the room and even more often is the personification of an idea or theme, this phrase is called an apostrophe. \r\nHere are some examples of apostrophes:\r\n\r\n\"O happy dagger! This is thy sheath; there rust, and let me die.\"\r\n\"O death, where is thy sting? O grave, where is thy victory?\"  \r\n\"Death, be not proud, though some have called thee \/ Mighty and dreadful, for thou art not so.\" \r\n\r\nLooking for more fun facts about language? &lt;a href=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/2014\/knowledge-is-power-using-idioms-to-give-power-to-your-writing\/ \"&gt;Read this post about idioms.&lt;\/a&gt; \r\n","post_title":"When is an apostrophe more than an apostrophe?","post_excerpt":"","post_status":"publish","comment_status":"open","ping_status":"open","post_password":"","post_name":"when-is-an-apostrophe-more-than-an-apostrophe","to_ping":"","pinged":"","post_modified":"2015-03-09 03:58:17","post_modified_gmt":"2015-03-09 10:58:17","post_content_filtered":"","post_parent":0,"guid":"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/?p=12295","menu_order":0,"post_type":"post","post_mime_type":"","comment_count":"0","filter":"raw"},{"ID":12259,"post_author":"6","post_date":"2015-03-05 06:47:23","post_date_gmt":"2015-03-05 14:47:23","post_content":"Sometimes life can be tough. Milne? &lt;\/b&gt;","post_title":"10 Winnie-the-Pooh Quotes that Will Make You Instantly Feel Better About Life","post_excerpt":"","post_status":"publish","comment_status":"open","ping_status":"open","post_password":"","post_name":"10-winnie-the-pooh-quotes-that-will-make-you-feel-better-about-life-instantly","to_ping":"","pinged":"","post_modified":"2015-03-12 06:55:01","post_modified_gmt":"2015-03-12 13:55:01","post_content_filtered":"","post_parent":0,"guid":"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/?p=12259","menu_order":0,"post_type":"post","post_mime_type":"","comment_count":"4","filter":"raw"},{"ID":11265,"post_author":"4","post_date":"2015-03-05 03:00:07","post_date_gmt":"2015-03-05 11:00:07","post_content":"[media]&lt;img class=\"aligncenter size-full wp-image-12195\" src=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/wp-content\/uploads\/2015\/03\/Blog-12345tgfds.jpg\" alt=\"I'm not a very good writer, but I'm an excellent rewriter.&quot; \u2014James Michener\" width=\"600\" height=\"315\" \/&gt;[\/media]\r\n\r\nThe author James Michener said, \"I'm not a very good writer, but I'm an excellent rewriter.\" If you're an aspiring author or someone striving for clarity in your professional or academic writing, you appreciate the methodical march of the rewriting process. Each word in a sentence has a job; cut those that do nothing. These six tips will help you achieve clear and concise writing.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Avoid the Passive Voice&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\n\r\nThe &lt;a href=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/2014\/empower-your-writing-transform-the-passive-voice\/\"&gt;passive voice&lt;\/a&gt; is flaccid. The subject becomes a helpless thing, acted on by outside forces. In a well-written sentence, the verb is powerful and precise, an active tool at the subject's command. For example:\r\n&lt;ul&gt;\r\n\t&lt;li&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Passive: &lt;\/strong&gt;&lt;em&gt;It is believed&lt;\/em&gt; by the\u00a0boxer that the power is there to cause his opponent to fall down.&lt;\/li&gt;\r\n\t&lt;li&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Active: &lt;\/strong&gt;The boxer thinks he has a knock-out punch.&lt;\/li&gt;\r\n&lt;\/ul&gt;\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Trim the Fat from Flabby Phrases&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\n\r\nSome writers use &lt;a href=\"http:\/\/www.quickanddirtytips.com\/education\/grammar\/because-due-to-since-and-as\"&gt;wordy phrases&lt;\/a&gt; when a single word will do.\r\n\r\nUse &lt;strong&gt;&lt;em&gt;because&lt;\/em&gt;&lt;\/strong&gt;\u00a0instead of:\r\n&lt;ul&gt;\r\n\t&lt;li&gt;Due to the fact that&lt;\/li&gt;\r\n\t&lt;li&gt;In light of the fact that&lt;\/li&gt;\r\n\t&lt;li&gt;Owing to the fact that&lt;\/li&gt;\r\n&lt;\/ul&gt;\r\nUse &lt;strong&gt;&lt;em&gt;about&lt;\/em&gt;&lt;\/strong&gt; instead of:\r\n&lt;ul&gt;\r\n\t&lt;li&gt;Concerning the matter of&lt;\/li&gt;\r\n\t&lt;li&gt;In reference to&lt;\/li&gt;\r\n\t&lt;li&gt;With regard to&lt;\/li&gt;\r\n&lt;\/ul&gt;\r\nUse &lt;strong&gt;&lt;em&gt;can&lt;\/em&gt;&lt;\/strong&gt; instead of:\r\n&lt;ul&gt;\r\n\t&lt;li&gt;Is able to&lt;\/li&gt;\r\n\t&lt;li&gt;Is in a position to&lt;\/li&gt;\r\n\t&lt;li&gt;Has the capacity to&lt;\/li&gt;\r\n&lt;\/ul&gt;\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Limit Prepositional Phrases&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\n\r\nUse an apostrophe, not a prepositional phrase, to show possession.\r\n&lt;ul&gt;\r\n\t&lt;li&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Bad:\u00a0&lt;\/strong&gt;It was the opinion &lt;em&gt;of the teacher&lt;\/em&gt; that Susan was ready for third grade.&lt;\/li&gt;\r\n\t&lt;li&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Better:\u00a0&lt;\/strong&gt;In the &lt;em&gt;teacher's opinion&lt;\/em&gt;, Susan was ready for third grade.&lt;\/li&gt;\r\n&lt;\/ul&gt;\r\nDon't use too many &lt;a href=\"http:\/\/www.quickanddirtytips.com\/education\/grammar\/how-to-kick-your-annoying-preposition-habit\"&gt;prepositional phrases&lt;\/a&gt; in a sentence.\r\n&lt;ul&gt;\r\n\t&lt;li&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Bad:\u00a0&lt;\/strong&gt;The ultimate point &lt;em&gt;of&lt;\/em&gt; the discussion &lt;em&gt;with&lt;\/em&gt; all &lt;em&gt;of&lt;\/em&gt; the people was to reach a consensus &lt;em&gt;of&lt;\/em&gt; the group &lt;em&gt;about&lt;\/em&gt; what to do with the leftover funds &lt;em&gt;from&lt;\/em&gt; the fundraiser.&lt;\/li&gt;\r\n\t&lt;li&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Better:\u00a0&lt;\/strong&gt;The group discussed options for the leftover fundraiser money and ultimately reached a decision.&lt;\/li&gt;\r\n&lt;\/ul&gt;\r\n&lt;a href=\"https:\/\/www.flickr.com\/photos\/nics_events\/2349630643\/sizes\/o\/\"&gt;&lt;img class=\"aligncenter size-full wp-image-11267\" src=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/wp-content\/uploads\/2015\/01\/Untitled6.png\" alt=\"Image via Flickr by Nic's events\" width=\"400\" height=\"265\" \/&gt;&lt;\/a&gt;\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Watch for Wordiness&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\n\r\nWordiness is a problem for many writers, whether it's redundancy, stating the obvious, or packing a sentence with excess detail and\u00a0&lt;a href=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/2013\/mistake-of-the-month-unnecessary-modifiers\/\"&gt;unnecessary modifiers&lt;\/a&gt;.\r\n&lt;ul&gt;\r\n\t&lt;li&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Wordy&lt;\/strong&gt;: Imagine &lt;em&gt;in your mind&lt;\/em&gt; what a caveman &lt;em&gt;from an earlier prehistoric time&lt;\/em&gt; must have thought about when he &lt;em&gt;originally&lt;\/em&gt; saw fire &lt;em&gt;for the first time&lt;\/em&gt;.&lt;\/li&gt;\r\n\t&lt;li&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Better&lt;\/strong&gt;: Imagine what prehistoric man thought when he saw fire for the first time.&lt;\/li&gt;\r\n&lt;\/ul&gt;\r\nFor precision and clarity, excise these words from your writing:\r\n&lt;ul&gt;\r\n\t&lt;li&gt;kind of&lt;\/li&gt;\r\n\t&lt;li&gt;sort of&lt;\/li&gt;\r\n\t&lt;li&gt;really&lt;\/li&gt;\r\n\t&lt;li&gt;basically&lt;\/li&gt;\r\n\t&lt;li&gt;actually&lt;\/li&gt;\r\n\t&lt;li&gt;generally&lt;\/li&gt;\r\n\t&lt;li&gt;typically&lt;\/li&gt;\r\n\t&lt;li&gt;for all intents and purposes&lt;\/li&gt;\r\n&lt;\/ul&gt;\r\nEdit your writing for redundant pairs such as:\r\n&lt;ul&gt;\r\n\t&lt;li&gt;true facts&lt;\/li&gt;\r\n\t&lt;li&gt;past history&lt;\/li&gt;\r\n\t&lt;li&gt;free gift&lt;\/li&gt;\r\n\t&lt;li&gt;unexpected surprise&lt;\/li&gt;\r\n\t&lt;li&gt;each individual&lt;\/li&gt;\r\n&lt;\/ul&gt;\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Combine Sentences&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\n\r\nIn many cases, you can combine information from two short sentences into one complex sentence without sacrificing clarity. Sentence variety also improves readability.\r\n&lt;ul&gt;\r\n\t&lt;li&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Wordy&lt;\/strong&gt;: The house next door is occupied by three widowed sisters. They moved here in 1985 with their three dogs and haven't left since.&lt;\/li&gt;\r\n\t&lt;li&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Better&lt;\/strong&gt;: Three widows and their dogs live next door, their home since 1985.&lt;\/li&gt;\r\n&lt;\/ul&gt;\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Avoid Vague Nouns and Noun Strings&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\n\r\nAll-purpose nouns, such as factor, situation, and area, lead to obscure writing.\r\n&lt;ul&gt;\r\n\t&lt;li&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Vague&lt;\/strong&gt;: A college education is an important &lt;em&gt;factor&lt;\/em&gt; in finding a job in the &lt;em&gt;area&lt;\/em&gt; of accounting.&lt;\/li&gt;\r\n\t&lt;li&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Better&lt;\/strong&gt;: For accounting jobs, a college degree is important.&lt;\/li&gt;\r\n&lt;\/ul&gt;\r\nWriters in a specialty niche such as healthcare or technology often slip into jargon, resulting in &lt;a href=\"http:\/\/opinionator.blogs.nytimes.com\/2013\/04\/05\/the-dark-side-of-verbs-as-nouns\/?_r=0&amp;gwh=A658FEF64C830252D96E405448776820&amp;gwt=pay&amp;assetType=opinion\"&gt;noun&lt;\/a&gt; strings that obscure meaning.\r\n&lt;ul&gt;\r\n\t&lt;li&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Vague&lt;\/strong&gt;: The mammography team is working on the &lt;em&gt;radiology technologist radiation protection quality improvement program&lt;\/em&gt;.&lt;\/li&gt;\r\n\t&lt;li&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Better&lt;\/strong&gt;: The mammography team will complete a quality improvement program for protecting radiology technicians from excess radiation.&lt;\/li&gt;\r\n&lt;\/ul&gt;\r\nWriting for clarity requires a ruthless eye for &lt;a href=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/2014\/5-tips-for-editing-your-own-work\/\"&gt;editing your work&lt;\/a&gt;;\u00a0it's helpful to walk away from your composition for a few hours and approach it with new eyes. Then cut the unnecessary, rewrite the unwieldy, and, in the words of Elmore Leonard, \"Try to leave out the parts that people skip.\"\r\n\r\n&lt;b&gt;Do you have any favorite techniques for clearing up your writing?&lt;\/b&gt;","post_title":"Make Your Writing Clearer: 6 Tips for Re-wording Sentences","post_excerpt":"","post_status":"publish","comment_status":"open","ping_status":"open","post_password":"","post_name":"make-your-writing-clearer-6-tips-for-re-wording-sentencesa","to_ping":"","pinged":"","post_modified":"2015-03-05 03:52:59","post_modified_gmt":"2015-03-05 11:52:59","post_content_filtered":"","post_parent":0,"guid":"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/?p=11265","menu_order":0,"post_type":"post","post_mime_type":"","comment_count":"9","filter":"raw"},{"ID":12221,"post_author":"1001005","post_date":"2015-03-04 15:53:50","post_date_gmt":"2015-03-04 23:53:50","post_content":"In honor of &lt;a href=\"http:\/\/www.internationalwomensday.com\/\" target=\"_blank\"&gt;International Women\u2019s Day&lt;\/a&gt;, we rounded up ten of our favorite quotes from women writers about reading.
