class=\"aligncenter size-large wp-image-12245\" \/&gt;[\/media]\r\n","post_title":"Words of wisdom from great women writers","post_excerpt":"","post_status":"publish","comment_status":"open","ping_status":"open","post_password":"","post_name":"words-of-wisdom-from-great-women-writers","to_ping":"","pinged":"","post_modified":"2015-03-05 02:28:28","post_modified_gmt":"2015-03-05 10:28:28","post_content_filtered":"","post_parent":0,"guid":"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/?p=12221","menu_order":0,"post_type":"post","post_mime_type":"","comment_count":"0","filter":"raw"},{"ID":12191,"post_author":"6","post_date":"2015-03-04 04:27:46","post_date_gmt":"2015-03-04 12:27:46","post_content":"&lt;i&gt;&lt;em&gt;&lt;em&gt;&lt;a href=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/wp-content\/uploads\/2015\/03\/mignonFogarty_0340_CreditDavidCalvert.jpg\"&gt;&lt;img class=\" size-medium wp-image-12085 alignleft\" src=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/wp-content\/uploads\/2015\/03\/mignonFogarty_0340_CreditDavidCalvert-272x410.jpg\" alt=\"Mignon Fogarty, Grammar Girl\" width=\"272\" height=\"410\" \/&gt;&lt;\/a&gt;Mignon Fogarty is the founder the Quick and Dirty Tips podcast network, the creator of of the Grammar Girl website (one of Writer's Digest's 101 Best Websites for Writers in 2012, 2013, and 2014), and the creator and host of the Grammar Girl podcast (Best Education Podcast in the 2012 and 2013 Podcast Awards).\u00a0&lt;\/em&gt;&lt;\/em&gt;&lt;\/i&gt;\r\n\r\n&lt;i&gt;The Grammarly team recently chatted with Mignon\u00a0about grammar,\u00a0language, and National Grammar Day (March 4).&lt;\/i&gt;\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;&lt;em&gt;Grammarly:&lt;\/em&gt;\u00a0How did you become such a recognized grammar expert?&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\n\r\n&lt;em&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Grammar Girl:&lt;\/strong&gt;&lt;\/em&gt; I\u2019m not certain how it happened. My first Grammar Girl project was the podcast, but when it launched, it was just a hobby and I was working full time, so I wasn\u2019t watching the traffic closely. Within a few weeks it was #2 in all of podcasting at iTunes and the success took me by surprise. Four months after the podcast launched, the &lt;em&gt;Wall Street Journal&lt;\/em&gt; picked the &lt;a href=\"http:\/\/www.quickanddirtytips.com\/grammar-girl\"&gt;Grammar Girl&lt;\/a&gt; website as their pick of the day, and I started getting book-deal offers and eventually partnered with Macmillan to write Grammar Girl books and to manage and expand the Quick and Dirty Tips network, of which Grammar Girl is a part.\r\n\r\nSince the beginning, my readers and listeners have been wonderfully enthusiastic and supportive. The success of Grammar Girl is all because of them. Early on, I got a lot of e-mail messages from listeners telling me how much they loved the show and that they had shared it with all of their friends.\r\n\r\nI also like to think that I\u2019m recognized as an expert because I thoroughly research every topic I cover. I see a lot of people commenting online about what they think are grammar rules, but they\u2019re going on their memory or what they learned in grade school, and they\u2019re often incorrect. Readers and listeners learn that they can trust that I have looked up the rules and history of a topic and that I\u2019m not just spouting my own opinions. In fact, I so diligently avoid inserting my opinion that at times my book editors have had to remind me that sometimes people actually do want to know what I think.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;&lt;em&gt;Grammarly:&lt;\/em&gt;\u00a0What is your biggest grammar pet peeve?&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\n\r\n&lt;em&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Grammar Girl:\u00a0&lt;\/strong&gt;&lt;\/em&gt;After years of answering people\u2019s questions about grammar, seeing how they struggle, and writing about it, I don\u2019t really have any pet peeves. The more research I do, the more I discover that many hard-and-fast rules are just consensus opinions or suggestions, such as the \u201crules\u201d about &lt;a href=\"http:\/\/www.quickanddirtytips.com\/education\/grammar\/split-infinitives\"&gt;splitting infinitives&lt;\/a&gt; or that using &lt;a href=\"http:\/\/www.quickanddirtytips.com\/education\/grammar\/top-ten-grammar-myths\"&gt;passive voice is always wrong&lt;\/a&gt;. I guess it mildly annoys me when I see &lt;a href=\"http:\/\/www.quickanddirtytips.com\/education\/grammar\/when-should-you-capitalize-words\"&gt;words capitalized&lt;\/a&gt; that shouldn\u2019t be, but I don\u2019t get too worked up about it.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;&lt;span style=\"line-height: 1.5;\"&gt;&lt;em&gt;Grammarly:&lt;\/em&gt;\u00a0Is there a grammar rule you don\u2019t mind bending\/breaking?&lt;\/span&gt;&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\n\r\n&lt;em&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Grammar Girl:\u00a0&lt;\/strong&gt;&lt;\/em&gt;I can\u2019t think of any hard-and-fast rules that I would break, but because the articles on my website are also the scripts to my audio podcasts, I write them in an informal, conversational style. For example, I often start sentences with conjunctions and use contractions. Those things aren\u2019t wrong, but sometimes people think they are, and it is definitely a casual writing style.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;&lt;em&gt;Grammarly:&lt;\/em&gt;\u00a0Oxford Comma, yes or no?&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\n\r\n&lt;em&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Grammar Girl:\u00a0&lt;\/strong&gt;&lt;\/em&gt;My Twitter fan @ravishlydotcom asked me this &lt;a href=\"https:\/\/twitter.com\/grammargirl\/status\/544600885482242048\"&gt;question&lt;\/a&gt; in December. I tend to favor the &lt;a href=\"http:\/\/www.quickanddirtytips.com\/education\/grammar\/serial-comma\"&gt;Oxford comma&lt;\/a&gt; because it makes things more clear and avoids the rare potential ambiguities.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;&lt;em&gt;Grammarly:&lt;\/em&gt;\u00a0Why is good grammar important? Isn't it enough that we all \"kind of\" understand each other?&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\n\r\n&lt;em&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Grammar Girl:\u00a0&lt;\/strong&gt;&lt;\/em&gt;Good grammar has become even more important today than it was ten or fifteen years ago. It\u2019s common to meet people online now, so the quality of your writing has a huge influence on the first impression you make on people. I like to say that instead of \u201cdressing for success,\u201d you need to \u201cwrite for success.\u201d I have an article that my friend Martha Brockenbrough (who founded &lt;a href=\"http:\/\/nationalgrammarday.com\"&gt;National Grammar Day&lt;\/a&gt; in 2008) wrote about the &lt;a href=\"http:\/\/www.quickanddirtytips.com\/education\/grammar\/does-grammar-really-matter\"&gt;importance of good grammar&lt;\/a&gt; and how it can help you stay out of jail, keep your job, and even find love.\r\n&lt;p style=\"text-align: center;\"&gt;&lt;b&gt;Did you find this interesting? Share this post with your friends!&lt;\/b&gt;&lt;\/p&gt;\r\n[media]&lt;a href=\" \"&gt;&lt;img class=\"aligncenter size-large wp-image-12159\" src=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/wp-content\/uploads\/2015\/03\/grammar-girl-670x352.jpg\" alt=\"5 #GrammarDay Questions with Grammar Girl\" width=\"600\" height=\"315\" \/&gt;&lt;\/a&gt;[\/media]\r\n\r\n&lt;hr \/&gt;\r\n\r\n&lt;i&gt;&lt;em&gt;&lt;i&gt;&lt;em&gt;&lt;em&gt;&lt;i&gt;&lt;em&gt;&lt;em&gt;Mignon Fogarty is the author of the New York Times best-seller Grammar Girl's Quick and Dirty Tips for Better Writing and six other books on writing. She was recently appointed to be the Donald W. Reynolds Chair of Media Entrepreneurship in the Reynolds School of Journalism and Advanced Media Studies at the University of Nevada, Reno.&lt;\/em&gt;&lt;\/em&gt;&lt;\/i&gt;&lt;\/em&gt;&lt;\/em&gt;&lt;\/i&gt;&lt;\/em&gt;&lt;\/i&gt;\r\n\r\n&lt;i&gt;&lt;em&gt;Thanks, Mignon, for your time!&lt;\/em&gt;&lt;\/i&gt;\r\n\r\n&lt;i&gt;&lt;em&gt;Curious to know what kind of grammar nerd you are? Take &lt;a href=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/2015\/celebrate-grammar-day-with-this-fun-quiz\/?quiz&amp;utm_source=Blog_org&amp;utm_medium=text_link&amp;utm_content=GrammarGirlQA&amp;utm_campaign=Grammar_Day_2015\"&gt;Grammarly\u2019s quiz&lt;\/a&gt; in honor of National Grammar Day.&lt;\/em&gt;&lt;\/i&gt;","post_title":"Q&amp;A with Grammar Girl, Mignon Fogarty","post_excerpt":"","post_status":"publish","comment_status":"open","ping_status":"open","post_password":"","post_name":"qa-grammar-girl-mignon-fogarty-2","to_ping":"","pinged":"","post_modified":"2015-03-04 04:27:46","post_modified_gmt":"2015-03-04 12:27:46","post_content_filtered":"","post_parent":0,"guid":"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/?p=12191","menu_order":0,"post_type":"post","post_mime_type":"","comment_count":"0","filter":"raw"},{"ID":11857,"post_author":"4","post_date":"2015-03-04 03:00:13","post_date_gmt":"2015-03-04 11:00:13","post_content":"[caption id=\"attachment_11859\" align=\"alignleft\" width=\"328\"]&lt;a href=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/2015\/qa-martha-brockenbrough\/marthab\/\" rel=\"attachment wp-att-11859\"&gt;&lt;img class=\"size-medium wp-image-11859\" src=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/wp-content\/uploads\/2015\/02\/MarthaB-328x410.png\" alt=\"National Grammar Day\" width=\"328\" height=\"410\" \/&gt;&lt;\/a&gt; Founder of National Grammar Day[\/caption]\r\n\r\n&lt;i&gt;Martha Brockenbrough is the founder of National Grammar Day and author of &lt;\/i&gt;&lt;a href=\"http:\/\/martha-brockenbrough.squarespace.com\/the-game-of-love-and-death\/\"&gt;The Game of Love and Death&lt;\/a&gt;, &lt;i&gt;which comes out &lt;span class=\"aBn\" tabindex=\"0\" data-term=\"goog_1331728985\"&gt;&lt;span class=\"aQJ\"&gt;April 28&lt;\/span&gt;&lt;\/span&gt; and has received starred reviews from Kirkus Books and Publishers Weekly. Martha\u00a0recently spoke with the Grammarly team to provide some insight into National Grammar Day and to share her perspective on language.&lt;\/i&gt;\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;&lt;em&gt;Grammarly:&lt;\/em&gt; You established National Grammar Day in 2008. When did you realize that such a holiday was necessary?&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\n\r\n&lt;em&gt;Martha:&lt;\/em&gt; \"Necessary\" might not be the first word I'd choose. Food, water, love, underpants. All of these are necessary things. But I knew National Grammar Day would be a lot of fun. Fun is necessary, too, and as soon as I learned the holiday did not yet exist, I set about creating it. I was inspired by the high school students I was teaching at the time. They needed a bit of help with their grammar, and I wanted to make the learning experience lively and positive. Everyone can probably remember that teacher who made grammar seem difficult or unpleasant. I wanted to show my students the fun they could have with language. They more they knew about how it worked, the more they could do, much in the same way you play better basketball when you know all of the rules.\r\n\r\nSpeaking of rules: Much has been said about the fact that many so-called rules in our language aren't. That's quite true. But it doesn't mean people don't have certain expectations about the grammar we use. Hiring managers, potential dates: People will judge you if your grammar is non-standard, just as they will judge you for wearing a Speedo to a black-tie event (even with a black tie, which would be the worst).\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;G: What is your biggest grammar pet peeve?&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\n\r\nM: I try not to keep too many pet peeves. That said, every time I see \"your\" instead of \"you're,\" my soul shrivels a little more.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;G: Is there a grammar rule you don\u2019t mind bending\/breaking?&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\n\r\nM: There are plenty of so-called \"rules\" that really and truly aren't. It's fine, for example, to begin a sentence with a conjunction. You probably don't want to do this a lot, because it makes your writing sound choppy. But it's perfectly fine style. Same goes for ending a sentence with a preposition.\r\n\r\nAs a novelist, though, I routinely and purposefully bend the language as many ways as I possibly can to create memorable characters who feel authentic. All we have with novels are words, and out of this, we create not only worlds, but all of their inhabitants. Books breathe, in many cases, because of the artful bending of words, punctuation, and expectations. Mark Twain, an absolute genius with language (and a proponent of simplified spelling), depended utterly on making rubber out of rules. Imagine how awful it would be if someone standardized the grammar in Huckleberry Finn. That would be like putting a tasteful blouse on the Venus de Milo.\r\n\r\nAgain, it's about context. If you're applying for a job with the Queen, spray the starch and follow the most formal conventions. If you're doing something else, then do whatever it takes to do it well.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;G: Oxford Comma, yes or no?&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\n\r\nM: It depends. I write for a variety of publications. Some follow Associated Press Style, which is a serial comma killer.\r\n\r\nSome don't. When I write books, for example, I use the Oxford comma.\r\n\r\nIf I were in charge of the world, I suppose I'd urge use of the Oxford comma. It's easy to point out cases where confusion arises without it. My favorite is the one that says, \"We invited the strippers, JFK, and Stalin.\" This is not the same as \"We invited the strippers, JFK and Stalin.\" (For the record, I would pay many folded single dollar bills to watch JFK and Stalin strip together.) What's more, we no longer live in an age where we're communicating via telegraph, so we don't need conserve characters in quite the same way we used to, except on Twitter.\r\n\r\nThat said, the opposite confusion can sometimes arise. Consider this: \"For my sister, an orangutan, and Jerome\u2026\" It's unclear whether the speaker's sister is an orangutan.\r\n\r\nThis is why you have to pay attention to every sentence you write. Communicating what you mean in a way that other people can understand is the goal. (That and inventing time travel so we can all catch that hot Cold War stripper act.)\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;G: Why is good grammar important? Isn't it enough that we all \"kind of\" understand each other?&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\n\r\nM: Tell that to the person who wrote the contract between Rogers Communication and Atlantic Canada. One rogue comma ended up costing Rogers something like $1 million a year. Most of us won't be in a situation like this, but any time you write a letter, a personal ad, a job application, a Facebook status post, or even a tweet, you're putting yourself into the world for all to judge and potentially misunderstand. Just as you wouldn't want to go outside with your pants only \"kind of\" zipped, you want to give yourself the best chance of making whatever connection you seek. It saves all sorts of heartache and embarrassment, not to mention the occasional heap of cash.\r\n&lt;p style=\"text-align: center;\"&gt;&lt;b&gt;Did you find this interesting? Share this post with your friends!&lt;\/b&gt;&lt;\/p&gt;\r\n[media]&lt;img class=\" size-large wp-image-12173 aligncenter\" src=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/wp-content\/uploads\/2015\/03\/grammarMartha-670x352.jpg\" alt=\"5 Questions for the Founder of Grammar Day, Martha Brockenbough\" width=\"600\" height=\"315\" \/&gt;[\/media]\r\n\r\n&lt;hr \/&gt;\r\n\r\n&lt;i&gt;Thank you, Martha! Happy &lt;a href=\"http:\/\/www.quickanddirtytips.com\/national-grammar-day\"&gt;National Grammar Day&lt;\/a&gt;.&lt;\/i&gt;\r\n\r\n&lt;i&gt;&lt;em&gt;Curious to know what kind of grammar nerd you are? Take &lt;a href=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/2015\/celebrate-grammar-day-with-this-fun-quiz\/?quiz&amp;utm_source=Blog_org&amp;utm_medium=text_link&amp;utm_content=MarthaBQA&amp;utm_campaign=Grammar_Day_2015\"&gt;Grammarly's quiz&lt;\/a&gt; in honor of National Grammar Day.&lt;\/em&gt;&lt;\/i&gt;","post_title":"Q&amp;A with Martha Brockenbrough, Founder of National Grammar Day","post_excerpt":"","post_status":"publish","comment_status":"open","ping_status":"open","post_password":"","post_name":"qa-martha-brockenbrough","to_ping":"","pinged":"","post_modified":"2015-03-04 03:33:16","post_modified_gmt":"2015-03-04 11:33:16","post_content_filtered":"","post_parent":0,"guid":"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/?p=11857","menu_order":0,"post_type":"post","post_mime_type":"","comment_count":"1","filter":"raw"},{"ID":12183,"post_author":"1001005","post_date":"2015-03-03 14:50:33","post_date_gmt":"2015-03-03 22:50:33","post_content":"[media]&lt;img src=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/wp-content\/uploads\/2015\/03\/234edxz-670x352.jpg\" alt=\"234edxz\" width=\"670\" height=\"352\" class=\"aligncenter size-large wp-image-12185\" \/&gt;[\/media]\r\n\r\n&lt;b&gt;Do you know the answer? Read on to find out which one is correct! &lt;\/b&gt;\r\n\r\nThere are many (perhaps countless) homophones in the English language. These are words and phrases that sound the same but are spelled differently and have different meanings. Here are three commonly confused homophones worth knowing:\r\n\r\n&lt;b&gt;1. A while vs. awhile&lt;\/b&gt;\r\n&lt;i&gt;A while&lt;\/i&gt; refers to a period of time. In this construction, \u2018while\u2019 acts as a noun.\r\n\"I haven\u2019t read any good books in &lt;i&gt;a while&lt;\/i&gt;.\"\r\n\r\n&lt;i&gt;Awhile&lt;\/i&gt; is an adverb and means for a period of time.\r\n\"I\u2019m going to think about this &lt;i&gt;awhile&lt;\/i&gt;.\"\r\n\r\nIn many cases, you can replace \u2018awhile\u2019 with the prepositional phrase \u2018for a while.\u2019 If you have a hard time remembering the difference between the two, you might want to adopt the prepositional phrase strategy.\r\n\r\n&lt;b&gt;2. Stationary vs. stationery&lt;\/b&gt;\r\n&lt;i&gt;Stationary&lt;\/i&gt; means not moving.\r\n\"Today I\u2019ll ride the &lt;i&gt;stationary&lt;\/i&gt; bike in my living room instead of going for a ride outside.\"\r\n\r\n&lt;i&gt;Stationery&lt;\/i&gt; is writing materials, envelopes, office materials.\r\n\"I bought some nice &lt;i&gt;stationery&lt;\/i&gt; to use for thank you notes.\"\r\n\r\n&lt;b&gt;Discreet vs. discrete&lt;\/b&gt;\r\n&lt;i&gt;Discreet&lt;\/i&gt; means respectful of privacy or secrecy; quiet; diplomatic; inconspicuous.\r\n\"I don\u2019t want anyone to know about my secret, so please be &lt;i&gt;discreet&lt;\/i&gt;.\"\r\n\"The cabin was nestled in a &lt;i&gt;discreet&lt;\/i&gt; corner of the forest.\"\r\n\r\n&lt;i&gt;Discrete&lt;\/i&gt; means individually separate and distinct and is often use in mathematical contexts.\r\n\"The syllabus was broken down into three &lt;i&gt;discrete&lt;\/i&gt; sections.\"\r\n\r\n&lt;b&gt; The correct answer is &lt;i&gt;discreet&lt;\/i&gt;.&lt;\/b&gt; Did you answer correctly? What other words do you hate to see confused?","post_title":"Spelling counts: make sure you use these words correctly","post_excerpt":"","post_status":"publish","comment_status":"open","ping_status":"open","post_password":"","post_name":"spelling-counts-make-sure-you-use-these-words-correctly","to_ping":"","pinged":"","post_modified":"2015-03-04 07:42:13","post_modified_gmt":"2015-03-04 15:42:13","post_content_filtered":"","post_parent":0,"guid":"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/?p=12183","menu_order":0,"post_type":"post","post_mime_type":"","comment_count":"0","filter":"raw"},{"ID":12083,"post_author":"4","post_date":"2015-03-03 05:00:43","post_date_gmt":"2015-03-03 13:00:43","post_content":"&lt;i&gt;&lt;em&gt;&lt;em&gt;&lt;a href=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/wp-content\/uploads\/2015\/03\/mignonFogarty_0340_CreditDavidCalvert.jpg\"&gt;&lt;img class=\" size-medium wp-image-12085 alignleft\" src=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/wp-content\/uploads\/2015\/03\/mignonFogarty_0340_CreditDavidCalvert-272x410.jpg\" alt=\"Mignon Fogarty, Grammar Girl\" width=\"272\" height=\"410\" \/&gt;&lt;\/a&gt;Mignon Fogarty is the founder the Quick and Dirty Tips podcast network, the creator of of the Grammar Girl website (one of Writer's Digest's 101 Best Websites for Writers in 2012, 2013, and 2014), and the creator and host of the Grammar Girl podcast (Best Education Podcast in the 2012 and 2013 Podcast Awards).\u00a0&lt;\/em&gt;&lt;\/em&gt;&lt;\/i&gt;\r\n\r\n&lt;i&gt;The Grammarly team recently chatted with Mignon\u00a0about grammar,\u00a0language, and National Grammar Day (March 4).&lt;\/i&gt;\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;&lt;em&gt;Grammarly:&lt;\/em&gt;\u00a0How did you become such a recognized grammar expert?&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\n\r\n&lt;em&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Grammar Girl:&lt;\/strong&gt;&lt;\/em&gt; I\u2019m not certain how it happened. My first Grammar Girl project was the podcast, but when it launched, it was just a hobby and I was working full time, so I wasn\u2019t watching the traffic closely. Within a few weeks it was #2 in all of podcasting at iTunes and the success took me by surprise. Four months after the podcast launched, the &lt;em&gt;Wall Street Journal&lt;\/em&gt; picked the &lt;a href=\"http:\/\/www.quickanddirtytips.com\/grammar-girl\"&gt;Grammar Girl&lt;\/a&gt; website as their pick of the day, and I started getting book-deal offers and eventually partnered with Macmillan to write Grammar Girl books and to manage and expand the Quick and Dirty Tips network, of which Grammar Girl is a part.\r\n\r\nSince the beginning, my readers and listeners have been wonderfully enthusiastic and supportive. The success of Grammar Girl is all because of them. Early on, I got a lot of e-mail messages from listeners telling me how much they loved the show and that they had shared it with all of their friends.\r\n\r\nI also like to think that I\u2019m recognized as an expert because I thoroughly research every topic I cover. I see a lot of people commenting online about what they think are grammar rules, but they\u2019re going on their memory or what they learned in grade school, and they\u2019re often incorrect. Readers and listeners learn that they can trust that I have looked up the rules and history of a topic and that I\u2019m not just spouting my own opinions. In fact, I so diligently avoid inserting my opinion that at times my book editors have had to remind me that sometimes people actually do want to know what I think.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;&lt;em&gt;Grammarly:&lt;\/em&gt;\u00a0What is your biggest grammar pet peeve?&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\n\r\n&lt;em&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Grammar Girl:\u00a0&lt;\/strong&gt;&lt;\/em&gt;After years of answering people\u2019s questions about grammar, seeing how they struggle, and writing about it, I don\u2019t really have any pet peeves. The more research I do, the more I discover that many hard-and-fast rules are just consensus opinions or suggestions, such as the \u201crules\u201d about &lt;a href=\"http:\/\/www.quickanddirtytips.com\/education\/grammar\/split-infinitives\"&gt;splitting infinitives&lt;\/a&gt; or that using &lt;a href=\"http:\/\/www.quickanddirtytips.com\/education\/grammar\/top-ten-grammar-myths\"&gt;passive voice is always wrong&lt;\/a&gt;. I guess it mildly annoys me when I see &lt;a href=\"http:\/\/www.quickanddirtytips.com\/education\/grammar\/when-should-you-capitalize-words\"&gt;words capitalized&lt;\/a&gt; that shouldn\u2019t be, but I don\u2019t get too worked up about it.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;&lt;span style=\"line-height: 1.5;\"&gt;&lt;em&gt;Grammarly:&lt;\/em&gt;\u00a0Is there a grammar rule you don\u2019t mind bending\/breaking?&lt;\/span&gt;&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\n\r\n&lt;em&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Grammar Girl:\u00a0&lt;\/strong&gt;&lt;\/em&gt;I can\u2019t think of any hard-and-fast rules that I would break, but because the articles on my website are also the scripts to my audio podcasts, I write them in an informal, conversational style. For example, I often start sentences with conjunctions and use contractions. Those things aren\u2019t wrong, but sometimes people think they are, and it is definitely a casual writing style.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;&lt;em&gt;Grammarly:&lt;\/em&gt;\u00a0Oxford Comma, yes or no?&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\n\r\n&lt;em&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Grammar Girl:\u00a0&lt;\/strong&gt;&lt;\/em&gt;My Twitter fan @ravishlydotcom asked me this &lt;a href=\"https:\/\/twitter.com\/grammargirl\/status\/544600885482242048\"&gt;question&lt;\/a&gt; in December. I tend to favor the &lt;a href=\"http:\/\/www.quickanddirtytips.com\/education\/grammar\/serial-comma\"&gt;Oxford comma&lt;\/a&gt; because it makes things more clear and avoids the rare potential ambiguities.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;&lt;em&gt;Grammarly:&lt;\/em&gt;\u00a0Why is good grammar important? Isn't it enough that we all \"kind of\" understand each other?&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\n\r\n&lt;em&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Grammar Girl:\u00a0&lt;\/strong&gt;&lt;\/em&gt;Good grammar has become even more important today than it was ten or fifteen years ago. It\u2019s common to meet people online now, so the quality of your writing has a huge influence on the first impression you make on people. I like to say that instead of \u201cdressing for success,\u201d you need to \u201cwrite for success.\u201d I have an article that my friend Martha Brockenbrough (who founded &lt;a href=\"http:\/\/nationalgrammarday.com\"&gt;National Grammar Day&lt;\/a&gt; in 2008) wrote about the &lt;a href=\"http:\/\/www.quickanddirtytips.com\/education\/grammar\/does-grammar-really-matter\"&gt;importance of good grammar&lt;\/a&gt; and how it can help you stay out of jail, keep your job, and even find love.\r\n&lt;p style=\"text-align: center;\"&gt;&lt;b&gt;Did you find this interesting? She was recently appointed to be the Donald W. Reynolds Chair of Media Entrepreneurship in the Reynolds School of Journalism and Advanced Media Studies at the University of Nevada, Reno.&lt;\/em&gt;&lt;\/em&gt;&lt;\/i&gt;&lt;\/em&gt;&lt;\/em&gt;&lt;\/i&gt;&lt;\/em&gt;&lt;\/i&gt;\r\n\r\n&lt;i&gt;&lt;em&gt;Thanks, Mignon, for your time!&lt;\/em&gt;&lt;\/i&gt;\r\n\r\n&lt;i&gt;&lt;em&gt;Curious to know what kind of grammar nerd you are? Take &lt;a href=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/2015\/celebrate-grammar-day-with-this-fun-quiz\/?quiz&amp;utm_source=Blog_org&amp;utm_medium=text_link&amp;utm_content=GrammarGirlQA&amp;utm_campaign=Grammar_Day_2015\"&gt;Grammarly\u2019s quiz&lt;\/a&gt; in honor of National Grammar Day.&lt;\/em&gt;&lt;\/i&gt;","post_title":"Q&amp;A with Grammar Girl, Mignon Fogarty","post_excerpt":"","post_status":"publish","comment_status":"open","ping_status":"open","post_password":"","post_name":"qa-mignon-fogarty","to_ping":"","pinged":"","post_modified":"2015-03-03 05:34:30","post_modified_gmt":"2015-03-03 13:34:30","post_content_filtered":"","post_parent":0,"guid":"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/?p=12083","menu_order":0,"post_type":"post","post_mime_type":"","comment_count":"0","filter":"raw"},{"ID":11259,"post_author":"4","post_date":"2015-03-03 02:00:51","post_date_gmt":"2015-03-03 10:00:51","post_content":"[media]&lt;a href=\"http:\/\/www.andertoons.com\"&gt;&lt;img class=\"alignleft size-large wp-image-12165\" src=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/wp-content\/uploads\/2015\/03\/cartoon-3432-670x352.jpg\" alt=\"Cartton about email at work by Mark Anderson at andertoons.com\" width=\"600\" height=\"315\" \/&gt;&lt;\/a&gt;[\/media]\r\n\r\nEven though email takes up well &lt;a href=\"http:\/\/mashable.com\/2012\/08\/01\/email-workers-time\/\"&gt;over a quarter&lt;\/a&gt; of the average working person's day, many people still don't have a knack for email etiquette. Often, the issue lies in separating personal email preferences from professional communication policies. Follow these tips for maintaining email etiquette at work, and you'll develop a more effective communication strategy in no time.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Use a Clear Subject Line&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\n\r\nThe subject line of your email is your first and only chance to make a good impression on your recipient. When you draw your subject out into a rambling prologue, you'll give the recipient the sense that your message is similarly lengthy and poorly structured. When you opt for short, single word subject lines, however, you run the risk of being too vague and not offering enough information.\r\n\r\nIn a professional setting, it's best to keep your subject lines &lt;a href=\"http:\/\/www.businessinsider.com\/email-etiquette-rules-everyone-should-know-2014-9\"&gt;clear and concise&lt;\/a&gt;. Tell your recipients what they can expect to read in your email without crossing the line into needless detail or flowery language.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;CC or BCC for Organization and Confidentiality&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\n\r\n&lt;a href=\"https:\/\/www.flickr.com\/photos\/rahulrodriguez\/9162677329\/sizes\/m\/\"&gt;&lt;img class=\"aligncenter size-medium wp-image-11261\" src=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/wp-content\/uploads\/2015\/01\/Untitled51-410x410.png\" alt=\"email, work, professional, Grammarly\" width=\"410\" height=\"410\" \/&gt;&lt;\/a&gt;\r\n\r\nCarbon copy (CC) and blind carbon copy (BCC) features offer helpful options for keeping your emails organized and confidential. Though you often carry on email-based conversations with a few key coworkers, you may find that you need to bring other colleagues into the picture, too. Opt for CC when including a colleague on an email for informational purposes only, with no expectation of a reply.\r\n\r\nSince a BCC essentially makes its recipient's email address invisible to everyone except the sender and the BCC recipient, this feature is best used for confidentiality purposes. When you need to include a colleague or a client in a conversation without alerting the other \"To\" recipients, opt for BCC. This feature is also helpful when emailing a list of business contacts whose email addresses must remain confidential.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Reply as Quickly as Possible&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\n\r\nHave you ever read an email and set it aside, only to reply a week or more later? Always reply to emails as quickly as you can, and never make your recipient wait for a reply for &lt;a href=\"http:\/\/www.lifehack.org\/articles\/work\/27-email-etiquette-tips-for-professionals.html\"&gt;more than 48 hours&lt;\/a&gt;. Remember that time is money, so when you delay your replies, you're only costing yourself and your recipient.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Choose Appropriate Language&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\n\r\nAs a general rule, your professional emails should not read the same way that your personal ones do. In professional emails, &lt;a href=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/2014\/conquering-email-in-the-workplace\/\"&gt;avoid trendy language&lt;\/a&gt;, overly casual phrases, and emoticons.\r\n\r\nDon't take it so far, however, that your messages come across as stuffy or too lofty. Also, when you choose your wording, consider your office culture. If your coworkers keep things casual, you can probably feel free to tone down your overly professional email.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Choose an Appropriate Signoff&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\n\r\nEmail users and experts eternally debate about the best words and phrases to use when &lt;a href=\"http:\/\/www.huffingtonpost.com\/grammarly\/email-signoff-etiquette_b_4809863.html\"&gt;closing an email&lt;\/a&gt;. A friendly \"cheers\" may work in personal emails, but it comes across as too casual in most professional settings. Signing off with a simple \"thanks\" may work in many situations, but it's not suitable when your message doesn't actually suggest gratitude. Make sure your signoff fits the occasion, given your message and your audience.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Call When Necessary&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\n\r\nSometimes email just isn't the right mode of communication, and it's best to tackle the business at hand with a phone call or an in-person meeting instead. When your communication gets too complex or personal, eliminating email may be helpful.\r\n\r\nThis rule of thumb is particularly true when &lt;a href=\"http:\/\/quickbase.intuit.com\/blog\/2012\/12\/03\/how-to-resolve-a-conflict-with-a-colleague\/\"&gt;issues with coworkers&lt;\/a&gt; arise. Since miscommunication can happen with email despite your best efforts, know when to take your conversations offline.\r\n\r\nMaintaining email etiquette at work can mean the difference between miscommunicating and getting the job done efficiently. What's your email pet peeve?\"","post_title":"Navigating Email Etiquette at Work","post_excerpt":"","post_status":"publish","comment_status":"open","ping_status":"open","post_password":"","post_name":"navigating-email-etiquette-at-work","to_ping":"","pinged":"","post_modified":"2015-03-03 04:20:13","post_modified_gmt":"2015-03-03 12:20:13","post_content_filtered":"","post_parent":0,"guid":"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/?p=11259","menu_order":0,"post_type":"post","post_mime_type":"","comment_count":"1","filter":"raw"},{"ID":11871,"post_author":"6","post_date":"2015-03-01 02:00:20","post_date_gmt":"2015-03-01 10:00:20","post_content":"&lt;script src=\"\/\/cdn.playbuzz.com\/widget\/feed.js\" type=\"text\/javascript\"&gt;&lt;\/script&gt;\r\n&lt;div class=\"pb_feed\" data-height=\"auto\" data-game=\"\/grammarly10\/what-kind-of-grammar-nerd-are-you\" data-tags=\"All\" data-recommend=\"false\" data-margin-top=\"0\" data-game-info=\"true\" data-comments=\"false\" data-shares=\"true\" data-embed-by=\"9661cd1e-019d-4243-ae75-a84f05aa29ab\" data-key=\"Default\"&gt;&lt;\/div&gt;\r\n&lt;code&gt;\r\nTo share this quiz with your readers, embed this in your blog post by pasting the following HTML snippet into your web editor:\r\n&lt;textarea style=\"width: 630px; height: 60px;\"&gt;&lt;a href=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/2015\/celebrate-grammar-day-with-this-fun-quiz\/?quiz&amp;utm_source=Blog_Share&amp;utm_medium=embed_code_link&amp;utm_campaign=Grammar_Day_2015\"&gt;&lt;img alt=\"Grammarly Grammar Nerd Quiz Feature Image\" src=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/wp-content\/uploads\/2015\/02\/Quiz_outreach_sample_image_-600x-516x410.png\" width=\"600\" height=\"477\" \/&gt;&lt;\/a&gt;&lt;\/textarea&gt;\r\n&lt;\/code&gt;\r\n&lt;b&gt;Please attribute this content to &lt;a href=\"http:\/\/grammarly.com\/grammar-check\"&gt;grammarly.com\/grammar-check&lt;\/a&gt;.&lt;\/b&gt;","post_title":"Celebrate Grammar Day with this fun quiz!","post_excerpt":"Grammar lovers come in all shapes and sizes\u2014and with different opinions on which approach to grammar is correct. So, you think you are passionate about grammar? Take this quiz to find out what kind of grammarian you truly are.","post_status":"publish","comment_status":"open","ping_status":"open","post_password":"","post_name":"celebrate-grammar-day-with-this-fun-quiz","to_ping":"","pinged":"","post_modified":"2015-03-03 03:20:42","post_modified_gmt":"2015-03-03 11:20:42","post_content_filtered":"","post_parent":0,"guid":"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/?p=11871","menu_order":0,"post_type":"post","post_mime_type":"","comment_count":"8","filter":"raw"},{"ID":12013,"post_author":"1001005","post_date":"2015-02-27 11:07:15","post_date_gmt":"2015-02-27 19:07:15","post_content":"[media]&lt;img src=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/wp-content\/uploads\/2015\/02\/Blog-peek-peak-670x352.jpg\" alt=\"peak pique peek\" width=\"670\" height=\"352\" class=\"aligncenter size-large wp-image-12017\" \/&gt;[\/media]\r\n\r\nTake a sneak peek at the correct ways to spell and use peak, peek, and pique.\r\n\r\n&lt;b&gt;Peak &lt;\/b&gt;means to reach a highest degree or maximum; to rise or extend into a peak or point; to form, or appear as, a peak:\r\nHe has reached the &lt;b&gt;peak&lt;\/b&gt; of his career.\r\nThe hiker\u2019s goal was to reach the mountain\u2019s &lt;b&gt;peak&lt;\/b&gt; on the third day of her journey.\r\n\r\n&lt;b&gt;Peek&lt;\/b&gt; means to look slyly, or with the eyes half closed, or through a crevice; to peep; to be only slightly, partially visible, as if peering out from a hiding place:\r\nThe gift is a surprise, so don\u2019t &lt;b&gt;peek&lt;\/b&gt;!\r\n\r\nWhen writing the phrase \u2018sneak peek,\u2019 people often mistakenly use the word \u2018peak\u2019 instead of \u2018peek.\u2019 It\u2019s easy to understand why this happens, because the spelling of \u2018peak\u2019 is similar to \u2018sneak,\u2019 but \u2018peek\u2019 is the correct word to use. Keep those sly mountaintops out of your writing and be sure you use \u2018sneak peek\u2019 correctly!\r\n\r\n&lt;b&gt;Pique&lt;\/b&gt; is most commonly used as a verb, meaning to stimulate interest of curiosity:\r\nThe article about global warming &lt;b&gt;piqued&lt;\/b&gt; my interest.\r\nIn an attempt to &lt;b&gt;pique&lt;\/b&gt; your curiosity about puppy adoption, I\u2019ll show you this cute video.\r\n\r\nPique also means a feeling of irritation or resentment resulting from a slight, especially to one's pride or to feel irritated or resentful. However, it\u2019s most frequently confused with \u2018peek\u2019 and \u2018peak\u2019 when used in the first way.\r\n\r\nTo learn more about confusing word pairs, &lt;a href=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/2014\/three-word-pairs-that-confuse-many-writers\/\"&gt;read this blog post about other words that writers often confuse&lt;\/a&gt;. ","post_title":"Take a sneak peek at the correct ways to spell and use these words.","post_excerpt":"","post_status":"publish","comment_status":"open","ping_status":"open","post_password":"","post_name":"take-a-sneak-peek-at-the-correct-ways-to-spell-and-use-these-words","to_ping":"","pinged":"","post_modified":"2015-02-27 11:07:15","post_modified_gmt":"2015-02-27 19:07:15","post_content_filtered":"","post_parent":0,"guid":"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/?p=12013","menu_order":0,"post_type":"post","post_mime_type":"","comment_count":"0","filter":"raw"},{"ID":11209,"post_author":"4","post_date":"2015-02-27 08:00:39","post_date_gmt":"2015-02-27 16:00:39","post_content":"[media]&lt;img src=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/wp-content\/uploads\/2015\/02\/calvin-essay-writing-670x352.jpg\" alt=\"Calvin and Hobbes Cartoon Essay Writing\" width=\"600\" height=\"315\" class=\"aligncenter size-large wp-image-11967\" \/&gt;[\/media]\r\n\r\nDo you want to write a five-paragraph &lt;a href=\"http:\/\/opinionator.blogs.nytimes.com\/2014\/05\/05\/the-perfect-essay\/?_r=0\"&gt;essay that makes your mama proud&lt;\/a&gt;? In case you missed it, here's the secret ingredient: structure. These seven tips will help you formulate the perfect five-paragraph essay.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Start With an Outline&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\n\r\nMapping out your essay before you begin writing helps you stay on point. Start by jotting down the following subheads, inserting ideas and research as you see fit.\r\n&lt;ul&gt;\r\n\t&lt;li&gt;Introductory Paragraph\/Thesis Statement&lt;\/li&gt;\r\n\t&lt;li&gt;First Paragraph&lt;\/li&gt;\r\n\t&lt;li&gt;Second Paragraph&lt;\/li&gt;\r\n\t&lt;li&gt;Third Paragraph&lt;\/li&gt;\r\n\t&lt;li&gt;Conclusion&lt;\/li&gt;\r\n&lt;\/ul&gt;\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Get to the Point\u00a0&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\n\r\n&lt;a href=\"https:\/\/www.flickr.com\/photos\/mrsdkrebs\/6812988187\/sizes\/m\/\"&gt;&lt;img class=\"aligncenter size-full wp-image-11211\" src=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/wp-content\/uploads\/2015\/01\/Untitled32.png\" alt=\"essay, five paragraphs, writing, students, Grammarly\" width=\"500\" height=\"375\" \/&gt;&lt;\/a&gt;\r\n\r\nIn your first few sentences, &lt;a href=\"http:\/\/grissomsgrammar.blogspot.com\/2008\/01\/eight-introduction-techniques-that-hook.html\"&gt;hook your reader&lt;\/a&gt; by telling her what she can expect to learn. Let's say you're writing about the perks of starting a cake decorating business. Explain your essay's focus in the introductory paragraph.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Good:&lt;\/strong&gt; \"Are you a culinary artist who dreams of having her cake and eating it, too? If so, a career in cake decorating could be right for you.\"\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Bad:&lt;\/strong&gt; \"First, find a recipe. Next, bake your cake. Now it's time to decorate. That's where the fun begins.\"\r\n\r\nThe second example beats around the bush too much. If you don't zero in on your main idea right away, you run the risk of &lt;a href=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/2014\/essay-writing-mistakes-the-3-ss-and-how-to-correct-them\/\"&gt;confusing your reader&lt;\/a&gt;.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Forecast Your Arguments in a Thesis Statement&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\n\r\nConclude your introductory paragraph with a thesis statement that ties your essay's three forthcoming arguments together.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Good:&lt;\/strong&gt; \"Owning your own cake decorating business can lead to artistic satisfaction, job security, and personal freedom.\"\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Bad:&lt;\/strong&gt; \"Owning your own cake decorating business is a great idea.\"\r\n\r\nThe second example is too vague. A quality thesis statement provides a concrete list of facts or ideas that your reader will internalize by the end of the essay.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Present Your Best Argument First&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\n\r\nPresent your best argument in the second paragraph. In this example, exactly why should your reader consider a career in cake decorating?\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Good:&lt;\/strong&gt; \"Decorators enjoy the artistic satisfaction of creating beautiful cakes for special occasions.\"\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Bad:&lt;\/strong&gt; \"People like frosting cakes.\"\r\n\r\nOur vague second example tells the reader something she already knows. Don't bore her with the obvious. Give her a compelling reason to jump into cake decorating.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Continue Your Arguments in Paragraphs Three and Four&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\n\r\nA five-paragraph essay cannot stand on one argument alone. The third and fourth paragraphs should continue your pro-decorating sentiment.\r\n\r\nRecall that the example thesis mentioned three cake-decorating perks: artistic satisfaction, job security, and personal freedom.\r\n\r\nAddress job security and personal freedom in paragraphs three and four. How does owning your own business provide job security? What are the benefits of being your own boss? If you're not sure, brainstorm ideas via a Google search. Play with search terms such as \"cake decorator,\" \"owning your own business,\" and \"personal freedom entrepreneur.\"\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Wrap Up Your Arguments&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\n\r\nWrap up your arguments by restating them in your concluding\u00a0paragraph. It's okay to repeat some of the same language you used in your introductory paragraph. Your conclusion is your last chance to persuade the reader, but make sure you don't introduce any new ideas.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Rest, then Review with Fresh Eyes&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\n\r\nOnce you've finished your essay, put it away for a while. You'll return to it with fresh eyes, and you'll undoubtedly notice things you could change. It's perfectly normal to draft three, five, or even ten versions of an essay before you're completely satisfied.\r\n\r\nIf you don't have time to set your piece aside, ask a friend to &lt;a href=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/2014\/the-editing-process-how-to-get-started\/\"&gt;edit it&lt;\/a&gt;. You might not agree with your friend's critique, but your eyes will open\u00a0to a reader's point of view. Running an online &lt;a href=\"http:\/\/www.quickanddirtytips.com\/education\/grammar\/what-is-plagiarism\"&gt;plagiarism check&lt;\/a&gt; is also smart.\r\n\r\nAll good writing adheres to a structure. When you write a five-paragraph essay, that structure is fixed and specific. However, the product you create within that structure is uniquely yours. What will your next five-paragraph essay be about?","post_title":"7 Tips for Formulating the Perfect Five-Paragraph Essay.","post_excerpt":"","post_status":"publish","comment_status":"open","ping_status":"open","post_password":"","post_name":"7-tips-for-formulating-the-perfect-five-paragraph-essay","to_ping":"","pinged":"","post_modified":"2015-02-27 08:03:25","post_modified_gmt":"2015-02-27 16:03:25","post_content_filtered":"","post_parent":0,"guid":"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/?p=11209","menu_order":0,"post_type":"post","post_mime_type":"","comment_count":"6","filter":"raw"},{"ID":11759,"post_author":"1001005","post_date":"2015-02-26 14:31:54","post_date_gmt":"2015-02-26 22:31:54","post_content":"[media]&lt;img src=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/wp-content\/uploads\/2015\/02\/23erfd-copy-670x352.jpg\" alt=\"nothing lasts forever apostrophe\" width=\"670\" height=\"352\" class=\"aligncenter size-large wp-image-11961\" \/&gt;[\/media]\r\n\r\nApostrophes seem to pop up in surprising places. If you\u2019re learning English as a second language, or even if you\u2019re a lifelong English speaker, it\u2019s a good idea to review the rules of apostrophe usage. The rules are relatively simple, and once you learn them you\u2019ll never wonder again if you should or shouldn\u2019t use an apostrophe.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;DO use apostrophes to show possession.&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\nWhen you\u2019re showing possession, use \u201c \u2019S \u201d after a noun. For example:\r\n&lt;i&gt;The ring belonging to Jacob \u2192 Jacob\u2019s ring\r\nThe car that Sheryl owns \u2192 Sheryl\u2019s car&lt;\/i&gt;\r\n\r\nIf the noun ends with an S, add the apostrophe after the S but don\u2019t add an additional S.\r\n&lt;i&gt;The shirt that belongs to Ross \u2192 Ross\u2019 shirt\r\nThe computer that the twins own \u2192 The twins\u2019 computer&lt;\/i&gt;\r\n\r\nIf the noun is plural, add the apostrophe after the pluralized noun if ending in S. No added S is needed. However, if the plural is irregular, you add an     \u201c \u2019S. \u201d\r\n&lt;i&gt;The costumes belonging to the actors \u2192 the actors\u2019 costumes (regular plural)\r\nThe laughter coming from children\u2192 the children\u2019s laughter (irregular plural)&lt;\/i&gt;\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;In general, DON\u2019T use apostrophes to make a word plural.&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\nPeople often mistakenly use apostrophes to make singular words plural. This is incorrect! \u201c \u2019S \u201d conveys possession and will indicate possession, not pluralization, to the reader. For example:\r\n&lt;i&gt;I bought apple\u2019s, banana\u2019s, and carrot\u2019s at the store.&lt;\/i&gt;\r\nThis is incorrect. No apostrophes are needed! Here\u2019s the correctly written sentence:\r\n&lt;i&gt;I bought apples, bananas, and carrots at the store.&lt;\/i&gt;\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Exceptions: &lt;\/strong&gt;\r\nAs with many rules in the English language, there are exceptions. The exception in this case, however, is extremely logical. If the plural of the term without the apostrophe could be confused with another word, then you should use an apostrophe to clarify that it is a plural and not the other word. Consider the following: \r\n\r\n&lt;i&gt;A\u2019s without the apostrophe would be confused for \u201cas.\u201d&lt;\/i&gt;\r\nWhen writing letters, if the standard plural form (adding S) creates a word that looks like a different word\u2014e.g. Is\u2014it is 100% OK to use an apostrophe. \u201cDot your I\u2019s!\u201d \r\n\r\n&lt;i&gt;Do\u2019s without the apostrophe would be confused for \u201cdos.\u201d&lt;\/i&gt;\r\nYou\u2019ll see an example of this in the blog title: Do\u2019s and don\u2019ts. The apostrophe in \u201cdo\u2019s\u201d is acceptable because it could be misread as the Spanish word \u201cdos\u201d without the apostrophe or as short form for the early operating system, MS-DOS. \u201cDon\u2019ts,\u201d however, does not need an apostrophe before the S to make it plural.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;DO use apostrophes to show that a letter has been removed.&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\nWhen you combine two words into one, you often use an apostrophe in place of the omitted letters. This is called a contraction. For example:\r\n&lt;i&gt;Do + not = Don\u2019t \r\nThey + are = They\u2019re\r\nIt + is\/has = It\u2019s &lt;\/i&gt;\r\nThe exception to this rule is won\u2019t, which is the contraction of will + not and has a stem change.","post_title":"Follow these guidelines to avoid an #apostrofail.","post_excerpt":"","post_status":"publish","comment_status":"open","ping_status":"open","post_password":"","post_name":"follow-these-guidelines-to-avoid-an-apostrofail","to_ping":"","pinged":"","post_modified":"2015-02-26 14:31:54","post_modified_gmt":"2015-02-26 22:31:54","post_content_filtered":"","post_parent":0,"guid":"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/?p=11759","menu_order":0,"post_type":"post","post_mime_type":"","comment_count":"1","filter":"raw"},{"ID":11251,"post_author":"4","post_date":"2015-02-25 08:00:32","post_date_gmt":"2015-02-25 16:00:32","post_content":"[media]&lt;img class=\"alignleft size-full wp-image-11841\" src=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/wp-content\/uploads\/2015\/02\/Fairy-tales-intelligent1.jpg\" alt=\"&quot;If you want your children to be intelligent, read them fairy tales. If you want them to be very intelligent, read them more fairy tales.&quot; \u2014 Albert Einstein\" width=\"600\" height=\"315\" \/&gt;[\/media]\r\n\r\nEvery year on February 26th, Tell a Fairy Tale Day celebrates the art of storytelling. Though you may not read traditional fairy tales very often any more, chances are that many of the story threads throughout your favorite books and movies have their roots in fairy tales. Here are six ways to have fun with storytelling on Tell a Fairy Tale Day.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Take a Trip to Your Local Library&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\n\r\n&lt;a href=\"https:\/\/www.flickr.com\/photos\/missindipop\/3389501692\/sizes\/m\/\"&gt;&lt;img class=\" size-medium wp-image-11253 alignleft\" src=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/wp-content\/uploads\/2015\/01\/Untitled41-273x410.png\" alt=\"fairy tale, reading, Grammarly\" width=\"273\" height=\"410\" \/&gt;&lt;\/a&gt;\r\n\r\nIf it's been a while since you've visited the library, make a trip to your local branch. Take the kids and settle in for story time with some real hardback books for an hour or two. Pick out your favorite &lt;a href=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/2014\/happily-ever-after-or-not\/\"&gt;classic fairy tales&lt;\/a&gt;, and start a conversation about how these timeless stories have influenced the movies and books that you love.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Make Fairy Tale Dice&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\n\r\nFor an easy craft project that the whole family will love, try &lt;a href=\"http:\/\/www.eriesprout.com\/Articles\/2014\/February\/Fairy-Tale-Day-Activities.html\"&gt;fairy tale dice&lt;\/a&gt;. Print out a template, cut out the dice, and even color them in if you like. Take turns rolling the dice, and do your best to weave an interesting story from the pictures that appear. Reference some of the stories you know, or if you find the classic fairy tales too &lt;a href=\"http:\/\/parenting.blogs.nytimes.com\/2011\/11\/28\/are-fairy-tales-really-for-children\/\"&gt;dark and outdated&lt;\/a&gt;, create an upbeat and modern tale instead. See how imaginative you can get with these basic prompts, and have fun telling a story.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Illustrate Your Own Tale&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\n\r\nIf your kids can't get enough of visual activities, illustrate a tale of your own together. Register for a free account on a &lt;a href=\"http:\/\/storybird.com\/\"&gt;storytelling website&lt;\/a&gt;, and get creative with the built-in artwork. Even though you don't have to draw your own pictures from scratch, you can still let your artistic side shine. Layer as many background images, character pictures, and story elements as you want to create something that's uniquely your own. You can invent a completely new story or collaborate with the kids to tell your version of a classic tale.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Write an Inventive Newsletter&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\n\r\nIf you have future journalists in your midst, get together to compose a fairy tale newspaper.\u00a0Find a template online and insert a variety of news pieces along with updates on some of the classic stories and characters. Has Goldilocks turned into a foodie since her porridge days? Is the Big Bad Wolf still up to no good? This project can be as funny or as serious as you want it to be.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Act It Out&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\n\r\nIf your kids prefer to tell stories through movement, there's no end to the fun you can have by acting out fairy tales. Whether they have acting chops or prefer a directorial role, compose a simple play based on a traditional story and act it out together. If a &lt;a href=\"http:\/\/www.earlylearninghq.org.uk\/stories\/\"&gt;shadow puppet&lt;\/a&gt;\u00a0play is more your style, download forms and puppet shapes for help with the setup, and get creative with the acting.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Eat a Fairy Tale Snack&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\n\r\nFood can serve as an excellent storytelling medium, too. Think about the visual elements from your favorite tales, and try to &lt;a href=\"http:\/\/thewinekat.com\/2012\/09\/10\/fairytale-foods-and-storybook-snacks\/\"&gt;represent these in snack form&lt;\/a&gt;. For the Three Little Pigs, find foods that can stand in for their construction materials, like pretzels for straw or fruit snacks for bricks. If you're feeling really ambitious, try to build the candy-themed house from Hansel &amp; Gretel.\r\n\r\nEven though these stories\u00a0can be casual and free form, the &lt;a href=\"http:\/\/www.quickanddirtytips.com\/education\/grammar\/the-brothers-grimm-rockstar-linguists\"&gt;Brothers Grimm&lt;\/a&gt;, whom we generally consider\u00a0the original fairy tale weavers, were actually serious wordsmiths. This may come as no surprise, given how their tales continue to influence even the contemporary stories that we tell today. Which fairy tale best tells the story of your life?","post_title":"6 Ways to Celebrate Tell a Fairy Tale Day","post_excerpt":"","post_status":"publish","comment_status":"open","ping_status":"open","post_password":"","post_name":"6-ways-to-celebrate-tell-a-fairy-tale-day","to_ping":"","pinged":"","post_modified":"2015-02-26 03:56:55","post_modified_gmt":"2015-02-26 11:56:55","post_content_filtered":"","post_parent":0,"guid":"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/?p=11251","menu_order":0,"post_type":"post","post_mime_type":"","comment_count":"5","filter":"raw"},{"ID":11701,"post_author":"1001005","post_date":"2015-02-25 05:23:21","post_date_gmt":"2015-02-25 13:23:21","post_content":"[media]&lt;img src=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/wp-content\/uploads\/2015\/02\/Blog-1344exe-670x352.jpg\" alt=\"Don&#039;t be &quot;two tensed&quot;!\" width=\"670\" height=\"352\" class=\"alignleft size-large wp-image-11847\" \/&gt;[\/media]\r\n\r\nWhen it comes to writing, there\u2019s no magic suggestion that will make your writing perfect. Good writing takes work, but it also takes organization. Here are a few organizational tips to help improve your writing:\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;1. Keep a schedule&lt;\/strong&gt; \r\nSome of &lt;a href=\"http:\/\/www.brainpickings.org\/2012\/11\/20\/daily-routines-writers\/\" target=\"_blank\"&gt;the best writers&lt;\/a&gt; keep to a strict writing schedule, so why shouldn\u2019t you? Establish a time and place to write every day and do your best to stick to your schedule. You might not always feel like writing during your daily writing \u201cappointment,\u201d but writing when you don\u2019t think you can is one of the best ways to overcome writer\u2019s block and possibly come up with some creative new ideas.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;2. Use an outline&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\nUsing an outline might give you flashbacks of essay writing in high school, but there\u2019s a reason those essays were so well written. Outlines help give structure to your writing. They help you see where you\u2019re going, anticipate roadblocks up ahead, and envision the entire novel, blog post, or short story as a whole. There are lots of free writing outline templates available online. &lt;a href=\"http:\/\/selfpublishingteam.com\/6-writing-outline-templates-and-3-reasons-to-use-them\/\" target=\"_blank\"&gt;Here\u2019s one to get you started&lt;\/a&gt;.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;3. Check your tenses&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\nOf course, you can check your verb tenses when you proofread for other spelling mistakes and grammatical errors. But there\u2019s a case to be made for checking your verb tenses first, or as you go through your writing, to help you stay organized and provide clarity along the way. If your writing is a mix of past- and present-tense verbs, reading back through your work is going to feel like jolting around in a time machine. If you check your verb tenses every few paragraphs, you\u2019ll be able to stay in the correct tense and have a much easier time editing your work later.\r\n\r\nAre you a writer?
