Puns about cats, puns about food, puns about science \u2014 we love them all! In honor of the final episode of Parks and Recreation, we decided to round up a few of our favorite puns from one of the show\u2019s punniest characters, Ben Wyatt. \r\n\r\n[media]&lt;img src=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/wp-content\/uploads\/2015\/02\/Grammarly_Parks-and-Rec_Municipal-Bond.png\" alt=\"Grammarly_Parks and Rec_Municipal Bond\" width=\"500\" height=\"320\" class=\"aligncenter size-full wp-image-11733\" \/&gt; [\/media]\r\n\r\n[media]&lt;img src=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/wp-content\/uploads\/2015\/02\/Grammarly_Parks-and-Rec_Calculater.png\" alt=\"Grammarly_Parks and Rec_Calculater\" width=\"500\" height=\"320\" class=\"aligncenter size-large wp-image-11735\" \/&gt; [\/media]\r\n\r\n[media]&lt;img src=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/wp-content\/uploads\/2015\/02\/Grammarly_Parks-and-Rec_Account.png\" alt=\"Grammarly_Parks and Rec_Account\" width=\"500\" height=\"320\" class=\"aligncenter size-large wp-image-11737\" \/&gt;[\/media]\r\n\r\n[media]&lt;img src=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/wp-content\/uploads\/2015\/02\/Grammarly_Parks-and-Rec_Moolah.png\" alt=\"Grammarly_Parks and Rec_Moolah\" width=\"500\" height=\"320\" class=\"aligncenter size-large wp-image-11739\" \/&gt;[\/media]","post_title":"The thing we'll miss most about Parks and Recreation: Ben Wyatt's punny humor.","post_excerpt":"","post_status":"publish","comment_status":"open","ping_status":"open","post_password":"","post_name":"the-thing-well-miss-most-about-parks-and-recreation-ben-wyatts-punny-humor","to_ping":"","pinged":"","post_modified":"2015-02-24 06:56:51","post_modified_gmt":"2015-02-24 14:56:51","post_content_filtered":"","post_parent":0,"guid":"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/?p=11731","menu_order":0,"post_type":"post","post_mime_type":"","comment_count":"0","filter":"raw"},{"ID":11505,"post_author":"1001005","post_date":"2015-02-19 12:32:00","post_date_gmt":"2015-02-19 20:32:00","post_content":"[media]&lt;img src=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/wp-content\/uploads\/2015\/02\/FunnyPhrases-ideal-_idea-670x352.jpg\" alt=\"the difference between idea and ideal\" width=\"670\" height=\"352\" class=\"aligncenter size-large wp-image-11691\" \/&gt;[\/media]\r\n\r\nAn &lt;strong&gt;idea&lt;\/strong&gt; is any result of mental activity; a thought, a notion; a way of thinking; an image of an object that is formed in the mind or recalled by the memory. For example:\r\nIt was his &lt;strong&gt;idea&lt;\/strong&gt; to enter the race.\r\nShe turned her &lt;strong&gt;idea&lt;\/strong&gt; of owning her own business into a reality.\r\n\r\nAs a modifier, &lt;strong&gt;ideal&lt;\/strong&gt; means optimal; being the best possibility. For example:\r\nIn an &lt;strong&gt;ideal&lt;\/strong&gt; scenario, everyone would do what they enjoy the most.\r\n\r\nAs a noun, an &lt;strong&gt;ideal&lt;\/strong&gt; is a perfect standard of beauty, intellect etc., or a standard of excellence at which to aim. For example:\r\nI want to be just like him. He is my &lt;strong&gt;ideal&lt;\/strong&gt;.\r\nGood character is important to me; I have high &lt;strong&gt;ideals&lt;\/strong&gt;. \r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Imply&lt;\/strong&gt; means to have as a necessary consequence; to suggest by logical inference; to hint, insinuate, or suggest tacitly and avoid a direct statement. For example:\r\nSaying that you think geometry is interesting &lt;strong&gt;implies&lt;\/strong&gt; that you would like to study it.\r\nBy rolling your eyes, are you trying to &lt;strong&gt;imply&lt;\/strong&gt; that you don\u2019t think I\u2019m funny?\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Infer&lt;\/strong&gt; means to introduce something as a reasoned conclusion; to conclude by reasoning or deduction, as from premises or evidence. For example: \r\nBecause you are wearing a hat, gloves, and a scarf, I can &lt;strong&gt;infer&lt;\/strong&gt; that it is cold outside.\r\nHe rolled his eyes at my joke, so I &lt;strong&gt;inferred&lt;\/strong&gt; that he didn\u2019t like my sense of humor.\r\n\r\nIt\u2019s worth noting that, in recent years, using imply and infer interchangeably has become quite common. However, if you want to be accurate in your writing, use them based on the definitions above.\r\n\r\nTo learn more about confusing word pairs, &lt;a href=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/2015\/dont-let-these-two-word-pairs-confuse-you\/\"&gt;read this blog post about aloud vs. allowed&lt;\/a&gt;. \r\n\r\n","post_title":"Learn how to use these tricky words correctly","post_excerpt":"","post_status":"publish","comment_status":"open","ping_status":"open","post_password":"","post_name":"learn-how-to-use-these-tricky-words-correctly","to_ping":"","pinged":"","post_modified":"2015-02-19 12:39:44","post_modified_gmt":"2015-02-19 20:39:44","post_content_filtered":"","post_parent":0,"guid":"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/?p=11505","menu_order":0,"post_type":"post","post_mime_type":"","comment_count":"1","filter":"raw"},{"ID":11501,"post_author":"1001005","post_date":"2015-02-13 16:27:19","post_date_gmt":"2015-02-14 00:27:19","post_content":"[media]&lt;img src=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/wp-content\/uploads\/2015\/02\/Tracy-Morgan-On-Doing-Good-Doing-Well-On-30-Rock-670x352.jpg\" alt=\"Tracy-Morgan-On-Doing-Good-Doing-Well-On-30-Rock\" width=\"670\" height=\"352\" class=\"aligncenter size-large wp-image-11639\" \/&gt;[\/media]\r\n\r\nAdjectives are descriptive words that modify (describe) nouns (persons, places, things, or ideas). They often tell you how many, which, and what kind. For example:\r\n\r\n&lt;i&gt;\"He baked a delicious, beautiful cake.\"&lt;\/i&gt; (What kind of cake is it? It is &lt;strong&gt;delicious&lt;\/strong&gt; and &lt;strong&gt;beautiful&lt;\/strong&gt;.)\r\n&lt;i&gt;\"Nine members of our group signed up for the yoga class.\"&lt;\/i&gt; (How many members signed up? &lt;strong&gt;Nine&lt;\/strong&gt;.)\r\n&lt;i&gt;\"Hand me the broken radio so I can try to fix it.\"&lt;\/i&gt; (Which radio? The &lt;strong&gt;broken&lt;\/strong&gt; one.)\r\n\r\nAdverbs are descriptive words that mostly modify verbs but can also modify adjectives and other adverbs. Adverbs most often tell you how, but they can also tell you where or when. Adverbs often end in -ly. For example:\r\n\r\n&lt;i&gt;\"She walked slowly through the parking lot.\"&lt;\/i&gt; (How did she walk? She walked &lt;strong&gt;slowly&lt;\/strong&gt;.)\r\n&lt;i&gt;\"Lately, I have felt like I need to make a change.\"&lt;\/i&gt; (When have I been feeling this? &lt;strong&gt;Lately&lt;\/strong&gt;.)\r\n&lt;i&gt;\"I prefer this coffee shop because it is nearer to my office.\"&lt;\/i&gt; (Where is the coffee shop? It is &lt;strong&gt;nearer&lt;\/strong&gt;.)\r\n\r\nThere are several adverbs that do not follow the regular -ly formation. The most common of these include: well, late, and straight. \u00a0Similarly there are other irregular adverbs that have evolved from adjectives. These are considered less correct, and we do not encourage their use in formal situations. These include: fast\/slow (increasingly used instead of quickly and slowly), hard\u00a0(increasingly used instead of forcibly or powerfully), wrong\u00a0(increasingly used instead of incorrectly).\r\n\r\nTo learn more about how to use adjectives and adverbs in your writing, &lt;a href=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/handbook\/grammar\/adjectives-and-adverbs\/ \"&gt;check out the Grammarly Handbook&lt;\/a&gt;.","post_title":"Two-minute Grammar: The Bare-bones Basics of Adjectives and Adverbs","post_excerpt":"","post_status":"publish","comment_status":"open","ping_status":"open","post_password":"","post_name":"two-minute-grammar-the-bare-bones-basics-of-adjectives-and-adverbs","to_ping":"","pinged":"","post_modified":"2015-02-13 16:27:19","post_modified_gmt":"2015-02-14 00:27:19","post_content_filtered":"","post_parent":0,"guid":"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/?p=11501","menu_order":0,"post_type":"post","post_mime_type":"","comment_count":"0","filter":"raw"},{"ID":11497,"post_author":"1001005","post_date":"2015-02-13 16:25:29","post_date_gmt":"2015-02-14 00:25:29","post_content":"[media]&lt;a href=\"http:\/\/www.bizarrocomics.com\" target=\"_blank\"&gt;&lt;img class=\"aligncenter size-large wp-image-11635\" src=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/wp-content\/uploads\/2015\/02\/preposition_cartoon-670x352.jpg\" alt=\"preposition_cartoon\" width=\"670\" height=\"352\" \/&gt;&lt;\/a&gt;[\/media]\r\n\r\n&lt;i&gt;\u201cVampires! Zombies! Werewolves!\u201d\r\n\u201cWhere?!\u201d\r\n\u201cBehind you!\u201d&lt;\/i&gt;\r\n\r\nThank goodness for prepositions. Imagine not knowing where the danger lay!\r\n\r\nPrepositions tell us where or when something is in relation to something else. When monsters are sneaking up on you, it is good to have these special words to tell us where those monsters are. Are they behind us or in front of us; are they near or far; will they be arriving in three seconds or at midnight?\r\n\r\nPrepositions are helpful words that define direction, time, location, and spatial relationships. They tell us where or when something is in relation to something else. For example:\r\n\r\n&lt;i&gt;\"The book is on the table beside you.\"&lt;\/i&gt; (&lt;strong&gt;On&lt;\/strong&gt; and &lt;strong&gt;beside&lt;\/strong&gt; are prepositions of location.)\r\n\r\nPrepositions can also be used to contrast ideas.\r\n\r\n&lt;i&gt;\"We went for a walk despite the rain.\"&lt;\/i&gt; (&lt;strong&gt;Despite&lt;\/strong&gt; is the preposition showing contrast.)\r\n\r\nHere are some commonly used prepositions:\r\n&lt;i&gt;about, above, across, after, against, along, among, around, at, before, behind, below, beneath, beside, between, beyond, but, by, despite, down, during, except, for, from, in, inside, into, like, near, of, off, on, onto, out, outside, over, past, since, through, throughout, till, to, toward, under, underneath, until, up, upon, with, within, without&lt;\/i&gt;\r\n\r\nSome common phrases used to require prepositions, but language has evolved to the point where they are now superfluous and sound silly. For example:\r\n\r\n&lt;i&gt;\"Could you get that box off of the table?\"&lt;\/i&gt;\r\ncan simply be written as:\r\n&lt;i&gt;\"Could you get that box off the table?\"&lt;\/i&gt;\r\n\r\n&lt;i&gt;\"The winner was awarded with a gold medal.\"&lt;\/i&gt;\r\ncan simply be written as:\r\n&lt;i&gt;\"The winner was awarded a gold medal.\"&lt;\/i&gt;\r\n\r\nUnfortunately, there don\u2019t seem to be any rules that will help you determine if you should use the proposition or not. The best thing to do is read the sentence aloud or read it to a friend and ask if the preposition sounds awkward, strange, or unnecessary.\r\n\r\nFinally, is it ever ok to end a sentence with a preposition? Yes, and &lt;a href=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/2014\/youve-been-lied-to-heres-why-you-absolutely-can-end-a-sentence-with-a-preposition\/ \"&gt;this blog post explains why&lt;\/a&gt;.","post_title":"Two-minute Grammar: The Bare-bones Basics of Prepositions","post_excerpt":"","post_status":"publish","comment_status":"open","ping_status":"open","post_password":"","post_name":"two-minute-grammar-the-bare-bones-basics-of-prepositions","to_ping":"","pinged":"","post_modified":"2015-02-13 16:25:29","post_modified_gmt":"2015-02-14 00:25:29","post_content_filtered":"","post_parent":0,"guid":"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/?p=11497","menu_order":0,"post_type":"post","post_mime_type":"","comment_count":"0","filter":"raw"},{"ID":11489,"post_author":"1001005","post_date":"2015-02-11 16:20:24","post_date_gmt":"2015-02-12 00:20:24","post_content":"[media]&lt;img src=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/wp-content\/uploads\/2015\/02\/President-Taft-670x352.jpg\" alt=\"write so that you can&#039;t be misunderstood\" width=\"670\" height=\"352\" class=\"aligncenter size-large wp-image-11493\" \/&gt;[\/media]\r\n\r\nThere is a time and place for complex, lyrical writing. Poetry, stylistic prose, love letters\u2026the list goes on. But when you\u2019re writing for work, school, or any other situation where your chief goal is to be understood, it\u2019s best to keep things simple. Here are a few ways to ensure that your writing is easy to understand.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;1. Read it out loud&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\nFrom time to time, all of us make the mistake of sending an email, turning in a paper, or handing a report to the boss without rereading what we\u2019ve written. Raise your right hand and say this aloud: \u201cI promise to reread my writing at least once before I consider it finished. If possible, I\u2019ll read it aloud.\u201d Now that you\u2019ve made the pledge, your ears can help you simplify any unnecessary complexity in your writing.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;2. Review your modifiers (dangling and otherwise)&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\nA modifier changes, clarifies, qualifies or limits a particular word in a sentence in order to intensify its presence within a body of work. Modifiers can be adjectives, adverbs, and phrases. Modifiers are helpful additions to a sentence, but using too many modifiers can complicate your language. \r\nTake this example from &lt;a href=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/handbook\/sentences\/modifiers\/2\/what-are-modifiers\/\"&gt;the Grammarly Handbook&lt;\/a&gt;:\r\n\r\n\u201cThe blond girl named Sarah, who was a foreign exchange student from England, quickly climbed the ladder of popularity during her junior year, smiling her way through cheerleading and an ASB presidency term she inched near the top and was a sure fit for junior prom queen.\u201d\r\n\r\nIf you remove the modifiers, this sentence could be written simply as:\r\n\u201cSarah was voted prom queen.\u201d\r\n\r\nWhile the first version of the sentence provides great depth and detail, the second version is much clearer and to the point. \r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;3. Let your punctuation guide you&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\nTake a look back at a piece of writing after you\u2019ve completed it. If you see a lot of dashes, semicolons, parentheses, and ellipses, consider breaking those sentences up into two or more sentences. While these punctuation marks are helpful tools, they can also be indicators of complex sentences. If you\u2019re trying to simplify your writing, breaking down these complex sentences is a great way to do it.\r\n\r\nTo read more about simple, clear writing, read &lt;a href=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/2015\/rewriting-101-how-to-add-clarity-to-your-sentences\/ \"&gt;this blog post about rewriting&lt;\/a&gt;.\r\n","post_title":"Follow these 3 guidelines to simplify your writing","post_excerpt":"","post_status":"publish","comment_status":"open","ping_status":"open","post_password":"","post_name":"follow-these-3-guidelines-to-simplify-your-writing","to_ping":"","pinged":"","post_modified":"2015-02-11 16:20:24","post_modified_gmt":"2015-02-12 00:20:24","post_content_filtered":"","post_parent":0,"guid":"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/?p=11489","menu_order":0,"post_type":"post","post_mime_type":"","comment_count":"3","filter":"raw"},{"ID":11367,"post_author":"1001005","post_date":"2015-02-09 03:38:41","post_date_gmt":"2015-02-09 11:38:41","post_content":"[media]&lt;img class=\"alignleft size-large wp-image-11429\" src=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/wp-content\/uploads\/2015\/02\/Vintage-card-who-whom-670x352.jpg\" alt=\"That refers things. Who and whom refer to people. Don't mix them. \" width=\"670\" height=\"352\" \/&gt;[\/media]\r\n\r\nThe basic rule for using &lt;strong&gt;that&lt;\/strong&gt; and &lt;strong&gt;who&lt;\/strong&gt; in formal writing is to use &lt;strong&gt;who&lt;\/strong&gt; when you\u2019re referring to a person, and &lt;strong&gt;that&lt;\/strong&gt; when you\u2019re referring to an object. For example:\r\n\r\nThis jacket was a gift from my cousin &lt;strong&gt;who&lt;\/strong&gt; buys me all my most fashionable clothes. (&lt;strong&gt;Who&lt;\/strong&gt; refers to my cousin, a person.)\r\n\r\nWatch out for the crack in the sidewalk &lt;strong&gt;that&lt;\/strong&gt; always causes me to trip. (&lt;strong&gt;That&lt;\/strong&gt; refers to the crack in the sidewalk, an object or thing.)\r\n\r\nWhile the basic that\/who rule is a good guideline to follow, it\u2019s not technically a hard-and-fast rule. It\u2019s generally frowned upon to refer to an object as &lt;strong&gt;who&lt;\/strong&gt; (although there are exceptions), but using &lt;strong&gt;that&lt;\/strong&gt; to refer to a person is becoming more and more accepted, especially in informal writing and speech. For example:\r\n\r\nHe is the person &lt;strong&gt;that&lt;\/strong&gt; is responsible for tonight\u2019s performance.\r\n\r\nIn this case, using &lt;strong&gt;that&lt;\/strong&gt; is acceptable, although some might argue that it makes the person sound less than human, and &lt;strong&gt;who&lt;\/strong&gt; should be used instead.\r\n\r\nTo learn more about word pairs, read &lt;a href=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/2015\/who-vs-whom-its-not-as-complicated-as-you-think\/ \"&gt;this blog post about the difference between who and whom&lt;\/a&gt;.","post_title":"Follow this simple guideline when using 'that' and 'who' in your writing","post_excerpt":"","post_status":"publish","comment_status":"open","ping_status":"open","post_password":"","post_name":"follow-this-simple-guideline-when-using-that-and-who-in-your-writing","to_ping":"","pinged":"","post_modified":"2015-02-09 12:37:01","post_modified_gmt":"2015-02-09 20:37:01","post_content_filtered":"","post_parent":0,"guid":"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/?p=11367","menu_order":0,"post_type":"post","post_mime_type":"","comment_count":"0","filter":"raw"},{"ID":11371,"post_author":"1001005","post_date":"2015-02-06 16:10:12","post_date_gmt":"2015-02-07 00:10:12","post_content":"[media]&lt;img src=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/wp-content\/uploads\/2015\/02\/11234-670x352.jpg\" alt=\"confusing homophones passed past\" width=\"670\" height=\"352\" class=\"aligncenter size-large wp-image-11419\" \/&gt;[\/media]\r\n\r\nHomophones are words that are pronounced the same, but have different meanings. Here are three pairs of confusing homophones:\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Passed vs. past&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Passed&lt;\/strong&gt; is the past tense of pass, which means to move from one place to another or to change from one state to another. (There are a few other meanings of the word pass, but for this example we\u2019re using the most straightforward meaning.) For example:\r\nThe tray of cookies was &lt;strong&gt;passed&lt;\/strong&gt; around the circle until everyone had one.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Past&lt;\/strong&gt; means the period of time that has already happened, in contrast to the present or the future.\r\nDon\u2019t worry about the argument we had yesterday; it\u2019s in the &lt;strong&gt;past&lt;\/strong&gt;.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Dye vs. die&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Die&lt;\/strong&gt; means to stop living; to become dead; to undergo death.\r\n\"I could &lt;strong&gt;die&lt;\/strong&gt; for you. But I couldn't, and wouldn't, live for you.\u201d (&lt;em&gt;The Fountainhead&lt;\/em&gt; by Ayn Rand)\r\n\r\nAs a noun, &lt;strong&gt;dye&lt;\/strong&gt; means a colourant. As a verb, &lt;strong&gt;dye&lt;\/strong&gt; means to color with dye.\r\nI want to make a big change. I\u2019m going to &lt;strong&gt;dye&lt;\/strong&gt; my hair red!\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Poring vs. pouring &lt;\/strong&gt;\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Pouring&lt;\/strong&gt; means flowing, as water does. It can be used in a few ways:\r\nClowns came &lt;strong&gt;pouring&lt;\/strong&gt; out of the overstuffed car, one after another.\r\nThe rain was &lt;strong&gt;pouring&lt;\/strong&gt; down; the water rushed in the gutter.\r\nOnce I finally set up my Facebook account, notifications and requests from my friends and family came &lt;strong&gt;pouring&lt;\/strong&gt; in.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Poring&lt;\/strong&gt; means investigating or examining closely:\r\nShe spent hours reading her Biology textbook, &lt;strong&gt;poring&lt;\/strong&gt; over every little detail.\r\n\r\nTo learn more about confusing word pairs &lt;a href=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/2014\/three-word-pairs-that-confuse-many-writers\/ \"&gt;read this blog post&lt;\/a&gt;.\r\n","post_title":"Learn the difference between these confusing homophones","post_excerpt":"","post_status":"publish","comment_status":"open","ping_status":"open","post_password":"","post_name":"learn-the-difference-between-these-confusing-homophones","to_ping":"","pinged":"","post_modified":"2015-02-06 16:10:12","post_modified_gmt":"2015-02-07 00:10:12","post_content_filtered":"","post_parent":0,"guid":"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/?p=11371","menu_order":0,"post_type":"post","post_mime_type":"","comment_count":"0","filter":"raw"},{"ID":11167,"post_author":"1001005","post_date":"2015-02-06 11:28:43","post_date_gmt":"2015-02-06 19:28:43","post_content":"[media]&lt;img src=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/wp-content\/uploads\/2015\/02\/14w457yughkj-copy-670x352.jpg\" alt=\"the difference between advice and advise\" width=\"670\" height=\"352\" class=\"aligncenter size-large wp-image-11391\" \/&gt;[\/media]\r\n\r\nThese two words sound very similar, but they mean different things. Here\u2019s how you can use them correctly:\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Advice&lt;\/strong&gt; is a noun and means an opinion recommended or offered, as worthy to be followed; counsel. For example:\r\nIf you want my &lt;strong&gt;advice&lt;\/strong&gt;, I think you should take the job in Chicago.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Advise&lt;\/strong&gt; is a verb and means to give advice to; to offer an opinion, as worthy or expedient to be followed; to counsel; to warn. For example:\r\nPlease &lt;strong&gt;advise&lt;\/strong&gt; me about what to do next. Should I move to Chicago?\r\n\r\nHere's an easy way to remember the difference: When you &lt;strong&gt;advise&lt;\/strong&gt; someone, you give them &lt;strong&gt;advice&lt;\/strong&gt;.\r\n\r\nLearn more about confusing word pairs in &lt;a href=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/2014\/the-basics-on-whos-and-whose\/ \"&gt;this blog post about who\u2019s and whose.&lt;\/a&gt;\r\n\r\n","post_title":"I must advise you to use these words correctly","post_excerpt":"","post_status":"publish","comment_status":"open","ping_status":"open","post_password":"","post_name":"i-must-advise-you-to-use-these-words-correctly","to_ping":"","pinged":"","post_modified":"2015-06-11 22:22:58","post_modified_gmt":"2015-06-12 05:22:58","post_content_filtered":"","post_parent":0,"guid":"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/?p=11167","menu_order":0,"post_type":"post","post_mime_type":"","comment_count":"0","filter":"raw"},{"ID":11219,"post_author":"1001005","post_date":"2015-02-04 12:29:34","post_date_gmt":"2015-02-04 20:29:34","post_content":"[media]&lt;img src=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/wp-content\/uploads\/2015\/01\/a-little-voice-670x352.png\" alt=\"a little voice\" width=\"670\" height=\"352\" class=\"aligncenter size-medium wp-image-11375\" \/&gt;[\/media]\r\n\r\nAlthough the overuse of periods isn\u2019t very common, the overuse of commas is. Do you know when to use a comma? Here are some example sentences:\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Because I want to learn more about biology, I\u2019m going to enroll in an online class.&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\nShould you use a comma? Yes.\r\nThis sentence contains two clauses\u2014&lt;i&gt;I want to learn more about biology&lt;\/i&gt; and &lt;i&gt;I\u2019m going to enroll in an online class&lt;\/i&gt;\u2014and begins with the subordinate \"because.\" When the sentence begins with a subordinate, you need a comma between the two clauses.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;I will come to your house, unless you would like to come to mine.&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\nShould you use a comma? No.\r\nThe subordinate \"unless\" separates the two complete sentences. When a subordinate is in the middle of the sentence, it doesn\u2019t need a comma.\r\nThe correct sentence is:\r\nI will come to your house unless you would like to come to mine.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;It\u2019s a beautiful day outside, let\u2019s go for a bike ride!&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\nShould you use a comma? No.\r\nThere are two sentences here: &lt;i&gt;It\u2019s a beautiful day outside&lt;\/i&gt; and &lt;i&gt;Let\u2019s go for a bike ride.&lt;\/i&gt; They should be separated by a period or a semicolon.\r\nThe correct sentence is:\r\nIt\u2019s a beautiful day outside. Let\u2019s go for a bike ride!\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Before we start the movie, I\u2019m going to make a bowl of popcorn.&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\nShould you use a comma? Yes.\r\n&lt;i&gt;Before we start the movie&lt;i&gt; is an introductory clause. A comma should follow introductory clauses and phrases.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;I need to eat a sandwich, and drink a glass of milk.&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\nShould you use a comma? No.\r\n&lt;i&gt;Eat a sandwich and drink a glass of milk&lt;\/i&gt; is a compound predicate. When compound predicates contain three or more predicates (verbs and any modifiers), they need commas. If there are fewer than three, a comma is not necessary.\r\nThe correct sentence is:\r\nI need to eat a sandwich and drink a glass of milk.\r\n\r\nFor more punctuation rules and guidelines, read &lt;a href=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/2015\/three-things-you-need-to-know-about-semicolons\/ \"&gt;this blog post about semicolons&lt;\/a&gt; or visit the &lt;a href=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/handbook\/punctuation\/\"&gt;free Grammarly Handbook&lt;\/a&gt;!\r\n","post_title":"Commas might be the most overused and abused punctuation mark. Let's fix that.","post_excerpt":"","post_status":"publish","comment_status":"open","ping_status":"open","post_password":"","post_name":"commas-might-be-the-most-overused-and-abused-punctuation-mark-lets-fix-that","to_ping":"","pinged":"","post_modified":"2015-02-04 12:31:18","post_modified_gmt":"2015-02-04 20:31:18","post_content_filtered":"","post_parent":0,"guid":"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/?p=11219","menu_order":0,"post_type":"post","post_mime_type":"","comment_count":"1","filter":"raw"},{"ID":11215,"post_author":"1001005","post_date":"2015-02-03 11:08:50","post_date_gmt":"2015-02-03 19:08:50","post_content":"[media]&lt;img src=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/wp-content\/uploads\/2015\/01\/375840139rgbdjs_sized.png\" alt=\"gender neutral pronouns\" width=\"600\" height=\"315\" class=\"aligncenter size-full wp-image-11343\" \/&gt;[\/media]\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;The reader must understand that they are at the mercy of the author\u2019s imagination.&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\n\r\nWhat\u2019s wrong with the sentence above? Some might say there is nothing at all is wrong with it. Others, however, will take issue with the use of \u2018they,\u2019 a plural pronoun, in place of the singular \u2018reader.\u2019 How can this sentence be corrected? Some would use \u2018he\u2019 in place of \u2018they,\u2019 with the understanding that masculine pronouns are a stand-in for proper nouns of either gender. Others would advocate \u2018he or she\u2019 in place of \u2018they,\u2019 because it includes both genders.\r\n\r\nGendered pronouns have a long history of causing grammatical confusion and debate. As far back as the 1800s, writers and linguists have proposed gender-neutral pronouns (including ze, co, hesh, thon, and zher) to solve the complicated issues that gendered pronouns cause in English. \r\n\r\nGender-neutral pronouns solve many grammatical problems, but why haven\u2019t they caught on in English? According to &lt;a href=\"http:\/\/www.newrepublic.com\/article\/116331\/gender-neutral-pronouns-will-we-ever-replace-she-and-he \" target=\"_blank\"&gt;John McWhorter at The New Republic&lt;\/a&gt;, that\u2019s because there are two kinds of words: open-class and closed-class. Nouns and verbs are among the open-class words and can be adapted or even made up depending on necessity and context. Pronouns and prepositions are closed-class words\u2014words that we use as tools to show the relationships between open-class words. Closed-class words are harder to adapt and change since they\u2019re already representing something else (for example, \u2018he\u2019 represents \u2018Brian\u2019). \r\n\r\nMcWhorter argues that since it\u2019s unlikely that a new gender-neutral pronoun will ever gain wide acceptance in English, it\u2019s okay to use the plural \u2018they\u2019  in place of \u2018he\u2019 or \u2018she.\u2019 Other alternatives include \u2018one\u2019 to mean \u2018a person,\u2019 and \u2018you\u2019 in certain contexts. \r\n\r\nMany other languages don\u2019t include gendered pronouns at all or include gender neutral pronouns in their structure. \r\n\r\nWill the English language eventually accept the use of \u2018they\u2019 as a singular gender-neutral pronoun? Will an alternative like ze gain widespread acceptance and use? Let us know what you think in the comments section.\r\n\r\nSources: &lt;a href=\"http:\/\/www.newrepublic.com\/article\/116331\/gender-neutral-pronouns-will-we-ever-replace-she-and-he \" target=\"_blank\"&gt;1&lt;\/a&gt;,&lt;a href=\"http:\/\/www.progress.org\/tpr\/gender-neutral-pronouns-for-english-2\/ \" target=\"_blank\"&gt;2&lt;\/a&gt;,&lt;a href=\"http:\/\/illinois.edu\/blog\/view\/25\/31097 \" target=\"_blank\"&gt;3&lt;\/a&gt;","post_title":"Are gender-neutral pronouns the wave of the future?","post_excerpt":"","post_status":"publish","comment_status":"open","ping_status":"open","post_password":"","post_name":"are-gender-neutral-pronouns-the-wave-of-the-future","to_ping":"","pinged":"","post_modified":"2015-02-06 12:24:34","post_modified_gmt":"2015-02-06 20:24:34","post_content_filtered":"","post_parent":0,"guid":"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/?p=11215","menu_order":0,"post_type":"post","post_mime_type":"","comment_count":"0","filter":"raw"},{"ID":11117,"post_author":"1001005","post_date":"2015-01-28 10:58:02","post_date_gmt":"2015-01-28 18:58:02","post_content":"[media]&lt;img src=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/wp-content\/uploads\/2015\/01\/FunnyPhrases-Dog-670x351.png\" alt=\"FunnyPhrases Dog\" width=\"670\" height=\"351\" class=\"aligncenter size-medium wp-image-11223\" \/&gt;[\/media]\r\n\r\nThe morning after a long night out, a friend might recommend you start your day with \u2018the hair of the dog.\u2019 No, she doesn\u2019t want you to rub your face up against her pet golden retriever. According to &lt;a href=\"http:\/\/dictionary.reference.com\/browse\/hair+of+the+dog+that+bit+you?__utma=1.1361675183.1421706690.1421706690.1421706690.1&amp;__utmb=1.1.10.1421706690&amp;__utmc=1&amp;__utmx=-&amp;__utmz=1.1421706690.1.1.utmcsr=google|utmccn=(organic)|utmcmd=organic|utmctr=(not%20provided)&amp;__utmv=-&amp;__utmk=125975181\" target=\"_blank\"&gt;dictionary.com&lt;\/a&gt;, to offer someone \u2018the hair of the dog\u2019 is to recommend that they consume a small amount of whatever caused their ailment. If you drank a lot of alcohol last night and you\u2019re feeling hungover, the \u2018hair of the dog\u2019 might be something like a bloody mary or mimosa\u2014a drink that has a little alcohol in it.\r\n\r\nWhere did this canine-centric phrase come from? It turns out that \u2018hair of the dog\u2019 is a shortened version of the phrase, \u2018the hair of the dog that bit me.\u2019 Back in medieval times, if someone was bitten by a rabid dog it was believed that putting some of that dog\u2019s hair (according to some sources, burnt hair) in the wound would help heal it.\r\n\r\nIf you're wondering about the origins of another word or phrase, let us know in the comments!","post_title":"Funny Phrases: The Hair of the Dog","post_excerpt":"","post_status":"publish","comment_status":"open","ping_status":"open","post_password":"","post_name":"funny-phrases-the-hair-of-the-dog","to_ping":"","pinged":"","post_modified":"2015-01-28 10:58:02","post_modified_gmt":"2015-01-28 18:58:02","post_content_filtered":"","post_parent":0,"guid":"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/?p=11117","menu_order":0,"post_type":"post","post_mime_type":"","comment_count":"0","filter":"raw"},{"ID":11163,"post_author":"1001005","post_date":"2015-01-27 03:13:05","post_date_gmt":"2015-01-27 11:13:05","post_content":"[media]&lt;img src=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/wp-content\/uploads\/2015\/01\/rytujh-670x352.jpg\" alt=\"dashes and hyphens\" width=\"670\" height=\"352\" class=\"aligncenter size-medium wp-image-11287\" \/&gt;[\/media]\r\n\r\nDashes and hyphens are both commonly used in writing, but which one is which? Dashes and hyphens look similar, but they\u2019re used in different ways.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;A hyphen (-) is used:&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\n1. To connect two or more words \r\nFor example: Daniel Day-Lewis, twenty-one, user-friendly\r\n2. To indicate a range of values \r\nFor example: At the wedding, family members will be seated in rows 11-15.\r\n3. To continue a word onto the next line when there is a line break \r\nFor example: Because the traffic is relatively light today, we cer-\r\ntainly have time to stop at the grocery store.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;A dash (\u2014) is used:&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\n1. At the beginning and end of a phrase to highlight parenthetical information \r\nFor example: An etymological dictionary is one of the few books\u2014no, it\u2019s the only book\u2014you\u2019ll ever need.\r\n2. To introduce new information, as a colon would with a list of items \r\nFor example: I opened the door, and there she stood\u2014my long-lost sister.\r\n&lt;b&gt;NOTE: Be careful to use the correct dash.&lt;\/b&gt; There are two kinds\u2014an en dash and an em dash. The em dash is the one you want to use in most writing and is the one we've used in the examples above. To make an em dash on a PC, hold down ctrl + alt + - (hyphen key). To make an em dash on a Mac, hold down option + shift + - (hyphen key). The en dash is used to separate numbers and measures. &lt;a href=\"http:\/\/blog.apastyle.org\/apastyle\/2010\/09\/computer-editing-tip-en-dashes.html\"&gt;Learn more here.&lt;\/a&gt; \r\n\r\nFor more information on using dashes in writing, check out &lt;a href=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/2014\/learn-how-to-use-dashes-in-your-writingb\/ \"&gt;our blog post.&lt;\/a&gt; ","post_title":"Dashes and hyphens are not the same","post_excerpt":"","post_status":"publish","comment_status":"open","ping_status":"open","post_password":"","post_name":"dashes-and-hyphens-are-not-the-same","to_ping":"","pinged":"","post_modified":"2015-01-30 13:50:24","post_modified_gmt":"2015-01-30 21:50:24","post_content_filtered":"","post_parent":0,"guid":"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/?p=11163","menu_order":0,"post_type":"post","post_mime_type":"","comment_count":"0","filter":"raw"},{"ID":11125,"post_author":"1001005","post_date":"2015-01-27 03:02:16","post_date_gmt":"2015-01-27 11:02:16","post_content":"[media]&lt;img src=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/wp-content\/uploads\/2015\/01\/y54ergwefds-670x352.png\" alt=\"split infinitive\" width=\"670\" height=\"352\" class=\"aligncenter size-medium wp-image-11283\" \/&gt;[\/media]\r\n\r\nAn &lt;strong&gt;infinitive&lt;\/strong&gt; is a simple verb combined with \u2018to.\u2019 For example:\r\nto sleep, to run, to fly, to hide\r\n\r\nRemember this formula:\r\nInfinitive = to + verb\r\n\r\nInfinitives can function as subjects, objects, adjectives, and adverbs. For example:\r\n\r\nTo wait for an answer seemed like a waste of time. (&lt;strong&gt;To wait&lt;\/strong&gt; is the subject of the sentence.)\r\n\r\nI can\u2019t stand it; I refuse to look. (&lt;strong&gt;To look&lt;\/strong&gt; is the direct object of the sentence.)\r\n\r\nIf you get bored easily, bring a book to read. (&lt;strong&gt;To read&lt;\/strong&gt; is an adjective modifying book.)\r\n\r\nMartin planned this event to show Diane how much he cares about her. (&lt;strong&gt;To show&lt;\/strong&gt; is an adverb explaining why Martin planned the event.)\r\n\r\nYou may have heard that 'splitting infinitives' is the worst of all grammar crimes. What does this mean? Splitting an infinitive simply means to add other words between the 'to' and the verb in an infinitive. For example:\r\n\u201cI plan to really enjoy the party.\u201d (The word 'really' is in the middle of the infinitive.)\r\n\r\nSplit infinitives are still frowned upon in formal writing situations. For this reason, split infinitives are best left out of a sentence. However, it is not wise to mangle a sentence simply to avoid splitting an infinitive. \r\n\r\nTo learn more about verbs read &lt;a href=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/2014\/an-introduction-to-verb-tenses\/ \"&gt;this blog post about verb tenses&lt;\/a&gt;.","post_title":"To infinitive...and beyond!","post_excerpt":"","post_status":"publish","comment_status":"open","ping_status":"open","post_password":"","post_name":"to-infinitive-and-beyond","to_ping":"","pinged":"","post_modified":"2015-01-29 14:16:51","post_modified_gmt":"2015-01-29 22:16:51","post_content_filtered":"","post_parent":0,"guid":"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/?p=11125","menu_order":0,"post_type":"post","post_mime_type":"","comment_count":"0","filter":"raw"},{"ID":11031,"post_author":"1001005","post_date":"2015-01-26 08:24:03","post_date_gmt":"2015-01-26 16:24:03","post_content":"[media]&lt;img src=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/wp-content\/uploads\/2015\/01\/7ryufhewj-670x351.jpg\" alt=\"reading joy of life\" width=\"670\" height=\"351\" class=\"aligncenter size-medium wp-image-11157\" \/&gt;[\/media]\r\n\r\nBeing the word nerds and language lovers that we are, it\u2019s no secret that we\u2019re also avid readers here at Grammarly. ","post_title":"Want to know what to read next? #GrammarlyChat has the answer.","post_excerpt":"","post_status":"publish","comment_status":"open","ping_status":"open","post_password":"","post_name":"want-to-know-what-to-read-next-grammarlychat-has-the-answer","to_ping":"","pinged":"","post_modified":"2015-01-27 11:06:29","post_modified_gmt":"2015-01-27 19:06:29","post_content_filtered":"","post_parent":0,"guid":"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/?p=11031","menu_order":0,"post_type":"post","post_mime_type":"","comment_count":"0","filter":"raw"},{"ID":11113,"post_author":"1001005","post_date":"2015-01-26 06:28:26","post_date_gmt":"2015-01-26 14:28:26","post_content":"[vid]\/\/www.youtube.com\/embed\/RPoBE-E8VOc[\/vid]\r\n\r\nConjunctions are short words like &lt;strong&gt;and&lt;\/strong&gt;, &lt;strong&gt;but&lt;\/strong&gt;, and &lt;strong&gt;or&lt;\/strong&gt;, that link two concepts, clauses, or parts of sentences. You can use conjunctions in many ways. Here are a few:\r\n\r\nConjunctions connect actions:\r\n&lt;i&gt;I went to the party &lt;strong&gt;and&lt;\/strong&gt; made some new friends.&lt;\/i&gt;\r\n(The conjunction &lt;strong&gt;and&lt;\/strong&gt; connects the two things I did: went to the party and made friends.)\r\n\r\nConjunctions connect short clauses that could be sentences on their own:\r\n&lt;i&gt;I have two older sisters. I have a cat.\r\nI have two older sisters &lt;strong&gt;and&lt;\/strong&gt; a cat.&lt;\/i&gt;\r\n(The conjunction &lt;strong&gt;and&lt;\/strong&gt; connects the two short sentences into one complete sentence.)\r\n\r\nConjunctions help make lists:\r\n&lt;i&gt;I brought sandwiches &lt;strong&gt;and&lt;\/strong&gt; grapefruit juice to the party.&lt;\/i&gt;\r\n(The conjunction &lt;strong&gt;and&lt;\/strong&gt; connects the two things I brought: sandwiches and grapefruit juice.)\r\n\r\nTo learn more about using conjunctions and other words that can be used as conjunctions, check out &lt;a href=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/handbook\/grammar\/conjunctions\/\"&gt;our Grammarly Handbook section on conjunctions&lt;\/a&gt;.\r\n\r\n(\"Conjunction Junction\", &lt;i&gt;School House Rock,&lt;\/i&gt; is published by &lt;a href=\"https:\/\/www.youtube.com\/channel\/UCP6OYn7B1t4DRhczjXxEP9g\"&gt;Disney Education Productions&lt;\/a&gt;.)","post_title":"What are conjunctions and how should I use them?","post_excerpt":"","post_status":"publish","comment_status":"open","ping_status":"open","post_password":"","post_name":"what-are-conjunctions-and-how-should-i-use-them","to_ping":"","pinged":"","post_modified":"2015-06-23 03:01:01","post_modified_gmt":"2015-06-23 10:01:01","post_content_filtered":"","post_parent":0,"guid":"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/?p=11113","menu_order":0,"post_type":"post","post_mime_type":"","comment_count":"0","filter":"raw"},{"ID":10865,"post_author":"1001005","post_date":"2015-01-23 11:12:22","post_date_gmt":"2015-01-23 19:12:22","post_content":"[media]&lt;img src=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/wp-content\/uploads\/2015\/01\/direct-objects_edited-1.png\" alt=\"direct indirect objects\" width=\"600\" height=\"315\" class=\"aligncenter size-full wp-image-11065\" \/&gt;[\/media]\r\n\r\nAn &lt;strong&gt;object&lt;\/strong&gt; is the part of a sentence that gives meaning to the subject\u2019s action of the verb. For example:\r\nAlice caught the baseball.\r\nSubject=Alice\r\nVerb=caught \r\nObject=baseball\r\n\r\nA &lt;strong&gt;direct object&lt;\/strong&gt; answers the question of who(m) or what. In the sentence above, you could determine that \u2018baseball\u2019 is a direct object by asking the question:\r\nWhat did Alice catch?\r\nShe caught the baseball. Baseball is the direct object.\r\n\r\nAn &lt;strong&gt;indirect object&lt;\/strong&gt; answers the question of to whom, for whom, or for what. For example:\r\nMax pitched Alice the baseball.\r\n\r\nMax (subject) pitched (verb) the baseball (direct object) to whom? He pitched it to Alice. Alice is the indirect object.\r\n\r\nTo learn more about sentence structure, &lt;a href=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/2014\/an-introduction-to-verb-tenses\/\"&gt;read this blog post about verb tenses&lt;\/a&gt;.\r\n\r\nGrammarly is nominated for the Crunchies Awards. &lt;a href=\"http:\/\/bit.ly\/vote4grammarly\" target=\"_blank\"&gt;Click here to vote for Grammarly!&lt;\/a&gt;","post_title":"A Grammar Lesson: Direct and Indirect Objects","post_excerpt":"","post_status":"publish","comment_status":"open","ping_status":"open","post_password":"","post_name":"a-grammar-lesson-direct-and-indirect-objects","to_ping":"","pinged":"","post_modified":"2015-01-23 11:12:22","post_modified_gmt":"2015-01-23 19:12:22","post_content_filtered":"","post_parent":0,"guid":"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/?p=10865","menu_order":0,"post_type":"post","post_mime_type":"","comment_count":"0","filter":"raw"},{"ID":11009,"post_author":"6","post_date":"2015-01-22 04:10:00","post_date_gmt":"2015-01-22 12:10:00","post_content":"[media]&lt;img class=\"alignleft size-large wp-image-11011\" src=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/wp-content\/uploads\/2015\/01\/whom-correctly-670x350.jpg\" alt=\"Is it hot in here or did you just use whom correctly?\" width=\"670\" height=\"350\" \/&gt;[\/media]\r\n\r\nIf you\u2019re like most English speakers, you know that there\u2019s a difference between who and whom but you aren\u2019t sure what that difference is. We\u2019re here to help.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Who&lt;\/strong&gt; should be used to refer to the subject of a sentence.\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Whom&lt;\/strong&gt; should be used to refer to the object of a sentence.\r\n\r\nWhen in doubt, try this simple trick: If you can replace the word with \u2018he\u2019 or \u2018she,\u2019 use &lt;strong&gt;who&lt;\/strong&gt;. If you can replace it with \u2018him\u2019 or \u2018her,\u2019 use &lt;strong&gt;whom&lt;\/strong&gt;.\r\n\r\nHere are two\u00a0examples:\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Who&lt;\/strong&gt; ate my sandwich? (Did he eat my sandwich? Did she eat my sandwich?)\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Whom&lt;\/strong&gt; should I talk to about labeling food in the refrigerator? (Should I talk to him? Should I talk to her?)\r\n\r\n&nbsp;\r\n\r\n&lt;hr \/&gt;\r\n\r\nGrammarly is a finalist in the \u201cBest Bootstrapped Startup\u201d category for the second year in a row. means having a feeling of nausea:\r\nI tried getting out of bed this morning, but I felt &lt;strong&gt;nauseated&lt;\/strong&gt;.\r\nI went on the carousel so many times that it made me &lt;strong&gt;nauseated&lt;\/strong&gt;.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Nauseous&lt;\/strong&gt; means causing nausea; sickening or disgusting:\r\nThe pile of stinky socks on your bedroom floor is &lt;strong&gt;nauseous&lt;\/strong&gt;; just smelling it makes me want to vomit.\r\nI can\u2019t stand the &lt;strong&gt;nauseous&lt;\/strong&gt; taste of cough medicine.\r\n\r\nAlthough the two words are increasingly used interchangeably to mean \u2018feeling sick,\u2019 now you know the true difference between them.\r\n\r\nTo learn more about words that are frequently misused interchangeably, &lt;a href=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/2014\/these-words-may-sound-similar-but-they-have-very-different-meanings\/ \"&gt;read this post about the difference between conscious and conscience. &lt;\/a&gt;\r\n\r\nGrammarly is nominated for the Crunchies Awards. &lt;a href=\"http:\/\/bit.ly\/vote4grammarly\" target=\"_blank\"&gt;Click here to vote for Grammarly!&lt;\/a&gt;","post_title":"Nauseated vs. nauseous","post_excerpt":"","post_status":"publish","comment_status":"open","ping_status":"open","post_password":"","post_name":"nauseated-vs-nauseous","to_ping":"","pinged":"","post_modified":"2015-01-22 03:03:26","post_modified_gmt":"2015-01-22 11:03:26","post_content_filtered":"","post_parent":0,"guid":"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/?p=10861","menu_order":0,"post_type":"post","post_mime_type":"","comment_count":"0","filter":"raw"},{"ID":10983,"post_author":"6","post_date":"2015-01-22 02:50:27","post_date_gmt":"2015-01-22 10:50:27","post_content":"[media] &lt;img class=\"alignleft size-full wp-image-10985\" src=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/wp-content\/uploads\/2015\/01\/then-than.png\" alt=\"Then vs. than. They are not the same.\" width=\"670\" height=\"350\" \/&gt; [\/media]\r\n\r\nIt's easy to say that typos and spelling errors like then and than are not a big deal. Sometimes they aren't, at least not in the sense that understanding is diminished.\r\nOther times, however, one letter can make a big difference in the meaning of a sentence. Consider the following examples:\r\n\r\n&lt;i&gt;I'd rather walk then take the bus.&lt;\/i&gt;\r\n\r\nvs.\r\n\r\n&lt;i&gt;I'd rather walk than take the bus. &lt;\/i&gt;\r\n\r\nBecause &lt;b&gt;then is used to signal the passage of time&lt;\/b&gt;, the first sentence means that the person wants first to walk followed by taking a bus.\r\n\r\n&lt;b&gt;Than is used to compare two things.&lt;\/b&gt; In the second example, the person's preference for walking is expressed in contrast to the other option, taking the bus.\r\n\r\n&nbsp;\r\n\r\n&lt;hr \/&gt;\r\n\r\nGrammarly is a finalist in the \u201cBest Bootstrapped Startup\u201d category for the second year in a row. With your help, we\u2019ll bring home this coveted award.&lt;b&gt;&lt;a href=\"http:\/\/bit.ly\/vote4grammarly\"&gt;Click here to vote for Grammarly!&lt;\/a&gt;&lt;\/b&gt;","post_title":"Then vs. Than: Why one letter matters","post_excerpt":"","post_status":"publish","comment_status":"open","ping_status":"open","post_password":"","post_name":"then-vs-than-why-one-letter-matters","to_ping":"","pinged":"","post_modified":"2015-06-23 07:00:53","post_modified_gmt":"2015-06-23 14:00:53","post_content_filtered":"","post_parent":0,"guid":"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/?p=10983","menu_order":0,"post_type":"post","post_mime_type":"","comment_count":"0","filter":"raw"},{"ID":10853,"post_author":"1001005","post_date":"2015-01-20 08:47:49","post_date_gmt":"2015-01-20 16:47:49","post_content":"[media]&lt;img src=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/wp-content\/uploads\/2015\/01\/ellipsis.jpg\" alt=\"Stop abusing the ellipsis . . . I mean it! \" width=\"600\" height=\"315\" class=\"alignleft size-full wp-image-10909\" \/&gt;[\/media]\r\n\r\nThose little dots often found in a sentence or quote are called an ellipsis. The term ellipsis comes from the Greek word meaning \u2018omission,' and that\u2019s just what it does: an ellipsis shows that something has been left out. You can use an ellipsis when you\u2019re quoting someone to show that you\u2019ve omitted some of their words. For example:\r\n\r\n\u201cI wore my new silver, strapless, floor-length, silk dress and matching shoes.\u201d  \r\ncould be shortened with an ellipsis to read:\r\n\u201cI wore my new\u2026 dress and matching shoes.\u201d\r\n\r\nYou can also use an ellipsis to show a pause in speech or the 'trailing off' of a sentence. You should only use the ellipsis this way in informal writing, however. For example:\r\n\r\n\u201cAndrew, can you, um\u2026 never mind, I forgot what I was saying.\u201d\r\n\u201cSo, do you think we should\u2026?\u201d\r\n\r\nHow many dots are in an ellipsis? The answer is three, unless the omitted material includes the end of a sentence. You can think of that as a three-dot ellipsis plus the period at the end of the sentence.\r\n\r\nFor more tips on punctuation, &lt;a href=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/2014\/the-ins-and-outs-of-using-quotation-marks-in-your-writing\/\"&gt;read this blog post about using quotation marks&lt;\/a&gt;.\r\n","post_title":"What are those dots called and how many of them are there supposed to be?","post_excerpt":"","post_status":"publish","comment_status":"open","ping_status":"open","post_password":"","post_name":"what-are-those-dots-called-and-how-many-of-them-are-there-supposed-to-be","to_ping":"","pinged":"","post_modified":"2015-02-10 14:37:02","post_modified_gmt":"2015-02-10 22:37:02","post_content_filtered":"","post_parent":0,"guid":"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/?p=10853","menu_order":0,"post_type":"post","post_mime_type":"","comment_count":"2","filter":"raw"},{"ID":10843,"post_author":"1001005","post_date":"2015-01-16 09:29:21","post_date_gmt":"2015-01-16 17:29:21","post_content":"[media]&lt;img src=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/wp-content\/uploads\/2015\/01\/12313.jpg\" alt=\"Pro Tip:REread your email before sending them.\" width=\"600\" height=\"315\" class=\"aligncenter size-full wp-image-10801\" \/&gt;[\/media]\r\n\r\nJanuary is the perfect time to make a fresh start. If you\u2019re interested in establishing some new habits this year, give one of these a try:\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;1. Reread your emails before you press send.&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\nIt happens to all of us at one point or another: You dash off a quick email, hit send, and then discover an embarrassing typo too late. Even if you\u2019re confident in your spelling and grammar skills, chances are that if you reread your email before you press send, you\u2019ll find at least one sentence to rephrase for clarity. Get in the habit of rereading your emails before you send them. It doesn\u2019t take too much time, and it\u2019s bound to save you a headache or two.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;2. Learn a new word every week (and try to use it in a sentence once a day).&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\nLanguage lovers, this one is for you! If you want to expand your vocabulary, try learning a new word every week. Whether you buy a word-a-day calendar or just open the dictionary to a random page, try to use your new word in a sentence each day of the week. You\u2019ll be amazed at how much more robust your vocabulary becomes.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;3. Be open to discovering new authors and reading new genres of books.&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\nIf you consistently favor reading a certain genre of book, go out on a limb and try something different. If you\u2019re a sci-fi fan, try picking up a nonfiction book. Romance lovers? How about reading some historical fiction? The same goes for preferred authors. Ask friends to recommend their favorite authors and give them a try. You just might discover your new favorite book!\r\n\r\nWhat new habits do you hope to establish in 2015? ","post_excerpt":"","post_status":"publish","comment_status":"open","ping_status":"open","post_password":"","post_name":"if-we-win-a-crunchies-award-justin-will-do-whatever-the-audience-picks","to_ping":"","pinged":"","post_modified":"2015-01-22 02:49:58","post_modified_gmt":"2015-01-22 10:49:58","post_content_filtered":"","post_parent":0,"guid":"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/?p=10805","menu_order":0,"post_type":"post","post_mime_type":"","comment_count":"0","filter":"raw"},{"ID":10713,"post_author":"1001005","post_date":"2015-01-15 14:09:24","post_date_gmt":"2015-01-15 22:09:24","post_content":"[media]&lt;img src=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/wp-content\/uploads\/2015\/01\/32wes.jpg\" alt=\"elmore leonard rewrite quote\" width=\"600\" height=\"315\" class=\"aligncenter size-full wp-image-10797\" \/&gt;[\/media]\r\n\r\nHave you ever finished writing a sentence only to reread it and be completely baffled at its structure? Sure, the sentence might be technically grammatically correct, but it sounds incredibly awkward.\r\nIn situations like these, it\u2019s best to step back and try to find a way to rewrite the sentence. For example:\r\n&lt;strong&gt;When you see your new friend, tell him or her that I said hello.&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\n\r\nAlthough using \u2018him or her\u2019 to indicate a person of whose gender you aren\u2019t aware is technically correct, the sentence above sounds formal and would likely come across as awkward in casual conversation. In this case, try rewriting the sentence:\r\n\r\nTell your new friend that I said hello. \r\nThe next time you see your new friend, say hello for me. \r\nSay hello to your new friend for me. \r\n\r\nAnother example:\r\n&lt;strong&gt;She took over the family business because, due to a series of unexpected events, her mother was unable to continue the administrative work that she had done faithfully for over twenty-five years.&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\n\r\nThis sentence is overly wordy and awkward. Try removing information from the sentence or breaking it up into two sentences:\r\n\r\nHer mother was unable to continue the administrative work that she had done faithfully for over twenty-five years. Therefore, Monica took over the family business. \r\n\r\nAnother example:\r\n&lt;strong&gt;People, parents mostly, who care about children should make an effort to teach their children the differences between right and wrong.&lt;\/strong&gt; \r\n\r\nThis sentence is repetitive and awkward. Try consolidating the information in the sentence:\r\n\r\nIf parents care about their children, they should teach them the differences between right and wrong.\r\n\r\nIf you can\u2019t tell whether or not your sentence is awkward, try reading it aloud. Your ears will often tell you before your eyes will if you should rewrite the sentence. \r\n\r\nFor more tips on clear and concise writing, read &lt;a href=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/2013\/trimming-the-fat\/\"&gt;this blog post by C.S. For example:\r\nA raindrop is a countable noun, since you can theoretically count a number of raindrops.\r\nThe rain is an uncountable noun because you can\u2019t identify its quantity.\r\n\r\nFor countable nouns, use the term 'fewer.' \r\nThe farther south I drive, the fewer raindrops I see on my windshield.\r\n\r\nFor uncountable nouns, use the term 'less.'\r\nIt has been a dry winter; there is less rain than usual.\r\n\r\nThe same principle applies for many and much.\r\nFor countable nouns, use the term 'many.'\r\nThere are many action figures on sale this weekend.\r\n\r\nFor uncountable nouns, use the term 'much.'\r\nThe store manager says they have too much inventory in the back room. \r\n\r\nFor more writing tips, read &lt;a href=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/2014\/learn-the-difference-between-some-and-any-in-less-than-a-minute\/ \"&gt;this post about the difference between \u2018some\u2019 and \u2018any.\u2019&lt;\/a&gt;\r\n\r\nGrammarly is nominated for the Crunchies Awards. &lt;a href=\"http:\/\/bit.ly\/vote4grammarly\"&gt;Click here to vote for Grammarly!&lt;\/a&gt;","post_title":"Is it 'less' or 'fewer'? ","post_title":"Treat your ears to these podcasts and audiobooks for book lovers and word nerds","post_excerpt":"","post_status":"publish","comment_status":"open","ping_status":"open","post_password":"","post_name":"treat-your-ears-to-these-podcasts-and-audiobooks-for-book-lovers-and-word-nerds","to_ping":"","pinged":"","post_modified":"2015-01-08 11:53:25","post_modified_gmt":"2015-01-08 19:53:25","post_content_filtered":"","post_parent":0,"guid":"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/?p=10533","menu_order":0,"post_type":"post","post_mime_type":"","comment_count":"0","filter":"raw"},{"ID":10529,"post_author":"1001005","post_date":"2015-01-06 09:08:50","post_date_gmt":"2015-01-06 17:08:50","post_content":"[media]&lt;img src=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/wp-content\/uploads\/2015\/01\/semicolon.png\" alt=\"semicolon\" width=\"600\" height=\"315\" class=\"aligncenter size-full wp-image-10579\" \/&gt;[\/media]\r\n\r\nSemicolons are possibly one of the most misused and misunderstood punctuation marks. In an effort to help clear up semicolon confusion, we answered three common questions about semicolons.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;1. When should I use a semicolon?&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\nIn formal writing, use a semicolon to connect two related independent clauses. Semicolons function like a soft period, separating the two thoughts but keeping the flow of the entire  sentence. \r\nFor example:\r\nLet\u2019s go for a drive; the weather is so beautiful.\r\nI can\u2019t find my car keys; where could I have left them?\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;2. Should I capitalize the first word after a semicolon?&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\nYou only need to use a capital letter after a semicolon if the word is a proper noun or an acronym. For example:\r\nLet\u2019s plan to get together next week; Tuesday works for me.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;3. Why are semicolons sometimes used in lists?&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\nYou can use a semicolon to separate short clauses in a list after a colon has been used. Using semicolons in this way helps clarify the ideas for the reader, and is particularly useful if the clauses have commas or other punctuation in them. \r\nFor example:\r\nI need the weather statistics for the following cities: London, England; London, Ontario; Paris, France; Paris, Ontario; Perth, Scotland; Perth, Ontario.\r\n\r\nTo learn more about tricky punctuation marks, read &lt;a href=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/2014\/how-to-use-a-colon-when-writing\/\"&gt;this blog post about using colons in your writing&lt;\/a&gt;.\r\n ","post_title":"Three things you need to know about semicolons","post_excerpt":"","post_status":"publish","comment_status":"open","ping_status":"open","post_password":"","post_name":"three-things-you-need-to-know-about-semicolons","to_ping":"","pinged":"","post_modified":"2015-01-06 09:08:50","post_modified_gmt":"2015-01-06 17:08:50","post_content_filtered":"","post_parent":0,"guid":"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/?p=10529","menu_order":0,"post_type":"post","post_mime_type":"","comment_count":"1","filter":"raw"},{"ID":10523,"post_author":"1001005","post_date":"2015-01-05 08:08:35","post_date_gmt":"2015-01-05 16:08:35","post_content":"[media]&lt;img src=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/wp-content\/uploads\/2015\/01\/Vintage-card34r2-670x351.png\" alt=\"Vintage card34r2\" width=\"670\" height=\"351\" class=\"aligncenter size-medium wp-image-10565\" \/&gt;[\/media]\r\n\r\nThe Oxford (or serial) comma is the final comma in a list of things. For example:\r\n\r\nPlease bring me a pencil, eraser, and notebook. \r\nThe Oxford comma comes right after eraser.\r\n\r\nUse of the Oxford comma is stylistic, meaning that some style guides demand its use while others don\u2019t. AP Style\u2014the style guide that newspaper reporters adhere to\u2014does not require the use of the Oxford comma. The sentence above written in AP style would look like this:\r\n\r\nPlease bring me a pencil, eraser and notebook.\r\n\r\nUnless you\u2019re writing for a particular publication or drafting an essay for school, whether or not you use the Oxford comma is generally up to you. However, omitting it can sometimes cause some strange misunderstandings, such as in this news notification from 2013: \r\n\r\n[caption id=\"attachment_10525\" align=\"aligncenter\" width=\"273\"]&lt;img src=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/wp-content\/uploads\/2015\/01\/1386725553.jpg.CROP_.promovar-medium2-273x410.jpg\" alt=\"Image via Twitter\" width=\"273\" height=\"410\" class=\"size-medium wp-image-10525\" \/&gt; Image via Twitter[\/caption]\r\n\r\nor in cases such as this one:\r\nI love my parents, Lady Gaga and Humpty Dumpty.\r\n\r\nWithout the Oxford comma, the sentence above could be interpreted as stating that you love your parents, and your parents are Lady Gaga and Humpty Dumpty. Here\u2019s the same sentence with the Oxford comma:\r\nI love my parents, Lady Gaga, and Humpty Dumpty.\r\n\r\nThose who oppose the Oxford comma argue that rephrasing an already unclear sentence can solve the same problems that using the Oxford comma does. For example:\r\n\r\nI love my parents, Lady Gaga and Humpty Dumpty.\r\ncould be rewritten as:\r\nI love Lady Gaga, Humpty Dumpty and my parents.\r\n\r\nWhat do you think about Oxford comma? Share your thoughts in the comments!","post_title":"What is the Oxford comma and why do people care so much about it?","post_excerpt":"","post_status":"publish","comment_status":"open","ping_status":"open","post_password":"","post_name":"what-is-the-oxford-comma-and-why-do-people-care-so-much-about-it","to_ping":"","pinged":"","post_modified":"2015-04-22 07:22:47","post_modified_gmt":"2015-04-22 14:22:47","post_content_filtered":"","post_parent":0,"guid":"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/?p=10523","menu_order":0,"post_type":"post","post_mime_type":"","comment_count":"6","filter":"raw"},{"ID":10539,"post_author":"1001005","post_date":"2015-01-05 07:04:15","post_date_gmt":"2015-01-05 15:04:15","post_content":"&lt;img src=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/wp-content\/uploads\/2015\/01\/morpheus_apostrophe.png\" alt=\"What if I told you that putting an apostrophe in the right place doesn&#039;t make you a grammar cop?\" width=\"510\" height=\"316\" class=\"alignleft size-full wp-image-10551\" \/&gt;[media][\/media]\r\n\r\nPossessive nouns and pronouns tend to be relatively straightforward. For example:\r\n\r\n&lt;i&gt;That is Sarah\u2019s breakfast.\r\nThat is her breakfast.\r\nThat is my breakfast. &lt;\/i&gt;\r\n\r\nThings get more complicated, however, when you start using joint possessive nouns and pronouns. For example:\r\n\r\n&lt;i&gt;That is John and Sarah\u2019s breakfast.\r\nThat is Sarah\u2019s and his breakfast.&lt;\/i&gt;\r\n\r\nWhen joint proper nouns are possessive (John and Sarah), only the last noun (in this case, Sarah) needs to be possessive. While it\u2019s possible that the sentence could be interpreted as being about John himself and also about Sarah\u2019s breakfast, the reader will likely infer that the breakfast belongs to both John and Sarah. \r\n\r\nWhen using joint possessive pronouns, however, &lt;i&gt;both&lt;\/i&gt; pronouns need to be possessive. It\u2019s often best to restructure the sentence rather than using the pronouns in the same structure as you would proper nouns. For example:\r\n\r\n&lt;i&gt;That is his and her breakfast.\r\nThis sentence structure sounds odd. It would be best to rewrite the sentence this way:\r\nThat is their breakfast.&lt;\/i&gt;\r\nor\r\n&lt;i&gt;That breakfast belongs to them.&lt;\/i&gt;\r\n\r\nAnother example:\r\n&lt;i&gt;That is Sarah\u2019s and my breakfast.\r\nThis sentence sounds awkward. It is best to restructure the sentence as:\r\nThat is our breakfast.&lt;\/i&gt;\r\nor\r\n&lt;i&gt;That breakfast is Sarah\u2019s and mine.&lt;\/i&gt;\r\n\r\nTo learn more about possessive pronouns, read &lt;a href=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/2014\/the-basics-on-whos-and-whose\/\"&gt;this post about who\u2019s vs. whose&lt;\/a&gt;.\r\n","post_title":"Learning to use joint possessive nouns and pronouns might make you a grammar cop.","post_excerpt":"","post_status":"publish","comment_status":"open","ping_status":"open","post_password":"","post_name":"learning-joint-possessive-nouns-and-pronouns-might-make-you-a-grammar-cop","to_ping":"","pinged":"","post_modified":"2015-01-05 16:00:03","post_modified_gmt":"2015-01-06 00:00:03","post_content_filtered":"","post_parent":0,"guid":"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/?p=10539","menu_order":0,"post_type":"post","post_mime_type":"","comment_count":"0","filter":"raw"},{"ID":10433,"post_author":"1001005","post_date":"2015-01-02 14:40:52","post_date_gmt":"2015-01-02 22:40:52","post_content":"[media]&lt;img src=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/wp-content\/uploads\/2014\/12\/no-smoking-aloud-fail.png\" alt=\"no-smoking-aloud-fail\" width=\"600\" height=\"315\" class=\"aligncenter size-full wp-image-10519\" \/&gt;[\/media]\r\n\r\nWords that sound similar are often confused. Here are two pairs of words that sound similar when you say them, but are spelled differently.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Aloud vs. allowed&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Aloud&lt;\/strong&gt; means audibly, as opposed to silent:\r\nYou have to speak your thoughts &lt;strong&gt;aloud&lt;\/strong&gt;; I can\u2019t read your mind.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Allowed&lt;\/strong&gt; means with permission or consent:\r\nHe is &lt;strong&gt;allowed&lt;\/strong&gt; to go to parties, but only on weekends.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Passed vs. past&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Passed&lt;\/strong&gt; is the past tense of pass, which means to move from one place to another or to change from one state to another. (There are a few other meanings of the word pass, but for this example we\u2019re using the most straightforward meaning.) For example:\r\nShe &lt;strong&gt;passed&lt;\/strong&gt; me the mashed potatoes so I could have another serving.\r\n \r\n&lt;strong&gt;Past&lt;\/strong&gt; means the period of time that has already happened, in contrast to the present or the future.\r\nLet's leave that silly argument in the &lt;strong&gt;past&lt;\/strong&gt;. It's time to move on and live in the present. \r\n\r\nFor explanations of other confusing word pairs read &lt;a href=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/2014\/learn-the-difference-between-these-commonly-confused-words\/ \"&gt;this blog post about the difference between then and than&lt;\/a&gt;.\r\n\r\n","post_title":"Don't let these two word pairs confuse you.","post_excerpt":"","post_status":"publish","comment_status":"open","ping_status":"open","post_password":"","post_name":"dont-let-these-two-word-pairs-confuse-you","to_ping":"","pinged":"","post_modified":"2015-01-02 14:40:52","post_modified_gmt":"2015-01-02 22:40:52","post_content_filtered":"","post_parent":0,"guid":"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/?p=10433","menu_order":0,"post_type":"post","post_mime_type":"","comment_count":"0","filter":"raw"},{"ID":10427,"post_author":"1001005","post_date":"2015-01-02 07:58:15","post_date_gmt":"2015-01-02 15:58:15","post_content":"[media]&lt;img src=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/wp-content\/uploads\/2014\/12\/EQWDERGle-670x351.png\" alt=\"mystery quote\" width=\"670\" height=\"351\" class=\"aligncenter size-medium wp-image-10515\" \/&gt;[\/media]\r\n\r\nWho said the quote above? &lt;strong&gt;Gustave Flaubert!&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\n\r\nGustave Flaubert was a French author, most famous for writing the novel &lt;em&gt;Madame Bovary&lt;\/em&gt;. Flaubert lived from 1821 to 1880 and had a habit of making bold statements like the one above. He is also credited with this quote: \r\n\r\n\u201cTo be stupid, and selfish, and to have good health are the three requirements for happiness; though if stupidity is lacking, the others are useless.\u201d","post_title":"Which writer is credited with this quote about reading?","post_excerpt":"","post_status":"publish","comment_status":"open","ping_status":"open","post_password":"","post_name":"which-writer-is-credited-with-this-quote-about-reading","to_ping":"","pinged":"","post_modified":"2015-01-02 07:58:15","post_modified_gmt":"2015-01-02 15:58:15","post_content_filtered":"","post_parent":0,"guid":"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/?p=10427","menu_order":0,"post_type":"post","post_mime_type":"","comment_count":"0","filter":"raw"},{"ID":10419,"post_author":"1001005","post_date":"2014-12-30 08:25:36","post_date_gmt":"2014-12-30 16:25:36","post_content":"[media]&lt;img src=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/wp-content\/uploads\/2014\/12\/Vintage-543le-670x351.png\" alt=\"reader&#039;s new year&#039;s resolution\" width=\"670\" height=\"351\" class=\"aligncenter size-medium wp-image-10499\" \/&gt;[\/media]\r\n\r\nKeep your love of books alive in the new year by making one (or all) of these three new year's resolutions:\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;1. Read more&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\nNo matter how much you read on a regular basis, chances are that you can read more. Find times in your day to fit in a bit of reading, even if it\u2019s just a few minutes. Keep a novel in your bag and read while you\u2019re in line at the grocery store. Bookmark some interesting news stories on your phone and read them during your morning bus ride. Read to your kids at night before bed. Make reading a regular part of your routine.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;2. Write regularly&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\nJust as you can fit in more reading time, you can also make space in your day for writing. Try waking up 15 to 20 minutes earlier two or three days per week, and use the extra time to work on a story. Or, if you\u2019re more interested in journaling, keep a notebook and pen on your bedside table so you can jot down some notes from the day before you fall asleep. If you set aside specific times to write and stick to a schedule, you\u2019ll be amazed at what you can accomplish.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;3. Share your passion&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\nIf you\u2019re a self-proclaimed book lover, share your love of books! When you finish a book, make a point to loan it to a friend or family member. If you read a great article online, email it to someone who you think might be interested in the subject matter. If you want to be really ambitious, start a monthly book club with a group of friends or coworkers and take turns choosing which book you read and discuss.\r\n\r\nThere are lots of ways to grow and expand your love of the written word in 2015. If your new year\u2019s resolution includes reading or writing, share it in the comments! ","post_title":"What the heck does \u201cAuld Lang Syne\u201d mean?","post_excerpt":"","post_status":"publish","comment_status":"open","ping_status":"open","post_password":"","post_name":"what-the-heck-does-auld-lang-syne-mean","to_ping":"","pinged":"","post_modified":"2014-12-29 08:08:05","post_modified_gmt":"2014-12-29 16:08:05","post_content_filtered":"","post_parent":0,"guid":"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/?p=10413","menu_order":0,"post_type":"post","post_mime_type":"","comment_count":"1","filter":"raw"},{"ID":10375,"post_author":"1001005","post_date":"2014-12-26 09:50:18","post_date_gmt":"2014-12-26 17:50:18","post_content":"[media]&lt;img src=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/wp-content\/uploads\/2014\/12\/4ole.png\" alt=\"4ole\" width=\"600\" height=\"315\" class=\"aligncenter size-full wp-image-10483\" \/&gt;[\/media]\r\n\r\nMany of the words we use in everyday conversation today originated from Latin roots. Even some of the abbreviations we use were originally Latin terms. Here are a few abbreviations we frequently use that you might not know are actually Latin:\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;a.m.&lt;\/strong&gt;, from the Latin ante meridiem, refers to the time before midday.\r\n&lt;strong&gt;p.m.&lt;\/strong&gt;, from the Latin post meridiem, refers to the time after midday.\r\n&lt;strong&gt;c.v.&lt;\/strong&gt;, from the Latin curriculum vitae, is the name for a document that contains relevant work experience and educational background information. Another commonly used term for c.v. is r\u00e9sum\u00e9. \r\n&lt;strong&gt;et al.&lt;\/strong&gt;, from the Latin et alii, means roughly \u2018and others.\u2019 \r\n&lt;strong&gt;etc.&lt;\/strong&gt;, from the Latin et cetera, means roughly \u2018and the rest.\u2019\r\n&lt;strong&gt;e.g.&lt;\/strong&gt;, from the Latin exempli gratia, means roughly \u2018for the sake of an example.\u2019\r\n&lt;strong&gt;i.e.&lt;\/strong&gt;, from the Latin id est, means roughly \u2018in other words,\u2019 or \u2018that is.\u2019\r\n&lt;strong&gt;lb.&lt;\/strong&gt;, from the Latin libra, translates to scales, and is used to indicate a one pound weight of measure.\r\n&lt;strong&gt;p.s.&lt;\/strong&gt;, from the Latin post scriptum, means roughly \u2018after what has been written.\u2019\r\n\r\nTo learn more about Latin abbreviations, read &lt;a href=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/2014\/know-your-latin-i-e-vs-e-g\/\"&gt;this post about the difference between i.e. and e.g&lt;\/a&gt;.\r\n","post_title":"The Latin origins of these common abbreviations might surprise you","post_excerpt":"","post_status":"publish","comment_status":"open","ping_status":"open","post_password":"","post_name":"the-latin-origins-of-these-common-abbreviations-might-surprise-you","to_ping":"","pinged":"","post_modified":"2014-12-26 09:50:18","post_modified_gmt":"2014-12-26 17:50:18","post_content_filtered":"","post_parent":0,"guid":"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/?p=10375","menu_order":0,"post_type":"post","post_mime_type":"","comment_count":"0","filter":"raw"},{"ID":10371,"post_author":"1001005","post_date":"2014-12-24 08:27:06","post_date_gmt":"2014-12-24 16:27:06","post_content":"[media]&lt;img class=\"alignleft size-full wp-image-10487\" src=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/wp-content\/uploads\/2014\/12\/punctuation1.png\" alt=\"punctuation\" width=\"600\" height=\"315\" \/&gt;\r\n[\/media]\r\n\r\nOne way to use dashes in your writing is to emphasize parenthetical information. Parenthetical information can also be put inside parenthesis \u2014 hence the term, parenthetical \u2014 but dashes call attention to the information, while parentheses take the emphasis away from the information.\r\n\r\nBe sure to use two dashes: one at the beginning of the parenthetical information and one at the end.\r\nHere are a few examples:\r\n\r\nWhile I was shopping \u2013 wandering aimlessly up and down the aisles, actually \u2013 I ran into our old neighbor.\r\nAn etymological dictionary is one of the few books \u2013 no, it\u2019s the only book \u2013 you\u2019ll ever need.\r\nThere has recently been an increase \u2013 though opposed fiercely by many people \u2013 in alternative education practices.\r\n\r\nTo learn more about ways to use dashes in your writing, read our &lt;a href=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/handbook\/punctuation\/dash\/\"&gt;Grammarly Handbook section on dashes&lt;\/a&gt;.","post_title":"Learn how to use dashes in your writing","post_excerpt":"","post_status":"publish","comment_status":"open","ping_status":"open","post_password":"","post_name":"learn-how-to-use-dashes-in-your-writingb","to_ping":"","pinged":"","post_modified":"2014-12-26 14:05:39","post_modified_gmt":"2014-12-26 22:05:39","post_content_filtered":"","post_parent":0,"guid":"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/?p=10371","menu_order":0,"post_type":"post","post_mime_type":"","comment_count":"2","filter":"raw"},{"ID":10359,"post_author":"1001005","post_date":"2014-12-23 09:13:05","post_date_gmt":"2014-12-23 17:13:05","post_content":"[media]&lt;img src=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/wp-content\/uploads\/2014\/12\/686tg.png\" alt=\"rudyard kipling quote\" width=\"600\" height=\"315\" class=\"aligncenter size-full wp-image-10423\" \/&gt;[\/media]\r\n\r\nQuotation marks are used to identify words that someone has said. When used within a sentence, quotation marks identify which part of the sentence is being quoted and which is not. Quotation marks always come in pairs; the first set \u201copens\u201d the quote and the second set \u201ccloses\u201d the quote. For example:\r\n\r\nHe said, &lt;strong&gt;\u201c&lt;\/strong&gt;I\u2019m going over to Jennifer\u2019s house for a few hours.&lt;strong&gt;\u201d&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\nThe poster on the wall read, &lt;strong&gt;\u201c&lt;\/strong&gt;Hang in there.&lt;strong&gt;\"&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\nNo matter what her professor said, Anna didn\u2019t think she was &lt;strong&gt;\u201c&lt;\/strong&gt;over-exuberant.&lt;strong&gt;\u201d&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\n\r\nTreat anything within quotation marks as separate from the rest of the sentence you\u2019ve written, and make sure it has its own correct punctuation. For example, if the quote is a full sentence, it must begin with a capital letter, even though it is within the larger structure of another sentence:\r\n\r\nThe little boy said, &lt;strong&gt;\u201c&lt;\/strong&gt;No matter what, I\u2019m not cleaning my room!&lt;strong&gt;\u201d&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\nThe N in \"No\" must be capitalized because it belongs to the quote. The exclamation mark goes inside the quotation marks because it belongs to the quote.\r\n\r\nTo learn about other kinds of punctuation, read &lt;a href=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/2014\/how-to-use-a-colon-when-writing\/\"&gt;this blog post about colons&lt;\/a&gt;. For example:\r\nIt\u2019s that time of year again, when &lt;strong&gt;Winter&lt;\/strong&gt; blankets all creatures with somniferous snow and whispers to them, \u201cRest, children, rest.\u201d","post_title":"A quick guide to proper nouns","post_excerpt":"","post_status":"publish","comment_status":"open","ping_status":"open","post_password":"","post_name":"a-quick-guide-to-proper-nouns","to_ping":"","pinged":"","post_modified":"2014-12-22 07:56:18","post_modified_gmt":"2014-12-22 15:56:18","post_content_filtered":"","post_parent":0,"guid":"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/?p=10365","menu_order":0,"post_type":"post","post_mime_type":"","comment_count":"0","filter":"raw"},{"ID":10279,"post_author":"1001005","post_date":"2014-12-18 08:59:25","post_date_gmt":"2014-12-18 16:59:25","post_content":"[media]&lt;img src=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/wp-content\/uploads\/2014\/12\/A-an-the.png\" alt=\"A an the\" width=\"600\" height=\"315\" class=\"aligncenter size-full wp-image-10355\" \/&gt;[\/media]\r\n\r\nArticles come before a noun and indicate if the noun is specific or general. For example:\r\nPlease give me &lt;strong&gt;the&lt;\/strong&gt; sandwich.\r\nor\r\nPlease give me &lt;strong&gt;a&lt;\/strong&gt; sandwich.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;\u2018The\u2019&lt;\/strong&gt; refers to a specific sandwich. The speaker wants a particular sandwich to be given to him or her. \r\n&lt;strong&gt;\u2018A\u2019&lt;\/strong&gt; refers to sandwiches in general. Any sandwich will do; the speaker just really wants a sandwich.\r\n\r\nAnother example:\r\nHe is walking down &lt;strong&gt;the&lt;\/strong&gt; street. (We know exactly which street he is walking down.)\r\nor\r\nHe is walking down &lt;strong&gt;a&lt;\/strong&gt; street. (He could be walking down any street, anywhere.)\r\n\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;\u2018A\u2019&lt;\/strong&gt; and &lt;strong&gt;\u2018an\u2019&lt;\/strong&gt; are both used to refer to general nouns.
