For example:\r\nI bought &lt;strong&gt;some&lt;\/strong&gt; candles.\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Some&lt;\/strong&gt; of my Facebook friends live in San Diego. \r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Some&lt;\/strong&gt; is only used in a question when an offer is being made, or there is a request for something. For example:\r\nDo you need &lt;strong&gt;some&lt;\/strong&gt; assistance?\r\nCould I have &lt;strong&gt;some&lt;\/strong&gt; more of that pecan pie?\r\n\r\nUse &lt;strong&gt;any&lt;\/strong&gt; when you\u2019re talking about something that is not specific. For example:\r\nI don\u2019t want &lt;strong&gt;any&lt;\/strong&gt; help.\r\nThere isn\u2019t &lt;strong&gt;any&lt;\/strong&gt; pecan pie.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Any&lt;\/strong&gt; also describes an indefinite or an incomplete quantity when the speaker can not, or does not need or want to specify a number or an exact amount. For example:\r\nHe does not have &lt;strong&gt;any&lt;\/strong&gt; friends on the team.\r\n\r\nTo learn more about the differences between similar words, read &lt;a href=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/2014\/the-basics-on-whos-and-whose\/\"&gt;this blog post&lt;\/a&gt; about who's vs. whose. ","post_title":"Learn the Difference Between 'Some' and 'Any' in Less Than a Minute","post_excerpt":"","post_status":"publish","comment_status":"open","ping_status":"open","post_password":"","post_name":"learn-the-difference-between-some-and-any-in-less-than-a-minute","to_ping":"","pinged":"","post_modified":"2014-12-18 08:50:20","post_modified_gmt":"2014-12-18 16:50:20","post_content_filtered":"","post_parent":0,"guid":"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/?p=10293","menu_order":0,"post_type":"post","post_mime_type":"","comment_count":"2","filter":"raw"},{"ID":10287,"post_author":"1001005","post_date":"2014-12-16 11:26:29","post_date_gmt":"2014-12-16 19:26:29","post_content":"[media]&lt;img src=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/wp-content\/uploads\/2014\/12\/verbs-in-bar.png\" alt=\"verb tenses\" width=\"600\" height=\"315\" class=\"aligncenter size-full wp-image-10343\" \/&gt;[\/media]\r\n\r\nWhen using multiple verbs in a clause, it\u2019s important to keep them in the same tense. For example:\r\nI &lt;strong&gt;went&lt;\/strong&gt; to the store, &lt;strong&gt;buy&lt;\/strong&gt; an apple, and &lt;strong&gt;ate&lt;\/strong&gt; it on the way home.\r\n\u2018Went\u2019 and \u2018ate\u2019 are both in past tense, but \u2018buy\u2019 is in present tense. \r\n\r\nTo fix this sentence, use \u2018bought,\u2019 which is the past tense of \u2018buy.\u2019\r\nI &lt;strong&gt;went&lt;\/strong&gt; to the store, &lt;strong&gt;bought&lt;\/strong&gt; an apple, and &lt;strong&gt;ate&lt;\/strong&gt; it on the way home.\r\n\r\nAnother example:\r\nI &lt;strong&gt;went&lt;\/strong&gt; to the store and &lt;strong&gt;bought&lt;\/strong&gt; an apple, and now I &lt;strong&gt;am eating&lt;\/strong&gt; it.\r\n\r\n\u2018Went\u2019 and \u2018bought\u2019 are both in past tense and \u2018eating\u2019 is in present tense. In this case, the two different verb tenses are in two separate clauses, separated by a comma, so it is ok that they\u2019re in different tenses. \r\n\r\nFor more information on verb tenses, read &lt;a href=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/2014\/learn-the-difference-between-gone-and-went-in-less-than-a-minute\/\"&gt;this blog post&lt;\/a&gt; about the difference between gone and went.\r\n","post_title":"An Introduction to Verb Tenses","post_excerpt":"","post_status":"publish","comment_status":"open","ping_status":"open","post_password":"","post_name":"an-introduction-to-verb-tenses","to_ping":"","pinged":"","post_modified":"2015-01-05 11:22:01","post_modified_gmt":"2015-01-05 19:22:01","post_content_filtered":"","post_parent":0,"guid":"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/?p=10287","menu_order":0,"post_type":"post","post_mime_type":"","comment_count":"1","filter":"raw"},{"ID":10319,"post_author":"6","post_date":"2014-12-11 05:30:38","post_date_gmt":"2014-12-11 13:30:38","post_content":"&lt;strong&gt;If book worms ruled the world, what do you think the world would look like? whose = belonging to who\/whom\" width=\"670\" height=\"321\" \/&gt;[\/media]\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Who\u2019s&lt;\/strong&gt; is a contraction for \u2018who is\u2019 or \u2018who has.\u2019 For example:\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Who\u2019s&lt;\/strong&gt; organizing our holiday gift exchange?\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Who\u2019s&lt;\/strong&gt; had experience organizing group activities?\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Whose&lt;\/strong&gt; is a possessive pronoun meaning \u2018belonging to who or whom.\u2019 For example:\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Whose&lt;\/strong&gt; gift is still sitting under the tree?\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Whose&lt;\/strong&gt; turn is it to clean up after the party?\r\n\r\nFor more tips on confusing word pairs, &lt;a href=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/2014\/who-vs-whom-its-not-as-complicated-as-you-might-think\/\"&gt;read this post&lt;\/a&gt; about who vs. whom.","post_title":"The Basics on Who's and Whose","post_excerpt":"","post_status":"publish","comment_status":"open","ping_status":"open","post_password":"","post_name":"the-basics-on-whos-and-whose","to_ping":"","pinged":"","post_modified":"2014-12-09 05:27:08","post_modified_gmt":"2014-12-09 13:27:08","post_content_filtered":"","post_parent":0,"guid":"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/?p=10173","menu_order":0,"post_type":"post","post_mime_type":"","comment_count":"0","filter":"raw"},{"ID":10201,"post_author":"6","post_date":"2014-12-08 03:44:39","post_date_gmt":"2014-12-08 11:44:39","post_content":"Odds are good that the words \"subjective and objective cases\" mean nothing to you. \"Case\" is grammarian and linguistic jargon for categories of nouns based on the function of the noun in relation to the verbs and prepositions in a sentence.  \r\n\r\nIt is even more confusing in English language because many cases have disappeared. Modern Ukrainian language has seven cases. Finnish has fifteen cases. Even German, from the same language family, has four. English has three, which is about as simple as it gets linguistically. Presently, English cases are rarely taught because most native speakers are expected to develop intuitively a \"feel\" for the correct case usage. \r\n\r\nHowever, many people still make mistakes, and the lack of educational reinforcement ends up making the confusion worse. This article will be focusing on two cases\u2014subject (nominative) and object, in particular with first-person pronouns. \r\n\r\n[media]&lt;img src=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/wp-content\/uploads\/2014\/12\/interchangeable-670x350.png\" alt=\"I and Me. They are not interchangeable. \" width=\"670\" height=\"350\" class=\"alignleft size-large wp-image-9963\" \/&gt;[\/media]\r\n\r\n&lt;i&gt;The first thing that must be understood is that &lt;strong&gt;I&lt;\/strong&gt; and &lt;strong&gt;me&lt;\/strong&gt; are not the same.&lt;\/i&gt; They are not synonyms. They are not interchangeable. &lt;i&gt;Why? They are different cases.&lt;\/i&gt;\r\n\r\n&lt;b&gt;Subject Pronouns, like I&lt;\/b&gt;\r\n\r\nSubject pronouns are those pronouns that perform the action in a sentence. They are &lt;b&gt;I, you, he, she, we, they,&lt;\/b&gt; and &lt;b&gt;who.&lt;\/b&gt; Any noun performing the main action in the sentence, like these pronouns, is a subject and is categorized as subjective case (nominative case). English grammar requires that the subject come before the verb in a sentence (except in questions). \r\n\r\n&lt;i&gt;&lt;b&gt;I make&lt;\/b&gt; cookies every Sunday for my co-workers.&lt;\/i&gt; \r\n\r\nIn this sentence, \"I\" is the actor (subject pronoun) performing the action of making (verb). To check for correct case, you should be able to replace &lt;b&gt;I&lt;\/b&gt; with any other subject pronoun, modify the verb according to person and number, and have a clear sentence. In a simple declarative sentence like this, it may seem unnecessary, but checking case this way is a good habit for when you are checking more complicated sentences. &lt;b&gt;Remember that subject nouns absolutely always are the actors in sentences. If action is implied, you should use subject nouns.&lt;\/b&gt;\r\n\r\n\r\n&lt;b&gt;Object Pronouns, like Me&lt;\/b&gt;\r\n\r\nObject pronouns are those pronouns that receive the action in a sentence. They are &lt;b&gt;me, you, him, her, us, them,&lt;\/b&gt; and &lt;b&gt;whom.&lt;\/b&gt; Any noun receiving an action in the sentence, like these pronouns, is an object and is categorized as objective case.* An object pronoun can also be used after prepositions, i.e. &lt;i&gt;\"I will go &lt;b&gt;with him&lt;\/b&gt;.\"&lt;\/i&gt; In this article, we will only focus on nouns as recipients as these are the most frequently confused. With few exceptions, English grammar requires that objects follow the verb in a sentence. \r\n\r\n&lt;i&gt;I &lt;b&gt;give them&lt;\/b&gt; cookies every week.&lt;\/i&gt; \r\n\r\nIn this sentence, \"I\" is the actor (subject pronoun) performing the action of making (verb). \"Them\" is the noun receiving the giving; it is the object. You wouldn't ever use a subject noun after \"give\" here. To most native speakers and even English learners, the following incorrect sentence should grate on the ears:\r\n\r\n&lt;i&gt;I &lt;b&gt;give they &lt;\/b&gt; cookies every week.&lt;\/i&gt;\r\n\r\nEek. Now consider the following example:\r\n\r\n&lt;i&gt;She &lt;b&gt;gave Jim and I&lt;\/b&gt; extra cookies.&lt;\/i&gt;\r\n\r\nCan you spot the problem? \"I\"\u2014just like \"they\"\u2014is a subject noun living in an object noun's place. The correct sentence should have \"me\" (the object pronoun) following the verb:\r\n\r\n&lt;i&gt;She &lt;b&gt;gave Jim and me&lt;\/b&gt; extra cookies.&lt;\/i&gt;\r\n\r\nTo check for correct case, you should be able to replace &lt;b&gt;me&lt;\/b&gt; with any other object pronoun and have a clear sentence. &lt;b&gt;Remember that object nouns always are the recipients in sentences. If an action is happening to a noun, you should use object nouns.&lt;\/b&gt;\r\n\r\n&lt;b&gt;Test Sentences&lt;\/b&gt;\r\n&lt;i&gt;Mark the following sentences as correct or incorrect. If the sentence is incorrect, write the correct form. Check your answers &lt;a href=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/2014\/the-basics-on-subject-and-object-pronouns-answers\/\"&gt;here&lt;\/a&gt;.&lt;\/b&gt;&lt;\/i&gt;\r\n\r\n1) Martha and Jim are in the office. \r\n2) She is telling him a joke.\r\n3) Jim made a copy of the report for Lucy and I.\r\n4) I have to go. Mark is calling for Lucy and me. \r\n5) Our co-workers love Martha's cookies. \r\n6) Jim and me are planning a surprise for Sue's birthday.\r\n7) We will give them extra cookies next week. \r\n8) Who did you give the money to?\r\n\r\n&lt;b&gt;How do you remember to use subject and object pronouns correctly? How did you do on the test sentences?&lt;\/b&gt;\r\n\r\n*There are two degrees of objective case that receive actions\u2014direct and indirect, which correspond to accusative and dative cases roughly. ","post_title":"The Basics on Subject and Object Pronouns","post_excerpt":"","post_status":"publish","comment_status":"open","ping_status":"open","post_password":"","post_name":"the-basics-on-subject-and-object-pronouns-b","to_ping":"","pinged":"","post_modified":"2014-12-08 03:44:39","post_modified_gmt":"2014-12-08 11:44:39","post_content_filtered":"","post_parent":0,"guid":"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/?p=10201","menu_order":0,"post_type":"post","post_mime_type":"","comment_count":"0","filter":"raw"},{"ID":10169,"post_author":"1001005","post_date":"2014-12-08 03:07:23","post_date_gmt":"2014-12-08 11:07:23","post_content":"[media]&lt;img src=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/wp-content\/uploads\/2014\/12\/786-670x321.png\" alt=\"What does the x in xmas mean?\" width=\"670\" height=\"321\" class=\"alignleft size-large wp-image-10195\" \/&gt;[\/media]\r\n\r\nWords change over time\u2014their meaning, their usage, and even their spelling. Take Christmas and its shortened version, Xmas, for example. We see Xmas written nearly everywhere, from holiday advertisements to Christmas cards. The word Christmas comes from the combination of the words \u2018Christ\u2019 and \u2018mass.\u2019 But where did the X in Xmas come from, and what does it mean?\r\n\r\nIn the Greek alphabet, X is the symbol for the letter \u2018chi.\u2019 Chi (or X) is the first letter in the Greek word for Christ. In the early days of the Christian church, Christians used the letter X as a secret symbol to indicate their membership in the church to others.\r\n\r\nIf you know the Greek meaning of X, Xmas and Christmas essentially mean the same thing: Christ + mas = Christmas.\r\n\r\nLooking for more holiday-themed fun? Vintage turkey, Thanksgiving ecard\" width=\"670\" height=\"350\" \/&gt;\r\n&nbsp;\r\n&lt;img class=\"alignleft size-large wp-image-9905\" src=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/wp-content\/uploads\/2014\/11\/Thanksgiving-your-caption_5th-670x350.png\" alt=\"Chapter 1 of &quot;Murder Most Fowl&quot; Vintage turkey, Thanksgiving ecard\" width=\"670\" height=\"350\" \/&gt;\r\n&nbsp;\r\n&lt;strong&gt;For more fun, check out the other &lt;a href=\"https:\/\/www.facebook.com\/grammarly\/photos\/a.158139670871698.33824.139729956046003\/923697527649238\/?type=1\"&gt;great caption suggestions&lt;\/a&gt;!&lt;\/strong&gt;","post_title":"Turkey-tastic ecards to share with your friends!","post_excerpt":"","post_status":"publish","comment_status":"open","ping_status":"open","post_password":"","post_name":"turkey-tastic-ecards-to-share-with-your-friends","to_ping":"","pinged":"","post_modified":"2014-11-26 02:52:26","post_modified_gmt":"2014-11-26 10:52:26","post_content_filtered":"","post_parent":0,"guid":"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/?p=9909","menu_order":0,"post_type":"post","post_mime_type":"","comment_count":"0","filter":"raw"},{"ID":9555,"post_author":"6","post_date":"2014-11-14 06:45:42","post_date_gmt":"2014-11-14 14:45:42","post_content":"[media]&lt;img src=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/wp-content\/uploads\/2014\/11\/grammar_doctor_color_colon_BLOG-670x350.png\" alt=\"I&#039;m a grammar doctor. Now let me check that colon.\" width=\"670\" height=\"350\" class=\"alignleft size-large wp-image-9557\" \/&gt;[\/media]\r\n\r\nColons often get confused with semi-colons, but they have different effects. \r\n\r\n&lt;i&gt;Don\u2019t look at me: look at the next guy!&lt;\/i&gt;\r\n\r\nThe colon makes the first part of the sentence unimportant. It\u2019s always the second part of the sentence that will give you the relevant information, although you need the first part of the sentence to tell you that; it\u2019s as if the first part of the sentence is the tattletale. Colons are used to make lists or to separate one idea from the one which follows.\r\n\r\n&lt;b&gt;Colon Between Independent Clauses&lt;\/b&gt;\r\n\r\nA colon can be used to separate two independent clauses when a) the second clause is &lt;i&gt;directly&lt;\/i&gt; related to the first clause (not just vaguely related) and b) when the emphasis is on the second clause. While you can also use a semi-colon or a period between two independent-yet-related clauses, the colon is a little softer than the period, but a little harder than the semi-colon.\r\n\r\nIn British English, the first letter after the colon is only capitalized if it\u2019s a proper noun or an acronym; in American English, the convention depends on the format but it\u2019s frequently capitalized. If your writing must follow a specific format, check the rules for that format before capitalizing (if in doubt, it might be safer to use the lower case).\r\n\r\n&lt;i&gt;Maggie wears a brimmed cap at all times: Strong light often gives her a headache.&lt;\/i&gt;\r\n\r\n&lt;b&gt;Colon With Independent Clause Followed By A List, A Quotation, Appositive, Or Other Idea Directly Related To The Independent Clause&lt;\/b&gt;\r\n\r\nAn independent clause is followed by a colon if the dependent clause is &lt;i&gt;directly&lt;\/i&gt; related to the independent clause. The dependent clause should clarify or give more information about the independent clause. The beginning of the dependent clause shouldn\u2019t be capitalized unless it begins with a proper noun or an acronym, or it\u2019s a quote which originally began with a capital.\r\n\r\n&lt;i&gt;We have everything we need for the baby: diapers, sleepers, toys, bottles and pacifiers.\r\n\r\nThe teacher spoke firmly to her students: \u201cPlease do not throw paper airplanes or spitballs at the back of my head!\u201d&lt;\/i&gt;\r\n\r\n&lt;b&gt;Other Uses Of Colon&lt;\/b&gt;\r\n\r\nWe also use a colon to separate hours, minutes and seconds when recording the time.\r\n\r\n&lt;i&gt;Come over around 8:15.&lt;\/i&gt;\r\n\r\nIn literature, it can be used to separate chapters and verses, or acts and scenes.\r\n\r\n&lt;i&gt;Turn to III: iv in the play.&lt;\/i&gt;\r\n\r\nColons can be used to identify the name of the speaker in a lengthy section of dialogue such as in a play.\r\n\r\n&lt;i&gt; Martin: Where have you been?\r\nAlan: Out. Walking.&lt;\/i&gt;\r\n\r\nIn a business letter, the salutation can be followed by a colon.\r\n\r\n&lt;i&gt;To Whom It May Concern:&lt;\/i&gt;\r\n\r\n&lt;b&gt;Misuse Of Colon&lt;\/b&gt;\r\n\r\nA colon should not separate a noun from its verb, a verb from its object or subject complement, a preposition from its object, nor a subject from its predicate. If you are unsure, it would be better to leave the colon out than misuse it.\r\n\r\nIncorrect: &lt;i&gt;The four directions are: north, south, east and west.&lt;\/i&gt;\r\n\r\nWhile this sentence does have a list, the colon is incorrectly used because it separates the verb &lt;i&gt;are&lt;\/i&gt; from its object &lt;i&gt;north, south, east&lt;\/i&gt; and &lt;i&gt;west.&lt;\/i&gt; The colon could be removed, or the sentence could be re-written to use the colon properly (with an independent clause on one side of the colon and the list on the other side).\r\n\r\nCorrect: &lt;i&gt;There are four directions: north, south, east and west.&lt;\/i&gt;\r\n\r\nIn the following, the colon should be removed from this sentence because it separates the preposition to from its objects &lt;i&gt;Rome, Israel&lt;\/i&gt; and &lt;i&gt;Egypt.&lt;\/i&gt;\r\n\r\nIncorrect: &lt;i&gt;When I graduate, I want to go to: Rome, Israel and Egypt.&lt;\/i&gt;\r\nCorrect: &lt;i&gt;When I graduate, I want to go to Rome, Israel and Egypt.&lt;\/i&gt;\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n","post_title":"How to Use a Colon When Writing","post_excerpt":"","post_status":"publish","comment_status":"open","ping_status":"open","post_password":"","post_name":"how-to-use-a-colon-when-writing","to_ping":"","pinged":"","post_modified":"2014-11-14 06:45:42","post_modified_gmt":"2014-11-14 14:45:42","post_content_filtered":"","post_parent":0,"guid":"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/?p=9555","menu_order":0,"post_type":"post","post_mime_type":"","comment_count":"1","filter":"raw"},{"ID":9513,"post_author":"1001005","post_date":"2014-11-13 16:45:38","post_date_gmt":"2014-11-14 00:45:38","post_content":"[media]&lt;img src=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/wp-content\/uploads\/2014\/11\/thanksgiving-free-books_edited-1-670x350.png\" alt=\"happy thanksgiving free books online\" width=\"670\" height=\"350\" class=\"aligncenter size-medium wp-image-9547\" \/&gt;[\/media]\r\n\r\nThanksgiving is almost here, and it can be a busy time of year. Between traveling, cooking, and spending time with family, how are you supposed to get any reading done?\r\nHere are two short, Thanksgiving-themed stories you can read online for free while you\u2019re waiting for your flight to grandma\u2019s house, killing time while the turkey cooks, or trying to digest the big meal.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;An Old-Fashioned Thanksgiving&lt;\/strong&gt; by Louisa May Alcott\r\nA family works together to create a Thanksgiving feast and strengthens their relationships in the process.\r\n\r\nExcerpt:\r\n\u201cNovember had come; the crops were in, and barn, buttery, and bin were overflowing with the harvest that rewarded the summer's hard work. English is weird.\"&gt;&lt;img src=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/wp-content\/uploads\/2014\/11\/english_is_weird_alarm_clock-670x350.png\" alt=\"english_is_weird_alarm_clock\" width=\"670\" height=\"350\" class=\"alignleft size-large wp-image-9529\" \/&gt;&lt;\/a&gt;[\/media]\r\n\r\n3 Words That Drive English Language Learners Bonkers\r\n\r\nEnglish language is a confusing language because so many different languages (from different language families) have played a role in what we speak today. Here are three words and phrases that aren't consistent or are just plain strange that can drive people learning the language to madness. \r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;1. Teachers taught, but preachers don't \"praught.\" &lt;\/strong&gt;\r\n\r\nThe past simple of teach is taught, but the past simple of preach is preached. \r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;2. Feet smell. Noses run. &lt;\/strong&gt;\r\n\r\nTo smell in English has two meanings. One is the act of detecting a smell, and the other is the act of producing an unpleasant smell. \"Feet smell\" is using the second definition and is a common collocation. A runny nose is a nose that is stuffed up and is producing a lot of mucus, usually during a cold or allergies. Feet can also run, but in that case it means to literally run.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;3. An alarm goes off by going on.&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\n\r\nThis one is strange because, like many phrasal verbs, the combination of the verb go and the preposition completely changes the meaning of the written phrase. In this case \"to go off\" means to start working or go into action. It is often used when describing a sound, e.g. \"The timer is going off. The food is done!\"\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Which funny examples can you think of? Here is a breakdown of how to use \u2018lay\u2019 in each verb tense.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Present tense:&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Lay&lt;\/strong&gt; needs a direct object:\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Lay&lt;\/strong&gt; all your cards on the table. (Cards is the direct object.)\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Lie&lt;\/strong&gt; doesn\u2019t need one:\r\nI love to &lt;strong&gt;lie&lt;\/strong&gt; down for a nap on Sunday afternoon.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Past tense:&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Lay&lt;\/strong&gt; is the past tense of lie:\r\nI love to &lt;strong&gt;lie&lt;\/strong&gt; down for a nap on Sunday afternoon.\r\nin the past tense becomes\r\nI &lt;strong&gt;lay&lt;\/strong&gt; down for a nap last Sunday afternoon.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Laid&lt;\/strong&gt; is the past tense of lay:\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Lay&lt;\/strong&gt; all your cards on the table.\r\nin the past tense becomes\r\nShe &lt;strong&gt;laid&lt;\/strong&gt; all her cards on the table.\r\n\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Past participle:&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Lain&lt;\/strong&gt; is the past participle of lie:\r\nI love to &lt;strong&gt;lie&lt;\/strong&gt; down for a nap on Sunday afternoon.\r\nin the past participle becomes\r\nI have &lt;strong&gt;lain&lt;\/strong&gt; down for a nap every Sunday afternoon for a month.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Laid&lt;\/strong&gt; is the past participle of lay:\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Lay&lt;\/strong&gt; all your cards on the table.\r\nin the past participle becomes\r\nShe has &lt;strong&gt;laid&lt;\/strong&gt; all her cards on the table.\r\n\r\nFor more grammar tips, check out our tutorials on &lt;a href=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/2014\/theres-an-easy-way-to-remember-the-difference-between-these-two-words\/ \"&gt;affect vs. effect&lt;\/a&gt; is an individual segment or piece of something: \r\nA computer is made up of many parts; if you hold the hard drive in your hand, you are holding &lt;strong&gt;a part&lt;\/strong&gt;.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Apart&lt;\/strong&gt; can mean that something separated from something else:\r\nThe islands were less than 50 miles &lt;strong&gt;apart&lt;\/strong&gt;.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Apart&lt;\/strong&gt; can also indicate an exception when it is paired with the word \u2018from\u2019:\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Apart&lt;\/strong&gt; ","post_title":"Follow this spelling rule to set your writing apart","post_excerpt":"","post_status":"publish","comment_status":"open","ping_status":"open","post_password":"","post_name":"follow-this-spelling-rule-to-set-your-writing-apart","to_ping":"","pinged":"","post_modified":"2014-11-06 17:05:46","post_modified_gmt":"2014-11-07 01:05:46","post_content_filtered":"","post_parent":0,"guid":"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/?p=9457","menu_order":0,"post_type":"post","post_mime_type":"","comment_count":"0","filter":"raw"},{"ID":9449,"post_author":"1001005","post_date":"2014-11-06 16:15:58","post_date_gmt":"2014-11-07 00:15:58","post_content":"[media]&lt;img src=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/wp-content\/uploads\/2014\/11\/past-participle-670x350.png\" alt=\"past participle\" width=\"670\" height=\"350\" class=\"aligncenter size-medium wp-image-9453\" \/&gt;[\/media]\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Went&lt;\/strong&gt; is the past tense of go.\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Gone&lt;\/strong&gt; is the past participle of go.\r\n\r\nExample:\r\nI &lt;strong&gt;go&lt;\/strong&gt; to the store. (present tense)\r\nI &lt;strong&gt;went&lt;\/strong&gt; to the store. (past tense)\r\nI &lt;strong&gt;have gone&lt;\/strong&gt; to the store. (past participle)\r\n\r\nIf you aren\u2019t sure whether to use gone or went, remember that &lt;strong&gt;gone&lt;\/strong&gt; always needs an auxiliary verb before it (has, have, had, is, am, are, was, were, be), but &lt;strong&gt;went&lt;\/strong&gt; doesn't.\r\n\r\nCorrect:\r\nI could &lt;strong&gt;have gone&lt;\/strong&gt; to the store yesterday.\r\n\r\nIncorrect:\r\nI could &lt;span style=\"color: #ff0000;\"&gt;have went&lt;\/span&gt; to the store.","post_title":"Learn the difference between gone and went in less than a minute","post_excerpt":"","post_status":"publish","comment_status":"open","ping_status":"open","post_password":"","post_name":"learn-the-difference-between-gone-and-went-in-less-than-a-minute","to_ping":"","pinged":"","post_modified":"2014-12-22 12:05:25","post_modified_gmt":"2014-12-22 20:05:25","post_content_filtered":"","post_parent":0,"guid":"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/?p=9449","menu_order":0,"post_type":"post","post_mime_type":"","comment_count":"0","filter":"raw"},{"ID":9407,"post_author":"1001005","post_date":"2014-11-04 09:19:54","post_date_gmt":"2014-11-04 17:19:54","post_content":"[media]&lt;img src=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/wp-content\/uploads\/2014\/11\/loose-lose.png\" alt=\"confusing word pairs\" width=\"670\" height=\"350\" class=\"aligncenter size-full wp-image-9409\" \/&gt;[\/media]\r\n\r\nThese words look and sound similar, but they mean different things. Here\u2019s a handy guide to using them correctly:\r\n\r\nLose vs. loose\r\nWhen you &lt;strong&gt;lose&lt;\/strong&gt; something, you have to find it again.\r\nWhen something is &lt;strong&gt;loose&lt;\/strong&gt;, it isn\u2019t tight enough.\r\n\r\nKernel vs. colonel\r\nA &lt;strong&gt;kernel&lt;\/strong&gt; is a part of a seed, nut, or fruit. Think of a kernel of corn. \r\nA &lt;strong&gt;colonel&lt;\/strong&gt; is a high-ranking officer, like a colonel in the army. \r\n\r\nWeary vs. wary\r\nWhen you feel &lt;strong&gt;weary&lt;\/strong&gt; you are very tired.\r\nWhen you are &lt;strong&gt;wary&lt;\/strong&gt; you are being cautious and careful.","post_title":"Three word pairs that confuse many writers","post_excerpt":"","post_status":"publish","comment_status":"open","ping_status":"open","post_password":"","post_name":"three-word-pairs-that-confuse-many-writers","to_ping":"","pinged":"","post_modified":"2014-11-04 09:19:54","post_modified_gmt":"2014-11-04 17:19:54","post_content_filtered":"","post_parent":0,"guid":"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/?p=9407","menu_order":0,"post_type":"post","post_mime_type":"","comment_count":"0","filter":"raw"},{"ID":9399,"post_author":"6","post_date":"2014-11-04 07:10:33","post_date_gmt":"2014-11-04 15:10:33","post_content":"[media]&lt;img src=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/wp-content\/uploads\/2014\/11\/work-less-read-more-card-670x350.png\" alt=\"Work less. Intellectual improvement tips. \" width=\"670\" height=\"350\" \/&gt;[\/media]\r\n\r\nFor most sapiosexuals, there are two extra words here.\r\n\r\nYour brain and the ability to use it effectively have an enormous impact on your day-to-day life. In everything from work to relationships to self-esteem, the mind matters. How we use our brains has an influence not only on how we perceive ourselves but how others perceive us. We've put together some tips to help you improve your brain function.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;1. Do more things manually.&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\n\r\nOften we use tools that automate or speed up processes. Now, it is not realistic for you to go without Excel for crunching big amounts of information but regularly doing a bit of number crunching by hand sans calculator will keep you sharp. Likewise you could ditch the GPS and the task manager for a map and a good ol' handwritten to-do list, respectively. (Read more about why handwriting is good for your brain &lt;a href=\"http:\/\/www.lifehack.org\/articles\/productivity\/you-will-remember-information-longer-you-hand-write-notes.html\"&gt;here&lt;\/a&gt;.)\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;2. Read. Then read some more.&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\n\r\nReading regularly not only improves your knowledge of a certain topic, but it also builds vocabulary, reinforces standard language conventions as well as gives you something interesting to discuss in conversation. If you are reading fiction, there is &lt;a href=\"http:\/\/www.scientificamerican.com\/article\/novel-finding-reading-literary-fiction-improves-empathy\/\"&gt;some evidence&lt;\/a&gt; that it can actually make you more empathetic and emotionally aware.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;3. Take care of your health.&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\n\r\nThis should be a no-brainer (pun intended). To keep your brain in top shape (and your entire body for that matter), you need to care for it. This means getting enough sleep, drinking more water, getting fresh air, exercising regularly as well as eating plenty of healthy fats, lean protein, fruits and vegetables. If this is not your strength, start with making one small improvement a week (e.g. replacing one soda a day with tea or water with lemon). If you struggle with it, keep trying. Sometimes it takes a while for a new habit, even a small one, to stick.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;4. Question everything.&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\n\r\nThis is not just a skeptic's mantra. Asking questions and thinking about the responses are the products of a curious mind. Curiosity is your brain's best friend; it can lead you to discover new ideas and perspectives. It can give you healthier perspective on failure (\u201cI have not failed. I\u2019ve just found 10,000 ways that won\u2019t work.\u201d - Thomas A. Edison). Curiosity forces your brain to consider new modes of thought, and this pushing outside of one's comfort zone is a great way to build brain power.\r\n\r\nThere are many other tricks and habits that can help you build a beautiful brain. &lt;strong&gt;What do you do to improve and maintain your grey matter?&lt;\/strong&gt;","post_title":"How to Make Your Mind More Attractive","post_excerpt":"","post_status":"publish","comment_status":"open","ping_status":"open","post_password":"","post_name":"how-to-make-your-mind-more-attractive","to_ping":"","pinged":"","post_modified":"2014-11-04 07:53:21","post_modified_gmt":"2014-11-04 15:53:21","post_content_filtered":"","post_parent":0,"guid":"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/?p=9387","menu_order":0,"post_type":"post","post_mime_type":"","comment_count":"0","filter":"raw"},{"ID":9379,"post_author":"1001005","post_date":"2014-11-03 10:53:01","post_date_gmt":"2014-11-03 18:53:01","post_content":"[media]&lt;img src=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/wp-content\/uploads\/2014\/11\/etc-et-al.png\" alt=\"etc et al\" width=\"670\" height=\"350\" class=\"aligncenter size-full wp-image-9381\" \/&gt;[\/media]\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Etc.&lt;\/strong&gt; alt=\"Irony (n) \u2014 drawings of trees on paper\" width=\"670\" height=\"350\" class=\"alignleft size-large wp-image-9341\" \/&gt;[\/media]\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;A quick clarification on irony&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\n\r\nMany people think that irony is anything that is unexpected. It is not\u2014well, not traditionally. Classically, irony (verbal) means that a phrase is used with a meaning opposite from the literal meaning or effect of the phrase. Irony (situational) means that an event or situation occurs with a different outcome or effect than what was intended. With irony, the appearance of something is incongruent with the reality of the phrase or situation.\r\n\r\nAccording to this interpretation, the above example is not considered true irony. It is a funny coincidence, however. and &lt;strong&gt;An&lt;\/strong&gt; are both indefinite articles, meaning they refer to an unspecified thing or quantity, rather than something specific. For example:\r\n\r\nAfter high school, I hope to take classes at the local community college. (The speaker is referring to a specific college and uses the definite article \u2018the.\u2019)\r\n\r\nAfter high school, I hope to attend a good college. (The speaker is referring to any college of a certain caliber and uses the indefinite article \u2018a.\u2019)\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;A vs. An&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\nUse &lt;strong&gt;A&lt;\/strong&gt; before words that begin with a consonant, or words that begin with a vowel but sound like they begin with a consonant:\r\n\r\nPlease give me &lt;strong&gt;a&lt;\/strong&gt; moment to collect my thoughts. (It's complicated because we tend to write as we speak.) This is particularly common with frequently used words ending in \"ed\"\u2014regular past participles of verb\u2014especially when the present tense or infinitive verb form has a near identical intonation to the past participle.\r\n\r\nIf all this jargon is making your head hurt, just consider how difficult it is to hear the difference between &lt;i&gt;suppose&lt;\/i&gt; and &lt;i&gt;supposed&lt;\/i&gt; or between &lt;i&gt;use&lt;\/i&gt; and &lt;i&gt;used&lt;\/i&gt;. You can't always trust your ear!\r\n\r\nHere is why it matters that you remember that final D when writing:\r\n\r\nIncorrect: \"I'm use to them.\"\r\n\r\nIs this a typo of &lt;i&gt;used&lt;\/i&gt; or &lt;i&gt;useful&lt;\/i&gt;? Perhaps &lt;i&gt;of&lt;\/i&gt; is missing? It's not clear. \r\n\r\nCorrect: \"I'm used to them.\"\r\n\r\nIncorrect: \"We use to make cookies often.\" \r\n\r\nIs a letter missing or an entire word missing?\r\n\r\nCorrect: \"We used to make cookies often.\" \r\n\r\nIncorrect: \"He is suppose to visit us.\"\r\n\r\nThis one is pretty easy to parse out with the error, but the missing D makes the writer appear careless. \r\n\r\nCorrect: \"He is supposed to visit us.\"\r\n\r\nBONUS: When you say \"supposably\" there is a 95% chance you really mean \"supposedly.\"\r\n","post_title":"You\u2019re not \u201csuppose\u201d to do anything. We have loads of contractions in English to improve speed and fluidity of speech. Unfortunately, when writing such words, many people neglect the correct form and spelling. \r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;A correctly formed contraction will have an apostrophe that replaces the letters that have been dropped in the merger. &lt;\/strong&gt;\r\n\r\nDo not &#8594; don't\r\n\r\nCould have &#8594; could've\r\n\r\nMight not &#8594; mightn't\r\n\r\nWe are &#8594; we're\r\n\r\nIt is\/It has &#8594; it's\r\n\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Correctly spelled contractions only drop the letters missing in the merger and do not change any of the remaining letters.&lt;\/strong&gt; \r\n\r\nShould have &#8594; should've NOT \"shoulda\" or \"should of\"\r\n\r\nDid not &#8594; didn't NOT \"didn\" or \"din\"\r\n\r\nThe exception is &lt;i&gt;will not&lt;\/i&gt;, which becomes &lt;i&gt;won't&lt;\/i&gt;.\r\n","post_title":"Coulda, woulda, shoulda. . . learned how to use contractions. Here\u2019s what you need to know.","post_excerpt":"","post_status":"publish","comment_status":"open","ping_status":"open","post_password":"","post_name":"coulda-woulda-shoulda-learned-how-to-use-contractions-heres-what-you-need-to-know","to_ping":"","pinged":"","post_modified":"2015-01-21 15:13:41","post_modified_gmt":"2015-01-21 23:13:41","post_content_filtered":"","post_parent":0,"guid":"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/?p=9087","menu_order":0,"post_type":"post","post_mime_type":"","comment_count":"0","filter":"raw"},{"ID":9057,"post_author":"6","post_date":"2014-10-15 04:09:53","post_date_gmt":"2014-10-15 11:09:53","post_content":"[media]&lt;img src=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/wp-content\/uploads\/2014\/10\/its_not_its.png\" alt=\"It&#039;s \u2260 its\" width=\"636\" height=\"365\" class=\"alignleft size-full wp-image-9059\" \/&gt;[\/media]\r\n\r\nFor some reason, many people use &lt;a href=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/handbook\/punctuation\/apostrophe\/\"&gt;apostrophes&lt;\/a&gt; (this thing: ' ) willy-nilly and usually not correctly. While putting apostrophes in unusual places is generally innocuous, leaving them out of words where they are needed can be confusing. If you still don't have a firm grasp on how to use apostrophes, it is time you learned once and for all. \r\n\r\nThere are three main rules for how to use and not use apostrophes. \r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Apostrophes are used to show possession. &lt;\/strong&gt;\r\n\r\nPossession is ownership. For many pronouns, we have special forms to show possession, e.g. my, mine, your, yours, their, etc. For all other nouns, we use an apostrophe  plus S to show possession. So to write \"The flower belongs to Jane\" using the possessive construction, we would write, \"The flower is Jane's.\" \r\n\r\nLearn more about possessive constructions &lt;a href=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/handbook\/punctuation\/apostrophe\/1\/apostrophe-use-possessive-case-of-nouns\/\"&gt;here&lt;\/a&gt;. \r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Apostrophes signify when a letter has been removed.&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\n\r\nSometimes, in English, we combine words; we shorten them. We do this for ease of use and fluidity of thought and speech. A large group of these merged, shortened words are called contractions, and you are probably so familiar with them that you don't even notice them. Did you notice that one? Maybe you didn't. Did you catch that one? &lt;i&gt;Don't&lt;\/i&gt; and &lt;i&gt;didn't&lt;\/i&gt; are contractions. They are mergers of two words; &lt;i&gt;do not&lt;\/i&gt; and &lt;i&gt;did not&lt;\/i&gt;, respectively. You'll notice that in both examples the O in &lt;i&gt;not&lt;\/i&gt; gets cut and replaced with an apostrophe in the contraction. There are also a few common words that drop letters. When letters are dropped, you should use an apostrophe, i.e. 'til.\r\n\r\nNow the truth is that you probably aren't going to confuse too many people by \"forgetting\" the apostrophe in many contractions. (I am going to get flack for that from some of our die-hards.) However, there is a reasonable case to be made for not skipping the apostrophe in contractions and shortened forms of words. (Am I redeemed?) \r\n\r\nFirst, not all contractions are obvious without the apostrophe. &lt;i&gt;Its&lt;\/i&gt; (possessive of &lt;i&gt;it&lt;\/i&gt;) and &lt;i&gt;it's&lt;\/i&gt; (contraction of &lt;i&gt;it is&lt;\/i&gt;) are great examples. There is a huge difference in meaning between them and forgetting the apostrophe can result in unclear or embarrassing sentences. &lt;i&gt;We're&lt;\/i&gt; is another tricky one. \r\n\r\nSecond, properly punctuated contractions make reading easier. Consider \"Were goin to get em at the store.\" Without the apostrophes, it's not immediately clear what the intended meaning of the sentence is. Also imagine how autocorrect might treat these words. \"Goin\" may get swapped for \"gone,\" and \"em\" may get swapped for \"I'm\" or \"in.\" Now consider the correctly punctuated sentence, \"We're goin' to get 'em at the store.\" Things are just clearer with properly placed apostrophes.\r\n\r\nFor more details on contractions and omissions, read &lt;a href=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/handbook\/punctuation\/apostrophe\/2\/apostrophe-use-contractions-and-omissions\/\"&gt;this article&lt;\/a&gt;. \r\n\r\nFinally, &lt;strong&gt;apostrophes are&lt;\/strong&gt; (almost) &lt;strong&gt;never needed with plural nouns.&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\n\r\nThe main reason not to use apostrophes with plural nouns (nouns of more than one) is that the apostrophe-S construction indicates possession! The meaning of \"Did you see an apples sale?\" and \"Did you go to Apple's sale?\" is clear. One is a sale of apples and the other is a sale at Apple. This rule also applies for writing numbers (as in 10s, 20s, 100s, etc.) and letters (\"Mind your Ps and Qs!\"). I'm not sure why so many people insist on writing numbers, letters, and acronyms with apostrophes, but it's unnecessary and still conveys possession.* If this is not making sense, check out &lt;a href=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/handbook\/punctuation\/apostrophe\/4\/apostrophe-use-forming-plurals-of-lowercase-or-upp\/\"&gt;our article&lt;\/a&gt;. \r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;What tips or tricks would you add?&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\n\r\n* There is one exception. When writing letters, if the standard plural form (adding S) creates a word that looks like a different word\u2014i.e. Is\u2014it is 100% OK to use an apostrophe. \"Dot your I's!\" ","post_title":"Why you absolutely should learn how to use apostrophes","post_excerpt":"","post_status":"publish","comment_status":"open","ping_status":"open","post_password":"","post_name":"why-you-absolutely-should-learn-how-to-use-apostrophes","to_ping":"","pinged":"","post_modified":"2015-01-21 15:13:11","post_modified_gmt":"2015-01-21 23:13:11","post_content_filtered":"","post_parent":0,"guid":"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/?p=9057","menu_order":0,"post_type":"post","post_mime_type":"","comment_count":"1","filter":"raw"},{"ID":9051,"post_author":"6","post_date":"2014-10-15 03:08:23","post_date_gmt":"2014-10-15 10:08:23","post_content":"[media]&lt;img src=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/wp-content\/uploads\/2014\/10\/easy_easy_to_buy_for_card-670x350.png\" alt=\"I&#039;m easy to buy for. ","post_title":"I\u2019ll admit it. ","post_title":"I\u2019m quaking with laughter.","post_excerpt":"","post_status":"publish","comment_status":"open","ping_status":"open","post_password":"","post_name":"im-quaking-with-laughter","to_ping":"","pinged":"","post_modified":"2014-10-15 03:04:41","post_modified_gmt":"2014-10-15 10:04:41","post_content_filtered":"","post_parent":0,"guid":"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/?p=9045","menu_order":0,"post_type":"post","post_mime_type":"","comment_count":"0","filter":"raw"},{"ID":9039,"post_author":"6","post_date":"2014-10-15 03:00:08","post_date_gmt":"2014-10-15 10:00:08","post_content":"[media]&lt;img src=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/wp-content\/uploads\/2014\/10\/but_first_coffee_fall_color-670x350.png\" alt=\"But first, coffee.\" width=\"670\" height=\"350\" class=\"alignleft size-large wp-image-9041\" \/&gt;[\/media]\r\n\r\nGood morning, everyone. ","post_excerpt":"","post_status":"publish","comment_status":"open","ping_status":"open","post_password":"","post_name":"and-then-coffee-always-coffee","to_ping":"","pinged":"","post_modified":"2014-10-15 03:00:08","post_modified_gmt":"2014-10-15 10:00:08","post_content_filtered":"","post_parent":0,"guid":"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/?p=9039","menu_order":0,"post_type":"post","post_mime_type":"","comment_count":"0","filter":"raw"},{"ID":9029,"post_author":"6","post_date":"2014-10-15 02:55:00","post_date_gmt":"2014-10-15 09:55:00","post_content":"[media]\r\n&lt;a href=\"http:\/\/www.dilbert.com\"&gt;&lt;img class=\"alignleft size-large wp-image-9139\" src=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/wp-content\/uploads\/2014\/10\/dilbert_aspect1-91to1-670x350.jpg\" alt=\"dilbert poor email writing\" width=\"670\" height=\"350\" \/&gt;&lt;\/a&gt;&lt;a href=\"http:\/\/www.dilbert.com\"&gt;Dilbert is a comic strip by Scott Adams, http:\/\/www.dilbert.com\r\n[\/media]&lt;\/a&gt;\r\n\r\n","post_title":"Stop making email errors today","post_excerpt":"","post_status":"publish","comment_status":"open","ping_status":"open","post_password":"","post_name":"stop-making-email-errors-today","to_ping":"","pinged":"","post_modified":"2015-01-21 15:12:48","post_modified_gmt":"2015-01-21 23:12:48","post_content_filtered":"","post_parent":0,"guid":"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/?p=9029","menu_order":0,"post_type":"post","post_mime_type":"","comment_count":"0","filter":"raw"},{"ID":8993,"post_author":"6","post_date":"2014-10-12 04:43:52","post_date_gmt":"2014-10-12 11:43:52","post_content":"[media]&lt;img class=\"alignleft size-large wp-image-8995\" src=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/wp-content\/uploads\/2014\/10\/zombies_passive_voice-670x350.png\" alt=\"If you can insert &quot;by zombies&quot; after the verb, it's passive voice. \" width=\"670\" height=\"350\" \/&gt;[\/media]\r\n\r\nRebecca Johnson you're a genius. Teachers everywhere should rejoice, and so should any students who haven't yet mastered passive voice. If you're still new to this and aren't sure how passive voice works or why Rebecca's work-around is so boo-tiful, let us explain. \r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Passive Voice&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\n\r\nOdds are high that you have, at some point in your life, had passive voice marked on an essay or piece of writing. Odds are higher that you probably had no idea what in the world that meant. Basically, it is this. Passive voice is when the noun being acted upon is made the subject of the sentence. (Active voice is when the noun doing the action is the subject.) Let me explain with an example. \r\n\r\n&lt;i&gt;\"The house was haunted.\"&lt;\/i&gt;\r\n\r\n\"The house\" is the noun being acted upon, in other words \"house\" is the object of the verb \"to haunt\". It's clear here that the house is not doing the haunting. It is not doing the action. It is receiving the action. However, it is the subject of the sentence, which makes this sentence a passive voice sentence. (In an active voice sentence, the noun performing the action should be the subject. In this case, the active voice version would be: \"Ghosts haunted the house.\")\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Using \"by zombies\" to help identify passive voice\r\n&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\nIf you are still having trouble understanding passive voice, here is where Rebecca's idea can help. Usually (but not always), passive voice can include the actor, usually following the verb. Basically, if you can add \"by zombies\" after the verb and it makes sense, you probably have passive voice. \r\n\r\n&lt;i&gt;\"The town was attacked (by zombies).\"&lt;\/i&gt;\r\n\r\nYes, this makes sense; therefore, it is a passive voice sentence. To make this sentence active, you will need to put the noun doing the action in the subject location of the sentence. That is: \"Zombies attacked the town.\" Now we can check for passive voice:\r\n\r\n&lt;i&gt;\"Zombies attacked the town (by zombies).\" &lt;\/i&gt;\r\n\r\nNo, this doesn't make sense; therefore it is active voice.\r\n\r\nThese are simple examples and not every passive voice sentence will be identifiable with this trick, but it will help for a significant number of examples. &lt;strong&gt;If you would like to help others learn how to identify the passive voice, leave your tips, tricks, and exceptions in the comments! &lt;\/strong&gt;\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n","post_title":"A scary-easy way to help you find passive voice! ","post_excerpt":"","post_status":"publish","comment_status":"open","ping_status":"open","post_password":"","post_name":"a-scary-easy-way-to-help-you-find-passive-voice","to_ping":"","pinged":"","post_modified":"2015-01-21 15:11:43","post_modified_gmt":"2015-01-21 23:11:43","post_content_filtered":"","post_parent":0,"guid":"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/?p=8993","menu_order":0,"post_type":"post","post_mime_type":"","comment_count":"8","filter":"raw"},{"ID":8983,"post_author":"6","post_date":"2014-10-12 03:56:19","post_date_gmt":"2014-10-12 10:56:19","post_content":"[media]\r\n&lt;img class=\"alignleft size-large wp-image-8985\" src=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/wp-content\/uploads\/2014\/10\/affect_effect_difference_text_card-670x350.png\" alt=\"Generally speaking, affect is a verb; effect is a noun.\" width=\"670\" height=\"350\" \/&gt;\r\n[\/media]\r\n\r\n&nbsp;\r\n\r\nUnlike many other spelling \"rules\" in the English language, there are only a few exceptions to this rule. Which is why it's remarkable that so few people understand it. So, here it goes:\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Affect\u2014with an a\u2014is a verb. &lt;\/strong&gt;\r\n\r\n\"The man's pleas didn't affect the judge.\"\r\n\"I was deeply affected by the film.\"\r\n\"How does that affect us?\"\r\n\"Her style of acting is too affected.\"\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Effect\u2014with an e\u2014is a noun. &lt;\/strong&gt;\r\n\r\n\"The man's pleas had no effect on the judge.\"\r\n\"The film had a profound effect on me.\"\r\n\"What will the effect be?\"\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;The exceptions (and why they almost aren't important for you.)&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\n\r\nThe main exceptions to this rule are simple.\r\n\r\nFirst, for some reason, some medical writing (especially in psychology) uses \"affect\" as a noun. Unless you are writing for a psychology journal, this exception probably won't apply to you.\r\n\r\nSecond, there are some fixed constructions that use \"effect\" in a verbal way to mean \"to accomplish\". The most common is \"to effect change.\" You may very well decide to use this phrase, but beware that it is now borderline clich\u00e9, and you'll likely be better off paraphrasing in your own unique way.\r\n","post_title":"One easy spelling rule that you should start using today","post_excerpt":"","post_status":"publish","comment_status":"open","ping_status":"open","post_password":"","post_name":"one-easy-spelling-rule-that-you-can-start-using-today","to_ping":"","pinged":"","post_modified":"2015-01-21 15:11:12","post_modified_gmt":"2015-01-21 23:11:12","post_content_filtered":"","post_parent":0,"guid":"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/?p=8983","menu_order":0,"post_type":"post","post_mime_type":"","comment_count":"0","filter":"raw"},{"ID":8933,"post_author":"1001005","post_date":"2014-10-09 15:11:29","post_date_gmt":"2014-10-09 22:11:29","post_content":"[media]&lt;img src=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/wp-content\/uploads\/2014\/10\/ieeg1.png\" alt=\"ieeg\" width=\"670\" height=\"350\" class=\"aligncenter size-full wp-image-9011\" \/&gt;[\/media]\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;i.e.&lt;\/strong&gt; is the abbreviation of the Latin phrase \u2018id est,\u2019 meaning \u2018that is.\u2019\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;e.g.&lt;\/strong&gt; is the abbreviation of the Latin phrase \u2018exempli gratia,\u2019 meaning \u2018for example.\u2019\r\n\r\nUse i.e. when you want to give further explanation for something.\r\nExample: After work I\u2019ll walk over to the Thunderdome, &lt;strong&gt;i.e.&lt;\/strong&gt;, the new sports arena a few blocks away.\r\n\r\nUse e.g. when you want to give a few examples but not a complete list.\r\nExample: I love drinking holiday-related beverages, &lt;strong&gt;e.g.&lt;\/strong&gt;, hot chocolate, apple cider, pumpkin spice lattes.\r\n","post_title":"Know your Latin: i.e. vs. e.g. ","post_excerpt":"","post_status":"publish","comment_status":"open","ping_status":"open","post_password":"","post_name":"know-your-latin-i-e-vs-e-g","to_ping":"","pinged":"","post_modified":"2015-01-21 15:10:48","post_modified_gmt":"2015-01-21 23:10:48","post_content_filtered":"","post_parent":0,"guid":"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/?p=8933","menu_order":0,"post_type":"post","post_mime_type":"","comment_count":"3","filter":"raw"},{"ID":8913,"post_author":"1001005","post_date":"2014-10-07 17:26:05","post_date_gmt":"2014-10-08 00:26:05","post_content":"[media]\u00a0&lt;a href=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/wp-content\/uploads\/2014\/10\/condescending.png\"&gt;&lt;img class=\"alignnone size-full wp-image-8915\" src=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/wp-content\/uploads\/2014\/10\/condescending.png\" alt=\"condescending\" width=\"670\" height=\"350\" \/&gt;&lt;\/a&gt;[\/media]\r\n\r\nThis pun came from one of our Facebook fans. the TV show &lt;em&gt;Downton Abbey&lt;\/em&gt; might remember a particularly witty exchange between Violet Grantham, the Dowager Countess of Grantham, and Matthew Crawley. Matthew explains that he will have time to catch up on his new duties during the weekend and the Dowager Countess asks, \u201cWhat is a \u2018weekend\u2019?\u201d\r\n\r\nWords evolve and develop over time, and some words that are incredibly common today were once virtually obsolete. third grader?","post_excerpt":"","post_status":"publish","comment_status":"open","ping_status":"open","post_password":"","post_name":"can-you-spell-better-than-a-third-grader","to_ping":"","pinged":"","post_modified":"2015-01-21 order. \" width=\"670\" height=\"350\" class=\"alignleft size-large wp-image-8825\" \/&gt; [\/media]\r\n\r\n","post_title":"What cool English word facts do you know?","post_excerpt":"","post_status":"publish","comment_status":"open","ping_status":"open","post_password":"","post_name":"what-cool-english-word-facts-do-you-know","to_ping":"","pinged":"","post_modified":"2014-10-01 05:19:17","post_modified_gmt":"2014-10-01 12:19:17","post_content_filtered":"","post_parent":0,"guid":"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/?p=8823","menu_order":0,"post_type":"post","post_mime_type":"","comment_count":"0","filter":"raw"},{"ID":8805,"post_author":"6","post_date":"2014-10-01 02:51:50","post_date_gmt":"2014-10-01 09:51:50","post_content":"[media]&lt;img class=\"alignleft size-large wp-image-8807\" src=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/wp-content\/uploads\/2014\/10\/you-had-me-at-670x350.png\" alt=\"You had me at the proper use of &quot;you're.&quot;\" width=\"670\" height=\"350\" \/&gt;[\/media]\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;10 Rules for Dating an English Language Lover&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\n\r\nDo you value strong English language skills? Perhaps more critically, would you like to impress someone who values strong English skills? There are several facts about English grammar and usage that will inevitably impress those who love proper grammar, punctuation, and spelling. Additionally, there are certain \"acts of love\" that will be a whole quiver of Cupid's arrows for wooing a English language lover.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;1. Always proofread. &lt;\/strong&gt;\r\n\r\nWhether you're sending a text message or a thoughtful love letter, always read, revise, and\u2014when necessary\u2014rewrite.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;2. Learn the differences between the most frequently used homophones. &lt;\/strong&gt;\r\n\r\nHomophones are words that sound the same but are spelled differently. Some of the most common mix-ups are &lt;em&gt;your-you're, to-too-two&lt;\/em&gt;, and &lt;em&gt;there-their-they're&lt;\/em&gt;. Here is &lt;a href=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/handbook\/mechanics\/spelling\/6\/words-that-sound-alike\/\"&gt;a great list&lt;\/a&gt; of some more of the most common homophones and how they are correctly used.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;3. Read more books. &lt;\/strong&gt;\r\n\r\nBookworms aren't the only ones who value books. English language lovers cherish books because reading helps build a strong vocabulary and improves thought construction in speaking and writing. (Though to be honest, most English language lovers are proud bookworms, and many proud bookworms are lovers of language.) It is always a great idea to be able to discuss passions with friends and lovers. In order to discuss books, however, you must\u2014you guessed it\u2014read books. If you are short on time, you can find some lists of quick-reads here and here.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;4. Stop misusing apostrophes. &lt;\/strong&gt;\r\n\r\nApostrophes are used only to show possession (ownership) or to signal missing letters, as in &lt;em&gt;we'll&lt;\/em&gt; (we will). They are never used to form the plural of a word. That is, you should never write: \"The cat's love to play.\" It is always \"The cats love to play.\"\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;5. Learn the proper use of &lt;em&gt;whom&lt;\/em&gt; and &lt;em&gt;whomever&lt;\/em&gt;.&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\n\r\n&lt;em&gt;Whom&lt;\/em&gt; is an objective form of &lt;em&gt;who&lt;\/em&gt;, just like &lt;em&gt;him&lt;\/em&gt; is the object form of &lt;em&gt;he&lt;\/em&gt;. Usually you should use &lt;em&gt;whom&lt;\/em&gt; wherever you would use &lt;em&gt;him&lt;\/em&gt;. \"To whom did you give the roses? To him.\" &lt;em&gt;Whomever&lt;\/em&gt; is a little more tricky but follows similar logic. &lt;em&gt;Whoever&lt;\/em&gt; is used as the subject of the sentence. &lt;em&gt;Whomever &lt;\/em&gt;is used as the object. So, for example:\r\n\r\n\"Whoever wants to go to the movies on Friday is welcome to join us.\"\r\n\r\n\"Give the package to whomever is at the front desk.\"\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;6. Write more.&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\n\r\nIt should come as no surprise that English language lovers are crazy about words. There is something about the permanence of the written word that intensifies thoughts. Writing letters and thoughts down\u2014whether on paper or beautifully in a text message\u2014will win the heart of a English language nerd.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;7. Never misspell &lt;em&gt;a lot&lt;\/em&gt;.&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\n\r\nA lot is always two words. Period.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;8. Avoid using non-words. &lt;\/strong&gt;\r\n\r\nThere are some words that you should not use around language pedants. Many English language lovers are fairly traditional in their interpretations of grammar and lexicon (vocabulary). Knowing this, it is probably safer to avoid using what are often considered non-words in English\u2014irregardless, supposably (actually a real word but often used incorrectly), etc.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;9. Learn basic punctuation and capitalization. &lt;\/strong&gt;\r\n\r\nYou probably learned the basics of capitalization and punctuation in elementary school but haven't reviewed them since. If you doubt the placement of that comma or the capitalization of the seasons (hint: you don't capitalize seasons), check out our writing guide on &lt;a href=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/handbook\/punctuation\/\"&gt;punctuation&lt;\/a&gt; and &lt;a href=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/handbook\/mechanics\/capitalization\/\"&gt;capitalization&lt;\/a&gt;.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;10. Correctly use &lt;em&gt;less&lt;\/em&gt; and &lt;em&gt;fewer&lt;\/em&gt; (as well as &lt;em&gt;much&lt;\/em&gt; and &lt;em&gt;many&lt;\/em&gt;).&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\n\r\n&lt;em&gt;Less&lt;\/em&gt; and &lt;em&gt;fewer&lt;\/em&gt; are commonly confused. (Next time you check out at the grocery store, look for \"12 items or less.\" Oops.) The same rule for remembering which to use works also for &lt;em&gt;much&lt;\/em&gt; and &lt;em&gt;many&lt;\/em&gt;. &lt;em&gt;Fewer&lt;\/em&gt; and &lt;em&gt;many&lt;\/em&gt; are used with countable, discrete items, like socks or coins. &lt;em&gt;Less&lt;\/em&gt; and &lt;em&gt;much&lt;\/em&gt; are used with uncountable, collective nouns, like milk or money.\r\n","post_title":"10 Rules for Dating an English Language Lover","post_excerpt":"","post_status":"publish","comment_status":"open","ping_status":"open","post_password":"","post_name":"10-rules-for-dating-an-english-language-lover","to_ping":"","pinged":"","post_modified":"2015-01-21 15:08:00","post_modified_gmt":"2015-01-21 23:08:00","post_content_filtered":"","post_parent":0,"guid":"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/?p=8805","menu_order":0,"post_type":"post","post_mime_type":"","comment_count":"2","filter":"raw"},{"ID":8801,"post_author":"6","post_date":"2014-10-01 02:28:53","post_date_gmt":"2014-10-01 09:28:53","post_content":"[media]&lt;img src=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/wp-content\/uploads\/2014\/05\/453c9cc26ba9fc75ff4046dd86c51f5b-537dcf1d4137f.png\" alt=\"grammar, nerd\" width=\"655\" height=\"342\" class=\"alignleft size-full wp-image-6412\" \/&gt;[\/media]\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;5 Signs That You\u2019re Hopelessly Addicted to Proper Grammar&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\nSo you've appointed yourself the honorary proofreader for your Facebook feed? There is nothing wrong with helping to improve the quality of your friends' (and friends of friends') writing, is there? If you're wondering if you're absolutely mad for good grammar, spelling, and punctuation, we put together a short list that should help you self-diagnose as a grammar addict. \r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;1. You check (and recheck and recheck) your online updates before posting. &lt;\/strong&gt;\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;2. You know how to use \"whom,\" and you're not afraid to use it in conversation. &lt;\/strong&gt;\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;3. You absolutely cannot imagine sending a text message without correct spelling and punctuation. &lt;\/strong&gt;\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;4. You regularly reference &lt;a href=\"http:\/\/www.amazon.com\/The-Elements-Style-Fourth-Edition\/dp\/020530902X\"&gt;Strunk and White&lt;\/a&gt;. \r\n&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\n&lt;strong&gt;5. You have turned down dates because of simple grammar errors or typos.&lt;\/strong&gt; \r\n\r\nHow many apply to you? What other signs of grammar addiction would you add?\r\n\r\n","post_title":"5 Signs That You\u2019re Hopelessly Addicted to Proper Grammar","post_excerpt":"","post_status":"publish","comment_status":"open","ping_status":"open","post_password":"","post_name":"5-signs-that-youre-hopelessly-addicted-to-proper-grammar","to_ping":"","pinged":"","post_modified":"2015-01-20 17:54:17","post_modified_gmt":"2015-01-21 01:54:17","post_content_filtered":"","post_parent":0,"guid":"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/?p=8801","menu_order":0,"post_type":"post","post_mime_type":"","comment_count":"0","filter":"raw"},{"ID":8739,"post_author":"6","post_date":"2014-09-29 10:21:39","post_date_gmt":"2014-09-29 17:21:39","post_content":"[media]&lt;img class=\"alignleft size-full wp-image-8741\" src=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/wp-content\/uploads\/2014\/09\/i-before-e.png\" alt=\"i before e\" width=\"670\" height=\"350\" \/&gt;[\/media]\r\n\r\nYou may have grown up hearing the spelling-related rhyme \u201cI before E except after C.\u201d This phrase is intended to help us remember how to spell words that include the letter combinations of \u201cie\u201d and \u201cei.\u201d However, the exceptions to this \u201crule\u201d have become so numerous that it\u2019s often more of a hindrance than a help.\r\n\r\nEven the extended versions of the rhyme:\r\nI before E except after C\r\nOr when sounded as \u201ca\u201d\r\nAs in \"neighbor\" or \"weigh.\"\r\nor\r\nI before E except after C\r\nWhen the sound is \u201cee.\"\r\nleave out a whole slew of exceptions.\r\n\r\n\u201cI before E except after C\u201d may often be quoted, but don\u2019t let yourself get tripped up by the rule that in implies. For example:\r\n\r\nDance the flamingo (Dance the flamenco)\r\nBeyond all apprehension (Beyond all comprehension)\r\nSupposedly, baseball great Yogi Berra once said, \u201cTexas has a lot of electrical votes.\" (He meant electoral votes.)\r\n\r\nOne of the most common malapropisms is \u201c&lt;strong&gt;for all intents and purposes&lt;\/strong&gt;.\u201d This phrase is often misspoken as:\r\nfor all intensive purposes\r\nor\r\nfor all intense and purposes.\r\n\r\nThe phrase \u201c&lt;strong&gt;for all intents and purposes&lt;\/strong&gt;\u201d originated in the 1500s and was mainly used in English law. Not all two-worded words in English have merged, however. Here are two of the most common words that people write as one that are still traditionally supposed to be separated. \r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;A lot&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\n\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;All right&lt;\/strong&gt; (increasingly accepted as one word, \"alright,\" but we don't suggest using it in formal settings.)\r\n\r\n\r\n","post_title":"A Very Short List Of \u201cWords\u201d that Are Written with Two Words","post_excerpt":"","post_status":"publish","comment_status":"open","ping_status":"open","post_password":"","post_name":"a-very-short-list-of-words-that-are-written-with-two-words","to_ping":"","pinged":"","post_modified":"2015-01-20 17:49:29","post_modified_gmt":"2015-01-21 01:49:29","post_content_filtered":"","post_parent":0,"guid":"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/?p=8553","menu_order":0,"post_type":"post","post_mime_type":"","comment_count":"0","filter":"raw"},{"ID":8533,"post_author":"6","post_date":"2014-09-18 06:17:46","post_date_gmt":"2014-09-18 13:17:46","post_content":"[media]&lt;img src=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/wp-content\/uploads\/2014\/09\/was_were_quiz_correction-670x350.png\" alt=\"How would you edit the following sentence? I wish I was traveling in Europe.\" width=\"670\" height=\"350\" class=\"alignleft size-large wp-image-8535\" \/&gt;[\/media]\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Did you find it? &lt;\/strong&gt;\r\n\r\nIt's the missing subjunctive mood. \r\n\r\n\"I wish I were traveling in Europe.\"\r\n\r\nSubjunctive mood is a technical term referring to the form of verb that we use to convey doubt, uncertainty or unrealness. In English, we mostly use subjunctive when we talk about desires, wishes or conditions that are not real. It usually turns up in dependent clauses. This a tough one to spot in English because the subjunctive form for many verbs looks the same as past simple tense. \r\n\r\n\"&lt;strong&gt;I had&lt;\/strong&gt; cake.\" (past simple, had)\r\n\r\n\"If &lt;strong&gt;we had&lt;\/strong&gt; cake, we would be eating it.\" (subjunctive mood, had)\r\n\r\nSubjunctive, specifically, should look just like the second-person singular past simple (past tense verb for you), which is why we should use \"were\" (second-person singular past simple) and not \"was\" (first-person, third-person singular past simple.)\r\n\r\n\"&lt;strong&gt;You loved&lt;\/strong&gt; swimming when you were a kid.\" (past simple)\r\n\r\n\"If &lt;strong&gt;you loved&lt;\/strong&gt; studying like you love swimming, you would get better marks!\" (subjunctive)\r\n\r\n\"&lt;strong&gt;She was&lt;\/strong&gt; a brilliant scientist.\" (past simple)\r\n\r\n\"I wish &lt;strong&gt;I were&lt;\/strong&gt; as dedicated to my research as she was.\" (subjunctive)\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;The good news? &lt;\/strong&gt;\r\nThe verb \"to be\" is practically the only verb that isn't the same for all conjugations. So that means, even if you are still struggling to understand the subjunctive, if you memorize that you should use \"were\" for unreal conditions (\"I wish\" and \"If\" phrases), you will be fine! \r\n\r\n\r\n&lt;i&gt;*There are two errors here, if you speak or use British English. In BrE, travelling is spelled with two Ls.&lt;\/i&gt;\r\n","post_title":"There is one glaring error here.* Make sure you can identify it. width=\"670\" height=\"350\" \/&gt;[\/media]\r\n","post_title":"The single most honest statement about proofreading ","post_excerpt":"","post_status":"publish","comment_status":"open","ping_status":"open","post_password":"","post_name":"the-single-most-honest-statement-about-proofreading","to_ping":"","pinged":"","post_modified":"2015-01-20 17:46:13","post_modified_gmt":"2015-01-21 01:46:13","post_content_filtered":"","post_parent":0,"guid":"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/?p=8425","menu_order":0,"post_type":"post","post_mime_type":"","comment_count":"0","filter":"raw"},{"ID":8411,"post_author":"6","post_date":"2014-09-11 11:54:50","post_date_gmt":"2014-09-11 18:54:50","post_content":"[media]&lt;img class=\"aligncenter size-full wp-image-8413\" src=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/wp-content\/uploads\/2014\/09\/whom-or-not-whom.png\" alt=\"whom or not whom\" width=\"670\" height=\"350\" \/&gt;[\/media]\r\n\r\nIf you\u2019re like most English speakers, you know that there\u2019s a difference between who and whom but you aren\u2019t sure what that difference is. We\u2019re here to help.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Who&lt;\/strong&gt; should be used to refer to the subject of a sentence.\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Whom&lt;\/strong&gt; should be used to refer to the object of a sentence.\r\n\r\nWhen in doubt, try this simple trick: If you can replace the word with \u2018he\u2019 or \u2018she,\u2019 use &lt;strong&gt;who&lt;\/strong&gt;. If you can replace it with \u2018him\u2019 or \u2018her,\u2019 use &lt;strong&gt;whom&lt;\/strong&gt;.\r\n\r\nHere are two\u00a0examples:\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Who&lt;\/strong&gt; ate my sandwich? (Did he eat my sandwich? Did she eat my sandwich?)\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Whom&lt;\/strong&gt; should I talk to about labeling food in the refrigerator? (Should I talk to him? Should I talk to her?)\r\n","post_title":"Who vs. Whom: It's Not as Complicated as You Might Think.","post_excerpt":"","post_status":"publish","comment_status":"open","ping_status":"open","post_password":"","post_name":"who-vs-whom-its-not-as-complicated-as-you-might-think","to_ping":"","pinged":"","post_modified":"2015-01-19 18:07:06","post_modified_gmt":"2015-01-20 02:07:06","post_content_filtered":"","post_parent":0,"guid":"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/?p=8411","menu_order":0,"post_type":"post","post_mime_type":"","comment_count":"1","filter":"raw"},{"ID":8387,"post_author":"6","post_date":"2014-09-11 06:02:35","post_date_gmt":"2014-09-11 13:02:35","post_content":"[media]&lt;img class=\"aligncenter size-full wp-image-8389\" src=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/wp-content\/uploads\/2014\/09\/you_cant_read_all_day_if.png\" alt=\"YOu can't read all day if you don't start in the morning.\" width=\"670\" height=\"350\" \/&gt;\r\n\r\n[\/media]\r\n\r\nWe\u00a0love Sunday reading.
