Here are 5 commonly misspelled words that contain double letters:\r\n\r\n1. &lt;strong&gt;Accommodate&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\nDon\u2019t let the double C and double M confuse you! This word can accommodate two of each letter.\r\n\r\n2. &lt;strong&gt;Possession&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\nThis word packed with the letter S. Start off with the word \u2018possess\u2019 instead of the word \u2018poses\u2019 and you\u2019ll be on your way to spelling it correctly.\r\n\r\n3. &lt;strong&gt;Embarrass&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\nHere\u2019s another word with two sets of double letters. Don\u2019t embarrass either double set by leaving them out.\r\n\r\n4. &lt;strong&gt;Committed&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\nTo remember the correct spelling of committed, think about committing to both the double M and double T.\r\n\r\n5. &lt;strong&gt;Misspell&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\nIronically, people often misspell the word misspell. It\u2019s another double double, so don\u2019t forget!\r\n","post_title":"5 Words You Should Start Spelling Correctly ","post_excerpt":"","post_status":"publish","comment_status":"open","ping_status":"open","post_password":"","post_name":"5-words-you-should-start-spelling-correctly","to_ping":"","pinged":"","post_modified":"2015-01-19 18:03:43","post_modified_gmt":"2015-01-20 02:03:43","post_content_filtered":"","post_parent":0,"guid":"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/?p=8195","menu_order":0,"post_type":"post","post_mime_type":"","comment_count":"0","filter":"raw"},{"ID":8185,"post_author":"6","post_date":"2014-09-03 13:43:25","post_date_gmt":"2014-09-03 20:43:25","post_content":"[media]&lt;img src=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/wp-content\/uploads\/2014\/09\/grammar_snob.png\" alt=\"grammar_snob\" width=\"700\" height=\"366\" class=\"aligncenter size-full wp-image-8189\" \/&gt;[\/media]\r\nGrammar snobs love to remind anyone who will listen: You should NEVER end a sentence with a preposition! Luckily for those poor, persecuted prepositions, that the rule isn\u2019t so hard and fast anymore. Here are a few preposition guidelines:\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Don\u2019t end a sentence with a preposition:&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\n\r\n1. In formal writing\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Incorrect:&lt;\/strong&gt; Who were the most articles written about?\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Correct:&lt;\/strong&gt; About whom were the most articles written?\r\n\r\nIn emails, text messages, and notes to friends, ending a sentence with a preposition is informal and accepted. However, if you\u2019re writing a research paper or submitting a business proposal, using formal language is your best option. In those cases, avoid ending sentences with a preposition.\r\n\r\n2. If something is missing\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Incorrect:&lt;\/strong&gt; He walked down the street at a brisk pace, with his waistcoat buttoned against the cold and a jaunty top hat perched atop.\r\nThe preposition 'atop' is missing an object. Let\u2019s try that again:\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Correct:&lt;\/strong&gt; He walked down the street at a brisk pace, with his waistcoat buttoned against the cold and a jaunty top hat perched atop his stately head.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;It\u2019s ok to end a sentence with a preposition:&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\n\r\n1. In informal writing or conversation\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Correct:&lt;\/strong&gt; To whom should I give a high five?\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Also correct:&lt;\/strong&gt; Who should I give a high five to?\r\n\r\nUnless you\u2019re a time traveler from another era, you\u2019ll probably use the second sentence when speaking. Informal language\u00a0is generally accepted in conversation and will likely allow your conversation to flow more smoothly, since your friends won\u2019t be distracted by your perfectly precise sentence construction.\r\n\r\n2. If the preposition is part of an informal phrase\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Correct:&lt;\/strong&gt; Five excited puppies is almost too many to put up with.\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Also correct:&lt;\/strong&gt; A good plate of spaghetti should not be so hard to come by.\r\n\r\nBoth \u2018put up with\u2019 and \u2018hard to come by\u2019 are commonly accepted informal phrases, and it\u2019s ok to end sentences with them. Note, however, that you should avoid these phrases in formal writing.\r\n","post_title":"You've been lied to. Here's why you absolutely can end a sentence with a preposition.","post_excerpt":"","post_status":"publish","comment_status":"open","ping_status":"open","post_password":"","post_name":"youve-been-lied-to-heres-why-you-absolutely-can-end-a-sentence-with-a-preposition","to_ping":"","pinged":"","post_modified":"2015-01-19 18:03:14","post_modified_gmt":"2015-01-20 02:03:14","post_content_filtered":"","post_parent":0,"guid":"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/?p=8185","menu_order":0,"post_type":"post","post_mime_type":"","comment_count":"1","filter":"raw"},{"ID":8167,"post_author":"6","post_date":"2014-09-02 15:07:47","post_date_gmt":"2014-09-02 22:07:47","post_content":"[media]&lt;img class=\"aligncenter size-full wp-image-8169\" src=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/wp-content\/uploads\/2014\/09\/bear_bare__resdesign2.png\" alt=\"bear_bare__resdesign2\" width=\"700\" height=\"366\" \/&gt;[\/media]\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;1. They\u2019re vs. their vs. there&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\n&lt;strong&gt;They\u2019re&lt;\/strong&gt; going to eat &lt;strong&gt;their&lt;\/strong&gt; watermelon over &lt;strong&gt;there&lt;\/strong&gt;.\r\n\r\nThe difference:\r\nThey\u2019re is a contraction of they and are.\r\nTheir is possessive, meaning that those people own the watermelon.\r\nThere is a place, meaning over in that area.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;2. Whole vs. hole&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\nThe &lt;strong&gt;whole&lt;\/strong&gt; group of ants marched around the edge of the &lt;strong&gt;hole&lt;\/strong&gt;.\r\n\r\nThe difference:\r\nWhole means the entirety of something.\r\nA hole is a hollow place or pit that you can fall inside.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;3. Threw vs. through&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\nIn a fit of frustration, he &lt;strong&gt;threw&lt;\/strong&gt; his cell phone &lt;strong&gt;through&lt;\/strong&gt; the open window.\r\n\r\nThe difference:\r\nThrew is the past-tense form of throw, which means to send something quickly through the air.\r\nThrough means from one side of an opening to the other.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;4. Allowed vs. aloud&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\nDuring silent reading time, students are not &lt;strong&gt;allowed&lt;\/strong&gt; to read their books &lt;strong&gt;aloud&lt;\/strong&gt;.\r\n\r\nThe difference:\r\nAllowed means that permission has been granted.\r\nAloud is something verbalized, or said out loud. You can see the word \u201cloud\u201d in the word aloud, which will help you remember the difference.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;5. Break vs. brake&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\n\u201cNow might be a good time to take a &lt;strong&gt;break&lt;\/strong&gt;,\u201d the Driver\u2019s Education teacher said as she stepped on the &lt;strong&gt;brake&lt;\/strong&gt;.\r\n\r\nThe difference:\r\nA break is a rest or pause from an activity.\r\nA brake is a device that slows or stops an action (like the brakes in your car).\r\n","post_title":"5 Homophones People Just Can\u2019t Seem to Keep Straight","post_excerpt":"","post_status":"publish","comment_status":"open","ping_status":"open","post_password":"","post_name":"5-homophones-people-just-cant-seem-to-keep-straight","to_ping":"","pinged":"","post_modified":"2015-01-19 18:02:45","post_modified_gmt":"2015-01-20 02:02:45","post_content_filtered":"","post_parent":0,"guid":"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/?p=8167","menu_order":0,"post_type":"post","post_mime_type":"","comment_count":"2","filter":"raw"},{"ID":8155,"post_author":"6","post_date":"2014-09-02 13:43:44","post_date_gmt":"2014-09-02 20:43:44","post_content":"[media]&lt;img class=\"aligncenter size-full wp-image-8161\" src=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/wp-content\/uploads\/2014\/09\/Similes_are_like_metaphors.png\" alt=\"Similes_are_like_metaphors\" width=\"700\" height=\"366\" \/&gt;[\/media]\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;1. Comparing Sunshine&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Simile:&lt;\/strong&gt; Your smile is like sunshine on a rainy day.\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Metaphor:&lt;\/strong&gt; You are the sunshine of my life.\r\n\r\nIn general, similes are more poetic statements and metaphors are more hyperbolic ones. If you want to flatter someone you can choose either a simile or metaphor, depending on how extreme your feelings are.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;2. Comparing Life&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Simile:&lt;\/strong&gt; Life is like a box of chocolates.\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Metaphor:&lt;\/strong&gt; Life is a highway, and I want to ride it all night long.\r\n\r\nThe words \u201clike\u201d and \u201cas\u201d usually distinguish similes from metaphors. Wondering if something is a simile? Just check for the words \u201clike\u201d or \u201cas\u201d and if you see them, chances are you have a simile.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;3. Comparing Foxes&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Simile:&lt;\/strong&gt; He\u2019s as sly as a fox\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Metaphor:&lt;\/strong&gt; He\u2019s a fox!\r\n\r\nWhen it comes to similes and metaphors, a few small words can change the meaning of a sentence drastically. Choose your words carefully when you\u2019re describing someone or something using a simile or metaphor, or you just might end up saying something you don\u2019t intend!\r\n","post_title":"Rhetorical Devices for Dummies: Making Comparisons","post_excerpt":"","post_status":"publish","comment_status":"open","ping_status":"open","post_password":"","post_name":"rhetorical-devices-for-dummies-making-comparisons","to_ping":"","pinged":"","post_modified":"2015-01-19 18:02:09","post_modified_gmt":"2015-01-20 02:02:09","post_content_filtered":"","post_parent":0,"guid":"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/?p=8155","menu_order":0,"post_type":"post","post_mime_type":"","comment_count":"3","filter":"raw"},{"ID":8139,"post_author":"6","post_date":"2014-09-01 07:16:40","post_date_gmt":"2014-09-01 14:16:40","post_content":"[media]&lt;img class=\"aligncenter size-full wp-image-8141\" src=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/wp-content\/uploads\/2014\/09\/eat-your-dinner.png\" alt=\"&quot;Eat your dinner&quot; is not the same as &quot;Eat . You're dinner.&quot;\" width=\"700\" height=\"366\" \/&gt;[\/media]\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;1. Eat your dinner. vs Eat. You\u2019re dinner. &lt;\/strong&gt;\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Normal:&lt;\/strong&gt; &lt;em&gt;Eat your dinner.&lt;\/em&gt;\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Cannibalistic:&lt;\/strong&gt; &lt;em&gt;Eat. You're dinner.&lt;\/em&gt;\r\n\r\nThat apostrophe (') is important! There are often a lot of hilarious (sometimes scary) sentences that come about when people confuse \"your\" and \"you're.\" To avoid looking like a creep, it's best to remember that &lt;strong&gt;your&lt;\/strong&gt; is the possessive form of &lt;strong&gt;you.&lt;\/strong&gt; It functions just like &lt;strong&gt;my&lt;\/strong&gt; does in relation to &lt;strong&gt;me.&lt;\/strong&gt; If you get confused by that, you can memorize &lt;strong&gt;you're as the contraction (short form) of \"you are.\"&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\nYour = the possessive form of you\r\nYou're = the short form of you are.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;2. Let\u2019s eat, Grandma. vs. Let\u2019s eat Grandma. &lt;\/strong&gt;\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Normal:&lt;\/strong&gt; &lt;em&gt;Let's eat, Grandma.&lt;\/em&gt;\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Cannibalistic:&lt;\/strong&gt; &lt;em&gt;Let's eat Grandma.&lt;\/em&gt;\r\n\r\nThe comma here is critical. The comma tells the reader that Granny isn't the object of the verb \"eat.\" That is, with the comma, Granny isn't on the main course tonight. The function of the comma here is to show that the sentence \"let's eat\" is directed to an audience, Grandma.\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;3. I love cooking, my dogs, and my family. vs. I love cooking my dogs and my family.&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Normal:&lt;\/strong&gt; &lt;em&gt;I love cooking, my dogs, and my family.&lt;\/em&gt;\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Cannibalistic: &lt;\/strong&gt;&lt;em&gt;I love cooking my dogs and my family.&lt;\/em&gt;\r\n\r\nSimilar to number two, the commas here keep the nouns \"dogs\" and \"family\" from becoming unintentional objects of the verb \"cooking.\" In general, use commas to break up the items in lists.\r\n","post_title":"3 Punctuation Mistakes That Can Make You Look Like A Cannibal","post_excerpt":"","post_status":"publish","comment_status":"open","ping_status":"open","post_password":"","post_name":"3-punctuation-mistakes-that-can-make-you-look-like-a-cannibal","to_ping":"","pinged":"","post_modified":"2015-01-19 18:01:32","post_modified_gmt":"2015-01-20 02:01:32","post_content_filtered":"","post_parent":0,"guid":"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/?p=8139","menu_order":0,"post_type":"post","post_mime_type":"","comment_count":"2","filter":"raw"},{"ID":8125,"post_author":"6","post_date":"2014-08-27 03:45:59","post_date_gmt":"2014-08-27 10:45:59","post_content":"At the 2014 Emmy Awards, Chris Hardwick humorously addresses Internet trolls and publicly clarifies the proper use of \"you're.\" #HardwickSmash\r\n \r\nAll we can say is: \"Thank you, Mr. Hardwick.\"\r\n\r\nWhat other grammar lessons would you like to have taught to Internet trolls?  \r\n\r\n[vid]\/\/www.youtube.com\/embed\/QrXiuVMu-Fg[\/vid]\r\n\r\nFootage from NBC and the Emmy Awards. ","post_title":"At the Emmys, Chris Hardwick Schools Internet Trolls in Grammar ","post_excerpt":"","post_status":"publish","comment_status":"open","ping_status":"open","post_password":"","post_name":"at-the-emmys-chris-hardwick-schools-internet-trolls-in-grammar","to_ping":"","pinged":"","post_modified":"2014-08-28 07:01:56","post_modified_gmt":"2014-08-28 14:01:56","post_content_filtered":"","post_parent":0,"guid":"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/?p=8125","menu_order":0,"post_type":"post","post_mime_type":"","comment_count":"0","filter":"raw"},{"ID":8101,"post_author":"6","post_date":"2014-08-22 07:59:36","post_date_gmt":"2014-08-22 14:59:36","post_content":"[media]&lt;img class=\"aligncenter wp-image-8237\" src=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/wp-content\/uploads\/2014\/08\/saw-seen_new_card-copy.png\" alt=\"For the love of good grammar, you saw\u2014not seen\u2014the new show last night. \" width=\"675\" height=\"353\" \/&gt;\r\n[\/media]\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;1) I seen vs. I saw&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\nIncorrect: \"&lt;strong&gt;I seen&lt;\/strong&gt; the movie last week.\"\r\nCorrect: \"&lt;strong&gt;I saw&lt;\/strong&gt; the movie last week.\"\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;2) I been vs. I have been&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\nIncorrect: \"&lt;strong&gt;I been&lt;\/strong&gt; just about to start the reading.\"\r\n\r\n5) &lt;strong&gt;I would of vs. I would have&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\nIncorrect: \"&lt;strong&gt;I would of&lt;\/strong&gt; gone with you if &lt;strong&gt;I knew&lt;\/strong&gt;.\"\r\nCorrect:\"&lt;strong&gt;I would have&lt;\/strong&gt; (would've) gone with you if &lt;strong&gt;I had known&lt;\/strong&gt;.\"\r\n\r\n&lt;strong&gt;Bonus:&lt;\/strong&gt; Be careful with past simple (I knew) and past perfect (I had known) constructions. Though they have similar meanings, it's best to use the tense that matches the tense in the first part of the sentence (I would have). This happens to you too, doesn't it?","post_excerpt":"","post_status":"publish","comment_status":"open","ping_status":"open","post_password":"","post_name":"hate-to-mispronounce-words-read","to_ping":"","pinged":"","post_modified":"2014-06-24 05:35:17","post_modified_gmt":"2014-06-24 12:35:17","post_content_filtered":"","post_parent":0,"guid":"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/?p=6885","menu_order":0,"post_type":"post","post_mime_type":"","comment_count":"0","filter":"raw"},{"ID":6865,"post_author":"6","post_date":"2014-06-23 07:28:28","post_date_gmt":"2014-06-23 14:28:28","post_content":"&lt;img src=\"http:\/\/www.grammarly.com\/blog\/wp-content\/uploads\/2014\/06\/shipshippingships.png\" alt=\"hyphen needed example\" width=\"700\" height=\"366\" class=\"aligncenter size-full wp-image-6867\" \/&gt;\r\n\r\nA hyphen can connect two (or possibly three\u2026 or four if you want to get really creative) words and turn them into a single adjective to modify a noun. The words can be nouns, adjectives, verbs or adverbs. Sometimes the same words used separately, and the meaning is entirely different than if the words are hyphenated. For instance, take a look at these two sentences, one of which uses a compound modifier:\r\n\r\nIncorrect: &lt;strong&gt;The table was covered by twenty odd books.&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\nCorrect: &lt;strong&gt;The table was covered by twenty-odd books.&lt;\/strong&gt;\r\nIn the first sentence, the books are being described as odd; in the second sentence, there are approximately twenty books covering the table. The second sentence uses a compound modifier.\r\n\r\nMore correct examples: \r\nWant some chocolate-chocolate-chip ice cream?\r\nWatch out!           status     : true,
          xfbml      : true,
          version    : 'v2.3'
        });
        FB.Event.subscribe('edge.create', function(href, widget) {
         $(document.body).trigger('Blog.LikedPage')
        });
      };

      (function(d, s, id){
         var js, fjs = d.getElementsByTagName(s)[0];
         if (d.getElementById(id)) {return;}
         js = d.createElement(s); js.id = id;
         js.src = "//connect.facebook.net/en_US/sdk.js";
         fjs.parentNode.insertBefore(js, fjs);
       }(document, 'script', 'facebook-jssdk'));
  </script>

       <header>
        <h1 class="title">

          #FunnyFriday: Yes, this is a cat in a shark suit cruising around on a vacuum
         </a>
        </h1>
